# 00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery

## Page 1

I I I I ARCHEOLOGICAL INVESTIGATIONS I IN THE DENTON CREEK FLOODPLAIN I Data Recovery Excavations at 41DL270, Denton and Dallas Counties, Texas I Edited By I Dana Anthony and David 0. Brown I with contributions by Dana Anthony, David 0. Brown, S. Christopher Caran, I J. Phillip Dering, Wolf A. Gose, Marilyn A. Masson, and Raymond W. Neck I David 0. ## Page 2

I ABSTRACT I Excavations at 41DL270 in Denton and Dallas Counties were conducted in the fall of 1992 and the spring of 1993 by archeologists from Hicks & Company. The investigations, undertaken at the I request of Brazos Electric Power Cooperative, Inc., of Waco, Texas, were carried out to recover archeological data from the site which would be impacted by electric power transmission line construction. Site 41DL270, which was originally reported by Dallas area avocational archeologist Bill I Young, was noted during the cultural resources survey of a proposed transmission line from the existing Coppell Substation to a proposed electric substation at Flower Mound. A series of backhoe trenches excavated at the site during the survey phase suggested that, although considerably disturbed, the site did I contain intact prehistoric cultural deposits. A data recovery plan was developed by Hicks & Company in conjunction with the Texas Historical Commission and the Advisory Council for Historic Preservation. This plan was implemented beginning in the fall of 1992. The plan, which is repeated as part of the I research design of this report, called for sample excavation units in and around archeological features eroding out of the cutbank of a large artificial drainage ditch which had been cut through the site years earlier. Emphasis was placed upon sampling the chronostratigraphic variability in cultural expressions I related to these features rather than on the opening of wide-area excavation units. Overall, 51 features were studied on the surface or in excavation units, with collections. made from a number of these. The collections show a site which is rich in faunal materials but rather poor in traditional archeological artifact I remains such as lithics or ceramics. No ceramics were recovered during the recent investigations and only 138 lithic artifacts were collected, including some from the surface, despite the presence of numerous gravel quarries in the immediate area which suggest that lithic resources may be available in I the local Trinity River terraces. Faunal materials recovered from the site were studied and provided some suggestion of changes in resource procurement systems or availability through time. This data is supported by stable isotope investigations undertaken as part of the study. I I I I I I I I I I

## Page 16

I I ACKNOWLEDGMENTS Many individuals were instrumental in the successful completion of this project. Foremost among these are the various personnel from Brazos Electric Power Cooperative. The environmental I section contract administrator, Dave McDaniel, was particularly helpful; his cooperation and assistance in facilitating various aspects of the project were extraordinary. Mike Sartain, right-of-way agent, was also extremely helpful with field communications and logistics. Much appreciation is also due the City I of Grapevine Parks Department, especially Ron Emrich and Joe Moore, for permission to excavate on City property. We are especially grateful to the Parks Department for their loan of a water pump when our second pump failed only days after excavations began. Dan Prikryl, formerly of the Texas Historical I Commission, and the original regulatory connection for the project at that agency, had long since taken a personal interest in the site and gave freely of his time to assist and advise us, even after leaving to take a new job with the Texas Water Development Board. His efforts to protect this site were a_key factor I in its preservation. Dr. David 0. Brown served as principal investigator and worked at the site for several days I during each of the field trips, providing invaluable assistance. Due to the many delays caused by the weather, the small field crew shifted personnel a number of times. Dana Anthony, the project archeologist, Mike Davis, and Terry Jones were present during all phases of the field work. Other field I personnel included Kathy Weedman, David Driver, Luc Litwinionek, Pamela Weiss, Nancy Beaman, Mark Boatwright, David Villarreal, Christina Leshly-Cojeen, Dale Lynch, and Eric Oksanen. Volunteers during these shifts included Dan Prikryl, then of the Texas Historical Commission, Brenda Whorton, and Paul and Jan Lorrain. Many other interested and interesting people visited the site and assisted us with I various aspects of the excavations, including Bill and Bobby Jean Young and Brenda Whorton, from the Dallas Archeological Society (DAS), Linda Lindsey from Southern Methodist University, Brian Jameson and Johnny Byers, both members of the Tarrant County Archeological Society (fCAS), and Duane Peter I from GeoMarine. Special thanks are extended to the Lorrains, who helped in more ways than we can detail here, and to Bill Young, who provided considerable information about the site and loaned us materials which he had collected there in previous years. I When the principal investigator and project archeologist returned to the site after the project had officially ended in November of 1993, members of both the Dallas Archeological Society and Tarrant I County Archeological Society aided the researchers in these efforts to recover a final series of samples from the site. Volunteer activity was coordinated by Jan Lorrain. In addition, the Lorrains, through the Dallas Archeological Society, were able to furnish us with a transit and stadia rod for the remainder of I mapping to be done. This mapping was conducted by Paul Lorrain and David Brown. Members of the two archeological societies who lent a hand included Vic Armstrong, Ann and Gary Blakely, Jim Blanton, Mark Cohen, Brian Jameson, Deanna and Stuart Johnston, Jan and Paul Lorrain, Matt Marian, Georgia I A. and Louis M. Selron. Much appreciation is extended to all of these people for their help as well as enthusiasm. I Dr. Christopher Caran served as project geomorphologist. Dr. Raymond Neck analyzed the molluscan remains from the site, Dr. Marilyn Masson analyzed the vertebrate faunal materials, and Phil Dering of the Palynology Laboratory at Texas A&M University contributed the archeobotanical analysis. I Dr. Wolf Gose, of the Department of Geological Sciences at the University of Texas at Austin, took an interest in the project and conducted paleomagnetic studies at the site. We wish to thank all of these specialists for their help in piecing together the parts of this study. In addition to those noted above, I Harold Krueger, Dave Drucker, and Marshall Otter of Geochron, Inc., more than earned their fees, xv I I

## Page 18

I INTRODUCTION I Dana Anthony and David 0. Brown Buried in the floodplain of Denton Creek, 41DL270 remained undisturbed for some 1000 years. I Topsoil quarrying activities in 1984 uncovered mussel shell, animal bones, pottery, and a few projectile points. These finds were first recognized and reported by Dallas Archeological Society (DAS) member Bill Young. Two years later, a large drainage ditch was dug adjacent to the creek, revealing the full I extent of the site. At least two cultural zones were apparent in the slopes of the ditch containing mussel shell concentrations, sandstone hearths, and scattered bone and lithic debris. Young reported this to the U.S. Army Corp of Engineers archeologist, Robert F. "Skipper" Scott IV, who along with Robert I Burton, formally recorded the site. Paul and Jan Lorrain, also members of the DAS, spent considerable time at 41DL270, updating the site form in 1989 and excavating several hearths. In September 1991, Dan Prikryl of the Texas Historical Commission (fHC) led a team including Young, the LorraJns, Brenda I Whorton, Ray Hubbard, and other members of the DAS in mapping the site as part of National Register of Historic Places (NRHP) nomination procedures. It was at this time that Hicks & Company archeologists conducting a cultural resources survey for a proposed transmission line first came across I the site. This survey had been initiated as the first stage of a cultural resources impact assessment for Brazos Electric Power Cooperative, Inc., (BEPC) of Waco, who were planning to construct and maintain I the proposed Wilkerson Electric Transmission Line between the existing electric substation at Coppell and a proposed new substation at Flower Mound. The proposed transmission line crossed Denton Creek near an existing gas pipeline right-of-way about midway along the site's location on the creek and turned I to follow the site's course for several hundred meters. Since the THC was actively trying to nominate the site to the NRHP at the time, it was assumed that the site would be determined eligible for the Register; all of the materials observed at the site during this phase supported this assumption. I Because of the clear importance of the site, discussions were immediately undertaken with BEPC regarding the site and possible impacts resulting from proposed transmission line construction. Neither I drastic alteration of the proposed route nor abandonment of plans for construction of the line were considered feasible given electric service needs in the community, but several potentially viable options were considered. These included: I 1) Altering the route of the line so as to follow the gas pipeline east toward the Coppell substation. This would greatly reduce the number of structures on the site. I 2) Altering the route of the line such that the line would parallel the ditch farther from the creek. Assuming that the site is associated with the modern creek channel, this would I increase the distance from the creek and thus minimize impact to the potentially more significant parts of the site which should be located closer to the creek. I 3) Leave the proposed line in place and assume responsibility for whatever archeological mitigation might be necessary. I Options 1 and 2 were found not to be feasible for a number of management, design, and fiscal reasons. Option 1 would have bisected the property adjacent to the creek and ruined plans for commercial development being entertained by the landowner at the time of the investigations. In addition, such a major change would have probably required a new Certificate of Convenience and I 1 I I


![Image 1 - Page 18](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p18_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 18*


---

## Page 19

I Necessity from the Public Utility Commission, entailing a long delay in the ultimate construction of a line whose construction was considered urgent because of the rapid population growth of the area. Option I 2 was not so drastic as 1, but moving the line eastward would have rendered any land between the transmission line and the ditch undevelopable and thus force BEPC to acquire the original right-of-way plus an additional 100 to 150 feet to the east. The cost of such right-of-way acquisition would have been I exorbitant and, given the lack of knowledge of the floodplain and archeological site formation, there was no guarantee that such a route would indeed avoid the site. This left the third option, archeological mitigation through data recovery procedures, as the only viable option. I Since it was considered completely impossible to alter the original route, which effectively took the line through the greatest apparent density of buried cultural materials at the site, a second phase of I investigations was planned as part of the survey. In March of 1992, archeologists returned to the site and conducted a series of ten backhoe trench excavations. These were dug to determine the extent and condition of the site near the points where the structures were to be placed. Careful examination of the I trench profiles, along with the aid of the visible features on the slopes nearby, identified what appeared to be several cultural zones represented by tiny flecks of charcoal, shell, and to some extent, bone. I Preliminary observations at the site, based on surface observations during survey and limited trenching activities, suggested that this open campsite was a limited or short-term residential site with ample evidence of food procurement and processing. Numerous clusters of burned rock observed along the eroded slopes suggested the presence of hearths for food processing. Deer bone was in evidence as I were numerous small scatters of mussel shell. Careful examination of the exposed remains suggested considerable dependence upon resources from adjacent Denton Creek. Although no structures were identified, burned daub was reported from Bill Young's collection {Prikryl 1992). From what was I already known from the previous investigations at the site, little was expected in terms of lithic tool and debitage recovery. Some lithic material was often seen close by the hearths or mussel shell features or were part of features which encompassed all types of cultural materials. After the last visits to the site, I however, it was felt the site contained more lithic material than was previously thought, although it was by no means abundant. Despite the damage to the site, there was ample evidence that portions of the site remained intact. The small amount of diagnostic artifacts (lithic and ceramic) collected from the site fall I within the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric I and II periods as defined by Prikryl (1990). Three radiocarbon dates on bulk soil collected from the testing phase, dating between 1250 and 3240 B.P., essentially support this interpretation. I Inspection and documentation of the backhoe trenches revealed well-preserved paleosols and strata well suited to special studies. In addition, preservation of faunal remains at the site seemed I excellent. With an emphasis on paleoenvironment reconstruction, the following research topics were · addressed as part of the investigations at 41DL270 and are discussed in detail in the research design section of this report. I 1) Paleoenvironmental reconstruction, including fauna/flora, climate, and geomor phology; I 2) Site formation processes; 3) Settlement pattern, including feature types and distribution (activity areas), as I well as length of occupation; I 2 I I

## Page 20

I 4) Subsistence/seasonality; and I 5) Cultural chronology. I Data recovery excavations at the site resulted in the tentative definition of nine cultural zones based on stratigraphy, absolute elevations, and the distribution of cultural materials. These occupation zones, which represent frequent reutilization of the site during the Late Holocene, have been dated I between about 850 and 4600 B.P. Special studies suggested changing patterns of faunal resource usage through time. Archeomagnetic studies were able to demonstrate soil strata that correlated with cultural acfivity at the site as well as whether certain burned features were in situ or not. The molluscan analysis I revealed how many different species were utilized during the occupations of 41DL270. Most of these species are still present in the drainages today. Stable isotope studies of freshwater shell were also conducted, with promising results for paleoclimatic reconstruction. I The data recovery phase of investigations at the site was conducted in four increments over a period of six months (November 2 to November 11, 1992; November 16 to November 19, 1992; I December 11 to December 13, 1992; and April 26 to May 5, 1993). Total field time, excluding travel, was 122 person days. The long field duration was due to many cancellations caused by rainy weather. In November 1993, David Brown, Principal Investigator, and Dana Anthony, Project Archeologist, returned to the site for two days primarily to collect samples from the gully area, which was underwater I during the last field session. They were aided in these efforts by members of the DAS and TCAS. The first part of this report of the data recovery investigation phase follows traditional I archeological report formats, with sections on the environmental and cultural setting following the introduction. These are followed by a special section which summarizes the sites previously recorded along Denton Creek prepared by the senior author/editor. The subsequent section on research design is I drawn from the data recovery plan prepared for the THC and Advisory Council for Historic Preservation. Following a brief section on methodology, individual sections report the excavation units, the geomorphological investigations, the backhoe trenches, and the features found at the site. A subsequent I chapter on the artifacts focuses on the primary artifact type recovered from the site - lithics. This is followed by a series of chapters on special aspects of material recovery from the site, including sections on faunal material by Marilyn Masson, molluscan remains by Raymond Neck, floral materials by Phil I Dering of the Palynology Laboratory of Texas A&M University, archeomagnetic investigations by Wulf Gose of the Department of Geological Sciences of the University of Texas at Austin, and freshwater shell isotope studies by David 0. Brown, currently at the University of Texas at El Paso. A series of several I concluding chapters, including one which summarizes previous investigations at mussel procurement sites and one summarizing paleoenvironmental studies at 41DL270, are authored in various combinations by Anthony and Brown. ## Page 22

I ENVIRONMENTA L SETTING I Dana Anthony and David O. Brown Site 41DL270 extends along Denton Creek through the northwestern portion of Dallas County I and into the southern portion of Denton County in North-Central Texas (Figure 1). This area lies within the West Gulf Coastal Plain physiographic province as defined by Fenneman (1938). The regional topography is gently rolling with elevations ranging from about 900 feet above mean sea level (amsl) in I the western part of Denton County to about 500 feet amsl in the southeastern part of Dallas County. The region is drained by the Trinity River. While the major North-Central Texas streams such I as the Brazos and Red River do not draw much lateral drainage from the region they flow through, the Trinity, as well as the Paluxy, Leon, and San Gabriel (between the Brazos and Colorado) developed in the upland plains between the valleys of the older and larger streams and serve as the drainage for these I upland prairies. Rivers such as the Trinity, rising close to the western margin of the Grand Prairie, descend rapidly, creating deep pathways which are significantly lower than the upland surface (Hill 1901 :64). Hill stated that the main trunks of what he termed second class streams, including the Trinity I in this definition, are usual Iy interrupted in flow, the water running in deep pools for some stretches of their I courses and again disappearing in dry stony channels. The laterals and sublaterals are dry except in times ofrainfall, when they carry off the surface water. (Hill 1901:64-65) Following Hill's definition, the main trunks would include the Elm Fork, East Fork, and West Fork, I which form the Trinity at their confluence. Denton Creek is a lateral which drains into the Elm Fork. Sublaterals of Denton Creek close to the project area include the southeastward-flowing Baker's Branch, the northeastward-flowing Cottonwood Branch, and several other unnamed tributaries, most flowing south I to southeast. Today the climate in North-Central Texas is humid and subtropical, with hot summers and I generally mild winters. Carr (1967) includes this area within his North-Central Texas climatic division of the Interior Region, which indicates May and September rainfall maxima. Precipitation is distributed throughout the year with an average of 35 inches per year. The heaviest rainfall, about 57 percent of the I annual total, falls between April and September, although July and August are not among the wettest months. Most of the thunderstorms occur in the spring. Average mean temperature is 65 degrees (Coffee et al. 1980; Ford and Pauls 1980). I The area in which 41DL270 is located is underlain by Upper Cretaceous Gulf Series sediments which occur in north-south bands that dip southeasterly toward the Gulf of Mexico. West of Denton I Creek, and the source of alluvial sediments deposited by the creek both prehistorically and historically, is the Woodbine Formation, the lowest and earliest of the Gulf Series. It comprises the remains of an ancient beach, hence the sandy characteristics of its constituent elements. It consists of sandstones, clay, I and shale (Barnes 1988). This formation dictates the particular soil types (mostly sandy loams) that form from the parent material which in turn have fostered the Cross Timbers vegetative zone. I To the east for the most part is the Eagle Ford Formation, the second oldest of the Gulf Series. It consists primarily of shale, sandstone, and limestone (Barnes 1988). The soils formed on it are calcareous clays which underlie the Blackland Prairie vegetative zone. To either side of Denton Creek in the area of 41DL270 lie Pleistocene fluvial terrace deposits consisting of a mixture of gravel, sand, I 5 I I

## Page 24

I silt, and clays. Thin soils characterize these terraces and archeological remains are usually mixed, at or I near the surface, lacking stratigraphic context. Along the creek itself, Holocene alluvial deposits consist of gravels, sand, silt, silty clay, and organic matter (Barnes 1988). I Robert T. Hill (1887) conducted one of the earliest available studies of the geology of this region and, in particular, of the Cross Timbers. The Cross Timbers was subdivided into the Western or Upper and Eastern or Lower sections, which were divided by prairie. Both of these Cross Timber zones run I in north-south bands covering approximately the same distance. The western edge of the Eastern or Lower Cross Timbers is clearly demarcated from the prairie to the west by a rise in elevation. Hill pointed out that the Lower Cross Timbers has a more fertile soil (sandy loam), resulting in a greater I variety of flora, and trees which reached a greater size than those associated with the more sterile sands of the Upper Cross Timbers (Hill 1887:292, 303). The absence of trees in the prairie between the two he ascribed to the inability of the soils to retain enough moisture and to an excess of carbonates (Hill I 1887:303). Perhaps most importantly, Hill correctly identified the origin of the Cross Timbers. He claimed I the chief agent of surface modification of the Cross Timbers (within the region he termed "Central Denuded Region") was subaerial erosion due to the underlying sandstones and clays, which in turn rest upon Cretaceous shales and limestone in the Lower Cross Timbers and Prairies to the west. The I sandstone strata dip below this limestone strata in the Upper Cross Timbers (Hill 1887:295,301). Earlier theories stated that the Cross Timbers were the result of ancient sea inlets, rivers, or lake beds (Hill 1887:292), not arenaceous sandy belts created by subaerial erosion of marine origin. I Hill also published another work on the prairies and Cross-Timbers for the United States Geological Survey (Hill 1901). In it he described the soils characteristics of each regime, and presented details of the Black and Grand Prairies and their subdivisions. He also reiterated his conclusions that the I prairies and Cross Timbers owed their morphology, soil, and vegetation to their underlying geological strata. While the clayey calcareous soils of the prairies are favorable for grasses, the porous sandy soils of the Cross Timbers favor forest growth. I Ellis Shuler was another geologist important in the early studies of the geology of the area. In one publication on the geology of Dallas County, he speculated that the Trinity River, which he described I as a slow-moving stream, has a smaller volume presently than during the Pleistocene based on apparently smaller size particles than those carried in the past, and a small stream size compared to its floodplain (Shuler 1918:25). While this speculation has considerable merit, he also stated the depth of the Trinity I River floodplain (at Dallas) was only a little more than 4 meters (Shuler 1918:25), a fact that Perring (1986, 1989a, 1990), among others, has demonstrated to be erroneous. Additionally, he attributed the gravels and sands within the Trinity drainage system to the Comanche and Carboniferous formations of I the western Cross Timbers and the Rolling Plains west of Dallas (Shuler 1918:28). Although there may be a small contribution from the upstream Comanchean and Pennsylvanian limestones, these gravels have since been shown to be quartzites and cherts derived from the Ogallala Formation of the Texas Panhandle I (Byrd 1971; Menzer and Slaughter 1971; Chris Caran, personal communication 1994). The soil association at 41DL270 is the Trinity-Frio, which includes deep clayey soils found on I the floodplains (Ford and Pauls 1980). The soil series mapped at 41DL270 are Frio Silty Clay and Trinity Clay. Frio Silty Clay is formed in recent alluvium on the floodplain. It consists of a brown silty clay surface layer which overlies a dark gray brown silty clay about 2 feet in depth. Extending to a depth of about 42 inches is a very dark grayish brown silty clay. Below this is a grayish brown silty clay to I 7 I I

## Page 25

I a depth of 60 inches. All these layers are moderately alkaline. The soil is well drained with moderately slow permeability and runoff (Ford and Pauls 1980:23). Trinity clay, occasionally flooded, are deep I clayey soils that have also formed in recent alluvium. It is a very dark gray clay about 60 inches thick and moderately alkaline. This particular soil is rather poorly drained with slow runoff and very slow permeability (Ford and Pauls 1980:39). I The vast majority of Denton County lies within the Eastern Cross Timbers while most of Dallas County, to the east, lies within the Blackland Prairie (Hatch et al. 1990). The portion of Denton Creek I in which 41DL270 is located straddles these two vegetation zones. The site's location afforded any occupants diverse ecotones from which to exploit species common to woodlands, grasslands, and the riparian environment along the creek itself. It is not known how closely the prehistoric ecotones at any I one time mirrored the modern ones (before Anglo settlement and development disturbed so much of this region), although the vegetation associated with the Eastern and Western Cross Timbers and Grand Prairie and the Blackland Prairie is dictated by the underlying geological units and the soils formed from I them (Hill 1887). Soils govern which types of vegetation can be supported by the constituent nutrients they contain. As the underlying geology has not changed, the climate and climate changes, particularly rainfall, played an important part in initiating or altering the growth of these vegetative zones, which in I turn would have an affect on the faunal species associated with each. However, this interplay is very complex and rainfall alone can not account for the development or perpetuation of these zones. Hill (1887) described the soils of the Eastern Cross Timbers and the Western Cross Timbers as I arenaceous; however, the Eastern Cross Timbers soils are ferruginous and more fertile than the Western Cross Timbers soil, which contains 97 percent insoluble silica (Hill 1887:293). The Eastern Cross Timbers soils afford larger tree growth as well as greater species diversity. Another example of the I importance of soil type to vegetation communities is that of Bruner (1931) in Dyksterhuis (1948:330), who refers to the Cross Timbers of Oklahoma, a region which can be favorably compared with that in Texas: "Climatically the area should be dominated by grasses but the open, porous soil permits the I growth of trees and, in places, turns the balance decidedly in their favor." Although rainfall may not be regarded as sufficient for such tree growth in the Cross Timbers, the soil's capacity to hold water is the overriding factor in the development and continuation of this zone. I Tharp (1926) originally characterized the Eastern Cross Timbers region as an oak-hickory forest. In looking at the combined Eastern and Western Cross Timbers, Bruner (1931) later described these I regions as an oak-hickory savannah. Later researchers, including Weaver and Clements (1938) and Dyksterhuis (1948) emphasized the savannah aspect of the region, suggesting that the endemic dominants are grasses. There are a number of recent summary characterizations of the Cross Timbers vegetation, I including those by Yates and Perring (1986:18) and Story (1990a:15). Most of the following description of the primary vegetation types of the Cross Timbers is taken from Hatch et al. (1990: 13). Dominant trees of the Cross Timbers include post oak (Quercus stellata), black jack oak (Quercus marilandica), I and hickory (Carya texana). Riparian species include pecan (Carya illinioensis), elm (Ulmus sp.), ash (Fraxinus sp.), cottonwood (Populus deltoides), sycamore (Platanus occidentalis), black willow (Salix nigra), and soapberry (Sapindus drummondii). The dominant grasses of this zone includes little bluestem (Schizachyrium scoparium var.frequens), big bluestem (Andropogon gerardii), indiangrass (Sorghastrum I nutans), switchgrass (Panicum virgatum), Canada wildrye (Elymus canadensis), lesser amounts of sideoats grama (Bouteloua curtipendula), blue grama (B. gracilis), hairy grama (B. hirsuta), Texas wintergrass (Stipa leucotricha), and buffalograss (Buchloe dactyloides). Species that were once plentiful include I western ragweed (Ambrosia cumanensis), littlesnout sedge (Carex microrhyncha), heath aster (Aster I 8 I I

## Page 26

I ericoides), gayfeathers (Liatris sp.), lespedeza (Lespedeza sp.), sageworts (Artemisia sp.), and tephrosias I (Tephrosia sp.). Common shrubs and vines include skunkbush (Rhus aromatica var.flabelliformis), saw greenbriar (Smilax bona-nox), bumelia (Bumelia lanuginosa), and poison ivy (Toxicodendron radicans). I What Hill (1901) referred to as the Black Prairie is synonymous with what is today known as the Blackland Prairie. He described its boundaries as the Red River on the north, the Eastern Cross Timbers on the west, the East Texas Timber Belt to the east, and at its narrow southern extent, the I Colorado River (Hill 1901:65). Derived from calcareous marl and chalky limestones, the soils are rich in lime. The action of humic acid in the decomposition of vegetative matter is likely the cause for the black color (Hill 1901 :66). Hill also noted that the difference between the Blackland Prairie and the I Grand Prairie to the west is that "the Grand Prairie is established upon firm, persistent bands of limestones, which are harder than the underlying clay substructure of the Black Prairie region, and which, under erosion, result in more extensive stratum plains and more angular cliffs and slopes" (HilJ 1901:72). I The Blackland Prairie's calcareous clays support a different vegetation community than the Cross Timbers sandy soils. I The native vegetation of the Blackland Prairie was a mixed tall and mid-grass prairie with trees confined to the streams. Various early attempts at reconstruction of the original Blackland Prairie were made by Weaver and Clements (1938) and Dyksterhuis (1946). According to the most recent I characterization (Hatch et al. 1990), little bluestem, big bluestem, indiangrass, tall dropseed (Sporobolus asper var. asper), and Silveus dropseed (S. silveanus) are the climax dominant species. Other important species listed include sideoats grama, hairy grama, silver bluestem (Bothriochloa laguroides), tall I dropseed (Sporobolus asper), and Texas wintergrass. Common forbs include asters, prairie bluet (Hedyotis nigricans var. nigricans), prairie clover (Dalea sp.), and late coneflower (Rudbeclda serotina). Mesquite, huisache (Acacia smallii), oak, and elm are invaders on poor rangeland and abandoned I cropland. Along the drainages, oak, elm, cottonwood (Populus sp.), and native pecan are still common (Hatch et al. 1990: 12). Site 41DL270 lies within Blair's (1950) Texan biotic province. This province is situated between I the grasslands of the Balconian and Kansan biotic provinces to the west and the forests of the Austroriparian province to the east (Dice 1943; Blair 1950). It is this geographic provenience integrating the forest and grassland species which defines the Texan province. No endemic species of vertebrates I is to be found in this province. Austroriparian species are usually restricted to the oak-hickory forests, floodplains, and to peat bogs and marshes (Blair 1950: 101). The grassland species are basically confined to the prairies. At the time of Blair's publication (1950) he listed 49 species of mammals, 2 land turtles, I 16 species of lizards, 18 anuran, 5 urodeles and 39 species of snakes. Deer tends to be a dominant fauna in archeological contexts, with turtle also very common (Brown 1989). Historically, bison do not appear to have been very common in the North-Central Texas region throughout much of prehistory (with the I possible exception of the period after A.O. 1200), probably due to the absence of the preferred short grasses (Lynott 1979; Huebner 1990). I Yates (1986:25-30) lists the many fish species, birds, reptiles, and amphibians common to the upper Trinity River. Many species of mussel shell have also been reported from archeological projects and are discussed elsewhere in this report. Most of these species are still present in the streams today. ## Page 28

I CULTURAL SETTING I Dana Anthony and David 0. Brown CULTURAL CHRONOWGY I The survey area lies within the North-Central Texas culture area as originally defined by Suhm et al. (1954). The chronology in this report follows that proposed by Prikryl (1990): Paleoindian (pre I 8500 B.P.), Early Archaic (8500-6000 B.P.), Middle Archaic (6000-3500 B.P.), Late Archaic (3500-1250 B.P.), Late Prehistoric I (1250-750 B.P.), and Late Prehistoric II (750-250 B.P.). In addition, Prikryl's synthesis of the Lower Elm Fork prehistory (1990) is the major source used here for cultural period I attributes in North-Central Texas, as the present study area lies within his original area of investigation. Considerable evidence has been recovered for Paleoindian occupation of the North-C~ntral Texas I area, but most of this evidence is in the form of surface artifact finds which generally provide little information other than the presence of such peoples in the area. Nonetheless, important patterns have emerged from the study of these finds (Prikryl 1990:66-69). I Most researchers, regardless of the region, agree that overall the Paleoindian period was characterized by a low population base of wide-ranging nomadic bands. In the Lower Elm Fork region, I the Paleoindian period is represented by projectile point types such as Qovis, Dalton, Plainview, Midland, Scottsbluff, San Patrice, and Golondrina. The inclusion of projectile point types from differing geographic locales (Dalton from northeast Texas and San Patrice from East Texas) as well as the fact that the majority of all the projectile points are made from non-local chert substantiates a model of highly I mobile groups of people utilizing this region (Prikryl 1990:66). To the southeast at Richland Creek, investigators also point out a wide-ranging nomadic adaption reflected by high quality, non-local chert as the material of choice (McGregor 1987: 192). Results of investigations at Aquilla Lake in the Brazos I River Drainage to the southwest, suggest the overlap of western and eastern Paleoindian traditions in the region, based on the occurrence of exotic projectile point types (Brown 1987a:1-7). I These peoples have also been described as nomadic big-game hunters and many of the earliest sites of this period in Texas are associated with now extinct large mammals of the Pleistocene era, such as mammoth (Mammutus columbz), camel (Came/ops hesternus), horse (Equus mexicanus), and bison I (Bison antiquus). After the extinction of B. antiquus, the modem bison (B. bison) became important toward the end of the period. However, in North-Central Texas, the only clearly associated large game animal so far isolated from a Paleoindian component is the remains of what may be one bison at the I Aubrey site (Perring 1989). Instead, smaller fauna have been documented at the Aubrey site during this period, including deer, rabbits, squirrel, turtle, bird, and snake (Perring 1989b). Although the lack of large fauna! remains may in part be due to the very limited number of excavations at Paleoindian sites, I other researchers have noted that Paleoindian sites may exhibit a more diverse fauna than that associated with a strictly defined big game hunting lifestyle (Black and McGraw 1986:36-37). I In the region of the survey, the vast majority of recorded Paleoindian sites are located on terraces with about two-thirds on tributaries and one-third on the Elm Fork channel itself (Prikryl 1990:68). This bias toward terrace sites may be a reflection of the lack of investigation of deeply buried floodplain sites. Sites buried within the floodplain are likely to go undetected during traditional survey unless the site has I been eroded or exposed in some manner. Although this is particularly true of older sites, sites from all periods may lie undiscovered beneath the surface of the floodplain. I 11 I I


![Image 1 - Page 28](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p28_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 28*


---

## Page 29

I Providing a firm date for the end of the Paleoindian period is difficult, primarily because of the gradual warming trend which marks the end of the Pleistocene. Clearly, however, the warming climates I at the end of the Pleistocene can be associated with noticeable cultural change. The later Archaic cultures, traditionally dated as beginning around 8500 B.P., were distinguished from the earlier Paleoindian cultures by increasingly regionalized traditions in the former with a perceived broadening I exploitation of the available resource base. As generally understood, these peoples began to settle into their environment, becoming familiar with the resources of the regions which they inhabited. This is a trend which must have begun during the latter part of the Paleoindian stage and continued throughout I most of the Archaic. The Early Archaic is approximately coeval with the long warm postglacial period variously I termed the Altithermal or Hypsithermal. Prikryl's synthesis of the Early Archaic (1990:69-71) reports pollen studies from the Washita River in southwest Oklahoma which indicate grasslands were dominant during the Early Archaic and the Cross Timbers likely did not develop until after about 4500 B.P. I Although no discrete Early Archaic components have been isolated, the projectile points from this period mirror the Paleoindian lack of regionalization, sharing attributes with east, central, south, and southwest Texas, as well as south Oklahoma; they are mostly made from non-local chert. Furthermore, as with the I preceding period, most of the known sites are located on terraces, probably reflecting a lack of floodplain investigations. While the Early Archaic period retained many technological similarities to the Paleoindian I period, the Middle Archaic hunter-gatherers are increasingly distinctive. In North-Central Texas, the Carrollton Focus, as originally defined by Crook and Harris (1952), encompasses both of these periods. Subsequent investigations have shown that it may be possible to identify distinct Early and Middle I Archaic assemblages which may have some characteristics in common with the better defined Middle and Early Archaic periods of surrounding areas (Prikryl 1990). In general, this Early to Middle Holocene period is characterized as warm and dry during the first part, becoming wetter around 4500 B.P. when I the Cross Timbers are thought to have first developed (Prikryl 1990). The projectile points also mirror a cultural change, becoming more regionalized toward the end of the period. The overall number of lithic artifacts manufactured from chert also decreases slightly, although chert still dominates the I assemblages from this period. Interestingly, the majority of Wells points, a projectile point type whose regional associations may lie to the east of the region and which is considered to occur during the earlier part of the period, are manufactured from quartzite (Prikryl 1990:52). Terraces still dominate the known I site locations, but again, this is probably due to the fact that cultural materials are easily exposed on the eroded terraces while deeply buried floodplain sites lie undiscovered. I The Late Archaic has generally been associated with Crook and Harris's Elam Focus. However, Prikryl's (1990) Lower Elm Fork region artifact analysis challenges their assessments and chronological markers. Gary points are by far the most numerous type of this period but also may extend into the Late I Prehistoric period. Other common Late Archaic types include Dallas, Trinity, Godley, Elam, Edgewood, Ellis, and Yarbrough. The most commonly used lithic material is the locally available quartzite as opposed to non-local chert. This is in marked contrast to the preceding periods and reflects a stronger regionalization. A supposed wetter climate during this period is thought to have expanded the Eastern I Cross Timbers and, with this expansion, increased plant species considered to be economically important to inhabitants of the region (Prikryl 1990). The development of the West Fork paleosol, defined by Ferring (1986), has been dated to the later part of the Late Archaic and the Late Prehistoric I and I coincides with this wetter period (although this interpretation has now been modified slightly by Ferring, personal communication 1992). The accepted interpretation of the Late Archaic as a time of increase in I 12 I I


![Image 1 - Page 29](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p29_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 29*


---

## Page 30

I population and site density is partially based on the above environmental model (Skinner 1981; Brown I 1987a, 1989). Prikryl's (1990) Late Prehistoric I period is marked by the introduction of new technologies, I including the bow and arrow and ceramics. Staggered introduction of these new technologies (beginning as early as 1750 B.P. in parts of East Texas) into existing cultural patterns suggests the possibility of a gradual transition between the Archaic and the Late Prehistoric but this aspect has been little studied. I Characteristic of this period in the region and the immediate study area (Prikryl 1990:77-79) is that projectile points (Scallorn, Steiner, and Catahoula) during the early stages of the period were more commonly made from quartzite while those from the latter part of the period included more chert I specimens. Furthermore, as with the other periods, known sites are most often found on terraces, although about one-third are known to exist on the floodplain. The climate is thought to have become drier around 1000 B.P. (Prikryl 1990). Climate stress and the need to exploit multiple ecological niches I in difficult times may partially explain the large number of sites located on the Cross-Timbers/Blackland Prairie boundary. The regionalization seen in the early stage appears to shift to one of greater mobility or, more likely, trade with neighboring groups (Prikryl 1990). I While the Late Prehistoric II phase may represent the actual introduction of peoples following a southward extension of the range of the bison in parts of Texas, in North-Central Texas this phase represents a strong dose of influence from the more settled agricultural peoples to the north and east. I A synthesis of the study region (Prikryl 1990:79-83) examines the marked changes from the preceding period. The dry climate proposed for the earlier Late Prehistoric I period is thought to have continued, and with it a change toward greater areas of grasslands (Prikryl 1990). These grasslands are thought to I have consisted primarily of short grasses, a possible explanation for the increase in bison remains from this period. Projectile points from this period include Fresno, Perdiz, Maud, Washita, and Harrell. The latter two are Southern Plains types and, along with Nocona Plain ceramics and bison scapula I horticultural tools, are further indications of the economic and cultural emphasis on bison. Prikryl (1990) also reports a high percentage of chert as the preferred lithic material, indicating the same trend mentioned above under the Late Prehistoric I. Although slightly different explanations are offered, the I pattern of lithic material usage over time is basically the same at Richland Creek (McGregor 1987: 187). Terrace sites are the dominant site type (for reasons which have already been discussed several times) wiiJ?. a large majority on the Cross Timbers/Blackland Prairie margin. A similar extremely high density I of sites was recorded along the Cross Timbers/Blackland Prairie ecotone south of the present study area at Aquilla Lake (Brown 1987b, 1987t). I Although the Historic period theoretically begins in Texas with the arrival of Alvar Nunez Cabeza de Vaca and the survivors of the Narvaez expedition on the Texas coast in 1528, there may have been earlier landings, notably by the expeditions sent by Francisco Garay, then governor of Jamaica, to I the mouth of the Rio Grande between 1519 and 1523 (Salinas 1990:22-24). In any case, the influences of European colonization were not strongly felt for several centuries. Unlike south and central Texas, where the earliest historic influences were from the south, the populations of North-Central Texas may I have received trade goods from the French at a very early time. That the settled villagers may have been more receptive to European ways is suggested by the successful defense of the Spanish Fort site on the Red River against the threat from the Spanish commander Diego Ortiz Parilla by the Wichita Indians. I Although native North-Central Texas groups such as the Wichita and the Waco survived as relatively cohesive cultural entities until well into the nineteenth century, many groups may have disappeared or had their cultural patterns heavily altered by the massive depopulation and cultural disintegration brought about by the advent of the European settlers. I 13 I I

## Page 31

I PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS I There have been several attempts to synthesize the cultural resources of the North-Central Texas region, the best among them including Yates and Perring (1986) and Prikryl (1990). Although some new work has been conducted in the area since then, this section does not attempt to improve upon those as I a complete regional synthesis. Instead, the focus of this section is threefold: 1) to examine the early development of cultural resource investigations, 2) to cite those which have taken place in the immediate area, and 3) to note those key projects which are referred to again in the subsequent sections of this I report. Much of this background has been drawn from the research conducted by Yates and Perring (1986) and Prikryl (1990). I In what may be the first archeological study of the region, Ellis Shuler, a geologist working in the area, published papers on Pleistocene fossils buried in the deposits of the Trinity River and the possibility of their association with human remains (Shuler 1923, 1934). This idea was one of his most I important contributions. At the Lagow Sand Pit, human skeleton remains were found and removed by quarrymen who described its stratigraphic position, which was later confirmed by Shuler and Robert Hill after examining the pit wall. The yellow sandy clay in which the skeleton was found also contained fossil antelope, bison, and mammoth. It was clear that the skeleton was not an obtrusive feature dug through I the overlying clays (Shuler 1923:333-334). At another locale, fossil elephant skulls and a nearly complete elephant skeleton had been found in the Trinity alluvial deposits, although without any human association. Nevertheless, Shuler fostered the idea of finding evidence of people along with other extinct mammals I in the North-Central Texas area (Shuler 1934:75-79). Geomorphological explorations continued to yield bits and pieces of archeological data. Pattillo's I (1940) study of the Trinity River terraces in the Carrollton area noted a human burial excavated by quarry workmen on the spur of a Pleistocene terrace overlooking Denton Creek (recorded as 41DL27). The site and the burial were described in detail by Albritton and Pattillo (1940) and Goldstein (1940a, 1940b). I The antiquity of the burial has never been determined. The first systematic survey of archeological sites in the upper Trinity River drainage was I conducted by R. K. Harris (1936), one of the best known and most accomplished of the avocational archeologists of the region. After the founding of the Dallas Archeological Society in 1939, Harris (1939, 1940, 1942, 1947), Forest Kirkland (1942; Kirkland et al. 1949), Rex Housewright (Housewright I et al. 1947, 1948), Robert Hatzenbuehler (Hatzenbuehler 1942; Harris and Hatzenbuehler 1948; Hatzenbuehler and Harris 1949), J.B. Sollberger (Sollberger and Harris 1949), and Lester Wilson (1941, 1946), among others, began an important series of avocational investigations of sites in the Dallas area I which were subsequently published in the publication of the newly organized society, The Record. Krieger's (1946) synthesis of the culture complexes of northern Texas was both the first I professional archeological description of the region and the first attempt to create a cultural sequence for the area. As Prikryl (1990:22) points out, he was hampered by the lack of Works Progress Administration excavations in the area, and made his observations primarily on the basis of regional collections such as that made by Harris as well as excavations at the Harrell site (the type site of the I Henrietta Focus), 150 miles to the west of the Dallas-Fort Worth area. The earliest professional archeological survey of the region was conducted by R. L. Stephenson; I as part of the Smithsonian's River Basin Surveys Program in conjunction with the Corps of Engineers and the National Park Service, he studied four proposed reservoirs: Grapevine (Stephenson 1948), I 14 I I


![Image 1 - Page 31](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p31_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 31*


---

## Page 32

I Benbrook (Stephenson 1949a), Garza-Little Elm (that is, Lake Lewisville), and Lavon (Stephenson I 1949b). This was also the earliest work in the immediate area of Denton Creek as Stephenson surveyed along the section of Denton Creek to be inundated by the planned Lake Grapevine in July 1948 (Stephenson 1948). Only 10 sites were recorded during this survey. All were surface sites located on I the terraces above the creek. Any deeply buried sites within the floodplain or lower terrace went undetected. I Stephenson (1952) established a simple chronology (Early Lithic, Late Non-Pottery, and Pottery) from the sites at Lakes Lavon and Lewisville and defined the Wylie Focus based on his excavations at the Hogge Bridge site and other sites in the area. Although Stephenson did not publish the results of I other test excavations which he conducted at sites in the Lake Lewisville area, Harris continued his efforts in this area, documenting sites such as 41DN353 (Harris 1950), 41DN6 (Harris 1951a), and 41DN28 (Harris 1951b). I Following Crook's (1952) investigations of the Wheeler site (41DL30), Crook and Harris (1952) defined the Carrollton Focus of the Trinity Aspect (from the Lake Dallas and Wheeler sites) and the Elam I Focus (primarily from the Wood Pit site). Later refinements were added to the definition (Crook and Harris 1953, 1954a, 1954b). Prikryl (1990:24-26) discusses the characteristics of these foci as well as the supposed relationship between the Albritton Clay and Pattillo Sand, which formed the stratigraphic context for the assumed relationship between these foci. I Suhm et al. (1954), in their synthesis of the prehistory of Texas, defined the North-Central Texas Culture Area to include the previously defined Henrietta, Wylie, Carrollton, and Elam Foci. They I questioned the relationship of some of the artifacts included as defining traits of these foci, such as the ubiquitous Gary type. Many archeologists working in the North-Central Texas region have since found the Carrollton and Elam Foci to be elusive at best, but it was not until Prikryl's thesis (1987a) that a I more substantial scheme was offered as a replacement. While avocational studies continued throughout the 1950s, including Crook and Harris' (1957) I investigations at the Lewisville site (41DN72), very few professional investigations were undertaken at this: time. In the 1960s, however, professional studies began in earnest with the series of reservoir sal~age projects undertaken by the Texas Archeological Salvage Project of the University of Texas at I Austin. Closest to the study area was the survey of Forney Reservoir, now known as Lake Ray Hubbard (Harris and Suhm 1963). Southeast of the project area, archeological surveys were conducted for the proposed Cedar Creek (Story 1965) and Bardwell Reservoirs (Shafer 1964). At Lake Ray Hubbard, I excavations were completed by Ross (1966) at the Glen Hill (41RW4) and Upper Rockwall (41RW2) sites and by Lorrain and Hoffrichter (1968) at the Lower Rockwall site (41RW1). At the proposed Bardwell Reservoir, Sorrow (1966) excavated the Pecan Springs site (41EL11) in Ellis County. I The late 1960s and early 1970s saw the beginning of extensive work in the region by archeologists from Southern Methodist University (SMU), including excavations by Hal Jenson (1968) I and Dessamae Lorrain (1969) and partial surveys at Aubrey Reservoir (Bousman and Verrett 1973) and Lake Grapevine (Perring 1975). The survey by Perring, which recorded 41TR55 and 41TR73, was only the second professional study in the immediate vicinity of 41DL270. Several SMU investigations were I also carried out at Lake Lavon during this period (Dawson and Sullivan 1973; Lynott 1975). Far to the south, SMU had begun survey of the Aquilla Creek area (Skinner et al. 1972). Although Prikryl (1990) does not include the Aquilla Creek area in his study, the artifacts recovered there suggest at least a very close relationship with areas to the north and east (Brown 1987a). SMU continued work here in the mid- I 15 I I


![Image 1 - Page 32](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p32_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 32*


---

## Page 33

I 1970s with testing projects undertaken by Lynott and Peter (1977) and Skinner et al. (1978). At its best, the emerging work by SMU, led by Lynott and Skinner, began to approach broader theoretical issues I which had not been broached before and began to move the focus away from the purely descriptive work of many of the DAS studies and away from the chronological ordering which seemed to be a primary research focus of the early professional efforts. I In the mid-1970s, university efforts in the region continued with a synthesis and attempted redefinition of the Wylie Focus in a University of Texas at Austin MA thesis by Marmaduke (1975) and I two field schools, one by the University of Texas at Arlington at the Northlake site (Morgan 1975) and another by the University of North Texas (then North Texas State University) at the Xerox-Lewisville project area (McCormick et al. 1975). I Although not directly related to the project area, another university-related study (by Tom Dillehay of the University of Texas at Austin) used data from sites in the North-Central Texas region to construct a model of human and bison interactions in Texas (Dillehay 1974). Several late 1970s syntheses I focused more directly on the theoretical issues relating to North-Central Texas archeology. McCormick (1976) summarized the North-Central Texas Archaic as part of a symposium originally organized by Hester (1976), suggesting a nomadic lifeway which moved on a north-south axis linking North-Central I Texas and the Southern Plains in pursuit of the migratory bison. He hypothesized movement along the Cross Timbers belt but, as Prikryl (1990:31) points out, his evidence for "permanent" sites along this wooded belt was not provided. Lynott's two contributions to these syntheses were his dissertation, which I modelled adaptive changes in the region and suggested a design for regional research (1977), and a later article criticizing Dillehay's (1974) bison presence-absence model in North-Central Texas (Lynott 1979). I The late 1970s and early 1980s brought a plethora of small cultural resource management surveys, many of which recorded no sites at all (Prikryl 1990), but also brought the initiation of a number of new major reservoir projects. Surveys were conducted at the proposed Tennessee Colony Lake I (Richner and Lee 1976, 1977, 1978), at Lake Joe Pool, then known as Lakeview (Skinner and Connors 1979), and at Ray Roberts (Skinner et al. 1982a; Perring et al. 1986). While the proposed Tennessee Colony Lake was scrapped in favor of Richland Chambers, extensive testing and mitigation projects were I carried out at Richland-Chambers (Bruseth and Moir 1987; Bruseth and Martin 1987; McGregor and Bruseth 1987), at Joe Pool (Raab 1982a; Peter and McGregor 1988a), and at Ray Roberts (Skinner et al. 1982b; Prikryl and Yates 1987; Perring 1989b). At about the same time, the final seasons of mitigation I were carried out at Aquilla Creek (Watson 1982; Brown 1987c). Collectively, these major reservoir projects undertaken during the 1980s have greatly expanded I upon the previously existing archeological data base. First and foremost, the amount of area covered by intensive survey has been dramatically enlarged. Overall, more than a 100,000 acres of terrain have been intensely examined and thousands of archeological sites recorded. Beginning with the cultural resource I survey projects of the 1970s and extending into the 1980s, it has become possible to see a more complete picture of the total variation in site types existing in the region. The number of sites excavated has also increased dramatically, particularly sites such as the Aubrey site (Perring 1989b), which was not found in the original conventional survey. These excavations, using high resolution recovery techniques, I precision mapping, and a variety of new scientific analytical techniques, have contributed much to the regional data base. Finally, the emphasis on environmental setting and geoarcheology has resulted in the emphasis on new standards in the recovery of paleoenvironmental data. Some of the paleoenvironmental I data from the region is discussed in greater detail in the concluding chapters of this volume. I 16 I I

## Page 34

I During the 1970s and 1980s, and into the 1990s, investigations continued by members of the I DAS. Their contributions even in the immediate area are too numerous to mention, but the work of Jan and Paul Lorrain along Denton Creek can be singled out (Lorrain and Lorrain 1988; P. Lorrain 1984, 1985, 1988; Cobb and Lorrain 1982; Yates 1982). The activities of these researchers and other active I members of the DAS such as Bill Young are all that stand between the sites of the Denton Creek area and destruction at the hands of the ever-encroaching Dallas-Fort Worth metroplex. I Several syntheses published in the late 1980s round out the perspective on previous investigations in.the region. Working under contract with the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers, Bonnie Yates and Reid Perring (1986) compiled an assessment of the previously recorded sites and the potential for site discovery I in the Trinity River Basin. This document is an extremely important compilation, providing brief descriptions of the most important archeological sites of the region and a critical synthesis of the Quaternary geology of the area by Perring (1986). Following this, Prikryl (1987a) elabor!1ted on the I immediate Dallas-Fort Worth area in his M.A. Thesis, which was subsequently revised for publication (Prikryl 1990). In particular, Prikryl attempted to sort out the poorly understood Archaic period of the region, substantially revising the somewhat erroneous concept of the Carrollton and Elam Foci originally I designated by Crook and Harris in 1952. While newly emerging information may ultimately modify some of the conclusions of these works, their enormous contributions will continue to be useful to area researchers for many years to come. ## Page 36

I PREIDSTORIC SITES ALONG DENTON CREEK I Dana Anthony The following presents a brief description of the recorded sites along Denton Creek from Lake I Grapevine to the confluence with the Trinity River, followed by a synthesis of these sites. Also included are the few sites which have been recorded along Cottonwood Branch, a north-flowing tributary of Denton Creek in Dallas County. Many sites have been identified and assigned State of Texas site I numbers by TARL along Baker's Branch and another unnamed tributary to its southwest, both south flowing tributaries located in Denton County. However, no recorded data other than map plotting is available for these sites, with the exception of one at the headwaters of Baker's Branch (41DN309). I The sites are arranged geographically, and in most cases also follow an order of the original recorder(s) of the site. The descriptions start with those sites in the Grapevine Dam area ~d proceed I downstream along Denton Creek to its confluence with the Elm Fork of the Trinity. Four sites below the confluence on the Elm Fork are also included because of their proximity. Deeply buried sites along the floodplain or lower terraces have only been recorded where they have been artificially exposed, I usually by construction or quarrying activities. In many cases, this is also true for upper terrace sites which were only noted after quarry pits were excavated. Many of the sites described here which were recorded before 1986 have already been described I in Douglass et al. (1986), and in Prikryl (1990). However, they are repeated here so the inventory on this section of Denton Creek is as complete as possible. The reader is referred to the above two sources for any additional information and/or other sites in the area not along the Denton Creek drainage. I THE DENTON CREEK SITES I Site 41TR33 As noted in the previous section, R. L. Stephenson (1948), in his survey of the proposed Lake I Grapevine, recorded 10 prehistoric sites (site forms were found for only two), among them site 41TR33. This site occupied about 3 acres on the edge of an upper terrace east of the dam and about 600 meters south of Denton Creek. It is described as an open campsite with some 73 artifacts documented from I surface collecting and from a donation of 21 artifacts by Mrs. H. E. Sanders, the landowner. An estimated 39 to 42 projectile points alone were recovered from this site. Stephenson supplies identification for only two types, Gary and Ellis. The next most common artifacts reported are scrapers, I one of which was identified as Alibates dolomite. In addition, three manos were also reported. This site reportedly yielded no pottery, arrow points, or faunal material. Also of importance is the absence of any apparent features on the surface. I Site 41TR34 I Another Stephenson discovery, this site of approximately 3 to 4 acres was located on an upper terrace overlooking Denton Creek to the north. This, too, was described as an open campsite. Although no features or artifacts were observed at the site at the time of the survey, "arrowheads and other relics" I had been reported. A site table with recovered or reported artifacts is included with the Stephenson report (1948), and although it mentions features in the title, none are recorded in the table. The table also records all I 19 I I

I sites as surface campsites on terraces. Arrow points were reported from only two of the sites, with three I sites listing no artifacts at all (these must be among those with collections reported by land owners). Manos were found at only one, 41TR33. Overall, the limited amount of data presented in Stephenson's report makes it very difficult to use but it is not unlike other reports from this time. I ~~ This site and the following two were recorded in the vicinity of the Grapevine Dam during the I 1970s, and include investigations by the DAS and Reid Perring, respectively. Best known as the site of the Gano Cabin, which dates to the mid-nineteenth century, 41TR15 also has a prehistoric component 1 designated 41TR15B (Granberry 1978). It is located on a high terrace or upland area above a tributary of Denton Creek on the east side of the Grapevine Dam. Apparently, most of the site, or the exposed portion of it, is on the ridge slope where a hearth was detected. Collected materials are described as "mostly non-pottery" (implying some pottery was found) and included projectile points ·and flakes. A I spring is nearby and no doubt played a role in site selection both prehistorically and historically. Site 41TR55 I Site 41TR55 was recorded by Perring during a survey of the Grapevine Dam area (Perring 1975:6-9). The site is located on the first terrace of the creek a maximum of 15 meters east of South Creek near its confluence with Denton Creek. At the time of the survey it was found to be eroding out I of both sides of the slough which cuts through the levee of the tributary. This levee was described as artificial, having been built up by construction activities close by the site. The aerial extent was estimated to be about 15 meters on the north side and about 10 meters on the south side of the slough. The depth I of this site was not determined. Cultural materials included dense concentrations of mussel shell, burned and unburned bone, burned rock, and lithic material (both chipped and ground). The chipped stone artifacts primarily comprise flakes and chips of chert, with lesser amounts of quartzite. The ground stone I tool was a mano fragment. Parts of this site may still be preserved beneath the surface, especially if it extends onto the narrow floodplain. I Site 41TR73 This site is located on a high terrace east of the dam approximately 350 meters south of Denton I Creek (Perring 1975:4-6). The site was exposed by an old road which extends to the top of the ridge. Here the soil is intact with underlying sandstone deposits. The site was estimated to extend at least 50 meters from the edge of the ridge as observed along the road, but the horizontal extent could not be I ascertained without testing. Cultural materials observed at this site consisted of non-diagnostic flakes and chips, including thinning flakes, mostly of quartzite. The quartzite cobbles observed were thought to be naturally occurring. No features or faunal material were noted. I Site 41DN309 As previously mentioned, numerous sites have been mapped along Baker's Branch and the I tributary to the south; however, no reports on these sites are available. From the map plottings, most sites appear to be on the upper terraces with a few on the floodplain. One of these sites was revisited by the original Wilkerson transmission line survey crew and formally recorded as 41DN309 (Anthony I et al. 1992:17). I 20 I I

## Page 38

I The site is a small Iithic scatter located near the headwaters of Baker's Branch, a major tributary I to Denton Creek. All of the artifacts observed were clustered on an approximately two meter square area of exposed sandstone bedrock. The surrounding area was inspected but no other artifacts were noted. The lack of other observed artifacts may reflect past collection activities at the site. Furthermore, the I artifacts which were concentrated on the exposed sandstone may have been placed there during previous investigations at the site. Except for areas of exposed bedrock immediately adjacent to the creek, the open areas around the site were covered by sparse short grasses providing excellent visibility. I Only about 15 artifacts were noted, but these were represented by three different materials - ch.ert, quartzite, and petrified wood. A non-diagnostic projectile point base was found along with two I possible retouched flakes; the remainder was Iithic debitage. No features were observed. Shovel tests and bank profiles examined in the adjacent right-of-way area revealed no evidence I of cultural materials. Depth of the site is unknown, but bedrock outcrops suggest that ,it may be considerably shallower than indicated by the shovel tests and profiles in the adjacent right-of-way area. The lack of cultural features and the paucity of artifacts from this site suggest a very ephemeral I occupation. Site 41TR117 I Among the most active current members of the DAS in the Denton Creek environs are Paul and Jan Lorrain and Bill Young. They have recorded more sites along the creek than anyone else and continue to do so as new sites are exposed by the construction and quarry activities which are so prevalent I in the area. In addition, these members also continue to monitor known sites and file updates on them as to any new impacts. With the exception of 41DL11, 41DN480, and 41DN270, 41TR117 and all of the following sites were recorded by the above researchers. I Site 41TR117 is located on an area of alluvial terrace 900 meters from Denton Creek. Cultural materials were restricted to lithics and included flakes, chips, and a point fragment. No features or fauna! I materials were observed and the known aerial extent of this site was not recorded. It was thought to be an Archaic site. Perceived future impacts include quarrying as well as potential activities from the road near which it is located. I Site 41DN483 I This prehistoric campsite is located in the floodplain about 200 meters northwest of 41DL270 at the northernmost point along the bank of the artificial drainage channel and south of modern Denton Creek. The site is estimated to be about 20 meters in length along the bank. It consists of a scatter of I fire-cracked sandstone extending from 1 to 2.2 meters below the present ground surface. Approximately 2 meters west of this feature, a small bone scatter is also apparent in the bank at 0.5 to about 1 meter below the surface. Several long bone fragments, apparently deer, were observed. Several meters further I west, a mussel shell was also included within the perimeter of the site. One thick quartzite biface fragment was observed at the site. No other artifactual material was noted. I This probable hearth and bone scatter, as well as the very dark gray silty clay in which they are found, are similar to the features and setting found at 41DL270. However, this site is not considered to be a part of 41DL270 due to the distance (200 meters) and the absence of any other exposed features in I 21 I I

## Page 39

I the vicinity which might link the two sites. Although the visible portion of this site is small, the possibility remains that deposits are still buried within the cut bank. I Site 41DN290 I This is known as the Wolters site and is one of the few in the Denton Creek drainage which has been tested (Cobb and Lorrain 1982). It was located on a low terrace approximately 200 meters northeast of Denton Creek and about 60 meters from a small spring-fed tributary which flows to the south. The I depth of the site is very shallow, with only about 20 centimeters of sandy loam atop limestone gravels. Features included two rock-lined hearths without pits or basins. The bases of these hearths were on the underlying gravel surface. Hearth 1 measured 40x58 centimeters with most of the individual stones I smaller than 10 centimeters. The other hearth was larger measuring 68x90 centimeters. The stones were described as flat sandstone; though a few were 10 centimeters in size, most measured 5 to 7 centimeters. Many diagnostic lithic artifacts were identified as to type and fall within the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric I and II periods. The majority of the debitage was of quartzite as opposed to chert. Over I 1800 pieces of debitage and 55 formal and informal tools were collected. Mussel shell and snail were also collected from the controlled excavations. Four different species I of mussel shells were identified - Amblema plicata, Lampsilis teres, Tritogonia verrucosa, and Truncilla truncata (o verall counts were not presented). The only snails were identified as Rabdotus. Rabbit, deer, and turtle were the most common faunal material identified out of a total of about 1900 specimens (Yates I 1982). The reported absence of fish bone could be attributed to collection methods (fish and scales were identified from the fine screening at 41DL270). A few shell-tempered plain potsherds were collected from this site. I Within two years Paul Lorrain (1984) reported that no evidence of this site remained due to quarrying. During this mining, however, another rock-lined hearth was exposed not far from the area I of controlled excavations. This hearth was larger than most described from the Denton Creek area or adjoining watershed. The excavators, including Lorrain, were able to determine that this feature measured 1.5 meters in diameter with a depressed center extending almost 30 centimeters from the upper I portion of the hearth into an orange clay. They also reported darkened soil with charcoal stains beneath the hearth rocks. Individual rock size was roughly 15 centimeters. No artifacts or faunal material was observed with this feature (Lorrain 1984:4-5). I Site 41DL326 I This site was originally recorded in 1983 by Bill Young, who subsequently filed an updated site form in 1991. It was revisited as part of the original Wilkerson transmission line survey as a portion of the line crossed the mapped site area. The site was situated on a Pleistocene terrace, described as T3 to T2, along the south bank of Denton Creek. The site was originally mapped as 100x50 meters, with a I cultural deposit estimated to be 50 to 60 centimeters in thickness. The site was described as an open campsite with a wide distribution and quantity of artifacts. I The only feature noted was a presumed storage pit that was exposed during the construction of a concrete retaining wall. This feature was dug from a sandy loam surface some 56 centimeters below the modern surface and extended into a reddish yellow clay to a depth of about 1.05 meters. It measured 48 I centimeters in depth with a width of 28 centimeters. It was capped by a ironstone slab which measured 53 centimeters in length and was 7.3 centimeters thick. The fill consisted solely of a dark humus. The I 22 I I

## Page 40

I date of this feature is unknown. Artifacts collected from this site include numerous preforms, blanks, I and bifaces, with fewer dart point fragments, retouched flakes, side and end scrapers, and a single graver. The vast majority of the informal tools were manufactured from what was described as Uvalde (quartzite?). I Some 30 whole or near complete projectile points were collected and illustrated on the updated site form. These include Calf Creek, Martindale, Carrollton, Wells, Pedernales, Yarbrough, Nolan, I Dawson Brazos, Dallas, Edgewood, Ellis, and Scallorn. For the most part, these range from Prikryl's (1990) Middle Archaic through his Late Prehistoric I periods. All but one of the five Carrollton points were reported to have been made from Edwards chert; the remaining one is made from a Brazos material. I Of the six Yarbrough projectile points collected, five were manufactured from Uvalde (one heat-treated) with only one manufactured from Edwards chert. Three Dallas points, two made of heat-treated Uvalde and one of Edwards chert, were the next most common Late Archaic type. What were described as two I Gary preforms were manufactured from heat-treated Uvalde, while the two Scallorn arrowpoints were made from Brazos and Uvalde. I At the time of the proposed transmission line survey no surface sign of this site was apparent in or near the right-of-way. Several shovel probes were conducted in the area but all were negative. The high terrace crossed by the line was devoid of any cultural remains, as was the area toward Highway 121 I to the east. The area to the north across Old Coppell Road was inspected and, in a large excavation (either a small quarry or a test excavation for the nearby highway), several fire-cracked sandstone features were observed eroding from the exposed surface. No other cultural materials or artifacts were noted, but the features appear to be in situ. I Due to all of the past and continued disturbances (including a nearby cement plant, a quarry, and the recent construction along State Highway 121), most of this site has undoubtedly been destroyed. I However, remains of this site may still be intact closest to Denton Creek. Certainly most or all of the site which had been present on the Pleistocene terrace is now destroyed. Only portions of the component buried in the Denton Creek lower terrace or floodplain may survive. I Bison Kill Site I Virginia and Bill Morris, aided by Dr. and Mrs. Joel Shiner, excavated this bison kill site on the left bank of Denton Creek a short distance upstream from 41DL297 (Morris and Morris 1970:2-5). It was discovered when the land owner was mining sandy loam. The bones were located 25 meters from I the creek on the first terrace. They were measured to be approximately 1.20 meters below the present ground surface within a dark loam. What was not destroyed by the front-end loader was hand excavated after the overburden was removed by machine. A skull, mandible, vertebrae, and rib bones appeared I to represent a single adult (Morris and Morris 1970:3). A Fresno point, a Late Prehistoric II type, was recovered from between two of the ribs. Examination of the vertebra by a pathologist found that a pointed object "such as the probable shaft of a spear or pointed stick" had been embedded between two I vertebra prior to the animal's death, resulting in deformed spinous process (Morris and Morris 1970:3). In addition, the presence of this object resulted in new bone growth, probable infection, and healing with callus. A second bison was represented by part of a mandible (Morris and Morris 1970:5). The authors I also state that the bones were not articulated, showed no signs of burning, and lacked any associated charcoal. Their conclusion was that this was not an abandoned kill but the bison was killed and butchered at this site (Morris and Morris 1970:5). The original wound described above apparently did not cause the immediate death of the animal. I 23 I I
