## Page 41

I Site 41DL297 I This was recorded as a campsite on the left bank of Denton Creek. The site had been exposed in the wall of a quarry pit and was estimated to be about 100 meters in extent, and 1 meter in depth. Lithics, bone, and shell were observed in the pit, as well as a possible hearth; the former were collected. I Based on the materials observed, 41DL297 was also assigned an Archaic date. Site 41DL296 I Just downstream of 41DL297 lies another site that was exposed in the slope of a quarry pit. Site 41DL296 is also on the left bank of Denton Creek, which is about 50-100 meters to the south. Described I as a campsite, material collected from the slope of the pit included flakes, chips, and bifaces, primarily of quartzite, burned bone, turtle shell, and mussel shell. No features were observed. This site was estimated to be about 100 meters in extent and 1 meter in depth. An Archaic date was tentatively assigned based on the projectile point types. I Site 41DL11 I This site, originally recorded by Harris and McElroy around 1940, was described as a surface camp and is located on a low upland ridge or high terrace ridge on the south side of Denton Creek within ½ kilometer of a number of more recently recorded sites, including 41DL297, 41DL296, 41DL252, I 41DL300, and the bison kill site. The site is situated on either side of a small draw which empties into Denton Creek and is mapped as 180x275 meters in size. Surface artifacts noted at the time of the survey included projectile points (described as both arrowheads and bird points), blades, scrapers, a hand ax, I knife, and manos. Fifteen ceramic sherds were also described. They were all shell tempered, plain, and ranged in color from grayish brown to brown. Neither features nor mussel shell were observed at the time. Harris and McElroy assigned a Caddoan cultural affiliation to the site and noted that it was well I known and had been the scene of numerous collections. Subsequent work at 41DL11 illustrated that the upper portion of the site extends to a depth of I 2.5 to 3 meters below the surface. Archeological materials, including artifacts and bison bone, were found to be eroding from these dark clay strata, while hearths, mussel shell, and other bone and artifacts were found below and to the upper portion of the underlying yellow-gray clays. These deeper cultural I deposits are dated to the Late Archaic (Perring 1986:90). Site 41DL252 I Site 41DL252 is located on the right bank of Denton Creek in close proximity to 41DL11, 41DL296, and 41DL300. The site was discovered in a borrow pit which had been dug into the floodplain, exposing burned rock, bone, shell (both burned and unburned), and lithic material (Lorrain I 1985). Aside from a bison kill site noted upstream (Morris and Morris 1970), this was one of the first sites discovered as of 1985 on the Denton Creek floodplain. The site was estimated to be about one acre in extent, and most of the material was observed to be concentrated in a 15-meter expanse along the pit I wall. The site lies about 2 meters below the present ground surface in a clay matrix observed to lighten with depth. The cultural zone is 45 centimeters thick. Rock-lined hearths, which are about 60 centimeters in diameter, were recognized some distance from the main concentration but basically at the I same depth. These two hearths had slight depressions in the center. Lorrain also points out the small size of the rocks (smaller than 7.5 centimeters). This small rock size and the lack of a pit may indicate I 24 I I

I Site 41DL300 This site is located in the floodplain near the confluence of Cottonwood Branch and Denton Creek. Cultural material was concentrated in a 15 meter area at a depth of 1.8 to 2.7 meters below the I ground surface as noted in the cut bank (a borrow pit apparently is located on the site, the cut bank may refer to it). Two features, a hearth and a concentration of large mussel shell, were reported. Material collected from the cut bank included bifaces, flakes and chips, dart points, a grinding stone, bone, and I mussel shell. As the hearth was located about 150 meters to the south of the main concentration of cultural material its association with it is not clear. It was 3 meters below the surface in sandy clay. The hearth, composed of limestone, was excavated and found to be 1.25 meters in diameter. It was I surrounded by a dark stained soil that extended to a diameter of about 1. 75 meters. Charcoal flecks, burned bone fragments, and minute mussel shell fragments were also observed. Neither the thickness of the feature was reported nor if it had a pit or was simply placed on an old surface. I Site 41DL295 Known as the Vista Ridge Site, this site was recorded by Jan and Paul Lorrain on the north or I left bank of Denton Creek approximately 2 meters below the ground surface. It was described as an open campsite on the north bank of a quarry pit 100 meters from the creek in an area earlier disturbed by quarrying and leveling for development of the Vista Ridge subdivision. At the time the site was first recorded in 1986, an approximate 100 meter concentration was noted in the area not yet leveled. I Thickness of the site was estimated at 50 centimeters. Although no features were noted, numerous cultural materials were observed and collected from the pit. These included bifaces, a uniface, Late Archaic projectile points (Edgewood, Elam, Gary, Godley types), flakes, chips, and a probable I hammerstone. Also noted was burned rock, bone, and shell, but no definable features. The surveyors noted the materials at this site were similar to those found at 41DL252 upstream and across the creek. On a 1988 updated site form by the Lorrains, they included inked illustrations of the formal tools I collected. A publication in The Record also illustrated these finds and noted the similarity of this site with 41DL252 (Lorrain and Lorrain 1988). The slopes of this pit had been seeded at the time of the survey to prevent further erosion. I Site 41DN480 I Site 41DN480, recorded by Paul Lorrain, Jan Lorrain, Bill Young, and Bobbie Jean Young, is located on an upper terrace overlooking Denton Creek approximately 1065 meters to the south. It is described as an open campsite, measuring about 30x30 meters. A compact yellow clay was noted about I 15 centimeters below the surface. The cultural material scattered about, both prehistoric and historic, may be eroded from the higher points or the top of the hill where no materials were observed. Two quartzite projectile points, (a Gary and a heat-treated possible Yarbrough), mussel shell, bone, teeth, I quartzite cobbles, and snails were collected from the surface. No features were noted. This site is also in a development zone. I Site 41DN270 This site, recorded by Southern Methodist University (SMU) archeologists, was located on a mid level terrace overlooking Denton Creek 700 meters to the south. The surface of the site was covered with I a sandy soil and thick grasses. Only a few flakes were noted, and the extent and depth of the site were not determined. I Site 41DL30 Among the earliest work in the area was that conducted by Harris, Crook, McElroy and the I Kirklands, members of the newly founded DAS. One of the most important sites recorded by this group was known as the Wheeler site, which along with the Dallas site was instrumental in the formulation of I 26 I I

## Page 44

I the Carrollton Focus, of the Trinity aspect by Crook and Harris (Crook 1952; Crook and Harris 1952, I 1953, 1954a). Like so many sites in this region, it, too, was exposed by gravel quarrying. The site was located on a lower terrace overlooking Denton Creek. At the time this site was first reported, the cultural materials were found to lie at a depth of about 1.2 to 1. 75 meters below the surface within a fluvial I brown sand [Pattillo Sand] situated atop a sterile clay [Albritton Clay] (Crook 1952:43,46). Notable at the site is the lack of recorded features and mussel shell. Bone was at best only I poorly preserved. Lithic artifacts were plentiful, however, and included projectile points, scrapers, choppers, cores, knives, drills, hammerstones, utilized flakes (Crook 1952:50). Later, Carrollton axes and net sinkers were also discovered (Crook and Harris 1953:21). The vast majority of the projectile I points were manufactured from non-local chert, while scrapers and other lithic artifacts were manufactured mostly from the local quartzite. What have been described as "clay blobs" were also very common. The author thought these might represent some feature associated with a structure s1:,1ch as daub I or flooring, although no postholes or any other indication of a house were noted. A portion of a human skull was also recovered from the quarry wall (Crook 1952:50-53). Based on the stratigraphy, Crook surmised this site and the associated artifacts to dated to about 3500 B.P. I Following initial investigations at the site, shell-tempered pottery was later found at the West Wheeler Site along with small triangular arrowpoints (Douglass et al. 1986:255). In addition, two I radiocarbon dates are reported from the site: A.O. 1575 ± 145 from the West Wheeler Site (X41DL02) and A.D. 1845 ± 145 from the western portion of the site (Douglass et al. 1986:243). Artifacts from this site span the entire period of occupation of North America from Paleoindian I to the Late Prehistoric II with most from mixed or uncontrolled contexts from the gravel pit wall in which the site was found. The definition of the Carrollton Focus was based on materials from the site (Crook 1952; Crook and Harris 1952, 1954a). Reexamination of these materials and the methods of I investigations by Prikryl (1990) suggest that the materials from this site are too poorly associated with the supposed context to support a narrow definition of the focus. I Site 41DL27 This burial site recorded by Harris was located on a spur of a Pleistocene terrace (the same I terrace 41DL30 is located on) overlooking Denton Creek approximately 180 meters to the east. It was found by the land owner while quarrying sand and gravel from the bank. As noted in the previous section, the burial was excavated by quarry workmen and subsequently analyzed by Marcus Goldstein I of the University of Texas as part of a WPA sponsored project (Goldstein 1940a, 1940b). The results were published in Albritton and Pattillo (1940). The skeleton, whose antiquity has never been determined, was found to be an aboriginal female of about 60 years. Of interest is the presence of osteoporosis on the skull, often a sign of dietary deficiencies. Today a trailer park occupies the site. Site 41DL12 I This is one of two sites recorded by Harris and McElroy on the floodplain of the Elm Fork of the Trinity near the Carrollton Dam. It too is described as a surface camp encompassing an area of I 180x45 meters. The site, which is on the right bank, had been exposed by gravel quarrying and from erosion on one edge by the Elm Fork. Arrowheads, bird points, a blade, and flakes were reported from the site. At the time of the initial recording of the site in 1940, the investigators also noted the presence of what appeared to be burned rock and mussel shells. ## Page 45

I Kirkland, and Robert Hatzenbueler returned to the site and excavated two features described as refuse pits which were later reported in 1he Record (Kirkland et al. 1949). I Pit A, exposed in profile in the edge of the gravel pit, was about 1.5 meters across and 68 centimeters deep and dug into a brown sand and clay matrix. Mussel shell was found midway and at the I bottom of the pit with a few pieces of burned rock in between (according to the illustrated profile). Two arrow points and two dart points were collected from above the uppermost layer of mussel shell (from the artifact sketches, one could tentatively identify one of the arrow points as a Scallorn; the dart points I as a possible Edgewood and possibly the stem of a Gary point). A few flint flakes and broken bone were also reported. I Pit B was located in an exposed midden, the upper surface washed away by overflow from the Elm Fork, thus exposing the feature. It consisted of a concentration of mussel shell within a matrix of ash and charcoal dug into a brown sand. Excavation revealed the feature to be approximately 75 I centimeters in width and about 30 centimeters in depth. The bottom of the feature was basically covered with charcoal with the mussel shells concentrated in the upper zone. A limestone chopper was found among the shells. I The excavators noted that about ¼ of the mussel shell from these features had been charred on the outer edges, usually in the area of the hinge (Kirkland et al. 1949). In this report, the authors also mention a pit excavated at the Butler Hole site (41CO2) on the East Fork in Collin County which I contained numerous shells charred in the same manner (Kirkland et al. 1949:17). A third pit, Pit C, was later excavated by Hughes and Harris (1951). It, too, was exposed in I the wall of the gravel pit. The old ground surface in this area of the site was described as a dark humus layer about 7 inches deep (Hughes and Harris 1951:7). The top of the pit had been truncated by gravel operations so the surface from which it was dug could not be determined. Hughes and Harris (1951:7) I surmised it must have been dug from the newly discovered pottery level which occurred in the top 5 or 6 inches of the old ground surface. This conclusion was based on the occurrence of a mussel shell hoe found within the pit. This artifact type had been found with pottery sites elsewhere on the East Fork as I well as the Red River (Hughes and Harris 1951:7). Flakes and bone found about 18 inches below the old ground surface were considered to be from a pre-pottery component. I Pit C was described as slightly bell-shaped and measured 31 inches in diameter. It extended to 35 inches below the old ground surface to the point where a gravel layer was reached. Burned mussel shell and burned rocks were found in the base of the feature. The mussel shell hoe mentioned above and I a shell scraper were also found in this bottom layer. The excavators noted that after the initial fire, the pit had been allowed to fill with refuse for some time, subsequently, a second fire was placed within the pit about 15 inches above the first. Burned rock and burned mussel shell were also encountered with the I second fire (Hughes and Harris 1951:7-8). Investigations conducted by Perring at this site during the 1980s revealed an archeological horizon approximately 3 meters below the surface (Perring 1986:89). Perring also reported several I hearths, artifacts, bone, and mussel shell. Charcoal from this horizon was radiocarbon dated to 1140 ± 70 B.P. (Perring 1986:89). I I 28 I I

I Site 41DL13 I Site 41DL13 was located on a sandy ridge of the Elm Fork across the river from 41DL12. It was described as a surface campsite measuring 41 Ox 182 meters. Even at the time of its original recording I by Harris and McElroy in 1940, this site was well known to collectors. The recorders describe and illustrate II arrow heads and bird points 11, resembling Scallorn and Perdiz, and several triangular non stemmed projectiles. Prikryl also reports Gary and Dallas projectile points from the Harris collection I (P~lkryl 1990:87). Harris and McElroy also reported flakes, a metate, mussel shell, and plain potsherds. Most of the sherds were reported to be grit-tempered, but two were shell-tempered. The artifact assemblage from this site denotes a Late Archaic through Late Prehistoric I and II occupation. I Subsequent investigations failed to note any remaining cultural materials. This site has been heavily disturbed by quarrying and park development (Douglass et al. 1986:249). I Site 41DL193 Sites 41DL193 and 41DL194 were recorded by Alan Skinner. Site 41DL193, consisting of a I single layer of mussel shell, burned rock, a few flakes, and possible charcoal, was found exposed in the cutbank of an abandoned gravel pit. At the time of the survey, the site measured 30 meters in length and was about 50 centimeters below the surface in black clay. It appeared to be in situ. It was recorded just I south of 41DL13 in the floodplain of the Elm Fork and may actually be part of the latter site. This area is now a City of Carrollton municipal park (Douglass et al. 1986:272). I Site 41DL194 Site 41DL194 is another site exposed in the cutbank of the same gravel pit as 41DL193. It was described as a thin lens of whole and broken mussel shell about 3 meters in length and 1.5 meters below I the surface. This occurs in the same sediment (black clay) as 41DL193. It, too, was apparently in situ. No artifacts or burned rock were observed in association with this shell lens. This is also part of the municipal park. I SUMMARY OF RECORDED SITES ALONG DENTON CREEK I The many surveys along Denton Creek by DAS members from the 1940s to the present, as well as other researchers from the University of North Texas (UNT), and SMU, have resulted in the recording of numerous sites both in the floodplain and on terraces overlooking the creek. Although a limited few I have been tested or excavated, these sites illustrate some interesting patterns in regard to site locality and types of features or material culture remains most likely to be found. I Not surprisingly, hearths and burned rock occur on both the upper terrace sites as well as the first terrace or floodplain sites. The only documented pit features have been recorded at just one site, 41DL12, actually on the Elm Fork floodplain just below its confluence with Denton Creek. The features I appear to have been utilized originally as roasting pits or ovens and subsequently as refuse pits. Although a few lithic artifacts were found in two of the pits and a mussel shell hoe and scraper were noted in another, burned rock and mussel shell were the major items found in all three of the pits. Only a small I amount of bone was recovered from one pit. These artifacts are thought to represent refuse. The excavators noted that many of the mussel shells were charred on the exterior, lending support to the roasting or baking function hypothesis. I 29 I I


![Image 1 - Page 46](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p46_img1.png)

## Page 47

I The two hearths excavated at 41DL299 in the Denton Creek floodplain were composed of burned rock apparently with some depth to them but not a pit. Mussel shell was the only faunal material I recorded for this site. Another hearth excavated on the floodplain at 41DL300 appears to have been placed directly on the old ground surface, as was the rock-lined hearth excavated in the floodplain at 41DL252 (with burned mussel shell), and the two rock-lined hearths excavated at 41DN290 on a first I terrace. The latter were resting directly on a gravel surface just 20 centimeters below the present ground surface. Obviously, there needs to be some soil depth in order to construct a basin or pit so they are more likely to be found in the floodplain or on the lower terraces. The differences between the different I types of hearths or cooking features may not be so much functional as dictated by the surface on which they are placed or dug from. I Of great interest is the presence or absence of mussel shell at the sites along Denton Creek as these are a quantifiable subsistence resource. Mussel shell concentrations or lens and accounts of them are far greater on the floodplain or lower terraces than on the higher or upper terraces. In this survey, I they are found at only one site situated on an upper terrace: 41DN480. Here they were found scattered on the surface along with bone, teeth, lithic artifacts, as well as historic materials. It would appear shell was not carried very far from its source of procurement even if a higher terrace was nearby. Where mussels are recorded, some form of bone, burned and unburned, is usually recorded as well. However, I whether the two items are a result of the same activity is not clear, particularly at sites which have not been excavated. Even in excavated sites, it is often difficult to prove or disprove direct association. I Some charred mussel shell have been noted at the above sites. Lorrain (1985, 1988) reported both burned and unburned shell recovered during testing at 41DL252. Charred shell was also reported from 41DL12 (Kirkland et al. 1949; Hughes and Harris 1951). I Only four of the sites so far have turned up any pottery - 41DN290, 41DL11, 41DL30, and 41DL13. While the latter site had both grit and shell-tempered types, the first three sites contain only I shell-tempered pottery. Diagnostic lithic artifacts appear to dominate the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods as currently defined for North-Central Texas. Lithic abundance or scarcity is difficult to assess from the sites which have not been tested. Of those sites that have been tested, 41DL290 I contained an abundance of lithic material. Material collected from the survey of 41DL326 resulted in the collection of 30 projectile points dating from the Middle Archaic to the Late Prehistoric periods. However, the relationship between various features and the quantity and types of lithic material remains I unclear. Faunal material (aside from mussel shell) reported from the above sites has usually been large I bones of mammals such a deer or bison since this is most readily seen at the survey stage. However, the few tested sites have revealed considerable numbers of rabbit and turtle remains as well. I From the results of his survey, Stephenson concluded that the sites along Denton Creek were occupied by small groups of people for short periods of time based on the few sites identified (10) and the type of sites they were (artifact scatters described as open campsites) (Stephenson 1948:2). He I speculated that the Denton Creek drainage area was lightly occupied, possibly due to the more desirable Elm Fork of the Trinity a few kilometers downstream. Of note is the fact that all of the sites are located on terraces (six on lower terraces and four on upper terraces) and the lack of any features, burned rock, or faunal remains. Only artifacts are reported, and only formal tools at that, and no mention is made of I flakes or debitage. As can be seen from other surveys along the creek, both on the floodplain and the terraces, features are abundant and the number and size of the sites so far recorded indicate a fairly I 30 I I

I intensive utilization of the Denton Creek drainage system. Many sites, however, probably were short I term occupations by small groups. Repeated visits would result in a large "site" area although at any one time the occupation was sparse. I I I I I I I I I I I I I I I 31 I I

## Page 50

I I PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS AT 41DL270 Dana Anthony As noted in the introduction to this report, 41DL270 was first recognized in 1984 by DAS I member Bill Young after quarry holes were excavated next to Denton Creek. Cultural remains represented by mussel shells, animal bone, pottery, and lithic material were observed in the backdirt. Two years later after a large drainage ditch was dug paralleling Denton Creek, a substantial area of the I site was exposed for the first time. Following this exposure, Skipper Scott and Bob Burton of the environmental section of the Fort Worth District office of the U.S. Army Corps of Engineers formally recorded the site. At the time of the field visit surface visibility was regarded as excellent, so many I features were plainly visible to a depth of at least 3 meters. The recorders made note of the site's proximity to a variety of habitats offering riverine, bottomland, and upland resources. Burned rock hearths, mussel shell concentrations, and what was described as a discrete component for bone boiling I (manifested by burned and fractured bone) were recorded. These features were observed to occur in the upper 1.5 to 2 meters of the deposits. Artifacts collected at the time included Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric diagnostics: one Gary point located about 2 meters below the present ground surface, and I an arrow point about 1 meter deep. Ground stone, deer and bison bone, and a possible human radius were also collected. The overall depth of the site was estimated to be 3 meters or more, judging from the exposed features in the ditch walls. I Only the southern portion of the site, which measured about 500 meters SW /NE by 100 meters NW /SE, was recorded at that time . Subsequent survey and update of the site form and site map in 1989 I by Paul and Jan Lorrain demonstrated the site limits actually extended some distance to the north by another 800 meters for a combined total of approximately 1300 meters along Denton Creek. The Lorrains noted that the many hearths observed at the site occur at different levels. In comparison to other sites along Denton Creek, they stated that more bone and deer teeth were present at 41DL270. Collected I artifacts included a Gary projectile point and corner-notched, expanding-stem Late Archaic types, flakes and chips, as well as a mano. I In addition to the updated site form and map, the Lorrains also excavated two of these sandstone hearths situated on the east wall of the ditch (Lorrain and Lorrain 1993). Both were composed of a single layer of stones, many reddened, 10 to 15 centimeters in thickness. Hearth 1 was found 2. 7 meters below I the ground surface (Figure 2). It was 0.8 meters in diameter. The sandstone comprising the hearth was flat, measuring about 5 to 10 centimeters. Hearth 2 was located 3.5 meters below the surface on top of a tan sandy soil [slope wash?] and was 0.45 meters in diameter (Figure 3). A single mussel shell and I charcoal were found in this hearth. Aside from the mussel shell and charcoal noted with Hearth 2, no other cultural materials were I found with the features themselves; however, quantities of shell and bone were located in close proximity. The Lorrains also noted that many of the hearth remnants were washed away during the heavy floods of 1990 (Lorrain and Lorrain 1993). I During the fall of 1991, at the time Hicks & Company archeologists first visited the site on survey, it was being investigated as part of the nomination process for the National Register of Historic I Places (under Criteria D). This effort was led by Dan Prikryl (1992), then at the Texas Historical Commission. Among the DAS members helping at the time were Bill Young, Ray Hubbard, and Brenda Whorton. Noting the site's occurrence in a buried stratified context with numerous features thought to I date primarily to the Late Archaic, Prikryl placed the majority of these features within what Perring 33 I I

## Page 52

I (1986) had defined as the West Fork paleosol. The development of this paleosol was thought to date I from about A.D. 100 to A.D. 1000 (Perring 1986) and coincides with the hypothesized wetter climate spanning the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric I periods. While some pedogenic development has clearly altered alluvial sediments at the site, the identification of the West Fork (defined on the West Fork of the I Trinity) or its equivalent has yet to be conclusively demonstrated along Denton Creek. Prikryl also noted Late Prehistoric materials above the suspected paleosol, and the possibility of earlier remains buried below the paleosol or the ditch bottom. The excellent pres~rvation of bone and charcoal was also I emphasized (Prikryl 1992). In short, the site was considered to contain valuable data pertaining to site chronology, subsistence, site structure, and function. In addition, the presence of the paleosol and features both within and outside of it was thought to show potential for studying cultural and I environmental changes during the Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods for the North-Central Texas area (Prikryl 1992). I As this nomination process was ongoing at the time of the survey for Brazos Electric, the question of eligibility for the National Register became a moot point. As two site forms had already been completed in addition to the documentation being prepared by Prikryl (1992), it was not necessary for I the Hick & Company team to fill out another site form. Subsequent to the survey, efforts focused on a testing research design to ascertain the limits of the site and its condition, particularly in the vicinity of the proposed transmission line structures (Anthony et al. 1992). I Erosion of the drainage ditch banks has no doubt greatly disturbed this site and torrential rains such as those during the past year have washed away materials associated with the recorded cultural features. In addition, the former fish hatchery and quarrying activities have also impacted the site prior I to the excavation of the channel. Nevertheless, a great many cultural features remain at least partly intact and it was suspected that intact portions of the site were likely to extend into the ditch banks, particularly to the east. The extent of disturbances was not known, however. I At the time of the survey, it was thought the site dated primarily to the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods based on diagnostic projectile points and ceramics collected at the site by previous I researchers, and the presence offeatures within what appeared to be the hypothesized West Fork paleosol. A series of backhoe trenches was seen as the best method to study this deeply buried site in regard to site extent and condition. Excavation of the numerous features already exposed in the cutbank could not I answer the questions pertinent to the testing phase. In addition to the trenching, a series of aerial photographs from Texas Natural Resources I Information Service were examined for evidence of historic land use changes and disturbances to the area around the site (Anthony et al. 1992). These included the 1942, 1953, 1958, 1964, 1972, 1984, and 1990 aerials from Dallas and Denton Counties. A 1989 aerial from Landiscor, Inc., provided by Brazos I Electric, was also inspected. The chronology of land use and disturbances illustrated by these sources included a fish hatchery, quarrying activities, roads, and the drainage ditch already mentioned. The Pleistocene terrace to the east of the site is basically obscured in the 1984 aerial photograph. The I drainage ditch, excavated in 1984, is first documented on the 1989 aerial which also illustrates erosional scars on the east wall of the ditch. From these aerial photographs and from ground inspection it became apparent that a large portion of 41DL270 had been removed by the drainage ditch, and a section closest I to the fish hatchery and quarries had been disturbed. South of the southernmost transmission structure (#12), the site may be relatively intact except for the ditch. I 35 I I


![Image 1 - Page 52](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p52_img1.png)

## Page 53

I The backhoe trenches were placed close to the planned location of five transmission line I structures to assess the presence or absence of the site at each location, and to test the condition of the site at each location. A series of six backhoe trenches were excavated beyond the east edge of the drainage ditch in an effort to expose the upper levels of the cultural zones exposed in the ditch wall and to ascertain the eastern boundary of the site. In addition, two trenches were excavated on the extreme I upper slopes of the ditch walls, and two more were dug near the bottom of the ditch close to the localities of some of the deepest observed features. It was hoped the latter four trenches would pick up the two initially identified cultural zones observed in the ditch walls and would possibly determine the depth of I the site, as well as how far it extended beyond the deepest feature observed in the ditch wall. The trenching conducted at the site during the testing phase encountered both intact strata and I cultural zones as well as disturbed areas. In at least four of the trenches thin cultural strata were defined extending from about 3 to 4 meters below the ground surface. These strata might best be referred to as micro strata due to their thin and sparse nature. Furthermore, all of the materials used to define these I cultural strata were subsistence remains (bone and mussel) or occupation debris (charcoal, burned rock fragment, burned clay) rather than artifacts. I Bulk soil samples from the upper two original cultural zones exposed in the ditch slopes were radiocarbon dated to 1250 ± 70 B.P. (Beta-52943; o 13C -22.3) and 2840 ± 80 B.P. (Beta-52944; o 13C - 21.0) (Anthony et al. 1992). It was during these investigations that a third cultural zone was identified I about 5 meters below the present ground surface with the bulk soil radiocarbon dated to 3240 ± 70 B.P. (o13C -20.4, Beta-52945) (Anthony et al. 1992). These dates fit in well with the proposed Late Archaic to Late Prehistoric I artifacts best represented in the collections made at the site. The lowest originally I defined cultural zone extended to more than 5 meters below the present ground surface at the top of the ditch. I While the site appeared intact where it was located within the trenches, the extent of the site was not so readily evaluated. All of the trenches excavated on top of the east bank encountered disturbed fill material to at least 3.5 meters below the surface. The disturbances noted on the aerial photos were documented by trenching in those areas. In addition to the soil dumped and leveled as a result of the I excavation of the drainage ditch, the floodplain sediments are so deep the backhoe could not reach beyond the modern fill. The only way to test the lower strata or cultural zones at this site was to do so from within the ditch itself. The eastern boundary of the site is still undetected beneath the surface. However, I the Pleistocene terrace to the north and east of the creek probably demarcated the site boundaries (Anthony et al. 1992: 19, 60). I Subsequent to the above investigations, core monitoring was also conducted by Mike Davis at the five locations close to the known limits of 41DL270 (Anthony et al. 1992:53-56). Due to the methods of coring, several problems were encountered during the monitoring of this operation. For one, I the core tube was only 3 to 4 inches in diameter; secondly, the coring machine greatly compacted the soil, negating a vertical control of the sample, as well as smearing it in the extraction process. Thus, the nature of the soil could not be ascertained once removed from the core. As these samples were to be I evaluated by geologists, only minimal handling of the soil cores was permitted. Charcoal specks were noted in several cores, but this is common at the site. They could, however, indicate cultural remains. Perhaps the most interesting and important data derived from the coring was that near Structure #10, the I area of greatest impact. Weathered sandstone was hit at about 6.20 meters below the surface, suggesting the maximum basal extent of the floodplain. I 36 I I


![Image 1 - Page 53](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p53_img1.png)

## Page 54

I I In short, the testing phase accomplished its major goals of examining the nature and condition of 41DL270 at the areas of potential greatest impact. While the eastern boundary of the site was not established due to the depth of the deposits, it can at least be safely said that the Pleistocene terrace deposits to the north and east of the exposed portion of 41DL270, visible on both USGS topographic I maps and aerial photographs, are the likely limits of the buried portion of the site, although it may have extended up onto this terrace. Where encountered in trenching operations, the cultural remains appeared to be intact though less obvious than on the eroded surface. I With respect to the suspected impacts at the site, it was initially noted that as many as seven or eight proposed transmission line structures were to be placed in the general area of the site, although at I least some of these were thought to be outside the boundaries of visible cultural materials. In fact, examination of exposed deposits along the drainage ditch during the survey phase clearly indicated that only a maximum of five proposed structures would actually be located on or near the defined site area. I Backhoe trenching during the second phase of archeological investigations at four of these five locations suggested that one of these five (Structure #8) was probably outside the area of the site and that cultural deposits had been partially if not completely excised from the area of another structure (Structure #11) I as a result of activities at the fish hatchery, which had been located on the site during the late 1940s and 1950s. Backhoe trenching further north showed that one other structure (Structure #9) was probably also located outside the known site area depending on its final design location. The gully that this structure I stands next to appears to mark the northern boundary of at least the upper cultural zones at the site. Finally, monitoring of core borings at several of the projected structure locations on and adjacent to the site confirmed the findings of the backhoe trenches and further indicates the possible presence of I disturbed fill deposits at Structure #12 (the structure which was not trenched out of the series of five initially thought to be on site). These results are discussed in greater detail in the initial survey and testing report (Anthony et al. 1992). I In summary, only one transmission line structure location (Structure #10) can be absolutely verified as located within an area of definite cultural deposits. Structure #9, located just north of Structure #10, may also penetrate intact cultural deposits, depending upon its placement relative to the I gully. Because of the depth of the site and the difficulty of reaching the lowest cultural zones from the surface, it is possible, but almost impossible to verify, that other structures may also impact deeply buried cultural strata at the site. I Overall, one approximately five-foot diameter hole (at Structure #10) will be placed through all three major original cultural zones initially identified at the site. As noted above, depending upon the I location of a second structure (Structure #9), it also may penetrate these three original cultural zones. Although the upper cultural zone appears to have been damaged or completely removed at the three remaining structure locations in and adjacent to the site, these structures could possibly penetrate deeper I cultural strata. Minimum cultural effect at Structure #10 only is thus an area of only approximately 6.6 square meters, while maximum effect (if all structures penetrated the suggested cultural zones present - including all three zones in single pole Structure #9 and triple pole Structure #12, and one or both of the I lower zones in Structures #7, #8, #9, #10, and #11) would be less than 25 square meters. However, several of these structure locations are expected to be disturbed, and the site is not thought to extend as far north as Structure #8, much less Structure #7. Farther to the east, the structures extend onto I Pleistocene terrace deposits within which the site will not be found. The maximum area of disturbance, thought to be extremely improbable, was nonetheless taken into account in the design of the data recovery plan for the site (Anthony et al. 1992). The research issues guiding the data recovery phase I investigations at 41DL270 are discussed in the following section. 37 I I

## Page 56

I I RESEARCH ISSUES David O. Brown In response to the need to conduct data recovery investigations at the site, a research design was I developed which could take advantage of 41DL270's unique potential to provide data concerning the prehistoric cultures of the region. Since the total impact of transmission line construction was relatively limited (to a maximum of 25 square meters and perhaps much less - see the brief discussion above and I the more detailed one in Anthony et al. (1992), the goal of the data recovery plan was to maximize data recovery from a minimum of excavation and correspondingly, with a minimum of new disturbance to this already damaged site. The mechanics of the data plan worked out in Anthony et al. (1992) is described I in the context of its implementation at the site in the subsequent chapter on methods, while the research issues guiding the choice of those methods is restated in this section from the original research design. I Three basic themes were chosen to guide the research: chronology, settlement/subsistence, and paleoenvironmental reconstruction. The site has excellent potential to inform about all three aspects of regional prehistory. The following sections discuss the primary issues of concern, phrasing a series of I simple questions which might be answered by data thought to be present at the site. While many of the individual chapters that follow offer some data relevant to these themes, a brief general summary of the data collected and the answers obtained is presented in the final chapter of this report. I CHRONOLOGY I The culture history of the North-Central Texas area is still beset by gaps in the available data. Prikryl's (1990) excellent synthesis of the available data has replaced the previously defined cultural sequence with a local sequence of six periods which he has proposed: I Paleoindian (pre-8500 B.P.) Early Archaic (8500-6000 B.P.) Middle Archaic (6000-3500 B.P.) I Late Archaic (3500-1250 B.P.) Late Prehistoric I (1250-750 B.P.) Late Prehistoric II (750-250 B.P.). I Prikryl (1990) points out that there are still many areas where our definition of the cultural history of the area is clearly inadequate. Although knowledge of the region has come a long way since Crook I and Harris' (1952, 1954a) very general outline of the cultural periods, there are still a number of unknowns regarding culture history and adaptations in the region. I Surprisingly, one of the lesser known periods is the Late Archaic. One of the reasons, of course, in addition to the dearth of excavations within the immediate region, is the highly regionalized nature of the Late Archaic; many artifacts assemblages and possibly many cultural adaptations seen during this I period are limited in geographic extent and thus little data is available from adjacent regions to help define this period in the North-Central Texas area. In contrast, many of the artifact styles, and presumably certain cultural adaptations found during the early Late Prehistoric period are widely I distributed throughout central Texas (albeit perhaps staggered chronologically - see Prewitt 1983). The investigation of culture history relies heavily on artifact styles and feature types, rather than I upon inferred functional and organizational changes. In theory, the former represent the latter. Recovery 39 I I


![Image 1 - Page 56](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p56_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 56*


---

## Page 57

I of culturally and functionally diagnostic artifacts, artifact associations and distributional patterns, and I feature types can aid in the understanding of chronological developments within the North-Central Texas area as well as geographic differences between the North-Central Texas inhabitants and the natives of other regions. I Diagnostic artifacts have been collected from 41DL270 by various researchers. Although the artifacts collected at the site are representative of a long period of occupation, the majority are consistent with Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric occupations in the region. Cross-dating of projectile points can I be used to place the site in a temporal framework and artifacts from the proposed excavations may help refine the projectile point chronology for the area. Prior to the current project, 41DL270 had already yielded three radiocarbon dates from humate samples and dates collected during the data recovery will I greatly aid in refining the chronology. Careful analysis of lithostratigraphic units in the terrace sediments coupled with radiocarbon dating of cultural charcoal, wood, bone, and other materials from these strata provide the best method of determining the period of site occupation. I The following questions regarding the chronology of site occupation were posed for the data recovery excavations at 41DL270. As with most of the questions posed in this section, these have been I asked before and some limited answers have been offered (Prikryl 1992). 1) What is the temporal span of site occupation at 41DL270? I 2) Was the occupation periodic or continuous? I 3) How does the temporal placement of the site relate to reconstructions of the prehistoric geomorphic setting? 4) How does the temporal placement of the site relate to the cultural sequence established I for the area? SEfTLEMENT/SUBSISTENCE I Limited test excavations at the site initially suggested that it was a residential site. Chipped stone and faunal materials recovered from the data recovery excavations, coupled with detailed analysis of the I function of the fire-cracked rock hearths and mussel shell concentrations, has the potential to indicate the season of occupation, the range of activities performed at the site, and the region exploited by site inhabitants. I Chipped stone tools provide potentially important information on site function. Uses of tools can be inferred through their form, context of discovery, wear and breakage patterns, and residues on their I surfaces. The sources of chipped stone material have been used to examine the interactions of prehistoric peoples. Chipped stone materials recovered from 41DL270 are generally thought to be from Trinity River gravels located a relatively short distance from the site. However, some materials brought from I longer distances may also be present. Chipped stone materials from the proposed excavations may be useful in examining the distribution of procurement areas for residents of the site and to examine changes in procurement areas. I Perhaps even more important than the chipped stone, which is relatively infrequent at the site, is the faunal assemblage present. Archeofaunas are one of the most important potential tools of I 40 I I


![Image 1 - Page 57](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p57_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 57*


---

## Page 58

I I researchers since, unlike tools, they reflect a more direct and immediate link to the environment. Both vertebrate and invertebrate fauna are examined to better understand the prehistoric adaptation. Fauna! remains can indicate the reliance on different animal species, seasonality of site use, and site function. I This data will also be of great relevance to understanding the site paleoenvironment as discussed in the following section. As with the above questions, the following set of questions reflects a preliminary attempt to I understand the subsistence patterns at the site, based upon data known or suspected to be present. 1) Does the site exhibit a range of functionally diagnostic tools indicating multiple I activities were performed at the site? 2) Are the mussel shell concentrations and fire-cracked rock hearths associated temporally? I Can they be associated with the use of the site for similar purposes or different functional uses? I 3) Are mortars, pestles, or other ground stone tools present that would suggest plant food processing? I 4) What is the procurement region for chipped stone materials found at the site? Does the form in which these materials arrive at the site suggest that procurement areas are near the site (i.e., represented by large numbers of cores and debitage) or that finished I materials are brought into the site from some distance (i.e., limited debitage present except for maintenance indicators). I 5) What does the fauna! material suggest about the reliance of the site's residents on large mammals, small mammals, or shellfish? Are there changes over time in faunal/shell proportions that can be related to climatic changes proposed to have been developed with the Late Archaic period? I PALEOENVIRONMENTAL DATA I The Middle Archaic of Central Texas has been described as corresponding with a mesic and/or cooler interval which may have prompted the development of more complex social systems (Weir 1976; Gunn and Prewitt n.d.; Brown 1989). This period in Central Texas, which Prewitt (1983) dates between I 4600 and 2250 B.P., is the approximate equivalent of the earliest part of Prikryl 's Late Archaic period in North-Central Texas. Portions of the Central Texas Late Archaic and Prikryl's North-Central Texas Late Archaic may have fallen within a slightly drier and/or warmer period. Subsequently, the latest Late I Archaic and the early Late Prehistoric may have fallen within another slightly more mesic interval during which the West Fork paleosol apparently formed. Finally, a slight warming and/or drying trend may be present during the Late Prehistoric. One or more of the following questions may be partly answered by I data from the site. 1) Do changes in climatic conditions indicated by changes in molluscan fauna and I osteological materials correlate with changes at the site in the distribution or nature of artifacts or features? I 41 I I

## Page 59

I 2) Do changes in climatic conditions correlate with major breaks in cultural strata? Can I climatic changes be related to stages in the cultural sequence established for the area? Several data sets are available for this analysis. These include gastropod fauna, other molluscan I fauna, and osteological materials from the site, as well as the soil matrix within which the site is found. In addition, isotope analysis of various materials, including bone and shell carbonates, can provide important paleoclimatic and paleoenvironmental data. I Dillehay (1974) has long ago proposed that changes in distribution of faunal remains during subperiods of the Archaic may have represented not only different aboriginal adaptations but differential availability of resources (i.e., bison in this case) during different periods, perhaps due to slight changes I in climate. Brown (1989) has suggested that even smaller fauna than bison may represent such changes, following Gunn and Prewitt's (n.d.) suggestions for climatic stress during different subperiods of the Archaic. Perring (personal communication 1992) has recently suggested that the paleoclimate of the I region may be in need of revision. Data gathered from 41DL270 may serve as an independent test of paleoclimatic revisions being proposed by Perring for the region. I I I I I I I I I I I 42 I I

## Page 60

I FIELD METHODS I Dana Anthony Because initial observations of exposed features and examination of trenches at the site predicted I that artifact density would be low, artifact recovery and analysis was not considered the main focus of the excavations planned for the site. Instead, the major focus of the investigations at 41DL270 was oriented toward the maximum recovery of ecofacts and environmental samples in the short field time I available. The methods employed took advantage of the drainage ditch which provided easy access to features at the site which would otherwise be invisible from the surface and too deeply buried for quick hand excavation (Figure 4). I The area of focus of the excavations was basically limited to the area of expected impact from the proposed transmission line on the east bank of the drainage ditch from Structure #1_0 north to I Structure #9 (Figure 5). From previous investigations this area was known to contain rich cultural deposits from which the definition of the initial three cultural zones was derived (Anthony et. al 1992). In addition, limited investigations were conducted during the testing phase as far south as Structure #12. I These initial excavations consisted of a series of seven backhoe trenches placed in the bottom of the ditch (three were placed by Structure #12, east of the ditch) to further explore the geomorphological setting of the site in addition to defining the extent of Old Cultural Zone III and any other cultural strata which I might lie even deeper. Following the data recovery plan, the intent of the investigations was to define, insofar as I possible, the vertical extent of the site and its northern boundary (the site extends well to the south of the impact area and no investigations were undertaken in this portion), and to collect data to answer the questions raised as part of that plan. These questions are repeated in the previous chapter. To undertake this, a series of backhoe trenches were excavated to better understand the stratigraphy at the site, and a I number of hand-excavated units were dug to collect data on cultural .features at the site. This section briefly outlines the methods used in this phase of the project. I As a part of the data recovery investigations, the original cultural zones were reassessed and a new series of nine cultural zones developed. These zones (Zone I being the most recent at the site and Zone IX being the oldest), were designated arbitrarily based on elevation and clustering of features. The I use of these zones, which are delineated in the chapter below describing features recorded at the site, was an heuristic tool to better understand the vertical distribution of remains. An in-depth discussion and interpretation of these zones is included in the final chapters of this volume. I STRATIGRAPIDC INVESTIGATIONS I Because the site lies buried in the alluvium of Denton Creek, it was considered imperative to understand the depositional and post-depositional history of the sediments at the site. In fact, since it was expected that artifact recovery would be relatively limited at the site, a complete understanding of and I documentation of the stratigraphy at the site was perhaps even more important than under usual conditions. To facilitate this, the project geomorphologist, Chris Caran, was consulted during the design phase of the project and was present during the excavation of the main group of mitigation backhoe I trenches at the site. During this period, the principal investigator, the project archeologist, and the project geomorphologist jointly and separately profiled several of the trenches and conferred closely to develop a preliminary geomorphic model of the site. I 43 I I

## Page 62

I I Following the trench excavations, Caran developed a sketch model of the general depositional section and a generalized model of the lithostratigraphic and chronostratigraphic units at the site. Following this model development, the entire crew was taken on a tour of the open trenches as well as I the large gully cut between the drainage ditch and Denton Creek (Figure 6) and given a briefing so that they might better place their individual excavation units within the preliminary site model. I Following Caran's visits to the field, further profiles, excavation data, and sample results were provided him to allow minor revisions of the preliminary model based on newly acquired data. Several meetings were held on this during the course of the write-up. The return of the final radiocarbon samples allowed completion of this site depositional model which is presented in its entirety in the following I chapter. In fact, this final model deviated very little from the original. MAPPING I During data recovery, all units, trenches, and surface features were mapped with a transit, stadia, and tape (Figure 7). Mapping of the units included the four corners and the datums. Mapping of the I trenches included shots at either end for location as well as the elevation at the deepest point. Surface features on the eroded ditch bank were mapped by observing the central highest elevation point from which they were thought to have eroded. In this way, we hoped we could get an elevation as close as I possible to the original surface on which they originated. Regardless how far the feature had eroded, each is represented by only one elevation, that of the highest point. Each feature was tagged with a flag on which the following information was documented or checked off as completed: recorded, mapped, I photographed, and collections. This system was especially useful as a backup to the field logs as anyone examining the features could see what had been completed and what still needed to be done without having the log book present. I A series of topographic shots were also compiled in order to complete an accurate contour map of the primary area investigated. Two permanent datums (A and B) were established and set in concrete. I Elevations above sea level are only approximate, derived from the USGS map elevation of the datum area. The resulting topographic map does not extend east beyond the fence which is the boundary of the of the BEPC right-of-way, nor does it extend west beyond the creek. The actual bank of the creek is I only estimated from aerial photos in most places. BACKHOE TRENCHES I A total of ten backhoe trenches were excavated during the mitigation phase at 41DL270. When recorded, these trenches were designated with the initials BHT, followed by an "M" (for mitigation) to differentiate them from trenches excavated during the survey/testing phase. The trenches were dug in I varying lengths to an average depth of 2.30 meters at their deepest point below ground surface. The deepest point was in the center of the trench with one end slanted or stair-stepped for easy entry (and exit). In some cases, the trench was widened and a small platform was excavated about half way down I the depth of the trench as an easy place to stand while recording and collecting samples and to allow inspection of the trench without descending below the limits set by the Texas Trench Safety Act or the OSHA Standards. This intermediate bench was particularly useful in Trench M2, which hit ground I water. After each trench was excavated, it was recorded on a Backhoe Trench Form and also profiled. Detailed descriptions were also provided by the geomorphologist. Efforts were made to photograph each trench, but lighting did not cooperate. Soil samples were collected from each zone within each trench. I In addition, humate samples were also collected from particular zones thought to be suitable for 45 I I

## Page 64

I I radiocarbon dating. Sediment samples from which snails could be extracted for habitat analysis were collected from two of the trenches, and a single pollen sample was collected from one trench. I HAND-EXCAVATED UNITS ' ;~-i. A total of nine units, investigating the features eroding out of the various cultural zones on the I east side of the drainage ditch, were placed upslope and center of the highest exposed part of the feature (Figure 8). In this way, any intact part still buried and preserved would be exposed. For the most part, the units were lx2 meters in size (with the exception of Unit 8 which was 2x2 meters) and oriented I longitudinal to the drainage ditch, affording the most coverage. One half of the unit (lxl meter) was excavated at a time. If an intact part of the feature was present, excavation would continue into the other half. If not, excavation would cease and move on to another feature. The depth of the excavation in I some cases correlated with the original ground surface on which the feature had been placed or dug from. Most excavation units continued below the intended feature and its associated ground surface. The units were excavated in ten-centimeter arbitrary levels except where the natural or cultural stratigraphy could I be followed. In these units, the matrix was usually the same throughout the levels so no natural break could be discerned. Generally, the matrix was a dark brown silty clay which increased in compactness and in carbonates (from threads to nodules) with depth. Thus, units excavated near the top of the slope I had fewer carbonate threads, whereas those excavated near the bottom of the slope or below contained extensive threading and nodules. As the units were on a slope, the first one or two levels did not cover the entire width of the unit, often reaching just half way across (Figure 9). Often, the first level was dug I in a 20 centimeter level to facilitate the removal of the overburden, which for the most part was disturbed sandy slope wash. Original Cultural Zone III, known to lie buried beneath the bottom of the ditch, was investigated I by a single 2x2 meter unit. This zone now corresponds with Cultural Zones V through VII. Prior to setting up the unit, Trench M6 had been excavated with a mussel shell feature located at a depth corresponding to the original Cultural Zone III or at an absolute depth of 140.83 feet. I A single lxl meter unit was excavated in the gully leading from the ditch to Denton Creek (Figure 10). Unit 9 was situated above and off center of the suspected pit feature revealed in the north I face of the gully. It was dug down from a bench on the upper gully wall instead of into the slope as the other units were, making it more of a traditional excavation unit than the wedges that were excavated around features exposed in the drainage ditch bank. I SCREENING I Once a couple of units had been excavated, it became clear that the 10 to 20 centimeter overburden could be quickly dry screened. In a few cases if nothing was found in the screen after several loads, the remainder was tossed. The first two units excavated, Units 1 and 3, were wet-screened I throughout all levels. This set up consisted of two screens - a ¼ inch wire mesh screen superimposed over window screening. The fine screening was chosen as an efficient means of collecting microfauna, flora, debitage, and other items which would easily slip through the larger screen. The compact silty clay I proved to be very difficult to water screen and very time consuming. After thoroughly water screening two lxl meter units, it was decided to confine our retrieval to ¼ inch wire mesh dry screening and depend on flotation for a representative sample of each excavated level. One exception was Unit 9; I practically every level was water screened (in the gully between Denton Creek and the drainage ditch) because of the potential for a pit feature (Figure 11). Ideally, every level would have been water I 47 I

## Page 67

I I screened, but mechanical problems with the water pump sometimes forced the crew to resort to other measures until the pump could be fixed or replaced. The length from the creek to the water screening station was about 80 feet (with the exception of the screens set up next to Unit 9). After trying several hookups, including two-inch fire hoses and one-inch garden hoses, the best solution was a combination I of the two. The fire hoses alone resulted in too much pressure, with the potential to damage delicate artifacts or blow them out of the screens and cause severe erosion of the gully below the screens. I SAMPLE COLLECTION Samples of sediment for miscellaneous analyses were collected from each defined sediment strata I in the trenches and from each level of the excavation units in a single 8x8 inch plastic zip-lock bag. Some of these samples were devoted to magnetic susceptibility analysis (see "Archeomagnetic Studies of Site 41DL270", this volume). Most were utilized in the laboratory to verify field descriptions of I sediments identified in the trenches and units. Flotation samples were collected in two 1-gallon plastic zip-lock bags corresponding to each unit I level and from individual feature fill. Humate samples, from selected zones, were collected in three 1- gallon plastic zip-lock bags. The locales for each sample within the trenches were plotted and then excavated back from the face of the profile to avoid contamination with exposed soils. Humate samples I from units were collected in two different ways. The first method involved collection from a column located off the east wall (or upslope, intact soil). The upper 10 centimeters and 5 to 10 centimeters of the face were discarded as they were likely to be contaminated, then the samples were collected in units I corresponding to the excavated levels. The second collection method consisted of taking samples from freshly exposed levels as the situation dictated if it was impractical to take a column sample (due to inaccessibility of the wall, a collapsed unit, or cement-like soil). Soil samples for gastropod analysis were taken in the same manner as the humate samples. Trench samples were collected in a single 1-gallon I plastic zip-lock bag. Charcoal for radiocarbon dating was collected from pristine contexts and wrapped in foil and I placed inside a plastic zip-lock bag. These contexts included hearth fill directly from the features, though not charcoal noted while hand excavating areas that were not part of a feature. Samples suitable for wood species identification were also collected from exposed contexts and wrapped in foil. I All shells comprising an excavated mussel shell feature were collected. These were wrapped in tissue or foil and placed in tissue lined plastic bags. Individual shells from unit levels collected from dry I screening were not wrapped individually. Shells mapped in place as features were bagged individually and numbered accordingly. Those shells collected for isotope analysis were wrapped in foil and then sealed in a plastic zip-lock bag. ## Page 68

I I BACKHOE TRENCH DESCRIPTIONS Dana Anthony I A total of eleven backhoe trenches were excavated during the mitigation phase of investigations at 41DL270. For the most part, these were placed in the areas of the site to receive the greatest impact from the placement of transmission line structures. This effort was concentrated on the east bank of the I ditch in the area from Structure #10 north to the location of Structure #9; six trenches were excavated in this area. It was in this general locality that the three original cultural zones identified from the survey/testing phase were defined. In addition, three trenches were excavated in the bottom of the ditch I in this same locality to help define the extent of the lowest zone (original Cultural Zone III) and to determine if any cultural zones might exist below it. The trenches in the bottom of the ditch also served as geomorphological profiles aiding in the interpretation of the floodplain history as well as relic channels I of Denton Creek. Since the area around Structure #12 (the southernmost structure to potentially impact the site) was I not sampled during the survey/testing phase, two trenches were excavated in that locality to determine if any significant cultural deposits were present. If it was found the site would not be impacted in this area, none of the planned hand excavation units would be placed in that locality, but instead concentrated I in the area discussed above. Brief descriptions of the soil profile zones are presented below. It should be noted that the term I "soil zone" used throughout this chapter is simply a convenience referring to sections of the entire "soil profile" and is not meant to imply that pedogenic development has occurred in each zone. Throughout the report, the terms soil and sediment are generally used interchangeably; where pedogenic development is emphasized the terms paleosol or pedogenically altered are used. I For a general overview of site stratigraphy, see the chapter on "Geomorphology and Quaternary Stratigraphy of Denton Creek". For a detailed discussion of the new cultural zones identified at the site, I see "Feature Descriptions" and "Archeological Synthesis" in this volume. All of the trenches were approximately 2 feet wide or the width of the backhoe bucket. Elevations, for the most part, are given in averages in meters below ground surface. The locations of these trenches are illustrated on the I 41DL270 site map (Figure 12). BACKHOE TRENCH Ml I This trench was placed roughly perpendicular to the shallow erosional gully on the west side of the ditch leading to the large gully cut between the ditch and Denton Creek. It was 4.5 meters in length I and reached a maximum depth of 3 meters (the water table was reached at 2.25 meters). The profile of the north wall of this trench is illustrated in Figure 13. I Zone 1 0.00 -0.35 meters - yellow brown silty loam with carbonates; subrectangular structure; loose, irregular lower boundary. I Zone 2 0.35 - 0.50 meters - same as above, but paler in color, and looser in structure. Zone 3 0.50 - 0.90 meters - brown silty loam with compact small carbonate inclusions; I irregular upper and lower boundary. I 51 I

I I Zone 10 1. 70 - 2.80 meters - dark grayish brown silty to clay loam; carbonates; root casts; regular boundaries. I Zone 11 2.80 + - mottled gray and yellow clay. What appears to be a relict channel of Denton Creek was identified in this trench; certainly the I deposits above Zone 10 appear to be inset into the Late Holocene terrace fill. Modern flood deposits mark the upper 0.50 meters of the trench. Below the modern zone, to about 2.00 meters below the surface are laminated sands and clays representing alluvial fills. Pluvial channel deposits comprise the lowest strata of the trench (see "Geomorphology and Quaternary Stratigraphy of Denton Creek", this I volume, for a geomorphological description). A humate sample from Zone 10 was radiocarbon dated to 1295 ± 120 B.P. (GX-18706; o 13C -20.7). No cultural features or materials were identified within this trench. ## Page 80

I I Bone and charcoal from Soil Zone 1 indicate a cultural zone. In the field, this was thought to correspond with the original Cultural Zone II defined during the testing phase. A bulk soil sample from this zone was radiocarbon dated to 3345 ± 180 B.P. (GX-19562; o 13C -20.5). Stratigraphically, this falls I within new Cultural Zone IV (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume, for an interpretation of this date). A humate sample from Soil Zone 5 was also analyzed and produced a radiocarbon date of 3615 ± 165 B.P. (GX-18707; o 13C -17.7). The stratigraphy of the soil zone as well as the date places it within I new Cultural Zone VIII. BACKHOE TRENCH MS I This trench was located about 15 meters southwest of the proposed location for Structure #12 (detail of Figure 12). It was oriented basically east-west. The trench measured 5.6 meters in length and I 2.0 meters in depth. As this trench was in close proximity to Trench M9, the profile of M9 was drawn as a representation of both. Feature 4, a burned clay feature, was found in the bottom of Trench MS in the dark silty clay with carbonates zone. After the feature was troweled and profiled it became apparent I that it was a burned tree root. No other signs of cultural materials were observed. BACKHOE TRENCH M9 I This trench was located just a few meters from Trench MS at the location of the proposed Structure #12. It was oriented east-west. The trench measured 4. 7 meters in length and was 3.25 meters in depth. The zone depths were measured at a point 0.60 meters from the west wall. The south wall profile is I depicted in Figure 19. Zone 1 0.00 - 0.28 meters - brownish yellow sandy loam; subrounded gravel; loose; root I action. Zone 2 0.28 - 1.05 meters - silty sand mixed with subrounded gravels; bioturbated (?); large I clay inclusions. Zone 3 1.05 - 1.13 meters - grayish brown with dark carbonates; root action; sand mottling I throughout. Zone 4 1.13 - 1.20 meters - brownish yellow clay; mottled with organic throughout (i.e., root I casts apparent). Zone 5 1.20 - 1. 70 meters - dark yellowish brown silty clay; organic rich, root action, charcoal I specks. Zone 6 1.70 -3.25 meters - dark grayish brown clay; organic rich, snails, root action, charcoal I specks. It was not determined if the charcoal specks noted in Soil Zones 5 and 6 were the result of cultural I practices, or simply associated with the rich organics comprising these zones. I 63 I I

## Page 85

I I CONCLUDING REMARKS Most of the backhoe trenches excavated during the data recovery phase sampled intact alluvial I deposits. The majority of these trenches were, in fact, excavated into what is thought to be primarily a Late Holocene terrace deposit. In general, the stratigraphy of this macro-stratigraphic unit is flat-lying and suggests relatively low energy flood deposition. Most of the cultural materials were found in areas I of fine-textured sediments where occasional evidence of stability or pedogenesis could be observed. Within the drainage ditch several trenches penetrated older alluvial deposits well below the lowest cultural material and one trench encountered evidence of channel cutting along the edge of the terrace. This area I marked the transition to the lower alluvial surface where the most recent cultural remains were found. For a discussion of this and other issues regarding the geomorphology of the site, see "Geomorphology and Quaternary Stratigraphy of Denton Creek", this volume. I Considerable evidence of pedogenic activity was noted in the trenches; several of the soil zones could probably be designated as pedostratigraphic units or even paleosols. Nonetheless, comparison of the soils in these trenches does not indicate a single clear and obvious "West Fork" paleosol as appears I to be visible in soil profiles exposed by Prikryl (1992) in his investigations at the site in conjunction with National Register nomination. These relationships are discussed in greater detail in the following chapter. I While there is abundant evidence of predominantly flat-lying stratigraphy across the entire Holocene terrace section, there is no clear and consistent single stratigraphic profile across the terrace. While some of this may be due to recorder error (four individuals were involved in recording trench profiles in I different combinations), all of the trenches were inspected by the principal investigator, geomorphologist, and project archeologist and the differences are noticeable. Thus, while it appears that there are no consistent north-south strata, there are a series of roughly equivalent alluvial facies which differ slightly I in texture and soil development. These differences may be attributable to shifting creek locations, which cause shifts in the size sorting of alluvial sediments through time. Once again, some general correlations are discussed in the subsequent chapter. I Finally, although the terrace and floodplain/lower terrace segments at the site are clearly Holocene, there is also possible evidence for an older alluvial fill unit buried within the Late Holocene terrace. I Certainly, much earlier Quaternary or even Tertiary age alluvial deposits are buried at the base of the section. This is also discussed in greater detail in the next chapter. I I I I I 68 I I


![Image 1 - Page 85](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p85_img1.png)

## Page 86

I I GEOMORPHOWGY AND QUATERNARY STRATIGRAPHY OF DENTON CREEK S. Christopher Caran I INTRODUCTION 1· Geomorphic investigations were conducted at archeological site 41DL270 on the floodplain and fluvil!1 terraces of Denton Creek, Dallas County, North-Central Texas, in conjunction with a cultural re source mitigation program. Denton Creek is a perennial tributary of the Trinity River, an interregional I drainage system. A previous archeological survey (Anthony et al. 1992) had disclosed extensive prehistoric cultural features in an intact, stratigraphic context. Mitigation was required because part of the site was slated for destruction through planned extension of a utility corridor. Initial evidence of I occupation included numerous buried features thought to be prehistoric hearths with charcoal. Lithic artifacts, including projectile points, were recovered in close association with the hearth-like features. The presence of lithic materials without ceramics indicated the site might be preceramic, probably Late I Prehistoric to Late Archaic. Radiocarbon assay of soil-humate and charcoal samples from cultural strata ranged from about 900 to 4200 B.P., confirming the cultural age assignment. A geomorphic assessment of the site was called for during mitigation in order to help define the vertical and horizontal extent of deposits containing cultural materials and to increase understanding of environmental conditions during I and following prehistoric occupation. A series of backhoe trenches and controlled excavations were completed at the site during the I mitigation phase, exposing a thick sequence (>5 meters) of late Quaternary deposits. Thick culturally-associated (and pre-cultural?) fluvial sediments are inset against Pleistocene fill-terrace gravels unconformably overlying Upper Cretaceous sandstone of the Woodbine Formation. A lower, historically I inundated flood terrace of early(?) to late Holocene deposits lies streamward from the Pleistocene gravels. Still closer to Denton Creek are a series of late Holocene to modern channel-margin, levee, and floodplain deposits that accreted as the channel migrated laterally (westward). Cultural remains were I recovered from the levee and flood-terrace deposits, which are bounded by largely acultural channel facies. Results of radiocarbon analyses of these deposits are consistent with field observations and available archeological data. I OBJECTIVES I Objectives of the present geomorphic investigation are to determine: (1) the genetic stratigraphy (mode of deposition of strata) and chronology of deposits exposed in the project area; (2) vertical and horizontal continuity of strata, particularly those from which cultural materials were recovered; (3) I stratigraphic integrity of the record of material culture; and (4) Holocene geomorphic history of the occupation site and surrounding landscape. This study involved a brief inspection of the project area, a review of relevant literature, and integration of available local and regional chronologic data pertaining I to fluvial systems comparable to Denton Creek. WCATION AND GEOMORPIDC CONTEXT I Archeological site 41DL270 is located in extreme northwestern Dallas County, Texas, within the corporate limits of the village of Flower Mound, along Denton Creek, approximately 3.4 kilometers downstream from the dam forming Grapevine Lake (Figure 1). This part of Dallas County is in the I Eastern Cross Timbers region (Soil Conservation Service 1981; Ferguson 1986) of the West Gulf Coastal Plain physiographic province (Fenneman 1946). The site encompasses part of the right bank, levee, I 69 I


![Image 1 - Page 86](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p86_img1.png)
