## Page 87

I I floodplain, flood terrace, and abandoned fill terrace of Denton Creek, a locally northeastward flowing, perennial tributary of the Elm Fork of the Trinity River (Figure 22). The project area parallels the active channel of Denton Creek, extending eastward from the water line onto the flood terrace. Highest I elevation at the site is approximately 145 meters amsl, > 11 meters higher than normal stream stage. The site lies within the valley of lower Denton Creek. Flow through this reach is currently I regulated by discharge from Grapevine Lake, an artificial reservoir constructed in 1952. In 1908 and 1948, however, historic floods crested at 10.9 and 9.3 meters above normal, respectively, at the point where present State Highway 121 crosses Denton Creek, approximately 3.5 kilometers downstream from I the site (Buckner and Shelby 1991:320-321). If these floods attained comparable stages at 41DL270, the entire site must have been inundated. In an effort to relieve flooding, a drainage ditch was constructed through the site in the late 1980s, in part following an abandoned meander loop (compare aerial I photographs from 1942 to 1990, Figs. 4 through 10, respectively, in Anthony et al. 1992). The importance of the site was first realized, in fact, when prehistoric cultural material was exposed in the newly excavated drainage ditch wall. This ditch is approximately 4 meters deep and was excavated I between the stream bank and the proximal portion of the flood terrace (Figure 22). Rising flood waters were intended to flow through the ditch rather than over the bank. Much of the project area was flooded as recently as the winter of 1992-93, however, by prolonged inundation of the ditch itself. More than I 0.5 meters of fluvial sediment has accumulated in the ditch since it was excavated. A natural levee at the stream bank may have limited overbank deposition on the flood terrace prior to excavation of the ditch, which allows flood waters to bypass the levee. In addition, the levee was breached at the site sometime after ditch construction. I PREVIOUS INVESTIGATIONS I Environment Few published references pertain directly to the environmental setting of the project area, although I studies conducted nearby provide useful comparative data. Yates and Perring (1986, Figure 1.1) compiled data pertaining to a broad range of environmental conditions in the upper Trinity River drainage basin, including the Denton Creek watershed. Local and regional climatic records are summarized by I Carr (1967), Orton (1969), Larkin and Bomar (1983), Griffiths and Bryan (1987), and Riggio et al. (1987). The local climate is continental subtropical with dry winters and hot, humid summers (Griffiths and Bryan 1987:6). At Dallas, 23 kilometers southeast of the site, mean annual temperature and I precipitation are 18.9" C and 912 millimeters, respectively, and maximum annual precipitation was 1401 millimeters in 1957 (Griffiths and Bryan 1987: 135). Discussions of Central Texas flood hazards by Caran and Baker (1986) are applicable, in part, to North-Central Texas. Flood-stage, surface-water I discharge, and water-quality data regarding Denton Creek were collected during at least part of the period from 1947 to the present at Grapevine Lake, approximately 3.4 kilometers upstream from the site, and at State Highway 121, approximately 3.5 kilometers downstream (Buckner and Shelby 1991:320-321). I Some aspects of the local physical environment are reasonably well defined. The project area and surroundings are shown on the Grapevine and Lewisville West 7.5' topographic maps (Geological Survey I 1981a, 1981b). Coffee et al. (1980) mapped the soils of Dallas County, including the current study area. Numerous investigators have discussed and/or mapped the pre-Quaternary geology of Denton Creek and vicinity, including: Hill (1901); Shuler (1918); Dodge (1968); Hawkins et al. (1974); Hendricks (1976); I and McGowen et al. (1987). Mineral resources and environmental geology of the local area have also been described, by Shaw (1928), Fry (1929), Pence (1951), Sampson (1976), and Allen (1984). Surface- I 70 I

## Page 89

I I and ground-water resources of Dallas County were treated by Cumley (1943), Leifeste and Hughes (1967), and Nordstrom (1982). Upper Cretaceous Woodbine Formation sandstones and Quaternary I valley-fill deposits are among the principal aquifers. Quaternary Stratigraphy and Geomorphology I For purposes of this discussion, the Quaternary Period is the interval from 1.64 million years ago to the present, which includes the Pleistocene Epoch, 1.64 million to 10,000 B.P., and the Holocene Epoch, 10,000 B.P. to today (Harland et al. 1990, Figure 1.7). The Holocene Epoch is here informally I divided into three equal episodes, "early," "middle," and "late," in addition to the "modern" interval, roughly corresponding to the present century. Known human occupation of North-Central Texas on an occasional or persistent basis spans the past 11,000 years, from the latest Pleistocene through the I Holocene (Perring 1990; Prikryl 1990). Geomorphic features and deposits representing the potential period of occupation are the focus of the current investigation, but aspects of pre-Holocene geology are discussed as appropriate. I Shuler (1923) was perhaps the first to associate cultural materials with Late Quaternary sediments and fossil vertebrate remains in this area. Quaternary deposits, including those predating human I occupation, were mapped and briefly described by Shaw (1928), Hendricks (1976), and McGowen et al. (1987). The most complete reviews and summaries of the Late Quaternary geology of this region are those of Perring (1986, 1989a). These compilations of existing data from the upper Trinity River valley I are exhaustive, but Perring (1986: 101) acknowledged the inherent limitations of available information, and stressed the need for improved chronologic control, paleoenvironmental data, and borehole testing of deeply buried strata. I Based on his investigations, Perring (1986, Figure 4.16) concluded that the geomorphic history of the Trinity River valley during the time of potential occupation has been dominated by net aggradation I following an episode of downcutting that produced a series of Middle(?) and Late Pleistocene fill-terrace deposits standing in relief above the channel. Continual deposition during the past 15,000 years has filled the Late Pleistocene paleochannel essentially to the level of terraces that were stranded as a result of Late Wisconsin (20,000 to 15,000 B.P.) downcutting (Richmond and Fullerton 1986). As a result, late I Holocene valley fill is inset against fluvial deposits of Wisconsin age. During extreme flood events, the Trinity River rises well above its banks, spreading waters with I a heavy load of fine, suspended sediment across the floodplain. The rising waters may also ascend the mouths of tributaries, impeding drainage and increasing flooding upstream. When flow begins to slacken, the flood waters are no longer sufficiently turbulent to hold even fine-textured sediment in suspension, I causing a veneer of laminated very fine sand, silt, and clay to be deposited, usually in that order. The floodplain of a major river like the Trinity, as well as the lower portions of its tributaries, may be inundated several times in a single year, resulting in rapid accretion of sediment layers, each characterized I by an upward decrease in particle size. At the mouth of Denton Creek, the Elm Fork of the Trinity River has a wide floodplain exhibiting large-amplitude meanders, numerous abandoned channels (scroll topography), and excised fill-terrace remnants (Geological Survey 1981c). These features are indicative I of a river with a large contributing drainage basin, flashy discharge, and high sediment load. The effect that aggradation in the Trinity valley may have had on reaches of Denton Creek above I its mouth is unclear. Ferring (1989b:1-18) described terraces of the Elm Fork of the upper Trinity as being "accordant with terraces of major tributaries," including Denton Creek. Certainly, the floodplain I 72 I


![Image 1 - Page 89](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p89_img1.png)

## Page 90

I I of lower Denton Creek is virtually accordant with that of the Trinity River proper. Perring (1988: 12, 17), in discussing Village Creek, a tributary of the West Fork of the Trinity in neighboring eastern Tarrant I County, concluded that thick clays of the modern floodplain (his Holocene terrace) probably were deposited, in part, under slack-water conditions in the aftermath of flooding along the Trinity River. In later studies throughout the Village Creek valley, Caran (1990a, 1990b) concluded that the detailed I history of tributary-valley filling is more complex than that outlined by Perring (1988), but found general agreement in the mechanisms and timing of Holocene aggradation proposed by Perring (1986, 1988, 1989a). These studies confirmed the late Holocene age of thick, inset flood-plain deposits, primarily I clays. Available evidence indicates that floods are common in the project area, although direct involvement of the Trinity may be comparatively infrequent. The extent to which local conditions at 41DL270 may have affected aggradation is discussed below. I No comprehensive geomorphic studies are known to have been conducted previously at 41DL270 or its immediate vicinity, although Perring (C.R. Perring, personal communication 1990) and Caran have I independently visited the area in conjunction with other projects. Anthony et al. (1992) discussed the stratigraphy of the site in relation to the distribution of cultural materials. McGowen et al. (1987) mapped and briefly described the Quaternary sediments of this area as part of their regional mapping effort, but provided few details and only a relative chronology of Quaternary deposition. Table 1 I illustrates the generalized regional stratigraphy, combining available numerical chronological data. The present assessment draws upon findings in other parts of the Trinity River valley to present an integrated summary of the stratigraphy of the site. I GEOMORPHOLOGY AND STRATIGRAPHY I Geologic investigations in the present study area involved description and selective sampling of a series of controlled excavations and backhoe trenches, as well as existing exposures along the walls of the drainage ditch and the gully breaching the levee. These exposures revealed stratigraphic sections as I much as approximately 2.8 meters deep. Trenches and hand excavations were distributed throughout the project area. Major depositional units were identified in each of the sections, and soil horizons were differentiated on the basis of field criteria. Soil descriptions and horizon designations were then I compared with typical profiles of the nominal soils of the project area as mapped by Coffee et al. (1980). Observations concerning the exposed sections are based on the results of field examinations and I testing, and include designations of genetically discrete stratigraphic units, as well as the soil horizons superimposed on them. Stratigraphic and sedimentologic descriptions follow the standards compiled by Dietrich et al. (1982). Soil properties were discussed in accordance with conventions defined by the Soil I Survey Staff (1993). Munsell colors (names and numerical designations) were determined in the field by David 0. Brown on ambient moist specimens under sunlight. Large, bulk soil samples for radiocarbon assay were collected by archeological crew members from fresh exposures at selected I locations. Roots and other obvious sources of contamination were avoided in sampling, and samples were immediately sealed and labelled in the field. I STRATIGRAPIDC SECTIONS Backhoe Trench M2 I Backhoe Trench M2 {BHT M2) was excavated on the floor of the drainage ditch, within the floodplain of Denton Creek, approximately in the center of site 41DL270 (Figure 12). This location has I 73 I

I I an elevation of approximately 141 meters amsl, or approximately 7.5 meters above water level in Denton Creek. Figure 23a (see also Figure 24) illustrates the complex stratigraphic section exposed in the I northeast-facing wall of this 4 meters long, 250-centimeter deep trench. In keeping with geological tradition, the stratigraphic units in Trench M2 and the other trenches are numbered sequentially from the bottom. [Note: Unit numbers in one profile are independent of those in another; that is, units of the same I number in two or more trenches are not necessarily correlative.] Unit 1. Deposits exposed in Trench M2 include five discrete genetic and pedogenic units. At the I base of the exposed section is Unit 1, a very clayey silty very fine to medium sand (sandy clay loam in pedologic nomenclature) probably representing a channel-axis of abandoned channel deposits subjected to incipient pedogenesis. Maximum exposed thickness of Unit 1 is 20 centimeters. Color of this unit is mottled, with a yellowish brown (lOYRS/6) groundmass and gray (10YR5/l) mottles. Consistence is I firm and structure is massive (structureless), probably as a result of intense bioturbation (root penetration). Unit 1 was covered by rising ground water (stable water table at 250 centimeters depth) after Trench M2 was excavated. The upper boundary of Unit 1 is an abrupt irregular erosional I unconformity inclining steeply to the northwest. Unit 2. Unit 2 is inset against the erosional unconformity on Unit 1, and probably post-dates Unit I 1 by an extended period. The texture of Unit 2 is a silty very fine to fine sandy clay (clay or clay loam). Consistence is firm and structure is massive. Maximum exposed thickness is 130 centimeters. Color is dark grayish brown (10YR3/2) with white calcareous root traces. Calcareous fine root traces are I abundant in the upper 30 centimeters, particularly at the southeastern end of the trench. Black carbonized fine root traces are common throughout. Gastropod shell fragments are uncommon. The upper boundary is smooth and gradual (heavily bioturbated), but inclined to the northwest. As a result, the depth to Unit I 1 varies from 150 centimeters at the southeastern end of the trench to 250 centimeters at the northwestern end. Unit 1 is a pedogenically-modified fluvial-channel deposit, consisting of a Btkb2 horizon (upper 30 centimeters) separated from an underlying Btb2/Bwb2 horizon by a smooth gradual boundary. A I radiocarbon (bulk humate) sample collected at 205 centimeters depth yielded a o 13C-corrected age of 1295 ± 120 B.P. The reliability and significance of this age determination are discussed below. I Unit 3. Unit 3 overlies Unit 2 and is also inclined to the northwest. The top of Unit 3 ranges from 53 to 199 centimeters depth. Maximum thickness of Unit 3 is 97 centimeters at the southeastern end of Trench M2, where this unit is truncated by Unit 4 (see discussion of Unit 4, below). Unit 3 is I a silty very fine to fine and rare medium sandy clay (clay or clay loam). Consistence is firm and structure is massive (structureless). Medium root traces are uncommon, as are small gastropod shell fragments. Color is very dark grayish brown (10YR3/2) with dusky red (2.5YR3/2) root mottles. The I upper boundary of Unit 3 is smooth and gradual, and inclines 35 degrees to the northwest. Unit 3 may be a slack-water deposit partly filling an abandoned channel. Pedogenic modification of Unit 3 is minimal {Clbl horizon). I Unit 4. Unit 4 includes four beds, all of which thicken to the northwest and are bounded by contacts inclined to the northwest. At the base of the unit, from 162 to 199 centimeters maximum depth (37 centimeters maximum thickness) as measured at the northwestern end of Trench M2, Unit 4 is firm I and massive (structureless), and consists of silty clayey very fine to fine sand (sandy loam), indistinctly mottled with silty very fine sandy clay (clay) and abundant very coarse sand-size fecal pellets of aquatic invertebrates. There are uncommon burrows to 1.5 centimeters diameter and 8 centimeters length I throughout this interval, which is dark grayish brown (2.5Y4 /2) with gray (10YR5/l) mottles. The basal bed of Unit 4 is delimited above by a smooth, gradual boundary, and overlain by interlaminated silty very I 75 I

Although the four discrete intervals differ slightly in texture, they may be treated as stacked components of a C2bl horizon. Unit 4, as a I whole, is an abandoned-channel (chute?) deposit, which may have remained water saturated until recently. Unit 5. Color of this interval varies from light olive brown to yellowish brown (2.5Y5/6 to I lOYRS/6). These beds are part of a C horizon capped with a 1 centimeter thick A/O horizon. Unit 5 appears to be a chute-like deposit that accumulated within the drainage ditch since the late 1980s. Excavation of the ditch, as well as subsequent erosion, apparently truncated the upper ends of the inclined I strata in this exposure prior to burial beneath Unit 5. I I because the section described is on the distal (landward) side of pie levee, where sediment typically spills over and down. A layer of charcoal among the burned rocks dips 8.5 degrees to the southeast. Relict I bedding in the lower half of Unit 1 parallels this layer, indicating the burned rocks were placed on a natural surface, or in a shallow pit dug to uniform depth below such a surface. Bedding in the upper half of the unit dips at a slightly different angle, but generally parallels the upper boundary, indicating I continuous aggradation following occupation. The upper boundary of Unit 1 is irregular and gradual. The lower boundary is the limit of exposure, which is defined by the distribution of slumped sediment. Slumping postdates gully incision. Unit 1 is a fluvial overbank deposit directly associated with prehistoric I occupation, and minimally modified by pedogenesis (Cb horizon). Unit 2. Unit 2 overlies the stratum (Unit 1) containing hearth-like clusters of burned rocks. Unit I 2 is a silty very fine sandy clay with rare medium sand (silty clay or silty clay loam). There are relict indications that Unit 2 accumulated as stacked upward-fining beds probably finer grained than those of Units 1 and 3. Consistence is hard, and the structure is strong medium angular prismatic breaking to I blocky. Rare fragments of charcoal or carbonized wood are dispersed throughout. Insect burrows are common, but inconspicuous, and gastropod fragments are uncommon. The upper boundary of Unit 2 is sharp and irregular, and inclines to the southeast subparallel to the lower. Approximately 6 meters ·1 southeast of the section described, Unit 2 forms the floor of the gully. This unit is a cumulic soil (Bwb horizon) developed in overbank deposits. During the period of aggradation, the sediment carried over the levee by flood waters was comparatively fine. This could indicate that the levee had attained enough I relief that it could contain all but the uppermost part of the water column, where the finest sediment is carried. Unit 3. Unit 3 consists of stacked upward-fining thin beds of silty very fine to medium sand I (sandy loam) grading upward to very fine sandy clay (sandy clay loam or sandy clay) in 7 to 12 centimeters thick beds inclined away from the present channel. Medium root traces obscure the contacts between many of the thin beds, particularly in the lower half of the unit. Insect(?) burrows extend as I much as 11 centimeters into the top of underlying Unit 2 and are filled with the sediment of Unit 3. There are a few granule-size rip-up clasts of Unit 2 sediment along the basal contact of Unit 3. Terrestrial gastropod shells and fragments are common in the lower 35 centimeters of this unit. I Lamination is preserved throughout, but is less conspicuous in the lower half. Unit 3 of the gully exposure may be correlative with the upper part of the section at Trench M2 (Unit 5). Unit 3 is a nearly fresh overbank deposit (C horizon). I Backhoe Trench MS I Backhoe Trench M8 was excavated southwest of site 41DL270 proper in an effort to characterize the high Pleistocene fill terrace into which Denton Creek valley is incised. Trench M8 was located in the abandoned gravel pit area between the site and State Highway 121 at an elevation of approximately I 144 meters amsl, or approximately 10.5 meters above Denton Creek (Figure 12). Based on reconnaissance at intact outcrops of the fill terrace, the thickness of gravel stripped from the area near the site may have been approximately 3 meters. I Chronologic Interpretation I Several kinds of evidence support a chronologic interpretation of the geomorphic history of site 41DL270. Diagnostic Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric projectile points have been recovered at the site. Although only a few of these diagnostic artifacts are in direct association with features or in well-defined I stratigraphic contexts, the presumed ages of these artifacts are generally consistent with available radiocarbon assays from the site. A series of radiocarbon analyses of soil humates and charcoal yielded ± ± ages of 875 125 B.P. to 4165 205 B.P. (see Table 29 for a complete listing of the radiocarbon dates from 41DL270). Geomorphic interpretation likewise indicates an apparent middle to late Holocene I (approximately 4000 B.P.) age of the cultural strata and associated landforms. Age of the deposits may be inferred using "proxy" indicators of relative and calibrated I chronostratigraphy. Soil development offers a basis for assessing relative age, and there are several methods whereby soil age can be estimated semi-quantitatively, based on calibrated rates of acquisition of certain soil properties (Harden and Taylor 1983). Coffee et al. (1980, sheet 1) mapped the soils of I the study area as the Frio silty clay (a Cumulic Haplustoll), and those of the adjacent Pleistocene fill terrace as a taxadjunct of the Silawa fine sandy loam (an Ultic Haplustalt). The Silawa series (A-Bt-C profile) was removed from the terrace with the gravel, as part of the gravel mining operation culminating I in the late 1980s. Development of an Ultic Haplustalf is indicative of thorough leaching of exchangeable cations from the soil profile. In this subhumid climate, where limestone lithoclasts compose a significant part of the alluvium, significant leaching requires many thousands of years. Therefore, the Silawa I taxadjunct is a clear indication of a Pleistocene soil and landscape. Some but not all of the soils observed in backhoe trenches and other exposures are consistent with I the Frio series, which has an A-Ck profile. Development of a calcic soil is one of the more readily quantifiable pedogenic rates (Gile 1975; Machette 1985; McFadden and Tinsley 1985). The proposed calcification rates are, however, predicated on work in arid climates, and on the assumption that the soil I parent material was noncalcareous. Calcic horizons could develop much more quickly under conditions presented in the study area. In fact, Perring (1988) and Caran (1990a) reported development of calcium carbonate concretions, representing Stages I+ and II carbonate accumulation (Machette 1985), in I floodplain soils of Village Creek in approximately 1800 to 5000 years. Caran (1990a, 1990b) tested this chronology using denudation and aggradation rates and found good agreement. Some of the calcic soils I 82 I


![Image 1 - Page 99](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p99_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 99*

## Page 100

I I and buried soils of the present study area exhibit similar degrees of calcification, and therefore may be middle to late Holocene. The radiocarbon age of soil humates from Unit 2 at Trench M2 is I approximately 1300 B.P. This buried soil (Btk horizon) had fine calcareous root traces, equivalent to Stage I carbonate accumulation. Available data indicate that the calcic horizon in Unit 2 of Trench M2 may, indeed, be approximately 1300 years old. I Examination of the cultural record and all radiocarbon assays for 41Dl270 demonstrates close consistency in the chronologies. The numerous features represented in Cultural Zones I through V are I between approximately 750 and 3400 years old, which is generally consistent with the age of diagnostic artifacts. Cultural Zones VI through IX yielded ages of approximately 3400 to 4200 years, which are at least compatible with the archeological evidence. The geomorphic history of this site-provides a I framework for resolving the apparent diachrony in these data. DISCUSSION AND SUMMARY I The present geomorphic investigation was intended to address four primary issues related to cultural resource assessment within the project area: 1) the age and genetic stratigraphy of deposits exposed in I the project area; 2) vertical and horizontal continuity of strata, particularly those from which cultural materials were recovered; 3) stratigraphic integrity of the record of material culture; and 4) late Holocene geomorphic history of the occupation site and surrounding landscape. Each of these issues is discussed I below and their implications for cultural resource assessment are summarized. Genetic Stratigraphy I The exposed sections (other than the coarse Pleistocene channel deposits exposed in Trench MS) consist of strata that are relatively fine grained. This sedimentation pattern is generally characteristic of floodplains, abandoned channels, and distal portions oflevees. Because the modem setting is a floodplain I and the deposits (to approximately 2 meters depth) are inferred to be no more than a few thousand years old, there isJittle doubt that these sections, including the material-bearing strata, are primarily composed of overbank, or ephemeral channel deposits related to episodic flooding. Pedogenic overprinting is I minimal in the upper parts of these sections and has not masked the nature of the deposits. Continuity of Strata I Some of the stratigraphic units of this project area appear to be horizontally continuous, but other strata have been eroded or were disrupted by gravel mining or excavation of the drainage ditch. I Although some strata are discontinuous, there is little variation in their thickness or lithology over the extent of the available exposure. These observations suggest the deposits originally extended laterally for at least tens of meters and may be relatively homogeneous and, perhaps, isochronous over those I distances. Local discontinuities that are not related to land use primarily result from flood erosion, which is sensitive to small variations in microtopography. Correlation and facies relationships provide mechanisms for determining the extent of deposits that have been disrupted or are exposed incompletely. I Parts of the section seen in Trench M2, for example, may be correlative with strata in the gully section and in Trench M3 (fable 2). Additional chronologic control could at least demonstrate synchroneity of deposition in different parts of the site. I I 83 I

Pleistocene) I Unit 1 (Late Cretaceous) I I Stratigraphic Integrity I The concentration of buried cultural features in the eastern bank of the drainage ditch and the northern wall of the gully indicate probable integrity of the deposits containing them. Consistent radiocarbon ages and the absence of bioturbation or erosive disturbance of these features also support this conclusion. Major disturbances of the cultural deposits seen in the gully wall are unlikely, because I stratification is conformable with the occupation surfaces on which the features were emplaced. Moreover, the ages of diagnostic cultural materials recovered at this site are consistent with the radiocarbon assays and geomorphic indicators of age. If, in contrast, the various ages were incompatible, I there would be reason to suspect an intrinsic lack of integrity. Bioturbation probably has been occurring throughout the period of occupation, but does not appear to have materially disrupted the features or the cultural deposits. Therefore, the stratigraphic integrity of the artifact assemblage does not appear to have I been compromised. Landscape Evolution I The Denton Creek floodplain has aggraded through overbank deposition throughout the late Holocene. Establishment of the prominent levee within the past 2000 years probably served to restrict I deposition farther east. In addition, an abandoned channel with roughly the same size and alignment as the present drainage ditch served as a locus of deposition, perhaps trapping sediment that might have spread laterally across the floodplain. Analogy to the composite geomorphic chronology of the upper I Trinity River valley suggests that the Denton Creek fill sequence is generally correlative with the Late Quaternary depositional record of the valley as a whole. In the Trinity valley, the period from 70,000 to 25,000 B.P. was one of net incision, whereas aggradation/stability, followed by additional incision, I occurred from 25,000 to 20,000 B.P. and from 20,000 to 15,000 B.P., respectively. These intervals are not clearly represented at site 41DL270, although it is likely that a local episode of soil development I 84 I


![Image 1 - Page 101](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p101_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 101*


---

## Page 102

I I (which produced the Silawa series) spanned all or part of this period. From 15,000 B.P. to the present, the inner valley of the Trinity has aggraded continually. Perring (1988) and Caran (1990a, 1990b) found I that floodplains of tributary streams in the Trinity basin also have thick Holocene deposits at grade with the Trinity floodplain. Denton Creek has more than 3 meters of what may be late Holocene fill inset against deposits containing Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric artifacts and capped with a "modem" I cumulic soil. Significant alteration of the original topography and drainage pattern at this site since the 1980s has, in some ways, reconstructed conditions as they may have existed in the middle and late Holocene. I Implications for Cultural Resource Management I Conclusions regarding the four issues discussed above bear directly on cultural resource management at site 41DL270, particularly for the potential for recovery of buried cultural materials. The geomorphic setting is a floodplain, where unconsolidated channel, levee, and floodplain deposits with I mild pedogenic overprints are well exposed. Floods of both Denton Creek and the Trinity River have caused waters laden with suspended sediment to inundate this site many times, but deposition was largely confined to the abandoned channel and levee. By itself, this process probably caused little disturbance I of cultural features, but the rate of sedimentation was rapid enough that rates of bioturbation and pedogenesis did not exceed accretion, thereby protecting the internal stratigraphic integrity of these deposits and their suite of artifacts. I Another consequence of overbank deposition on a low-relief floodplain is that it often produces laterally continuous strata of relatively uniform thickness. The horizontal and vertical continuity of many strata at this site impose minimal variation on the stratigraphic context of artifacts, allowing systematic I investigation of cultural resources. Erosion and major excavation projects in recent decades have disrupted the continuity of some strata locally, but the known artifact-bearing deposits are relatively intact. The high concentration of cultural materials attests to the limited effects of middle to late I Hol°.cene aggradation and pedogenesis. I I I I I I I 85 I

## Page 104

I I EXCAVATION UNIT DESCRIPTIONS Dana Anthony I A total of 15 hand-excavated units were initiated at the site during the data recovery phase. Because of weather difficulties and fire ant problems, not all of these units were completely excavated. I The excavation unit discussions below present information on location, stratigraphy, artifact recovery, faunal/floral materials, and cultural zone association. Some of this data is presented in greater detail in other sections of this report. For detail of the artifacts recovered from these units, for example, see the I "Artifact Analysis" chapter. Additional illustrations of features discussed in this section appear in the following "Feature Descriptions". The vertebrate faunal, molluscan, archeobotanical, and archeomagnetic data presented below are from the chapters by Masson, Neck, Dering, and Gose respectively. Finally, I the cultural zones mentioned in this chapter are analytical tools which are discussed in more detail in several other chapters of this report, including "Feature Descriptions" and "Archeological Synthesis". Unit locations are shown in Figure 12. I UNIT 1 I This was a lx2 meter unit set up in order to investigate Feature 1, a small concentration of burned sandstone (Figure 26). The south half of the unit was excavated in five levels to a total depth of 45 centimeters (to 60 centimeters below datum) (Figure 27). The excavated portion of this unit extends from I Cultural Zone II into Cultural Zone III. Level 1 began at various starting elevations and extended to 0.21 centimeters below datum. From there, the levels were dug in 10-centimeter increments as no natural stratigraphy was apparent other than the upper few centimeters of slope wash. The first level extended I a little more than half way cross the unit. The matrix was loosely compacted and bioturbated as noted by worm trails. The soil was ¼ inch dry screened. No cultural materials were collected from this level. Level 2 extended across the south half of the unit. Evidence of bioturbation continued into this I level. The bottom of Feature 1 was defined in this level (Figure 57). Charcoal flecks were visible throughout the level but too small for samples. Burned bone (turtle) concentrated close to the feature itself, unburned bone including catfish, minnow, snake, bird, a few mussel shells fragments, a chert I flake, and 40 land snails were recovered from the ¼ inch wet and fine screen. The next level reached across the entire unit. Cultural material in the first few centimeters and thought to be associated with this feature were recovered from ¼ inch wet and fine screening. Charcoal flecks were still present although I to a lesser degree. Identified faunal material included deer bone fragments, two of which showed gnaw marks. The fine screen sample included rodent, bird, turtle (some burned), gar, minnow, catfish, and other small vertebrates. Three flotation samples were collected from the feature fill. Archeobotanical I analysis of the flotation material identified only a few specimens representing goosefoot (Chenopodium sp.) and live oak (Quercus virginiana). I Level 4 was below the Feature 1 zone. As with the previous levels, bioturbation was noted throughout the unit. Near the bottom of the level in the southeastern quad of the unit, two sandstone rocks were exposed along with charcoal flecks, ash, snail shell, and one mussel shell. Another charcoal I and ash concentration and worm trail was noted in the southwest quad. The matrix from this level was ¼ inch wet and fine screened. Approximately 45 land snail were recovered as well as two water snails and three mussel shell halves representing two different species. In addition, a single utilized flake and I a bone fish hook were also recovered. The sandstone, charcoal and ash described above may well represent another feature or at least a disturbed portion of one. The final level, Level 5, witnessed no change in the soil. Tiny carbonate flecks and occasional charcoal smears were observed. Fewer ecofacts I 87 I

## Page 107

I I were recovered from this level than the previous two. Materials recovered from ¼ inch wet screen included 43 land snails and 6 water snails, two small gnawed deer fragments, and two chert tertiary flakes that appear to have been heat-treated. The fine wet screening produced small mammal fetal fragments, I a mustelid incisor, bird, turtle, minnow, snake, and cotton rat remains. The excavation of this unit revealed that the greater part of the Feature 1 burned rock had already I eroded. Few remains were left buried with the exception of the associated ecofacts. The greatest amount and diversity of material recovered from this unit was in association with the Feature 1 layer. The wet screening no doubt was instrumental in the recovery of the small fauna which otherwise may have gone I undetected in the hard clay or fallen through the screen. In short, Feature 1 was revealed to be remnants of a surface hearth with perhaps evidence of another hearth 20 centimeters below. Aql}atic resources appear to have been important during the time period represented by this feature within Cultural Zone I II. UNIT2 I This was a lx2 meter unit set up to excavate the burned clay feature designated as Feature 2. The corner stakes were established but work never proceeded from there. The presence of a large and very I healthy fire ant mound discouraged work at this locale. A quick-acting insecticide was applied to the mound, but the odor was so offensive and noxious that all ideas of working at the feature were soon abandoned. Close inspection of the feature prior to abandonment, particularly its position on the ground I surface, suggested to the investigators that this feature might be the result of a modern tree burn. UNIT3 I This was a lx2 meter unit established upslope of Feature 3 to determine if any intact portion remained (Figure 28). As with the other units, the first level started from various surface levels as the unit was on a slope and extended about 1/a way across the unit. Most levels were dug in 10-centimeter I arbitrary increments. It was not until the third level that the excavated area extended across the entire unit. Total depth of this whole unit was 50 centimeters below datum (datum was 19 centimeters above northeast comer) but total excavated depth was approximately 30 centimeters. This excavation as well I as Feature 3 was concentrated in Cultural Zone I, except for the lowest level, which extended into Cultural Zone II. I The north half of the unit was excavated first and screened through ¼ inch wet with fine screen below. The south half was only ¼ inch dry screened because by the time that portion was excavated, the crew had abandoned the water screening operation and felt that the sample from the north half would I suffice. The first level of the south half was excavated to the feature level instead of in 10-centimeter increments as we knew what depth it lay on. The upper north half of Level 4 (to about 35 centimeters below datum) encompassed Feature 3. Below this (including Level 5, which covered the entire unit) were I areas devoid of and below Feature 3. The feature was found to extend throughout the unit and continue into both the east wall (upslope) and the north wall (Figures 54 and 55). I Levels excavated above the feature layer (the north half of Level 2 and the south half of Level 1) included the recovery of a small amount of land snail, a cotton rat lower jaw and teeth, and miscellaneous vertebrate long bone diatheses fragments, some of which were identified as deer. Below the feature only I a few land snails and mussel shells were recovered. I 90 I

I I Those levels associated with Feature 3, within Cultural Zone I, (the south half of Level 2, Level 3, and the upper north half of Level 4) produced a limited quantity of other faunal material; identified I fragments are deer, cottontail, turkey, unidentified bird, and an unidentified rabbit or bird fragment. I UNIT4 This was a lx2 meter unit placed immediately upslope of the east end of Trench CD 1 (e xcavated I during the testing phase). A few pieces of burned sandstone had been knocked loose from the surface of the east wall profile during the excavation of that trench and it was thought a hearth might be preserved upslope and below the surface. The west wall of the north half of the unit coincided with the I east wall profile of the trench. Excavation was started in the southern half of the unit. This extended for only two levels to a depth of 30 centimeters below datum. No sign of the feature was observed. The north half was then excavated to the same level with the same results. UNITS I This unit was laid out as a lx2 meter unit upslope of exposed mussel shell in the general vicinity of Features 9 and 10 (mussel shell concentrations) although the features were at a lower level. This area, I and the area all the way to Unit 3 to the north, has an almost continuous exposure of features. As no features or cultural materials were apparent in the unit itself, the first 20-centimeter level was not screened, but was carefully examined during the removal of the matrix. As no sign of a feature was observed in this unit, the crew moved to another more promising location. I UNIT6 I This was a lx2 meter unit placed upslope of a small scatter of sandstone and a few mussel shells. The primary reason for choosing this particular locale, however, was the presence of Mesodon snails which had not been observed elsewhere at that time (they later were identified in Unit 9 as well). The I north half of this unit was excavated in five 10-centimeter arbitrary levels with the matrix ¼ inch dry m. screened. This unit was confined to Cultural Zone II and the extreme upper part of Cultural Zone Small pieces of sandstone were collected from the first level. Only a few snails were noted in the I uppermost 30 centimeters. From Level 4 through the bottom of the excavation at 50 centimeters below datum, a few pieces of burned sandstone, six mussel shells representing five different species, and 50 land snails (primarily Mesodon) were collected. This is in stark contrast to the first three levels above. These I materials tended to cluster toward the north end of the unit and may indicate more remains extend in that direction. The only deer bone collected from the unit was from this north area in the lowest level. Turtle was also collected from the lowest level. Flotation and soil column samples were taken from the I east wall of the unit. Mesodon was not identified from these samples. Snail species identified from the soil samples reflect open woodland in the upper and lower levels, with the mid-elevations of the unit representing gallery woodland. It is unclear if the sparse cultural remains reflect a remnant feature or I scattered remains. I 92 I

## Page 110

I I UNIT7 I This was a lx2 meter unit laid out on the upper slope in the vicinity of numerous exposed features. With attention given to other units and features, it was decided to abandon this locale. I UNITS This was the only 2x2 meter unit excavated at the site (Figures 29 and 76). It was set up adjacent I to the north wall of Trench M6 to investigate mussel shell Feature 44 exposed in that trench in one of the lowest cultural zones (at an elevation of 140.83 meters amsl) identified up to that time. We also hoped to reach the zone in which bison bone had been identified at an even lower depth (140.16 meters I amsl) from Trench MS a few meters to the north. Initially, we planned to use the backhoe to remove the overburden to about \0 centimeters above a single mussel shell (or about 0.50 meters above Feature 44) in the east wall of Trench M6 and start controlled excavations from that level. It was thought that I this lone shell could represent another buried feature or cultural zone above that already recognized in the bottom of the trench. I The crew was unable to use the backhoe as planned. Prior to the setup of this unit, the backhoe had become stuck trenching in the bottom of the ditch near this location. Because of the time and considerable effort taken to rescue the backhoe it was decided the crew would have to backfill the trench I (french MlO) by hand. This decision also meant the backhoe could not be used to remove the overburden down to the zone where Unit 8 excavations were to start. I The overburden was removed by hand in a roughly 2 meter square area. In so doing, cultural materials such as pieces of mussel shell, charcoal, and bone bits were noted. Removal of the overburden continued and more material was observed, this time a burned clay with charcoal feature near the western boundary of the unit area. Approximately 10 to 20 centimeters of this feature and surrounding dirt was I removed during the removal of overburden. Once the unit was actually laid out following the contour of the ditch, a 20-centimeter bulk was left between it and Trench M6. The unit was then divided into four quadrants with excavations in IO-centimeter arbitrary levels. The first level in all quadrants varied I in actual amount of dirt removed due to the unevenness of the starting surface; in some areas less than 10 centimeters of dirt was removed, in others more than 10 centimeters. Excavations began in the A and B quadrants with the idea of providing a profile and guide for the other two quadrants. All matrix was I ¼ inch dry screened. The burned clay and charcoal feature, designated Feature 19, was then more carefully examined I as it was exposed in the C and D quads. It was investigated to the bottom of the first level, where it appeared to branch out, suggesting it was a burned tree root. Materials recovered from these quadrants included a large quantity of land snails (circa 40), a quartzite flake, chert flakes (one utilized) and chip, I a chert projectile point fragment, and a quartzite chip. This latter chip happens to fit with another chip recovered from the bulk area prior to separation of the unit into quadrants. Bone fragments of deer and other mammals were also identified. It should be noted that these remains may well be associated with I the other two features, which are more likely to be of cultural origin than Feature 19. Another argument for the lack of association between these items and Feature 19 is that the latter was first recognized several centimeters above the layer from which the former were recovered. I The two quadrants with the remaining portion of the feature were left intact as attention turned to quadrants A and B. Much time had been expended on the first level in this unit and it was decided to I 93 I

## Page 112

I I concentrate the effort on the A and B quadrants for quick excavation to our goal as stated above. I Features 22 and 23 were defined within quadrants A and B, respectively. These are described in more detail in the following section and occur within Cultural Zone V. In brief, Feature 22 was a small concentration of charcoal and lightly discolored earth that extended into the north wall of Quadrant A I from Level 1 into Level 2. Materials collected from the quadrant but not the feature itself included faunal remains, shell, and lithics. Identified faunal material was confined to deer bone. An abundance of land snails and a few water snails were also collected. Lithic material consisted of a quartzite chip with a I utilized edge. Feature 23 was a burned clay feature (orange in color) that extended into the bulk between the unit I and Trench M6. It, too, extended from Level 1 into Level 2. Materials from Level 1, or the surface from which Feature 23 was dug, included some 40 land snails, 2 water snails, burned and unburned bone (including miscellaneous mammal and unidentified vertebrates), burned mussel shell edge fragments, chert I chips, and a quartzite flake and chip. The materials collected from quadrants A and B could well be associated with any feature from this I unit originating from the same surface. It is impossible to definitely assign any of these to either Feature 22 or 23. The two features may be contemporary. It is safe to say, however, that the materials are associated with this particular surface. The nature of the two features appears to be cultural but this I cannot be proven. Other materials collected from this unit below the originating surface of the features described above include a concentration of turtle bone fragments (likely representing a single individual), some I mammal bone, and bird bone. Lithics included quartzite and chert debitage, a chert utilized flake, and a Yarbrough dart point of a grainy tan chert. Overall, it was noted that cultural material dropped off below the second level. This unit, as with the others, produced a large quantity of land snails, which also I dropped off below Level 2. There were a very few water snails in all levels. Faunal material was absent below Level 2 as well, with the exception of a single bird bone fragment. Charcoal flecks were abundant as observed at other locales. Flotation samples were collected from feature fills. While excavating the I unit, the artifacts were observed to slope toward the west or toward the creek as well as to the south. Only 10 centimeters were excavated from quadrants C and D. A and B were excavated to a depth I of 0. 70 centimeters below datum, where mussel shell was contacted in Level 5 (a few were also recovered from the base of Level 4) at approximately the same level as the mussel shell feature in Trench M6. This falls within Cultural Zone VII. There was little doubt that these shells represented the same I feature. Eight shells, all identified as Amblema plicata, were collected. No other cultural materials were collected from this zone, however. I Excavations were halted with the completion of Level 5 due to rain. Upon return to the site in December of 1992, disaster had befallen Unit 8. About 30-40 centimeters of the east wall had slumped in over the protective black plastic. Once the water was bailed out and the plastic removed it was found I that the eastern portions of quadrants C and D had also caved in. The datum nail was also knocked out. It was left uncovered to dry out. After a day and a half the crew was forced to leave again because of heavy rains. Unfortunately, Unit 8 continued to be a muddy mess throughout the excavation and was I never reopened. I 95 I

## Page 113

I I UNIT9 This unit was excavated in order to investigate the potential pit feature in the north profile of the I gully leading to Denton Creek (Figure 30). It was set above and off center of the feature in order to bisect it. After the top of the gully was leveled off a lxl meter unit was laid out and excavated in nine 10-centimeter arbitrary levels. All the matrix from this unit was fine wet-screened. The dark brown I sandy clay in this unit was relatively uniform all the way through except for the feature areas. A large quantity of land snail was recovered from the screens from all levels excavated. Interestingly, this was the only other unit where Mesodon was identified. I The first six levels were devoid of definable features and contained only snails. Deer, cottontail, I turkey, and miscellaneous birds were identified, however, from the mid levels. It was only when Features 28 and 33 were definitely identified in Levels 7 through 9 that any mussel shell was recovered. I Feature 28 was identified in the western portion of the unit extending from Level 7 through 9. Its point of origin at about 139 .15 meters amsl was just a few centimeters above the point of origin of Feature 33, at 139.05 meters amsl. The former is a sand-filled pocket outlined by a border of charcoal. I Within the Feature 28 fill one Quadrula mortoni, one Lampsilis hydiana, and 2 Lampsilis teres were collected. The Feature 33 burned clay and charcoal zone was in the eastern portion of the unit. Only two Amblema plicata were collected from this feature. Interestingly, turtle bone, including unidentified I testudine from Feature 28 and mud turtle from Feature 33, was recovered from both features. Plant remains, including goosefoot and /lex sp., were identified from the Feature 28 fill. The exact relationship of Features 28 and 33 to the others described in the gully walls is not clear. I The charcoal and burned areas would indicate an association, as these traits are common to all the features (or components of one large feature) in this area of the site. Both features continued beyond the area excavated in Unit 9. It would appear that they continue in depth, which at this point is but a few I centimeters above the charcoal lens noted in the north face of the gully and the surface on which Features 6 and 8 lie and from which Feature 7 was dug (Figure 48). Excavations were halted due to the rain and subsequent flooding of the gully (Figure 31 ). I UNIT 10 I This was a lx2 meter unit located atop the densest portion of Feature 16 (Figures 32 and 67). This feature consisted of burned sandstone, mussel shell, bone, and lithic material exposed on the lower slope in the area of high feature density within Cultural Zone IV. Both halves of this unit were excavated at I the same time. It was excavated in two levels to a maximum depth of 30 centimeters along the east or upslope wall. All matrix was ¼ inch dry screened. The excavations did not reach all the way to the west wall of the unit as that ground surface area was level with the excavated portion in the rest of the I unit (Figures 9 and 32). However, the feature was fully exposed in this area of the unit. Excavations stopped just short (1-2 centimeters) of the surface level of the feature across the unit although it was exposed in places. When we returned the next spring, the unit was overtaken by fire ants. I Nevertheless, interesting information was gained from this unit. As discussed elsewhere, most of the features defined at this site are clearly composed of a single dominant material type, i.e., mussel shell I or sandstone. Feature 16 represents a composite of cultural materials found at the site, with bone slightly more dominant. In this sense, Feature 16, like Features 9, 15, 24, 36, and 37, may be said to represent I 96 I

I a discrete occupation surface. At least certain elements of Feature 16 appeared to be in situ. Fire I fractured sandstone at various places within the unit were clearly in place. This feature also appears to extend to the east or I upslope as well as to the north and west. Cultural material associated with each feature is described in the feature section. Materials I collected from below the defined surface of either feature included some turtle and vertebrata and small amounts of snail. These materials are so close in elevation to the features, or at least to Feature 36, that their association can not be ruled out. I I I 100 I I

## Page 120

I I UNIT 12 Unit 12 was placed upslope of the mussel shell concentration, Feature 9 (Figures 8, 35, 72, and I 73). It measured lxl meter and was excavated to a depth of 65 centimeters below datum. This unit was excavated in seven levels, most of which corresponded to what was thought to be distinct cultural surfaces as opposed to the arbitrary IO-centimeter levels employed in many of the other units. Upon excavation I of Feature 9, other materials were found to be in association with this feature or at least one could not differentiate the material from the feature area. From the point where the feature was initially exposed to the bottom was roughly 10 centimeters. Other cultural materials included a heat-treated utilized chert I chip, chert and quartzite debitage, and a barbed dart point. Faunal material was fairly diverse with deer, cottontail, and turtle recovered. At the lowest level of the feature evidence of burning (in the form of burned sandstone, bits of clay, and small pieces of charcoal) were noted. A burned mussel shell was also I observed. Except for a fragment, no mussel shell was noted below 44 centimeters. Below the feature only a few snails, a mussel shell fragment, a small amount of miscellaneous unidentified vertebrate bone, a few pieces of burned clay nodules, a quartzite chip, and some orange I sandstone fragments, were collected. A mussel shell was recorded in the northwest wall at 57 centimeters, apparently not part of Feature 9. The paucity of material below the feature suggests that the material that was found in the area of the feature is a real association. The feature appears to continue I into the south, east, and north walls of the unit where it should remain buried until erosion exposes more of it. Snails analyzed from the Feature 9 zone are species most representative of an open habitat. Below the feature the snail analysis indicated an open habitat with flooding. I Two soil zones were apparent in the east wall profile (Figure 36). The upper zone is a dark brown sandy clay loam, which includes some slope wash, and the lower zone is a dark brown silty clay loam I with calcium carbonates. A soil crack, approximately 1 centimeter in width, extended from the surface of the unit to near the base. This crack was filled with a light sandy matrix and indicates the shrink-swell nature of the soil and the possibility for at least small items to be displaced. I UNIT 13 I This unit was placed upslope of the exposed portion of the Feature 10 mussel shell concentration which was located northeast of Unit 12 within Cultural Zone III (Figures 37, 61, 63 and 64). A lx2 meter unit was laid out; only the north half was excavated due to the presence of Feature 35, and the I subsequent establishment of a large fire ant mound which occupied most of the south half. The unit was dug to 46 centimeters below datum. All matrix was ¼ inch dry screened. The north end of the unit was disturbed by a highly eroded sand-filled rill feature which upon excavation reached the same depth in the I northwest comer as the final depth of the unit. However, Feature 10 did not extend this far. Feature 35, consisting of burned sandstone and a ring of burned clay (Figure 38 and 61), was noted in the northeast comer of the unit below this disturbance which was at a much higher elevation due to the slope. I As the first level of this excavation was intended to reach to a few centimeters above Feature 10, the top 34 centimeters was removed. It was at this elevation (142.03 meters amsl) that the top of the Feature 35 sandstone was first noted. From here the feature areas and area between them were treated I as separate lots not excavated in levels per se. As an example, Levels 2a and 2b were confined to the eastern portion of the unit in the area of Feature 35 in order to keep separate the matrix from this feature and the area of Feature 10 in the southwest comer. Level 3 comprised the rest of the unit. It was in this I latter level that Feature 10 was first exposed. It should be noted that the fill from within and around each 103 I I

I I feature was kept separate from the level matrix. Level 4 also extended across the unit, but the Feature 10 matrix was collected as Feature 10 instead of Level 4, as was the case for Feature 35. I Soil in this unit was a dark brown silty clay with calcium carbonate threads throughout the levels. Feature 35 extended from the 142.03 meters amsl (within Cultural Zone 111) to the bottom of the unit at 141.57 meters amsl. Excavations did not proceed beyond this level due to time constraints. However, I it may safely be said that the burned clay and charcoal elements of this feature continued into both the east and north walls of the unit as revealed in the profile (Figure 39). There is no way to know if more stones are still buried outside the unit. As mentioned in the feature and archeomagnetic sections, these I stones were found to be totally out of place. This suggests they represent a discard pile or a very disturbed hearth. Due to the depth of the burned earth and charcoal bits, it seems likely this feature is in place although the stones have been disturbed. They do not appear to be boiling stones which cobbles I would be better suited for. As the surface on which Feature 10 lies is approximately 10 centimeters below the surface on which Feature 35 originated, they are not thought to be associated. I Cultural material outside the features themselves was very sparse in this unit. No material that could definitively be assigned fo Feature 35 was identified. Snail collected from the screen was restricted to the first and third levels or to those lots not specifically collected from features. Snails from the surface elevation matrix of the Feature 35 sandstone (at 142.03 meters ams)) were analyzed and found I to be indicative of an open woodland habitat. Feature 10 was recorded as a single layer with base elevations varying 3-4 centimeters but I averaging 141.96 meters amsl. The shells were noted to slope toward the east, which was also found in other units. The feature was observed to continue into the south wall as well as to the west or downslope. Some deer and turtle bone was collected which may be contemporary with the Feature 10 I occupation. Other bone identified appears both below the level on which Feature 35 originated and above the surface of Feature 10. Snails analyzed from matrix collected at the bottom of the unit between the Feature 10 and Feature 35 areas (or the surface on which Feature 10 was deposited) suggested an open I habitat with flooding. This difference in habitat (open, flooded habitat versus open woodland) as recorded by snails within less than ten centimeters is quite interesting. It may represent very slow deposition with concomitant environmental changes or perhaps very rapidly shifting surface conditions I at the time of deposition. UNIT 14 I This unit was excavated in an effort to determine the nature of the burned sandstone feature designated as Feature 34 exposed mid-slope in the area of high feature density within Cultural Zone N I (Figure 40). It was a lxl meter unit laid out upslope of the feature and excavated in five 10-centimeter arbitrary levels to a depth of 60 centimeters below datum. The matrix was ¼ inch dry screened. The feature was defined within Level 3 at an elevation of 141.66 meters amsl. There did not appear to be I a slope to the surface on which this feature was found. At this level, the unit was expanded 25 centimeters to the west as the feature appeared to extend in that direction. Carbon flecks were noted throughout the feature zone and continued for another five centimeters below the feature. In addition, sandstone bits and burned clay nodules were also present. I Numerous land snails were collected from the screens, but recovery radically dropped off below the feature zone in the lower part of the unit. Identified faunal material from this feature zone included I deer, turkey, miscellaneous bird, rodent, turtle, unidentified mammal, and vertebrate bone. A single 107 I I

I I chert tertiary flake was recovered from the feature zone. In addition, a possible deer jaw fragment was identified from matrix below the feature zone. I Archeomagnetic analysis of four of the supposed hearth stones indicated they were in situ (Figure 69). Charcoal samples were collected for radiocarbon dating. The analysis of the flotation sample identified red maple. Snail species analyzed from soil samples from the Feature 34 zone were indicative I of an open woodland habitat. The same analysis from below the feature zone indicated a gallery woodland and below that a flood deposit. As with Unit 13, these changes in micro-habitat are quite interesting but not fully understood. They may well reflect rapidly changing surface conditions or even shifting proximity to the creek channel. I After excavation of this unit and its extension, it was assumed that this feature probably does not extend to the west or downslope any further than was already exposed. It may very well extend in the I other directions. The scattered nature of Feature 34 and the fact that four of its stones were found to be in place suggests most of the other stones comprising this hearth must have been removed without disturbing the others. I UNIT 15 I Unit 15 was placed upslope of Feature 25, the lithic scatter. It was a lxl meter unit laid out off of the northwest corner of Unit 1 using the same datum. The unit was excavated in two arbitrary levels of 20 centimeters and 10 centimeters, respectively. The matrix was ¼ inch dry screened. The surface I of this unit was marked by a number of small erosional rills. Feature 25 was noted on the surface by a quantity of chert flakes eroding from what appeared to I be a single point marked by the pin flag (Figure 62). Each rain would expose more material, leading the crew to believe a chipping station might be preserved beneath the slope. Unfortunately, this excavation revealed neither a feature nor many cultural remains. Only three chert flakes and one chert chip were I recovered from both levels. In addition, three mussel shells identifiable as two species, a burned mussel fragment, a few pieces of burned clay nodules, and a small piece of burned sandstone were noted. Land snails recovered from the screen were the most plentiful item (circa 20). Evidence of faunal species I include bone from snake and turtle, as well as a burned mammal fragment. A total of four soil samples were taken in 5-centimeter increments from the second level. Of note are the snails examined from the soil samples, which suggest an open habitat coinciding with an elevation within Cultural Zone III. I As mentioned, this unit did not add further information to the interpretation of Feature 25. It was the opinion of the excavators that the feature, if preserved at all, would lie intact further upslope from where the unit was placed. I I I I 111 I I

## Page 130

I I FEATURE DESCRIPTIONS Dana Anthony I A total of 45 cultural features were recorded as part of the mitigation phase investigations at 41DL270. These were concentrated in the vicinity of the greatest impact to the site, which also coincided with the location of the unit excavations and backhoe trenches. No attempt was made to record all of I the exposed features at this large site as that would have required all of the field time and more. Here, the full description of each feature is presented. Hand excavation units were set up on 11 of these exposed features and are discussed in detail under the unit descriptions. In several cases, additional I features were identified while excavating a unit. The special analysis chapters also discuss these features in terms of faunal remains, mussel shell remains, shell isotope analysis, and archeomagnetic properties. The reader is referred to them for detailed discussions. All measurements are given by a north/south grid I orientation. The features were all initially exposed on the surface of the east slope of the ditch unless otherwise noted. The soil exposed in the ditch profile was generally a dark brown silty clay, becoming more clayey with depth. The features written in the present tense refer to those that were not excavated or only lightly collected from. They were observed to be intact as of the last visit to the site in I November of 1993. Conversely, excavated features or those extensively collected from are referred to in the past tense. I While most of the features were photographed, recorded on a feature form, and occasionally collected, a few that had been identified during the testing phase were not revisited. These were originally designated with letters (and in one case with a number). It is not clear if these features had I completely eroded away and disappeared by the data recovery phase, if they were covered by dense vegetation, or if they were simply missed. In any case, these are now designated as Features 46-52. Because they were not revisited, the amount of information available is minimal. Additionally, a few I features which were recorded could not be mapped because of rain on the last day in the field. The approximate location of these features is known, however, and they are included within their appropriate cultural zone. I The topographic map of the site (Figure 12) shows the location of the features which were mapped. Figure 41 is a stylized profile of the site which shows features, excavation units, and backhoe trenches I within their respective cultural zones along the east bank of the ditch. Some of the trenches from the testing phase (Anthony et al. 1992) are included on this profile and are distinguished by C and D prefixes. Trench MlO is also included although its location was in the bottom of the ditch). This figure I illustrates the density as well as horizontal and vertical distribution of features at the site. During the initial field investigations at the site it was observed that surface features seemed to I cluster at particular levels. In the testing phase, three preliminary cultural zones were indicated (Anthony et al. 1992). During the data recovery phase, it was recognized that this initial view was too simplistic. Still, an analysis of the elevation and location of the features suggested vertical (and perhaps horizontal) clustering. Attempts to define such clusters in the field were unsuccessful, however. I Analysis of the feature location data in the lab showed that there was considerable overlap in feature elevations along the east bank of the drainage ditch. In order to facilitate the analysis of changing I patterns through time, a series of arbitrary cultural zones was created, based on the assumption that cultural stratigraphy at the site was, like the general stratigraphic trend of the terrace section, horizontal or roughly flat lying across the site (although some of the surfaces on which features occurred were I observed to slope to the south and east, the amount of slope was measured in a few centimeters; thus the 113 I I

## Page 132

I I actual slope is on the micro level instead of a macro level. These arbitrary cultural zones were thus based solely on clusters of features at certain elevations I which were divided where apparent slight breaks in feature occurrences were found. These breaks were in some cases only ten to twenty centimeters. Nonetheless, it was felt that such vertical/temporal partitioning was important to understanding change at the site and ultimately nine clusters were identified I and described as cultural zones. These arbitrarily designated zones are not intended to correlate with any regional cultural chronology, nor do they indicate clear cultural differences at the site. Rather, they were intended solely as a heuristic device to analyze the features in a series of discrete vertical zones. I Note that, with the exception of the area investigated in the gully leading to the creek, all of the zones are stratigraphically related in the Late Holocene terrace fill. Based on several radiocarbon dates and interpretation of the general stratigraphy of the site, this lower terrace or floodplain area in the gully I has been included with Cultural Zone I, the highest in the Late Holocene terrace fill. A more extensive discussion of these zones and their relation to the regional culture chronology is included in the concluding chapters of this report. The features described below are presented under the cultural zone I in which they occur, from latest to earliest based on elevation, rather than in an artificial numerical order. CULTURAL ZONE I - CREEK BANK I Cultural Zone I, extends from mean elevations of 138.95 to 137.54 meters amsl in the large gully between the ditch and Denton Creek. It contained the burned rock and burned earth lenses exposed in I both the north and south face profiles of the gully (Figures 11, 43, 48, 49 and 51, 52). Three corrected but uncalibrated dates from this zone range between 1295 ± 120 B.P. (in the channel inset area) to 875 ± 125 B.P. (see Table 29 for a complete listing of radiocarbon dates). Estimated dates for the I. occupations of this floodplain zone range from about 750 to 1000 B.P. (note: estimated dates are based on calibrated radiocarbon dates which were adjusted to create a logical sequence; see Table 32). No lithic material was recovered from this zone, either on the low terrace adjacent to the creek bank or from the I terrace on the east bank of the drainage ditch. Very little faunal material was recovered from this zone. Feature 28 I Feature 28 was first identified as a linear sand pocket bounded by a charcoal layer containing sandstone flecks, snail fragments, and charcoal (Figure 42). It was recognized in Levels 7 through 9 during the excavation of Unit 9. The unit was located on the north face of the gully leading from the I ditch to Denton Creek in order to investigate the so-called pit feature (Feature 7). In the original field notes Feature 28 was referred to as Feature 1, but as all features recorded at this site were numbered sequentially, the project archeologist renumbered it to remain consistent. The feature was first recognized I as such at approximately 139.15 meters amsl and was excavated to a level of 138.75 meters amsl or 40 centimeters in depth. Excavations were never completed so the final depth of this feature remains unknown. However, the uppermost elevation was indicative of the level from which this feature probably I originated within Cultural Zone I. Feature 28 varied in outline as it was excavated but at last exposure measured approximately 0.80x0.45 meter. In the lower portion of the excavated feature, a greater intensity of charcoal was noted as well as burned clay. Mussel shells were found in proximity to the I charcoal and one of the shells was reported to be charred on its exterior. Four mussel shells were identified representing three different species: Lampsilis teres (2), Quadrula mortoni (1), and Lampsilis hydiana (1). In addition, a turtle limb fragment was also identified. ## Page 134

I I The presence of the burned sandstone and clay, along with the mussel shell indicated this feature may have been used to process mussels. The depth indicated a pit rather than a hearth, which could have been used for roasting, one suggested method of shell processing. The relationship of this pit to the I features exposed below on the gully profile was not clear, although this area was heavily utilized and burned and this feature was most likely related to those below it at least in function. In fact, Feature 28 was just a few centimeters above Feature 8. I Feature 33 I Feature 33 was a dense concentration of charcoal and burned orange clay identified within Levels 7 to 9 in Unit 9. It measured approximately 1.40x0.50 meters when last excavated. As with Feature 28, also exposed in Unit 9, the excavations were never completed. This feature was at least 30 centimeters I thick, originating from an elevation of 139. 05 meters amsl within Cultural Zone I. This feature may very well be related to Feature 28 although it clearly did not intrude into the sandy matrix that characterized the fill of the former feature. Turtle carapace/plastron fragments were identified, as well as two Amblema plicata shells and one La.mpsilis teres. I Feature 8 I This is the westernmost burned rock and charcoal feature recorded from the north face profile of the gully (Figures 43, 44, and 48). It is approximately 0. 75 meter in length and 0.12 meters thick. Its base lies at 138.88 meters amsl which corresponds with Cultural Zone I. This feature is likely associated I with Features 6 and 7 described below as well as with the burned earth lens which is in the same feature zone. As with Feature 6, there is no discernable pit; instead, the feature is set directly onto the dark brown fine sandy clay surface. I Feature 6 I This is another burned rock and charcoal feature exposed in the north wall of the large gully connecting the west side of the ditch to Denton Creek (Figures 43, 45, 46, and 48). It is approximately 0.37 meters wide and 0.25 meters at the thickest point. It lies at an elevation of 138. 77 meters amsl I within Cultural Zone I. There is no pit associated with this feature; instead it lies within a lens of burned earth which is an average of 10 centimeters thick and discernable along this profile for a length of more than six meters (Figure 43). A charcoal sample from this feature has been radiocarbon dated to 875 ± o I 125 B.P. (GX-19692; 13C -25.2). This is the youngest date so far recorded at 41DL270. Feature 7 I Feature 7, in the north face of the gully, is about 0.50 meters to the west of Feature 6 and approximately 0.30 meters below it (Figures 43 and 48). It, too, is composed of burned rock and charcoal of about the same size as Feature 6. It is situated at 138.47 meters amsl within Cultural Zone I I. Upon first inspection of the profile, this feature appeared to be an earlier or at least lower feature than Feature 6 and Feature 8. However, after carefully scraping the entire profile prior to drawing a thin line of burned earth appeared to demarcate a pit with Feature 7 defining the base (Figures 43 and 47). The I top of this "pit" or the level from which it was dug is equivalent to the level on which Features 6 and 8 (as well as the burned earth lens described above) lie. The eastern side of this "pit" is outlined by a nearly vertical line; the western side is outlined by a curved line extending back to the burned earth lens I which leads to Feature 8. Unit 9 was set up to investigate this possible pit feature (Figures 30 and 48). 117 I I
