## Page 138

I I It was never determined if this feature was actually a pit due to continual flooding of the gully during the last field session. No samples were collected from this feature. ;JI I The three features described above, Features 6, 7, and 8, as well as the burned earth lens which was not assigned a separate feature number, all represent intensive utilization of this area of the site (closest to the present-day creek bank). They are probably all associated and date from the same I occupation. In fact, it may be preferable to refer to this group of features as components of one large feature which encompasses all of the burned areas in the gully. The degree and spatial distribution of the burned stone and charred earth indicate a cultural origin as opposed to field burning. A charred earth I lens with areas of charcoal is also observed in the south wall of the gully and correlates with the elevations recorded for the north wall (Figures 43 and 51). The remaining gully profile to the west of Feature 8 was more disturbed, having been cut back I dramatically and covered with more vegetation. The investigated part of the gully is devoid of vegetation, probably from the frequent flooding. Since the gully walls were not profiled beyond this point, the possibility of more features to the west was not confirmed. In addition to time constraints, the I slope of the walls was such that it was felt that cleaning would increase erosion and thus the ultimate destruction of this part of the site. I Feature 31 Feature 31 consisted of a charcoal-filled shallow pit with burned sandstone in the south wall of the I gully (Figures 49 and 50). It had been freshly exposed at the bottom of the gully by the flood waters which run through the gully to the creek after each heavy rain. This feature was approximately 20 centimeters in diameter and depth when exposed. It was located within a dark brown clay loam about I 1.80 meters below a burned clay and charcoal stratum which extends about 5 meters along the wall (Figures 51 and 52). This stratum corresponds with the charcoal and burned earth lens described for the north profile. Species identification samples were collected from the outer five centimeters of the feature, I then charcoal samples were collected from the freshly exposed portion. The charcoal sample was radiocarbon dated to 1095 ± 125 B.P. (GX-18705; o 13C -25.8). I This feature and the others located in this gully testify to the intensive utilization of this part of the site during the Late Prehistoric period. People were camping on what would have been the creek bank or lower terrace very close to the old channel. This is further substantiated by this feature's low surface I elevation of 137.54 meters amsl, within Cultural Zone I, the lowest so far documented at the site. The features in the north wall, about 1 meter above Feature 31 (but still within Cultural Zone I), are situated on the aggraded terrace dating to 875 ± 125 B.P. I CULTURAL ZONE I - Tl TERRACE Cultural Zone I (fl Terrace) extends from mean elevations of 142.70 to 142.47 meters amsl on I the east bank of the drainage ditch (Figure 41). Occurring in this zone are Features 3 and 40 (two mussel shell features), which occur among the highest cultural elevations recorded at the site. As noted elsewhere, no lithic material was recovered from this zone. A single radiocarbon date was collected from I this zone during the testing phase. The corrected, uncalibrated date is 1250 ± 70 B.P. (fable 29). Estimated dates for the occupation of this zone are 650 to 1300 B.P. (fable 32). I 121 I I

## Page 143

I Feature 13 I Feature 13 consists of a concentration (26) of small sandstone and limestone cobbles observed on the upslope surface approximately 1 meter southwest of the Feature 3 mussel shell concentration (Figures I 53 and 54). The stones lie in a roughly circular configuration which measures about 35 centimeters in diameter. They are at an elevation of 142.70 meters amsl, the second highest recorded for any features at the site or within Cultural Zone I. The proximity to Feature 3 appears to be coincidental as this I feature is at least 30 centimeters below Feature 13. Although it is not certain that this small cluster of stones is a prehistoric cultural feature, it is possible that it may represent discarded boiling stones used in food processing. No samples were collected from this feature. I Feature 40 I Feature 40 was one of the northernmost features recorded on the ditch slope. It is a small mussel shell concentration measuring approximately 1.50x0.70 meters at an elevation of 142.60 meters amsl, placing it at the top of Cultural Zone I. This is the highest recorded feature so far at the site. Two I erosional rills bisect this feature. Only about 9 mussel shells were counted; none of these were collected. Feature 3 I Feature 3 consisted of a dense concentration of mussel shell with the exposed surface and excavated portion within Unit 3 measuring approximately 1.S0xl.40 meters (Figures 28, 54, and 55). It was found to be about one to two shells thick, although there were sparse, scattered places within the unit that were I three to five shells thick. This thickness corresponds to no more than about 3 centimeters. The base of the feature was at a mean elevation of 142.52 meters amsl, which lies near the top of Cultural Zone I. I Approximately 221 identifiable mussel shell halves representing nine different species were collected from this feature in addition to numerous unidentifiable fragments. Only four species were present in significant numbers; 106 Amblema plicata, 54 Quadrula nwrtoni, 23 Tritogonia verrucosa, and I 21 Lampsilis teres. The remaining species, Lampsilis hydiana, Toxolasma texasensis, Truncilla truncata, Plectomerus dombeyanus, and Potamilus purpuratus, were represented by counts of six valves or less. Interestingly, Neck (this volume) notes that there are no records of the occurrence of P. dombeyanus in I the upper Trinity River drainage, suggesting that these shells were brought in from further down the Trinity or from drainages to the east. These may have been brought in for the shells instead of as food. However, no apparent cultural modification was noted on the shells. I From the feature zone itself, fragments of deer, cottontail, turkey, and miscellaneous bird bones were identified. No lithic material was observed or collected. The snail identified from this feature zone I is indicative of a gallery woodland habitat. Additionally, plant species identified from flotation samples from the Feature 3 matrix included goosefoot, elm, and live oak. I Although a portion of this feature was found exposed on the surface (Figure 56), the greater part was found to be intact, buried upslope within the unit. The eastern extent (still buried upslope) was not determined beyond the confines of Unit _3 but shell was observed to continue into the east wall as well I as the north wall, indicating intact buried portions of this feature still exist. I Feature 41 I This feature is a small concentration of burned sandstone and bone. It is located to the north and downslope of Feature 40 at an elevation of 142.48 meters amsl within Cultural Zone I. It measures I approximately 60x60 centimeters. No collections were made from this feature. CULTURAL ZONE II I Cultural Zone II extends from mean elevations of 142.36 to 142.15 meters amsl (Figure 41), corresponding with estimated dates of 1300 to 2000 B.P. (Table 32). It contained three features which I were excavated, Features 1, 29, and 36. The first was a small burned sandstone concentration which included only one chert flake, but was well represented by the faunal remains of fish, birds, turtle, and deer. Feature 29 was a mussel shell concentration slightly above Feature 36, a light -scatter of shell, I bone, and burned sandstone. Feature 30 I Feature 30 was an ovoid-shaped charcoal concentration recorded in the south half of Unit 11. It extended from an elevation of 142.44 to 142.27 meters amsl in the upper zone of Cultural Zone II and above Feature 29. It was characterized by charcoal flecks with minor indications of burned sandstone I and burned clay in a compacted silty matrix. A narrow profile trench excavated through this feature revealed that it narrowed with depth. It also contained many roots throughout. It appeared to be a tree root rather than a cultural pit. No samples were collected. I Feature 1 I Feature 1 consisted of a small concentration of fire-cracked sandstone rocks (Figures 26 and 51). It measured approximately 0.80x0.60 meter. Additional fire-cracked rocks were also strewn about close to the hearth and were probably once associated with it. The feature was found to lie at a depth of I 142.27 meters amsl, which corresponds with the upper portion of Cultural Zone II. Unit 1 was placed upslope of this feature to .investigate it. No apparent pit was observed in the feature profile, although charcoal specks were present beneath the stones themselves. A small amount of fragmented bone (some I burned and some unburned), two identifiable mussel shell halves, and a chert tertiary flake were collected from the same level as the base of the hearth and are presumed to have been associated with it. The identifiable faunal remains included unburned bone of bird, catfish, minnow, snake, and deer. Charred I turtle remains were also identified. Analysis of a flotation sample from the feature zone identified live oak and goosefoot. I Upon excavation of Unit 1 only a few stones were found to be in place beneath the surface. The major concentration of Feature 1 observed on the surface represents the eroded portion of this feature; little or no intact portion remains. Upon excavation of Unit 11, the feature was found to extend in all directions beyond the walls of the unit. It was found to lie at a mean elevation of 142.25 meters amsl, which places it in the middle of Cultural I 128 I I

I Zone Il. The shells were found to be one layer or about 3 centimeters thick. Most of the shells were I observed to be ventral side down. Only a few were found on edge. Overall, the bottom of the feature was noted to slope a few centimeters to the east as well as to the south. I A total of 201 mussel shells were collected from this feature and six species were identified: Quadrula mortoni (103);,Alnblema plicata (15); Lampsilis teres (13); Lampsilis hydiana (6); Potamilus purpuratus (2); Quadrula apiculata (2). Neck (this volume) observed two shells from this feature to be I culturally modified with drilled holes or rounded notches (Figures 82c and 82d). Faunal material identified from this feature matrix included deer, turtle, and other unidentified mammal bone. Land snails analyzed from the feature matrix were indicative of a gallery woodland habitat. I Feature 36 I Feature 36 consisted of mussel shell, burned sandstone, charcoal, and bone scattered throughout the north half of Unit 11 and into Unit 1 lA (Figures 34 and 60). It was separated from the Feature 29 mussel shell feature by 3 to 4 centimeters of apparently sterile sediment. Whether Feature 36 is truly I a separate event from Feature 29 cannot be positively ascertained; in any case, the temporal separation between these two features, if any, is likely to be minimal. Feature 36 was 2 to 3 centimeters thick on a surface at a mean elevation of 142.22 meters amsl within Cultural Zone II. Approximately four shells along the eastern wall were found to be a few centimeters deeper than the rest, which all tended to cluster I at a specific elevation. However, because the surface on which the feature lay was observed by the excavators to be sloping toward the east, these four shells were included with this feature. I Tiny flecks of charcoal were dispersed across the occupation surface, but not around the burned rocks. These rocks were small and reddened but not fractured. Archeomagnetic analysis of three of these rocks confirmed the field suspicion that they were out of place. This compares with the results I from Feature 35, and indicates that these are disturbed hearth stones. Due to the flat and angular shape of these stones it is felt that they would not have been as suitable for boiling stones as quartzite and limestone cobbles which occur in the vicinity of the site. I The mussel shell was found to be scattered throughout the unit unlike some other recorded mussel shell features which are more concentrated. Nevertheless, their occurrence on a surface which varied I only a few centimeters indicates they are contemporary. A total of 32 mussel shells were collected and these species were identified as Quadrula mortoni (22), Alnblema plicata (9), and Tritogonia verrucosa (1). Bone from this feature area was identified as turtle carapace and fragments of deer rib. This shell I feature was somewhat unusual in the amount of other apparently contemporary material. This feature was not mapped in due to the rain at the end of the project. I It should fall within Cultural Zone Il. I I earth and charcoal extend some 13 centimeters in depth, these stones are thought to be disturbed hearth stones from this feature (or maybe another nearby hearth). I Feature 25 Feature 25 was a lithic scatter observed to be eroding downslope at an elevation of 141.97 meters I amsl or within Cultural Zone III (Figure 62). It measured approximately 1.0x2.60 meters wher-e exposed. This feature was unique in that it was the only feature recorded which was solely composed of lithic materials, including flakes, chips, and an uniface. Chert was found to be the dominant material type; only one quartzite shatter was collected. Heat treatment was. noted on some of the chert. This lithic I material was collected mainly from the upper portion and from the lower meter of the feature. Initially collected in November 1992, another collection was made in May 1993, after the frequent rains had exposed more material (these are discussed in more detail in the lithic section). Material eroding from I this feature may well have contributed to the lithics collected as part of Feature 24, which is adjacent to this feature and arbitrarily separated by a narrow erosional rill. While it is felt that this feature probably represents an in situ chipping station, an activity not defined elsewhere at the site, it could also represent I secondary discard from chipping. The generally small size of the materials does not support intentional discard, however. Unit 15 was set up at the upper exposed portion of this feature after the surface collection to try and define the point of origin and determine if any remains were intact below the I surface. Excavation revealed that little of the feature was intact within the confines of the unit. It was not determined if intact portions were still present further upslope. I Feature 10 Feature 10 was a mussel shell concentration upslope and north of Feature 9. When originally I recorded, only about 20 mussel shells were exposed on the surface (Figure 63). Most of these were whole or with few fractures, indicating this feature was more recently exposed than many others observed. Also noted on the surface with this feature was a deer mandible and teeth and a few nodules I of burned clay, all of which were collected along with the exposed mussel shell before the intact portion was excavated in Unit 13. A few pieces of burned sandstone were also downslope of the feature. I Once excavated, the fully exposed portion of this feature (including that originally exposed on the surface) measured 0.40xl .10 meters (Figure 37). Judging from the shells exposed in the south wall profile, the feature continued into the other half of the unit which was not excavated. The feature was found to be a single layer, with the exception of a small concentration of shell which was found to I overlap. Bottom elevations varied by 4 centimeters, with the mean at 141.96 meters amsl. This placed it within the lower end of Cultural Zone III. The surface on which this feature lay sloped to the east with a greater tendency for the shells to be lying flat in that direction (Figures 61 and 64). The shells in the I central portion of the feature tended to be at more of an angle (this was also the area of overlap). The westernmost shells exhibited both positions more or less equally. No apparent pattern was observed in regard to which side of the shell, exterior or interior, was facing down. It is exposed in a small erosional gully on the slope and measures about 0.90x0.90 meter and was at an elevation of 141.66 meters amsl. On that basis the feature was assigned a separate designation. Cultural material observed and collected from this feature was limited to a single quartzite split cobble. Two bivalve halves and a long bone were observed outside the arbitrary limits of the feature but were not collected. I Feature 34 This feature was a light scatter of burned sandstone first noted on the surface at about the same I elevation as Features 38, 37, 16, 26, 27, 42, 32, and 15. Unit 14 was excavated to investigate this feature (Figures 40 and 69). Upon excavation it was found to be relatively intact and lie on a surface at a mean elevation of 141.66 meters amsl within Cultural Zone IV. It occurred throughout levels 2 I through 4. It measured approximately lxl meter as exposed. Archeomagnetic analysis of four stones from this features revealed them to be in situ. It is suggested these four stones represent an intact portion of an otherwise scattered hearth. Deer bone and unidentified mammal bones, including a modified bone I (Figure 83b), were identified from this feature area. A single chert tertiary flake was recovered from the feature zone. This feature represents a relatively intact hearth at a level so far identified as correlating with a period in which deer procurement was a major subsistence activity. I Feature 32 I Feature 32 consists primarily of a widely dispersed scatter of about 15 mussel shell halves. Three pieces of burned sandstone, each about 50 centimeters apart, were also noted in the lowest portion of the feature. Bone was scattered among the mussel shell but most appears to be concentrated at the north I central end of the feature. This feature is located in the area of dense cultural deposits close to Features 15, 42, 26, and 27. It measures 1.50xl.60 meter but inadvertently was not shot in during the intensive mapping operations at the site. However, it clearly lies within Cultural Zone IV. Materials collected I in November 1993 included deer and small unidentified vertebrate bone, and a piece of shatter. I I I 139 I

I .1 Feature 48 This feature, originally designated as Feature D, is a small scatter of burned rock and mussel shell. It is the southernmost feature in this cultural zone, nearly 20 meters south of Feature 11. Its elevation I is 141.50 meters amsl. This feature was not relocated during the data recovery phase. Feature 18 I Feature 18 is a small scatter of long bone noted on the surface downslope of Features 40, 41, and 43. It measures approximately 0.70x0.50 meter, and was recorded at an elevation of 141.49 meters amsl, I placing it at the bottom of Cultural Zone IV. The bone was not collected. CULTURAL ZONE V I Cultural Zone V extends from about 141.40 to 141.10 meters amsl (Figure 41), and encompasses an estimated time range between 3100 and 3400 B.P. (fable 32). Only three cultural features, Features I 19, 23, and 22, were recorded in this zone. A bulk soil sample from the testing phase effectively dates the top of this zone to 2840 ± 80 B.P. with a calibrated one-sigma range of 3136 to 2790 B.P. (fable 31). A Yarbrough dart point (Figure 78b) from this zone supports the Late Archaic date. I Feature 19 I . This was a burned clay feature first exposed during the removal of overburden to start hand excavation of Unit SC (Figures 29 and 76). It had no definitive shape but roughly measured 1.0x0.65 meter. The contact, or upper elevation was at about 141.34 meters amsl or within the upper portion of Cultural Zone V. The burned clay colors ranged from yellow to orange to dark red and brown. No I stones were present with this feature. The feature tended to branch out, and this along with its irregular shape may indicate a tree root. This feature was never fully excavated; thus its status as a cultural or non-cultural feature was not absolutely determined. I Feature 23 Feature 23 was a semi-circular, orange-colored, burned clay feature located along the southern edge I or bulk of Unit 8B (adjacent to Trench M6), thus responsible for its semi-circular shape (Figures 29 and 76). It measured approximately 0.80x0.45 meter. The top of this feature was encountered at approximately 141.34 meters amsl during the excavation of the first level. This is presumed to be the I surface on which this feature originated and places it within Cultural Zone V. The feature was about 15 centimeters at its thickest. No apparent pit was discernable in the profile; instead the heat from the fire on the surface thermally altered the soil below, resulting in its burned appearance. Some burned mussel I shell fragments were noted within this feature. Other burned shell and burned bone were collected from the levels in which this feature was identified. This feature is tentatively interpreted as a cultural fire feature or hearth. It did not display the funnel-shape or branching as Feature 19 did. The fill from this I feature was collected but was not analyzed. Feature 22 I Feature 22 was exposed in the north wall of Unit 8 while clearing overburden to begin excavation. It was a somewhat circular concentration of charcoal with brownish black clay in the interior (Figure 29). I The exposed portion measured approximately 13x20 centimeters. It was first noted at 141.29 meters amsl 148 I I

Estimated cultural zone dates are slightly older, ranging from 3500 to 3800 B.P. (fable 32). I Feature 44 This mussel shell feature was exposed in Trench M6 at an elevation of 140.83 meters amsl within I Cultural Zone VII. Its full extent was never ascertained because the excavation of Unit 8, which was opened in order to investigate this feature, had to be halted at the level where the feature was just becoming apparent. It can safely be said that this was a concentration rather than a few scattered shells I that extended at least one meter from the trench into the unit. This is the deepest recorded mussel shell concentration at 41DL270. A total of five bivalves were collected from Level 5 of Unit 8 and were identified as Amblema plicata (two were pulled for shell isotope analysis). I CULTURAL ZONE VIII I Only Feature 45 was assigned to this zone, which ranges in elevation from about 140.50 to 140.00 meters amsl (Figure 41). A bulk soil sample from this zone yielded a radiocarbon age of 4165 ± 205 B.P. (fable 29). The estimated dates for this occupation of the site range between 3800 and 4100 B.P. I (fable 32). It was one of the southernmost features investigated in the Hicks & Company studies, as well as the lowest feature at the site. That this is a cultural feature is supported by occasional finds of fragments of shell and isolated burned rocks at approximately this level in the small gully. ## Page 169

I DISCUSSION I Overall, 51 features were recorded at the site. This is a relatively complete sample of the features exposed in the eroded bank of the ditch in the area sampled, but _reflects only a small fraction of the total assemblage observed at the site (which extends several hundred meters south of the study area). The I sample studied also reflects a bias against those features found in Zones VI and below which are not exposed on the surface. I Feature types include rock concentrations, bone concentrations, mussel shell concentrations, and combinations of the above. In addition, there was a single lithic concentration and several burned clay areas which were recorded as features. Only a few of the rock features were associated with pits; most I of the features studied appeared to lay directly on the surface. By far the most visually distinctive and interesting feature of the types present at the site was the mussel shell concentration. I The recorded mussel shell features (those features that are solely or primarily shells amassed in a concentration instead of a few shells scattered on the surface or in an excavation unit) are thought to represent single event discard piles. We were unable to demonstrate the initial site of processing but I presume it would have been fairly close to the area of discard. The features are dispersed throughout the upper seven cultural zones. In all, there were eight features which met these criteria (Features 40, 3, 29, 36, 17, 10, 9, and 44). They occur in Cultural Zones I, II, ill, IV, and VII. However, six out I of nine (66%) of these features (40, 3, 29, 36, 17, and 10) cluster at the higher elevations within Cultural Zones I (Tl terrace) through ill (at 142.60 to 141.96 meters amsl), or between the estimated years 650 to 2500 B.P. It is also during this estimated time period, particularly within Cultural Zones I and II that I fewer lithics were collected, implying fewer activities which would have relied on stone tools (such as inussel shell procurement, processing, and consumption). There were also fewer mammal remains recovered from Zones I and II than from the zones immediately below, further suggesting mussel collection was a key activity at the site during this time, perhaps due to population and/or environmental I stress (although even at their peak mussel biomass is significantly less than other resources). Mussels were augmented by other aquatic resources such as fish and turtle. A slight rise in lithic production is seen in Zone ill correlating with the demonstrated increase in deer remains. Cultural Zone VII is I significant because it contains the deepest and oldest mussel shell feature yet recorded. In regard to mussel species prevalence through time, there tends to be a shift between the two most I common species, Amblema plicata and Quadrula mortoni. The former comprises the greatest percentage in the earliest and latest mussel shell features excavated; Feature 9 (Zone IV), and Feature 3 (Zone I), respectively. The shell features excavated from Zones II (Features 29 and 36), and ill (Feature 10) I reverse the trend with the latter species dominant. A few other species also show significantly high numbers and percentages to have been played an important role in the ancient diet (Tritogonia verrucosa, Lampsilis teres, and Lampsilis hydiana). The significance of the temporal variations in shell remains and I the role of cultural utilization of these resources requires more data and analysis of this type in the region cautions Neck (this volume). Species diversity is also discussed in the chapter "The Denton Creek Paleoenvironment". I Most of the features recorded at 41DL270 were found to fall within Cultural Zone IV, only 31 centimeters thick. The only discrete mussel shell feature (9) recorded in this zone occurs in the lower I part at a mean elevation of 141.62 meters amsl, with an estimated date of 2500 B.P., a separation of about 600 years from the latter shell features. The majority of lithic material also falls within this zone, the highest percentage of utilized flakes and almost half of the projectile points having been encountered I 152 I I


![Image 1 - Page 169](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p169_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 169*


---

## Page 170

I within the lower part of Zone IV and the extreme upper part of Zone V (fables 3 and 4). Of note, is I the fact that no lithics were recovered below Zone V. Many features contained at least small amounts of bone. The greatest amount and percentages were I collected from Cultural Zone IV. Several features, many containing deer, occur at about the same elevation and within close horizontal proximity within the zone (Features 37, 38, 16, 26, 27, 42, 32, and 15). The faunal analysis indicates at least three individual deer are represented by the bone associated I with these features (see Masson, this volume). In addition, different ages are represented, indicating deer were exploited in more than a single season. Thus, despite the horizontal and vertical proximity of these featiires, they appear to represent a series of unrelated short-term events at the site instead of a single I episode. Deer were a preferred resource for much of the time but changes in procurement resources have been documented at 41DL270. As mentioned above, changes in deer procurement may be reflected in the converse percentages of mussel shell concentrations as well as lithic remains. Further dlscussion of I the implications of the changing faunal assemblages is contained in the chapter "Vertebrate Faunal Remains from 41DL270", and the concluding chapters of this volume. I Burned rock was prevalent throughout Zones I - IV. While burned earth/clay features were identified in Zone V, burned rock was not associated with them. Although it is assumed some type of roasting or baking was involved in mussel preparation, only a small quantity of burned rock was found I in association with the major shell features excavated. This could well be a product of small unit size. Of those shell features which had burned rock, the stones from Feature 36 were demonstrated to be out of place, suggesting that they had been dumped there possibly along with the discarded mussel shell. Archeomagnetic analysis of two other burned rock features (34 and 35) demonstrated the former was at I least partially intact, while the latter was out of place (see Gose's chapter this volume). Most of the burned rock, which is usually found with some bone, a few fragments of shell, or a very limited amount of lithic material, would be interpreted as hearths or remnants of hearths and the high percentage of I mammal bone tends to support this. The stones do not appear to be boiling stones since it is believed · that sandstone, the major hearthstone resource type present at the site, would seem to be an inefficient heat conductor for boiling; quartzite cobbles would appear to be more appropriate for that task. The I hearths could well have been used for both mammal and mussel shell preparation. However, it can not be demonstrated if these two activities were carried out at the same hearth at the same time, or if in some cases the hearths were used for one purpose and in other cases for another. Their appearance would I remain the same. Although the paucity of features within the deepest cultural zones (VI - IX) may be a product of I sampling, it is thought that whatever time period these zones actually represent Qikely the Middle Archaic) was a time when the site occupation was at a minimum. This is supported by the soil susceptibility analysis performed by Gose (this volume) which indicates occupation of the site at these I low elevations was much less than that demonstrated for the zones above. I I I 153 I I

## Page 172

I ARTIFACT ANALYSIS I Dana Anthony This chapter presents descriptions of the artifacts recovered from 41DL270 during the testing and I data recovery phases of the project. The artifacts are presented in two broad categories of lithics and miscellaneous, the latter including bone and shell. In addition, the Bill Young collection, generously loaned to the project by Mr. Young, was examined and is briefly described and illustrated. Although I pottery is lacking in the project collection, four sherds which were collected by Mr. Young (two shell tempered and two sandy paste types) were examined. Also important in Young's collection are reported pieces of what appears to be daub (suggesting the presence of a structure) and a boatstone (Prikryl 1992). I Although excavators at 41DL270 found small pieces of burned clay (often in nodule form) by areas which had been burned, these could not be identified as daub. Lithic material comprises the vast majority of the artifacts recovered, but even they are scant compared with other sites investigated nearby. I LITHIC ANALYSIS I The lithic material collected from 41DL270 during both the testing and data recovery phases was very sparse. As already mentioned, after the survey and testing phase, it was felt the site was lithic poor and therefore had little to contribute toward lithic studies for the North-Central Texas area or for the site in particular. Although it now appears that the stone tool inventory from 41DL270 may be larger than I originally expected, the data set is still small; however, that in itself may be a useful analytic tool. While quantitative studies may not be totally reliable, some interesting patterns have emerged. I Research Issues Issues to be addressed by the lithic analysis included a temporal framework as well as a projectile I point chronology. The diagnostics already collected from the site generally fall into the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric I and II periods defined for the region. Radiocarbon dates from the testing phase supported these sequences and cultural periods. I Relevant to subsistence and settlement of the site, it was hoped that the context of lithic tools and the manufacturing debris would clarify activity areas as well as the function of the tools. The author had I erroneously reported the lack of scrapers, utilized flakes, or knives collected from 41DL270 (Anthony et al. 1992:21). After examining the lithics from the data recovery phase as well as the Bill Young collection, it is now known that processing tools such as these, although perhaps infrequent, were indeed I present at the site. In regard to exchange or interactions among prehistoric peoples of the region it was hoped that I material types could shed light on this issue. Chronological changes in material type could also be compared with that demonstrated by Prikryl (1990), and observed by other researchers (McGregor 1987; Brown 1989). The distribution of, and changes in procurement areas for the site's residents might also I be studied by material type. The types of the lithic artifacts present at the site might indicate the distance of procurement sources. As example, if the source was nearby, one would expect quantities of cores and debitage. If the source was far, there should be a larger percentage of finished tools with less debitage I except for resharpening flakes. Although not all of the research issues could be addressed due to the paucity of the data set, the issues of temporal framework, chronological markers, and to some degree, activity areas were addressed. I 155 I I


![Image 1 - Page 172](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p172_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 172*


---

## Page 173

I Procurement areas and exchange systems were more difficult questions and remain unanswered. In fact, I it cannot be stated with certainty that Ogallala-derived gravels were exposed in the immediate vicinity of 41DL270, although it is thought they may have been exposed at a distance of about 1.5 kilometer downstream at 41DL326. These issues are discussed further in the following sections. I Methods The lithic material was recovered by controlled excavations as well as from surface features I recorded during the data recovery phase. The lithic artifacts were sorted according to material and morphological type. The author would like to note the difficulty in distinguishing between the Ogallala derived coarse-grained chert and the quartzite in this collection, particularly those specimens suspected I of being heat-treated. While the quartzite was much coarser than the chert and contained other minerals or different colors of quartz, the average chert specimen was also coarse grained. Chert suspected of being non-local was always of a highly vitreous fine-grained material and was noted where suspected. 1· Non-local in this case refers not only to a lack of a particular material in the collections of 41DL270, but also material not thought to be within a few miles of the site or within the Ogallala gravels. The unidentified material, designated other, is very coarse grained and may well be a form of quartzite, but I it could also be siltstone. It is gray with tan inclusions. Debitage was sorted into flakes, chips, and shatter categories as defined by Crabtree (1972). I Flakes were sorted according to type or stage of reduction as well as by dimensions, which were measured only on whole flakes. Other recorded attributes included the presence or absence of a platform (and whether it was crushed or not) and a lip. This system was utilized in an effort to distinguish soft I hammer percussion from pressure flaking. All of these traits were then sorted according to material type for each attribute on each type of flake. Only whole flakes were used for these sorts. I Formal and informal tools included projectile points, beveled unifaces (commonly referred to as scrapers), utilized flakes, and battered cobbles. Very little in the recognizable tool category was recovered. A microscope was used on all lithic specimens as a means of identifying material type as well as signs of utilization. In addition, polish was also looked for. A comparative use wear analysis was not I conducted as this was beyond the scope of the collection and project. All of the lithic material was finally sorted according to absolute elevation and the corresponding cultural zone. I A total of 138 individual specimens of lithic material were collected from 41DL270. All are chipped stone artifacts with the exception of two battered quartzite cobbles. · The lithic material is arranged first by the debitage category and is followed by informal and formal tool types. The most basic I data sets are presented in Tables 3 and 4, respectively. They provide the following information: provenience, artifact type and material, mean metric elevations, and cultural zones. I Lithic Material The vast majority of the material is from Ogallala-derived gravels - quartzite and a coarse-grained I chert most often tan in color, and an unidentified material referred to as "other". The former are sedimentary (orthoquartzites) and metamorphic (metaquartzites), while cherts are, with rare exception, sedimentary. Cherts are silicas of microcrystalline and cryptocrystalline structure. Of major importance I to this study is whether or not the Ogallala materials commonly found in the region were available to the inhabitants of 41DL270 or if the occupants had to go some distance, to the Trinity River, for instance, to acquire their material. Chris Caran, project geomorphologist, examined a trench excavated on top of I 156 I I

## Page 176

I I the Pleistocene terrace and found Woodbine-derived sandstones and limestones, but no quartzites or chert (see Caran's chapter this volume). According to Caran, (personal communication 1994) the Ogallala gravels do not occur uniformly, but rather in discrete patches. Due to the limited trenching as well as the extensive modern quarrying and other land disturbances at the site, it was not determined if the I Ogallala material was exposed prehistorically. Among the best regional sources on lithic resources are Holliday and Welty (1981) and Banks I (1990). Although Holiday and Welty (1981) concentrates on the Llano Estacado, the inclusion of the Ogallala formation and its constituent gravels is pertinent to the North-Central Texas region. More pertinent to the study area and more recent is the study by Banks (1990), whose study area includes New I Mexico, Texas, southeast Colorado, southern Kansas, Oklahoma, the southwest corner of Missouri, western Arkansas, and Louisiana. I In describing major material types used in the manufacture of stone tools, Banks (1990) differentiates between lithic resource and lithic source. While lithic resource refers to any material suitable for tool making and found in archeological contexts, lithic source alludes to the actual geological I formation and location (Banks 1990:3). Of major significance to the North-Central Texas region within which 41DL270 is located is the widespread yet localized occurrence of redeposited Ogallala gravels (source) containing quartzites and cherts (resource). Actual quarries, here defined by Banks as long-term I and intensive, with excavated pits and specialized tools are not common (Banks 1990:4). Lithic sources from 41DL270 are referred to as procurement areas. It is presumed that the occupants of 41DL270 and many of the sites along Denton Creek acquired their lithic resources from the surface or from outcrops. I Banks also makes the case for the matter of expediency in chert collection and modification in the form of surface materials most available to the group (Banks 1990:5) as does Binford's model (1979). Banks (1990) refers to such sites as "workshop-site types" and states that they are most often found on or near I gravel deposits. As mentioned, the Woodbine Formation contains "sporadic but many localities" of Uvalde Gravel veneers, but these are not derived from the Woodbine itself (Banks 1990:56, and Caran, personal I communication 1994). This formation produces the sandstones so prevalent in North-Central Texas and at 41DL270 which underlie the Cross Timbers. Hematite and limonite are also found in the Woodbine. Uvalde Gravels is a term commonly used to include those materials, particularly chert and quartzite, I found in upland gravel deposits on interfluves between major rivers in Texas. Originally defined by Hill, these lithic resources have been discussed by Byrd (1971), Menzer and Slaughter (1971), Ritchie and Slaughter (1972), Holliday and Welty (1981), Brown (1987d), and Banks (1990). The actual situation I with Uvalde Gravels is not well understood and deposits from several different sources laid down at several different times are frequently uncritically included in this rubric. Banks' comment is appropriate here. I As if lithic resource identification of in situ cherts were not complicated enough, the Uvalde Gravel certainly provides an added dimension to the complexities in many parts of Texas and I elsewhere. . . . As it currently stands Uvalde Gravel is an ubiquitous term that is applied to almost all relict gravel deposits on late Tertiary and Pleistocene terraces as well as upland interfluves left as erosional remnants of the retreating High Plains that once extended as far I to the east as the vicinity of Fort Worth (Banks 1990:56). Menzer and Slaughter (1971 ), discussing the upland gravels of the Dallas area, note that the Trinity I River interfluvial gravels originated from the southern Rocky Mountains (Ogallala Formation) during the 159 I I

## Page 177

I late Pliocene. Despite the author's apparently incorrect assertion that the Ogallala materials represent an I in situ Ogallala Formation lag deposit extending as far east as the Dallas area rather than alluvially redeposited gravels (Caran, personal communication 1994), they provide a wealth of information on the actual gravels. Important to the study of 41DL270, one of the upland collection locations in the Menzer and Slaughter study was from the northwest comer of Dallas County at Northlake, within a stone's throw, I so to speak, of the site. The authors noted that only about 10 percent of the material was chert, with 90 percent orthoquartzite and metaquartzite (Menzer and Slaughter 1971:217). Petrographic comparison of the sample units with known outcrops of Pennsylvanian and Cretaceous age gravels from the Trinity I drainage illustrated the former could not have derived from the latter. They surmised the most probable sources were from eastern or northeastern New Mexico. I Another important factor is that the region in which 41DL270 is located is totally lacking in chert outcrops. Such outcrops occur far to the west and southwest of the project area. Local, chert is found within the redeposited gravels whose locations, as mentioned above, are sporadic. Banks (1990: 109) I remarks that prehistoric inhabitants of the region were apparently forced to utilize whatever was available, and such appears to be the case at 41DL270. I Material type has been documented as a rough chronological indicator for the North-Central Texas region (McGregor 1987; Brown 1987a, 1987d; Prikryl 1990). McGregor's analysis of the lithic material from the Richland-Chambers Reservoir data recovery phase resulted in the demonstration of temporal I differences in material types (McGregor 1987:185-195). For that project the lithic material types were sorted into five varieties: 1) chert; 2) quartzite; 3) Pisgah Ridge Chert; 4) silicified wood; and 5) other. Both projectile points and lithic debitage were analyzed (McGregor 1987: 187). He found that chert use I was highest before and during the Middle Archaic and lower during the Late Archaic and early part of the Late Prehistoric. There was a slight rise in the use of chert again (although the analysis relied on projectile points only for this data) in the St Elmo phase dated A.O. 1300 to 1650 (McGregor 1987: 192) I which coincides with Prikryl's Late Prehistoric Il period. Reductions in territory size and settlement mobility resulted in fewer visits to the source, thus causing differential chert use over time (McGregor 1987:192). In addition, McGregor points out the chert is more frequent to the west of the project area (particularly along the central Brazos), which would have been a likely source for chert during those I periods of greater use. He also studied the distribution of Pisgah Ridge chert (McGregor 1987: 189-195) and found that it too decreases in distribution over time from the Middle Archaic to the Late Prehistoric which further supports his interpretation cited above. I At Aquilla Lake, Brown (1987a, 1987d) found a slight tendency for Late Prehistoric sites to have higher percentages of chert (ranging upward from 97 percent), while Late Archaic and earlier sites tended I to have slightly lower percentages (ranging between 88 and 97 percent). More important to the percentage than the antiquity of the sites, however, was the distance of the sites from upland gravel sources. Sites closer to upland gravel sources tended to have higher percentages of chert and, conversely, I lower percentages of chert were found at those sites located farther from upland gravels. Finally, data from some of the sites and upland gravel localities suggested the possibility that chert sources had been depleted through long utilization. An examination of natural gravel resources showed lower percentages I of chert at procurement localities near defined archeological sites. In Prikryl's study of surface collections (1990) along the Elm Fork he analyzed projectile points I and divided them into chert, quartzite, and unclassified. He found that most of the quartzite was Ogallala. Most of the chert was thought to be non-local. Beginning with the Paleoindian period, cherts account for about 94 percent of the total. From the Paleoindian through the Early and Middle Archaic I 160 I I

## Page 178

I I there is a slow decline in chert use of roughly 10 percent for each period, with corresponding increases in quartzite utilization. By the Late Archaic, quartzite comprises 62 percent of the collection and remains slightly higher than chert throughout the Late Prehistoric I period, although the percentage falls I somewhat. But by the Late Prehistoric II period, chert is once again the dominant material at 70 percent (Prikryl 1990:83). Prikryl has attributed these diachronic changes in material type to changes in group mobility and size. In periods where there was a greater proportion of chert (here assumed to be non local), groups were smaller in size and covered larger territories. With a greater reliance on quartzite, I particularly in the Late Archaic, the territory range is decreased as population increases. Along with a greater reliance on quartzite, technological change in the form of heat-treatment to improve knapping quality is also considered more typical of the period. The increase in chert in the Late Prehistoric II I suggests either greater mobility or trade connections (Prikryl 1990:83). Debitage I Debitage collected from the site is shown in Table 3. I FLAKES Only one primary flake was recovered from 41DL270 during the data recovery phase. The I material is a fine-grain black chert with a tan cortex. It was the only specimen of this material observed at the site in either artifact or raw material form and is probably non-Ogallala in origin. The flake measures 16x26x7 millimeters and lacks a lip, which indicates hard hammer percussion. It was recovered I from Feature 16 within Cultural Zone IV. A total of 13 secondary flakes were collected from the site. Overall, the material type was evenly I distributed, with seven of the flakes made of quartzite and six made from chert. However, when sorted by cultural zone/elevation it was noted that all of the quartzite secondary flakes were from features assigned to Cultural Zone IV between the elevations of 141.52 to 141.70 meters amsl. The secondary I flakes identified as chert were all from Feature 25, which corresponds with Cultural Zone mat a mean elevation of 141.97 meters amsl. As discussed in the feature section, Feature 25 is the only feature so far identified that consisted solely of lithic debitage, and as such, is one of the few that can be specified as a particular activity area or chipping station. The material is so similar (all are reddened except for I one that is tan with red just at the cortex) that they all appear to be from the same cobble or larger flake. This is supported by the presence of both secondary and tertiary flakes, as well as chips and shatter from this feature. All of the chert appears to be Ogallala derived. I A sort of all whole secondary flakes revealed that 50 percent have no lips. When sorted by material, it was revealed that 57 percent (4) of those made of quartzite have no lips, while 29 percent (2) I do and 14 percent (1) have crushed platforms. A reverse trend was seen with the chert secondary flakes, with 60 percent (3) having lips, compared with 40 percent with no lips. None were identified with crushed platforms. The "other" material saw an even distribution between those with and without lips. I Most of the quartzite secondary flakes were manufactured by the hard hammer percussion method, while most of the chert secondary flakes were manufactured by soft hammer. The other material was a draw between the two. I The tertiary flakes were sorted the same way as described above. Twenty-one percent have no lips, while 66 percent have lips and 10 percent have crushed platforms. When these were sorted by material I the majority of quartzite tertiary flakes, or 70 percent (16), were found to have lips while no lips and 161 I I

## Page 179

I crushed platforms comprised 13 percent (3) each. Of the chert tertiary flakes, 63 percent (17) had lips I and 30 percent (8) did not. Only 7 percent (2) were found to have crushed platforms. The single tertiary flake of other material had a lip. Although a larger percentage of chert flakes were made by hard hammer than the quartzite examples, most of the tertiary flakes were manufactured from soft hammer I percussion. Overall, including quartzite, chert, and other material of whole flakes, 29 percent (19) had no lips, 61 percent (40) have lips, and 9 percent (6) have crushed platforms. It is evident that the great majority I of these flakes were produced by the soft hammer method and, combined with pressure flaking, make up 70 percent of the sample. This is consistent with the smaller thinning or resharpening flakes which the majority of the debitage is thought to represent. This also means that most of the initial reduction I occurred elsewhere (probably at the source) with finishing accomplished at the site. More secondary flakes were made by hard hammer regardless of material while most of the tertiary I flakes were make by soft hammer and a few by pressure flaking. This supports the view that the Ogallala materials from which most of the lithic artifacts were made were not exposed at 41DL270. Instead, gravels were selected elsewhere (perhaps as close as 41DL326, 1.5 kilometers to the east) and initially I reduced on the spot. Secondary reduction and thinning as well as resharpening of tools already finished took place at 41DL270. This is further supported by the lack of cores and primary flakes. I Table 5 presents mean length, widths and thickness of each flake type by material. In general, it can be seen that the flakes recovered from the site are small. Although the total sample size is also small, several trends are notable. Overall, the quartzite flakes are thicker and tend to be wider than the flakes I of chert and other material. The converse, that chert flakes are somewhat longer and thinner, also seems to be true. I The whole flakes were also sorted according to the Cultural Zones in which they occurred (Table n 5). Of the 66 flakes, 33 are chert, 30 are quartzite, and 3 are other material. From Cultural Zones and m, 92 percent of the complete flakes are chert, none are of quartzite, and 8 percent are of the other material for a total of 24 specimens. The whole flakes from Cultural Zones IV and V reverse the trend, I although not as markedly as above, with 26 percent of chert, 71 percent of quartzite, and 2 percent of the other material, for a total of 42 flakes. Clearly the later zones are producing an overwhelming majority of chert flakes. Zones Il and m range in estimated years from about 1300 to 2500 B.P. (see I Table 32). Zone IV and V have estimated date ranges from about 2500 to 3400 B.P. I Table 5. The Provenience and Measurements of Lithic Materials from 41DL270. ## Page 185

I It would appear that the occupants of 41DL270 were selecting for chert, even from among the I Ogallala gravels which most are thought to come from, during the time periods thought to coincide with the later part of the Late Archaic. Conversely, quartzite was more common in those periods thought to correlate with the earlier part of the Late Archaic. This is the reverse of what McGregor (1987) and I Prikryl (1990) have found. However, the real and estimated dates used here may not correlate well with the cultural periods used by the above researchers at Richland Chambers and the lower Elm Fork, respectively. The problem with bulk soil and humate dates is discussed in Brown's last two chapters in this volume. I CIDPS AND SHATTER I A total of 29 chips and 11 shatter fragments were collected. Neither the incomplete artifacts (chip) 4t nor the pieces of shatter were measured, as the size was not considered very meaningful this analysis. None, however, were larger than about 30 millimeters at the greatest measurement. Most of the shatter I was of quartzite, while the chip category was fairly evenly divided between quartzite and chert. As with the other categories most of the quartzite chips were from Feature 15 (all quartzite with reddened areas), while most of the chert chips were from Feature 25 (which was all chert). Chips were found in Cultural I m Zones through V. Informal Tools I A total of 13 informal tools were recovered from the excavations at 41DL270. This category includes utilized flakes (nine), beveled unifaces presumed to be scrapers (two), and two quartzite cobbles I with battering scars. The latter tools are included here as these are the only non-chipped stone tools in the assemblage. Both informal and formal tools are shown in Table 4. I UTILIZED FLAKES This category includes nine utilized flakes, six of chert, one of quartzite, and two of "other" material. While the vast majority of the utilized flakes are from Cultural Zone IV, one of the tools of I unknown material was from Cultural Zone Il, none were recovered from Zone m, and two were recovered from Zone V (Unit 8, Level 1). This latter context also produced two projectile points. Two of the utilized flakes were recovered from Unit 12 in levels thought to be associated with Feature 9 in I Cultural Zone IV. These are individually described below. The numbering includes sequential specimen numbers for each category, lot numbers, and the individual sample number within each particular lot. I Specimen 1 (Lot 108 #1) This is an utilized flake of unknown material of light tan and gray color. It is not as coarse grained I as quartzite but appears too sandy for chert. The original form is a tertiary flake with a lip apparent on the proximal end. Both sides of the distal portion of this flake, on the dorsal surface, show utilization scars in the form of small nicks. The cross-section is piano-convex and it measures 17x13x2.5 I millimeters. Despite this small size, the flake is a complete specimen. It was recovered from Unit 1, Level 4 at 142.13 meters amsl within Cultural Zone Il. The only other artifact found in this level was a bone fishhook (described under miscellaneous artifacts). Two tertiary flakes were found in the level I below. I 168 I I

I I Specimen 2 (Lot 175.1 #4) This utilized flake is of gray to purple quartzite. The flake has snapped across one end so the original size is unknown. The resulting chip is 16x20x3 millimeters. Tiny flake scars are apparent on I one of the dorsal edges. This artifact was collected from the surface of Feature 21, which consisted primarily of sandstone with bone and some mussel shell. This feature was mapped at a mean elevation of 141.79 meters amsl in Cultural Zone N. Other artifacts collected from this feature included two I tertiary flakes and one shatter. Bone was identified as deer, bird, large mammal, and unidentified vertebrae. I Specimen 3 (Lot 194 #1) This utilized flake is made of a light tan, fine-grained chert. A darker tan cortex reIPains along I one edge. It is triangular in shape and piano-convex in cross-section, measuring 17x24x5 millimeters. A lip is apparent, indicating soft hammer percussion. Tiny utilization scars appear on the left lateral edge and flake scars are apparent on the ventral and dorsal surface. The right lateral edge is broken, resulting I in an convex scar. This arc-shaped portion also has very light utilization scars closest to the left lateral edge. This tool was recovered from Unit 12, Level 3 at a mean elevation of 141.59 meters amsl in Cultural Zone N. This level encompassed Feature 9, a mussel shell concentration. Other artifacts I collected from the feature area include three chert and quartzite flakes and chips, and a Motley projectile point of light gray chert. Bone recovered from the strata thought to be associated with the Feature 9 included deer and turtle, and unidentified vertebrata. I Specimen 4 (Lot 192 #1) I This is the smallest tool recovered from 41DL270, but as it is broken along two edges its original size cannot be estimated. The remaining portion, measures 15x7x2 millimeters. The material is a grainy gray chert. The utilization is along the remaining edge in the form of small flake scars which are on one side only and come to a point at one end. It appears that this small point was created by the break: rather I than intentionally. As with the artifact described above, this is also from Unit 12, but from the first level, above Feature 9. It is from a mean elevation of 141.84 meters amsl in Cultural Zone N. No other materials were collected. I Specimen 5 (Lot 174 #3) I This is an utilized flake (Figure 77a) of tan chert with some reddened spots noted on both the dorsal and ventral sides. It is one of the largest tools of this type recovered, perhaps because it is essentially complete. It measures 33x26x5mm, and is piano-convex in cross-section. It has a lip and a I pronounced bulb. The area of utilization, noted by small flakes scars, are predominately on the distal end on the dorsal surface. There is also some wear on a small portion of the right lateral edge. This artifact is from the surface collection of Feature 20, consisting of burned sandstone at a mean elevation I of 141.52 meters amsl in Cultural Zone N. Three pieces of quartzite debitage were also collected. Specimen 6 (Lot 176 #1) I This utilized flake is of unknown material, gray with light tan inclusions. A tertiary flake of this same material was also collected from the same context. The tool described here is a secondary flake I with cortex at the proximal end and is complete. No lip is apparent on this flake. It measures 25x19x3 169 I I

It is on this basis that it was placed in this category. If indeed the polish is real, it would have functioned as a perforator. Additional lithic material. in the form of flakes and chips as well as another utilized flake and a projectile point fragment were also collected from Level 1 across the unit. I Specimen 9 (Lot 128 #2 and Lot 129 #1) These two lots are from Unit SB, Level 1 and Unit SB, Level 2, respectively, and represent two I pieces of the same secondary flake. When reconstructed, they measure 32x29x10 millimeters. The material is a gray/tan, fine-grained chert with a dark tan cortex. It is thought to be a non-local material. The cross-section is piano-convex. A lip is present as well as a prominent bulb. The utilization scars I are along the left ventral edge, including the small piece which broke off. There is also another break on the right side of the artifact. A total of 15 pieces of debitage and two projectile points were also collected from these two levels of this unit, which produced more lithic material than any other controlled I excavation. At the same time, it was the largest unit excavated. UNIFACES I Specimen 1 (Lot 177 #2) I This uniface is from the Feature 25 surface collection at a mean elevation of 141.97 meters amsl m. corresponding with Cultural Zone As noted previously, this feature solely consisted oflithic material, of which this was the only tool. It is made from a tan chert flake and is.concave-convex in cross-section. I Primary retouch is apparent only on the bit end, although there are signs of wear or utilization along the right lateral, dorsal edge (Figure 77b). The steep edge angle of approximately 60 degrees is consistent with functional interpretations as scrapers. No indication of polish was observed under the microscope. I This tool measures 21xl7xl7 millimeters. Despite the small size, this tool is complete, although the edge may certainly have been retouched several times to reduce its overall length. ## Page 189

I Specimen 2 (Lot 171 #13) I This tool is from the surface of Feature 15 within Cultural Zone IV. Along with this single tool, 21 pieces of quartzite debitage were also collected. This tool is made of a purplish quartzite with a I reddened area on the ventral surface. It is piano-convex in cross-section. The only retouch is on the bit end which has an angle of roughly 45 degrees. No other signs of modification were noted. It measures 28x21x10 millimeters. Bone collected from this feature included deer and possibly bison. I PROJECTILE POINTS Specimen 1 (Lot 255 #1) I This projectile point as well as the following one were collected from the surface, of Feature 51 during the testing phase (Figure 77c). This feature was mapped at a mean elevation of 141.78 meters I amsl, which places it near the top of Cultural Zone IV. Typed as a Sca/,lom, it was manufactured from a chert material which is red in color, presumably from heat-treating. Prikryl found that the Sca/,lom points he examined from his investigations centered on the Elm Fork were evenly distributed between I quartzite and chert (Prikryl 1990:58). The point is comer notched with a narrow, expanding stem. The base is straight, as are both serrated blade edges. It is bi-convex in cross-section and is estimated to be about 90 percent complete with the extreme tip broken in a snap rather than crushed. It measures I 27x16x4 millimeters. It is likely that this arrow point actually came from a higher elevation as it was situated on a small erosional rill. I Specimen 2 (Lot 155 #2) This is the second projectile point collected from Feature 51 (Figure 77d). It is a Godley made I of a purple quartzite. Measuring 39x22x8mm, this point is essentially complete. One edge is convex while the other edge is straight. Both of these edges are serrated. It has an expanding stem with a convex base. The shoulders are squared and stronger than most Gary types. The base and stem are I lightly ground. It is bi-convex in cross-section. Due to its thickness, it is likely to have been resharpened from its original size. A few reddened areas on this specimen may indicate heat-treatment. Prikryl found that these point types are most commonly made of quartzite {Prikryl 1990:55, 57). This I tool was found a few centimeters from the Sca/,lom described above, but was not in the erosional rill. Instead, it appeared to be freshly eroded from the elevation at which the feature was mapped (see above). Specimen 3 (Lot 245 #1) I This projectile point was collected from the surface of the east slope of the ditch (Figure 77e). It was designated an isolated find as no other cultural material was noted (Figure 12). It was shot in at an I elevation of 141.67 meters amsl. The point was made of a grainy tan chert. The broad stem is broken straight across below the shoulders so the form can not be determined. The remaining portion measures 32x21x8 millimeters. It is bi-convex in cross-section, and thick for its size, again indicating I resharpening. The blade edges are straight and fairly smoothed. The tip is crushed, probably from impact. The shoulders are asymmetrical, perhaps indicating either a Gary type, or a Godley which both share this characteristic. It is at least a Late Archaic type common to this region with the weak shoulders I and barb. I 172 I I

## Page 190

I I Specimen 4 (Lot 243 #1) A single projectile point was the only lithic material collected from the surface of Feature 38, a small concentration of bone which included bison, deer, and rabbit. The feature was mapped at a mean I elevation of 141.64 meters amsl, within Cultural Zone IV. This projectile point was made of a dark tan chert. It is broken in several places so the final form can not be confidently ascertained (Figure 78a). However, it does share characteristics of Late Archaic point types in North-Central Texas. It has a I slightly expanding stem with a rounded base. Neither the stem nor base is ground, but instead, the two sides of the stem are smoothed, perhaps by hafting. The one remaining shoulder is very weak. The tip is fractured and crushed. It is bi-convex in cross-section and measures 39x14x7 millimeters. The body I is very narrow which also suggests resharpening. The breaks on this projectile point include a fracture along the upper half on one edge and a fracture on the other edge which continues from the tip to the top of the stem, obscuring the shoulder area. Both of the lateral edge scars terminate in step fra~res. The I location and type of fracture indicate this tool was damaged on impact rather than from maintenance. It most closely resembles a Palmillas in form. I Specimen 5 (Lot 196 #1) This projectile point was recovered from Feature 9, (within Unit 12, Level 3), mixed with the I mussel shell (Figures 74 and 78c). The exact elevation of the projectile was mapped at 141.59 meters amsl within Cultural Zone IV. It was made from a light gray, very fine-grained chert with a waxy appearance. No other lithic material similar to this was recovered from the investigations at 41DL270, I nor observed in the Bill Young Collection. The projectile point measures 66x37x9 millimeters and is bi convex in cross-section. The extreme tip is broken as well as the end of one of the barbs. It has a narrow expanding stem and a convex base which are very small for the size of the point and the comer notches. The two blade edges are straight and very finely serrated. Types like this (Motley) are common I to Poverty Point and Motley Place in Louisiana and have dates of 1500 B.C. To 500 B.C. They are reported to usually be made of exotic cherts (Turner and Hester 1993:162). I Some interesting observations were made when this artifact was shown to some other archeologists. Glen Goode, of the Texas Department of Transportation, pointed out the small size of the stem and the off-center ridge line indicated this projectile had been reworked due to the original stem having broken I off or that it may have originally been a biface that was reworked into a dart point. Chris Caran, project geomorphologist, said the material was similar to novaculite due to its fine grain and waxy sheen but that it was not as dense. Britt Bousman, at the time, of the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory, tested I it for its fluorescence capabilities which were positive. Although this technique has been used as a test for Edwards chert which also fluoresces, the technique has not been used on other known chert sources to determine if the phenomenon is widespread or specific to a few material types. At any rate, it was I agreed that this is a chert material, not from the immediate area of Denton Creek. The source remains unknown. I This point occurs at close to the same elevation as bison bones from Feature 24 just a few meters to the south on the ditch slope. Also, within about 10 centimeters of this elevation is Feature 38 and Feature 15 which also contained bison bones. Identified bone recovered from the Feature 9 zone and I Unit 12 included deer and turtle. It is interesting the point was found amid mussel shell. A utilized flake and a few pieces of debitage were also associated with the feature. I 173 I I

## Page 192

I I Specimen 6 (Lot 133 #2) This is a barb fragment from a projectile point collected from Unit 8D, Level 1, at a mean elevation of 141.44 meters amsl or within Cultural Zone V. It was made from a fine-grained, light tan I chert. As only the barb and part of the body are left the form can not be determined. The fragment is bi-convex in cross-section and measures 19x22x6 millimeters. The size and barb shape formed from basal notching, are attributes more commonly found on earlier dart points than those generally assigned I to the Late Archaic period in this region. Fauna material identified from this level and the next include primarily turtle, but also deer, rabbit, and bird. I Specimen 7 (Lot 129 #2) This projectile point, of a very coarse tan chert, was recovered from Unit SB, Level 2 (Figure I 78b). It was mapped at a mean elevation of 141.19 within Cultural Zone V. The stem is wide and slightly expanding with both edges ground very smooth. The base is slightly convex and is also ground very smooth. It has weak shoulders and straight blade edges. The cross-section is bi-convex, and it I measures 40x20x8 millimeters. The edges are steeply retouched indicating resharpening. This dart point has been identified as a Yarbrough. A greater number of these types have been found to be made of quartzite (Prikryl 1990:55). I Projectile Point Summary I The cultural periods thought to be represented by the above described projectile points include the Late Prehistoric I (Scallorn); Late Archaic (Godley, a possible Gary or Godley, Motley, Yarbrough, and an unidentified dart point); Middle Archaic (a basal notched barb fragment). The projectile points stratigraphic positions are also consistent with the cultural periods designated for the site. All of the I above lie within the projected 41DL270 Cultural Zone IV, except for the Yarbrough and barb fragment from Unit 8 which is within Cultural Zone V, and also at the deepest elevation. The Scallorn tits at the top of Cultural Zone IV due to its high elevation. However, this Scallorn was also collected with the I Godley point. As these were surface finds, as with all but three points above, their elevation of origin may be wrong. However, as mentioned, the Scallorn was situated on an erosional rill indicating that its point of origin was actually from above or from a later Cultural Zone. I The material types do not exactly follow the projected sequence as presented by Brown (1987d), McGregor (1987) and Prikryl (1990). In this scenario, the Late Archaic is dominated by quartzite with I the ratio of the quartzite to chert narrowing toward the end of the period and into the Late Prehistoric I. By the Late Prehistoric II, chert is once more the dominant material as it was throughout the Paleoindian, Early and Middle Archaic periods. However, these three periods saw a steady, albeit small, I decline in the chert to quartzite ratio through time until chert fell below the average during the Late Archaic. Three of the projectile points are of a non-local chert. The source of the remaining three coarse-grained chert projectile points are thought to occur within at least a few miles of the site in I Ogallala gravels found along the Trinity if not at 41DL270 itself. Only one point was identified as quartzite. I I 175 I I

## Page 193

I QUARTZITE COBBLES I Specimen 1 (Lot 159 #1) This cobble tool was collected from Feature 16 within Unit 10, Level 2 (the cobble was actually I lying on the surface; the slope of the unit included a portion of the present day surface as Level 2). The feature contained a mix of burned sandstone, bone, mussel shell, and lithic material. It measures 68x47x37 millimeters. The cortex is tan while the interior is purplish as displayed by a fresh flake scar. I An old break at one end, however, shows how the interior color can change to a tan color although parts of purple are still present. The cobble is somewhat piano-convex in cross-section with the piano side having an area worn smooth and flat yet not damaging the cortex as the battering has done. The battering I is evident on the intact end as well as on one side. Twelve other specimens of lithic material were collected form this feature and the unit excavated to investigate it. This consisted primariJy of quartzite flakes and chips. I Specimen 2 (Lot 179 #1) I This is a split cobble from the surface collection of Feature 26, a small concentration of burned sandstone within Cultural Zone IV at a mean elevation of 141.66 amsl. This cobble was the only piece of cultural material collected from the feature. It has a dark tan cortex with a purple interior. The I cobble has been split in half, the scars of which are on one end. This end is also crushed and displays step fractures. I Bill Young Collection The following presents a brief description of the Bill Young lithic collection, loaned to the project I through the generosity of Mr. Young. A total of 28 specimens were examined with many of them also illustrated. An emphasis was placed on diagnostic projectile points, flake tools (utilized or retouched, and scrapers), and possible cutting tools such as bifaces. Dan Prikryl had also examined this collection during the NRHP documentation of 41DL270 (Prikryl 1992) and discussed this with the senior authors I as to point and material types. This collection was not included with the above analysis as exact proveniences are unknown. While the collection is basically lacking in debitage, it does provide an assortment of the various formal and informal tool types so far found at the site and serves as an I important adjunct to those few tools collected during the data recovery phase. Without this collection, for example, we would have been unaware that Nocona Plain pottery was present at the site. I The following are arranged according to the same morphological types as those presented above and a select few are illustrated. A microscope was not available at the time this collection was loaned to us so some signs of utilization or wear may have gone unnoticed. The numbering system follows that I of Mr. Young with DNl preceding all subsequent numbers which were assigned by the author. UTILIZED FLAKES I Lot DNl-1. This is a large utilized flake made from a very coarse material with white quartz veins. It measures 58x23x13 millimeters. The utilization scars on this artifact are along the left dorsal edge. I PROJECTILE POINTS Gary I Six Gary points were examined from the Bill Young collection. They have triangular bodies and contracting stems with convex bases. None of the bases have been ground. Shoulder shape varies from I very weak to strong, but without barbs. In addition, five of the shoulders are asymmetrical. While this may be an aspect of manufacturing, it may also signify resharpening or differential use of the blade edges. The blade edges are straight and the shoulders are squared. The base is concave or split. The right lateral edge in particular displays retouch or resharpening flake scars. The tip is squared off.
