## Page 201

I Discussion or Lithic Analysis I As the collection essentially consists of flakes, with a small quantity of formal and informal tools, little could be deduced about particular activities through time other than a small degree of manufacture and resharpening. However, when the percentage of formal and informal tool types is compared with I the overall total of lithic material collected, or with the amount of flakes, their number corresponds well with the expected average ratio for tools versus debitage. I The small size of the utilized flakes in most cases is due to the fact they are broken along one or more edges. Two essentially complete specimens however, Lots 174 and 128/129, are quite a bit larger than the rest of the inventory and imply that at least some of the utilized flakes were at least this size. I The Motley point, a type commonly found in Louisiana and east Texas, was recovered at the same elevation and cultural zone (IV) as the bison bone found with Feature 24 just a few meters to the south, I as well as Features 15 and 38 to the north. The time period coincides with the postulated warmer period which also saw a movement of people from the east to hunt bison (Brown 1989). Identified bone recovered from this feature within Unit 12 included deer and turtle. The fact this point was found amid I mussel shell is very interesting. A utilized flake and a small amount of debitage were also recovered. It is assumed that the procurement and processing of mussel shells did not include a lithic tool kit, so the utilized flake and projectile point can be inferred as being associated with the procurement of bison or deer. Tests should be conducted to determine the necessity and use wear of cutting mussels from their I shell after baking or roasting. However, Feature 9 is the only mussel shell feature with a utilized flake found in apparent association. I The asymmetry of many Gary points is interesting and may be a reflection of hafting or differential use of the blades. In fact, some researchers have suggested that some Gary projectile points may have functioned as knives (Galm 1978; Galm and Flyn 1978 - cited in Prikryl 1990:55), while others suggest I a multi-purpose function at least for those Gary points subtyped as Camden variety which are dated at 1700-1200 B.P. (Perttula and Skiles 1988:75-77). The authors, basing these interpretations on lithic artifacts recovered from 41RA65, an Early Ceramic -early Caddoan site, note the presence of asymmetry I of the blades, serration of the edges, and beveling as indicators that these tools were continually resharpened while still hafted. In addition, they surmise cutting and scraping tasks were performed based on the presence of crushing and step fractures on the lateral edges. Furthermore, impact fractures on the I tips indicate their use as projectiles. These multi-purpose Gary points were all made from Ogallala derived gravels of either chert or quartzite (Perttula and Skiles 1988:75-77). I It would be interesting to see if there is a correlation between function, i.e., cutting, scraping, projectiles, etc., and either the weak or prominent shoulder on these asymmetrical types. It was found that the two Godley points as well as an unidentified specimen also share the attributes of alternate I beveled edges and asymmetry of the shoulders. The following general statements can be made in regard to the lithic analysis. I 1) In terms of correlating lithic material types with chronological markers the analysis was basically successful. However, as previously noted, the results differ from those by other researchers in the North-Central Texas area (McGregor 1987; Prikryl 1990). This may be due largely to non I conforming radiocarbon dates collected from 41DL270. Both of the above researchers basically explain the changing ratios of preferred chert versus quartzite as the dominant material type as I 184 I I


![Image 1 - Page 201](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p201_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 201*


---

## Page 202

I results of decreased mobility due to population increases and corresponding shrinking territory I bases. Whether this is the case at 41DL270 is not fully understood. Chert is the dominant material in the later part of the Late Archaic and quartzite is most prevalent in the cultural zones thought to correspond with the earlier part of the Late Archaic. Perhaps our distribution is more I of a product of environmental changes in the relative availability of large game such as deer versus lesser products such as mussel and other aquatic resources. How these environmental changes affected population movement is not well understood. I 2) We were able to determine a few activity areas such as Feature 25, and perhaps Feature 15. However, these features may simply represent discard dumps as opposed to actual activity areas. I More utilized flakes were in lower parts of Cultural Zones IV and in Cultural Zone V, roughly 2500 to 3400 years ago, or during the early part of the Late Archaic. It is during this period and earlier ones that fewer mussel shell concentrations are found, suggesting a greater availability of I (or interest in) other animal products, particularly mammals which would appear to require a more extensive lithic tool kit for procurement as well as processing. Conversely, fewer lithic materials are associated with the upper two cultural zones in which mussel shell appears to be a major I subsistence activity. As discussed previously, it is thought that few if any lithic tools would be required for the procurement, processing, and eating of mussel. I 3) The study of procurement areas and exchange or interactions among prehistoric peoples was a bit ambitious for a project of this nature. Little data was recovered which would effectively address either of these questions. However, it is reasonable to assume from the predominance of soft hammer or pressure flaking that initial reduction was elsewhere, indicating procurement areas off I site. Exactly where this was is unknown but it could have been as close as 1.5 kilometers away at 41DL326. I 4) The assumption that cores and more debitage indicates a nearby material source, while more finished tools and resharpening flakes indicates a source further away (or off site) was addressed in looking at the materials from the site. No cores were recovered from the excavations. Of the I 138 lithic specimens collected, 118 were debitage. The debitage was strongly biased toward tertiary specimens, most very small. The presence of more lips and crushed platforms also suggests that initial reduction was elsewhere as stated above. There were actually few formal tools I recovered from the data recovery phase. However, the Bill Young collection does illustrate that a significant amount were present and the soft hammer, pressure flaking indicates a more final reduction or resharpening of tools. In addition, many of the tools examined were resharpened I although flakes could not be matched with formal tools, especially with the Bill Young collection. In short, it is suggested that the redeposited gravels of the Ogallala which were the source of most I of the lithic artifacts recovered from 41DL270 were not exposed prehistorically at the site itself. Rather, they were gathered and initially reduced elsewhere, the location of which is unknown. Some models of procurement, however, suggest that the source would probably not be very far away (Brown 1987d). I Additionally, the lithic resources were probably gathered along with other activities such as food procurement. The vast majority of lithic debitage as well as tools was recovered from the zones with the highest percentage and biomass of deer and other mammal. Few were recovered from those zones in I which mussels were more heavily depended upon. The implications of environmental or population stress in regard to subsistence is addressed in the final two chapters of this volume. I 185 I I

I MISCELLANEOUS NON-LITIDC ARTIFACTS I The following five artifacts were manufactured from bone and freshwater mussel shell. They are illustrated to scale. I Bone FISIIllOOK I This is a carved bone fish hook from Unit 1, Level 4, within Cultural Zone II at a mean elevation of 142.13 meters amsl (Figure 83a). The stem is broken; the remaining portion measures 13x7x2.5 I millimeters. Of note, fish and turtle bone were recovered from fine screening in levels other than Level 4. The fish hook was carved from mammal bone and apparently of too large a size for the minnow size taxa recovered from this unit (see "Vertebrate Faunal Remains from 41DL270", this volume). I CARVED BONE I This is a mammal bone which has been carved into a conical shape (Figure 83b). It measures 17x7 millimeters. Where not damaged, the bone has a polished appearance. It was recovered from Unit 14, Level 4, or within Cultural Zone IV at a mean elevation of 141.60 meters amsl. No other cultural materials were found in this level. I Shell I DRILLED MUSSEL SHELL This shell artifact has a small hole at the umbo on the left valve (Figure 83c). The hole is not I perfectly cylindrical and appears larger on the interior than the exterior. The mussel species is identified as a Lompsilis teres. It was recovered from the Feature 9 mussel shell concentration in Unit 12, which places it within Cultural Zone IV. I A Quadrula mortoni shell with a cylindrical hole in the umbo was recovered from Feature 29, in Unit 11 (Figure 83d). Its context places it within Cultural Zone II. The hole was drilled on the left I valve. The beak has also been flattened. NOTCHED SHELL I This shell was also recovered from Feature 29, within Cultural Zone II. It is aLampsilis teres with two notches carved on the posterior edge of what appears to be the left valve (Figure 83e). I It is interesting that two of the three shell artifacts recognized in the collection were recovered from the Feature 29. This may indicate a small activity area close by to the discard pile represented by the I feature. These shell artifacts appear to have been broken prehistorically and may have been discarded because of that. Also of interest are three shells recovered from Feature 3 in Cultural Zone I that Neck (this I volume) describes as being exotic (Plectomerus dombeyanus) to the 41DL270 area as they are not normally found this far up the Trinity. ## Page 206

I VERTEBRATE FAUNAL REMAINS FROM 41DL270 I Marilyn A. Masson INTRODUCTION I A sample of 1121 animal bones was recovered archeologically from excavation units, backhoe trenches, and surface features at 41DL270. Deer was the most frequent taxon identified (N=251), I although bison (N= 164), turtle (N=230), and bird (N=57) were also numerous in the assemblage (Table 6). Bison was recovered from a limited number of contexts, primarily from a single backhoe trench (N = 125) and three features on the site's surface (N = 39). In contrast, deer was recovered from 55 I contexts examined, a pattern which reflects its importance as a dietary staple in this region over time. Turtle, found in 20 separate contexts, represents a consistently-exploited taxon of lesser significance due to their proportionately smaller mass and edible meat weight. The role of turtle in the Denton Creek I prehistoric diet is reflected in Table 6, which indicates that they make up only 3.6 percent of the entire sample by bone weight, but 20.5 percent of the sample by count. Rabbit (primarily cottontail) and bird (primarily turkey) were found in limited numbers of contexts. Frequencies of carnivores, small rodents, I and snakes are very low in the assemblage. Table 6. Taxa Recovered from Denton Creek (41DL270). I Count % Weight % Bison 164 14.6 475.9 45.4 I Deer 251 22.4 435.7 41.6 Mammal 105 9.4 28.1 2.7 I Carnivores 2 0.2 7.1 0.7 Rabbit 9 0.8 3.4 0.3 I Rodents 4 0.4 0.4 0.0 Bird 57 5.1 20.1 1.9 I Turtle 230 20.5 38.1 3.6 Snake 1 0.1 0.1 0.0 I Vertebrata 298 26.6 39.6 3.8 TOTAL 1121 100 1048.5 100 I I Most of the deposits at 41DL270 are believed to be the remains of short-term episodes (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). Thus, while faunal remains within broad chronological time I periods are not likely to be contemporaneous, they can be used to make general comparisons of the role of hunting in subsistence patterns of this region over time. I 189 I I

## Page 207

I METHODS I The bone sample examined here from 41DL270 represents the fraction collected in ¼" hardware cloth screens. The use of samples from this screen size have been criticized for its bias toward larger taxa (Casteel 1972; Grayson 1984; DeMarcay and Steele 1986; Shaffer 1992) in archeological settings I that are comprised primarily of small taxa such as fish, snake, or rodents. While small bone fragments are observed in 1/16" flotation samples from Denton Creek, the quantity of small animal bone is minimal. The analysis of ¼" samples is thus considered to provide a reliable and representative view of prehistoric I diet in North-Central Texas, where large and medium-sized game were the primary foci of hunting activities. I Sorting and identification of the sample was performed in the laboratory of Hicks & Company, using comparative collections of this facility and those of the Texas Archeological Research Laboratory and Vertebrate Paleontology Laboratory of the University of Texas at Austin. Bone from each unit was I sorted according to taxon and element, sided and aged if possible, and examined for evidence of burning, cutting, gnawing, or other modifications. Each element or set of fragments was then weighed and entered directly into spreadsheets created in @Quattro Pro (Borland, Inc.). Deer teeth were aged where possible, I using a photographic and descriptive guide published by the Texas Department of Parks and Wildlife (Ramsey and Shult 1990) and comparative specimens of known age. I The use of different methods of quantification are necessary in zooarcheology to provide a balanced perspective on relative frequencies of bone fragments of different taxa. Different physical properties and cultural practices affect the fragmentation of species of different sizes. Variables considered in this analysis include bone count, bone weight, biomass, and the minimum number of individuals (MNI). I Each measurement has its own bias that is balanced by considering the alternative methods of quantification. Bone count can inflate the proportions of species that have a high degree of fragmentation or a high number of elements. Bone weights can exaggerate the role of larger taxa, but provides an I important balance for intertaxon variability in bone fragmentation. For example, unidentifiable fragments can make up 60-70 percent of a sample according to count, but often represent less than 5 percent of a sample by weight. Biomass is calculated from taxa-specific allometric formulas derived from bone I weight, developed from live specimens over the past 15 years (Casteel 1978; Wing and Brown 1979; Reitz and Cordier 1983; Reitz and Scarry 1985; Reitz et al. 1987; Prange et al. 1979). The use of biomass formulae is to discern a more proportionate measurement of the contribution of each taxa to the I diet. MNI is calculated using the maximum number of either a singly-occurring element or paired bilaterally symmetric elements (also matched for age and size) present for each taxon in a sample. However, the use of MNI must be tempered with caution, as this measurement is highly susceptible to I sampling (Grayson 1978, 1981). Culturally-patterned distribution of carcass elements across an archeological site in specialized localities of butchering or consumption can remove or concentrate anatomical proportions. The archeological sampling of such activity areas could result in over I representation and underenumeration of particular taxon in MNI assessments. Tables and graphs were created for each general cultural horizon. Percentages of numerical I frequencies and weights were calculated by programming the spreadsheets, which also included the formulae through which biomass is estimated from bone weight. The use of all three measurements (counts, weight, and biomass) allows a more balanced quantification of an assemblage than any single I variable used in isolation. I 190 I I

## Page 208

I I PREVIOUS RESEARCH I A broad range of animal resources would have been available from the Late Archaic through Late Prehistoric periods in the Denton Creek area. Located in the Texan biotic province (Blair 1950), several distinct ecological habitats meet in this part of North-Central Texas. These include the Blackland Prairie, the Grand Prairie, the Brushy Prairie, and the Eastern Cross Timbers. These environmental zones are I currently home to a long list of mammals, birds, turtles, reptiles, and fish. Many of these species have been recovered from archeological sites, indicating their presence and exploitation by prehistoric inhabitants of the area. I Primary taxa that have been identified from sites in the Denton Creek area include white-tailed deer, bison, pronghorn, cottontail rabbit, black-tailed jackrabbit, opossum, raccoon, fox squirrel, box and I pond turtles, and turkey. A range of smaller mammals, rodents, and reptiles are also reported (Martin 1988; Brown 1989). Deer, however, appear to have been singularly important in the diet throughout the Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods of this region (Brown 1989:219). Brown (1989:211) points out that I deer provide more than ten times the meat than any other taxon that was regularly available in the region. Bison, sporadically present, would have far exceeded the contribution of deer; however, this resource is lacking from many archeological components of this area. Antelope are also infrequently found among I North-Central Texas faunal assemblages such as the McDonald site, where two fragments were found (Brown 1989:216). In comparing the relative frequency of rabbits among North-Central Texas archeological assemblages to their frequency in the natural habitat of the Aquilla Lake area, Brown I (1989:224) suggests that this taxon may have been underutilized due to a specialization in deer exploitation (1989:220). Such specialization may have been permitted by populations living at subsistence levels well below the carrying capacity (Brown 1989:230). I Martin et al. (1988:331) discuss the high level of productivity of the Timber Creek environment compared to the prairie and creek margins. They outline the evidence for a seasonal relationship between I ripening acorns, wildlife that consume them, and human hunting patterns. In this model, the abundance of food causes the animals to aggregate, providing opportunities for productive hunting forays and multiple kills. An additional benefit is the fact that acorn season, in the fall, is the time when animals sucb as deer are fattest (Martin et al. 1988:334; Smith 1975: 137). This attractive situation is I compounded by the evidence for human reliance on acorn resources as well (Fritz 1985; Creel 1986). In a catchment area analysis performed for Aquilla Lake environments of Bottomland and Cross Timbers forest, Brown (1989:209) determined that over ten billion kilocalories of botanical resources and over 137 I million kilocalories of animal resources were available. Brown notes that the majority of the plant contribution is provided by acorns in the Cross Timbers area. I Fauna distinct to prairie environments appear to have made less of a contribution to the diet in North-Central Texas than those of the oak forests. The primary taxa from these areas that are reported in significant numbers are jackrabbits and prairie chickens, according to Martin (1988:332). Bison and I pronghorn do not appear to have been present in sufficient numbers in this region to have been regularly exploited, as these authors observe. It appears from the archeological record that bison were not plentiful in this area until around A.D. 1200 (Lynott 1979:95; Martin 1988:332). I Aquatic species that have been found at archeological sites are primarily fish and reptiles (mainly turtles). Making a comparison to Mississippian subsistence models, Martin (1988:332) suggests that I aquatic animals may have supplemented summer diets, when deer populations were more dispersed. I 191

## Page 209

I I Bone preservation at 41DL270 has been described as superior to that observed at other sites on Denton Creek (Prikryl 1992; Anthony et al. 1992:21). Among the contexts examined, little differentiation is observed in the degree of bone preservation. Taphonomic processes are not thought to I have resulted in the destruction of faunal remains from different time periods. Chronological comparisons among zones of 41DL270 are therefore considered valid. I RESULTS The most important variable in interpreting the results of faunal remains from archeological sites I is the consideration of context. The means by which bone samples are grouped from spatially separate units and levels into chronostratigraphic aggregates critically influences the reliability of synchronic and diachronic dietary reconstruction (Grayson 1984). In the presentation of results below, eight zones containing faunal bone are described (zones I, II, III, IV, V, VI, VIII, and IX) in chronological order. I The zones are first examined as composite samples, followed by an assessment of faunal remains found within features associated with each zone. For a broader perspective, the zones are subsequently grouped into composite samples falling within the Middle Archaic, Late Archaic, and Late Prehistoric periods of I North-Central Texas prehistory. The temporal placement of faunal materials into zones is based on interpretations of the stratigraphy of 41DL270 provided by David Brown and Dana Anthony (personal communication August 1993, and "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). I Cultural Zone IX I A single faunal bone was recovered from 41DL270 at an elevation associated with Zone 9 (Table 7), which is thought to fall within Prikryl's (1990) North-Central Texas Middle Archaic period. It is a 1 fragment of the long bone diaphysis of a deer (Table 8). It is notable that this staple of the prehistoric diet in Denton Creek prehistory is the only element present in the earliest record at the site. However, it is unfortunate that the sample size is not larger to allow more valid comparisons from this period. I Table 7. I Table 8. Faunal Skeletal Elements from Zo11es at 41DL270. I TAXA ELEMENT SIDE N I Turkey unidentified 1 Distal phalange 1 I Bird diaph. frgs. 23 five burned Unidentified unidentified 5 I vm Cultural Zone I Faunal bone from Zone VIII was exclusively limited to bison, recovered from a single context located in a backhoe trench (Trench-MS). Zone VIII is believed to date to the end of the Middle Archaic. The remains of what is believed to be a single individual are represented in 125 fragments (Table 7). I The number of fragments is inflated by a high incidence of recovery-related breakage of a long bone, probably the metacarpal that remains partially intact. The bison may have been a female, as the size of the elements appear small. Skeletal elements recovered include a proximal and distal right metacarpal, I a left calcaneus, a sesamoid, and a carpal (Table 8). These remains indicate that bison was present in the Denton Creek area at the end of the Middle Archaic. As no other Zone vm contexts with faunal bone were excavated, the relative significance of bison compared with other taxa at 41DL270 is I impossible to determine. It is perhaps indicative, however, that no other taxa were recovered from the backhoe trench where the bison was found. This pattern might suggest a specialized focus on bison at this time. I Cultural Zone VI I Four bone fragments were recovered from Zone VI. These fragments were identified as segments of the long bone diaphysis of a medium-sized bird (Tables 7 and 8). Zone VI at 41DL270 is dated to the beginning of the Late Archaic period (see" Archeological Synthesis", this volume). As faunal remains from this zone are limited to a single context and a small sample, little information can be discerned about I strategies of animal exploitation during this time. Cultural Zone V I The faunal remains found within Cultural Zone V included 222 fragments. Zone Vis associated with the early part of the Late Archaic in the estimated 41DL270 chronology (see "Archeological I Synthesis", this volume). In contexts from this zone, a diverse range of species is identified, including deer, rabbit, bird, and turtle (Table 7). A single small rodent (cotton rat) element was also found. Deer represents the majority of the sample according to weight and biomass, particularly when the probability I is considered that fragments classified as "mammal" are actually those of deer. However, turtle fragments are far more numerous in the sample than those of deer and mammal. Turtle also forms a significant portion of the sample by weight (44.6%) and biomass (31.6%). Compared to other zones, I the importance of turtle relative to deer in Zone V is notable. This increase in the use of an aquatic resource has been previously suggested to indicate a summer occupation of sites in North-Central Texas I 200 I I

## Page 218

I I (Martin 1988:332). Alternatively, this use of turtle, combined with the presence of rabbit and bird in the sample, may represent a less specialized and more diversified subsistence strategy. Such a strategy could be the result of changing environmental factors that resulted in an increase in the abundance of aquatic species available for exploitation. I Cultural Zone IV I The largest sample of faunal remains from 41DL270 zones is found in Zone IV, totalling 515 fragments. Zone IV is believed to date to the early half of the Late Archaic (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). The greatest range of species is also found from contexts within Zone IV I (Table 7). Identified taxa include bison (N = 39), deer (N = 168), raccoon (N = 1), cottontail rabbit (N=7), small rodent (N= 1), unidentified mammal (N=57), turtle (N= 16), turkey (N=9), and bird (N = 8). In this zone, deer represent the vast majority of the sample according to bone weight (72.3 %) I and biomass (67 .3 %) . Bison represent the second most significant taxon in the sample, but only compose 10.2 percent of the sample weight and 11.5 percent of the biomass. Turtle, a taxon of secondary significance in samples from other 41DL270 zones, is present in markedly low frequency (N = 16) in I Zone V. Turtle represents only 3.1 percent of the bone count and even less of the sample weight (0.8%) and biomass (1.1 %). These low figures underscore the significance of deer in Zone V. I Bison elements in Zone IV are limited to long bone diaphyseal fragments (Table 8), identified by their pronounced cortical thickness. The skeletal elements of deer are well represented from all areas of the anatomy in Zone IV. Tooth, jaw, crania, pelvis, scapula, rib, vertebra, phalange, metacarpal, I metatarsal, carpal, tarsal, patella, tibia, ulna, radius, and humerus fragments were identified in addition to numerous fragments of long bone diaphysis and epiphyseal cancellous bone. The remains of at least three animals are present in the sample, as indicated by deer teeth of different ages found in Features 16, I 27, and 37 (Table 9). Only two animals are indicated by the postcranial remains, which include a right and left calcaneus of unmatched size and two left proximal radii. This full representation of the deer skeleton suggests that the entire carcass was transported to this location for consumption. However, certain elements may have been removed for grease extraction. I Identified elements of the raccoon are limited to a single tooth. Rabbit and rodent elements include long bone diaphyses, an acetabulum, and a distal radius, which amount to an MNI of one individual. I Turkey leg fragments are present. The low numbers of elements from these small taxa make it impossible to discern patterns of carcass distribution in the archeological record. Concentrations of animal bones found in features within Zone IV are assessed below. I Cultural features at 41DL270 include concentrations of fire-cracked sandstone, mussel shell, or burned clay. Faunal bone, including deer, bison, and bird was observed in some of these features during I a previous survey of the site (Anthony et al. 1992: 19). Features remains were collected either directly from the site's surface or in formal excavation units. Sixteen features from the mitigation phase contained faunal bone, as listed in Table 10. Eight of these are associated with Zone 4. In Zone IV, two clusters I of features are observed according to elevation, described below. Features 9, 24, and 38 were mapped in at depths ranging from 141.54 to 141.64 meters amsl, and I thus fall within Zone IV. Feature 24 was a concentration of sandstone, mussel shell, lithic flakes, and 11 bone fragments (Table 10). Among the faunal remains, seven fragments of bison and four fragments of deer bone are identified (Table 11). All are from long bone diaphyses of these animals (Table 12). At this same depth range (141.62 meters amsl), Feature 9 was recorded in Unit 12. Feature 9 was a 201 I I

## Page 226

I I Features 15, 16, 27, and 37 are also associated with Zone IV, as they were found at a depth range of 141.65-141.73 meters amsl. Features 15, 27 and 37 were recovered from the site's surface, while I Feature 16 was recovered in Unit 10 (fable 10). Feature 15 consisted of burned sandstone, mussel shell, and 30 bone fragments that were identified exclusively as bison and deer. Bison remains were from fragmented long bone articular ends and deer was represented by a proximal tibia (fables 11, 12). I Feature 27 was composed of burned sandstone, mussel shell, and clay. One piece of bone was found in Feature 27 and identified as deer. Feature 37, a concentration of sandstone, mussel, lithics, and bone, contained deer and rabbit bone only (fable 11). In addition to long bone fragments (including a proximal I radius), teeth and vertebra fragments of deer were identified (fable 12). Rabbit elements were limited to limbs. No burned bone was observed in these features. Feature 16 was a concentration of mussel and burned sandstone from which 106 bone fragments were recovered. This is the most bone recovered from I any of the features at 41DL270. The bone was almost exclusively identified as deer with the exception of a raccoon jaw and tooth. The deer skeleton is well represented in this feature (fable 12), as portions of the front and rear limbs (left), pelvis (right), ribs, and vertebra were found. I Features 15, 16, 27, and 37 were thought in the field to be possibly associated with the same occupation of 41DL270 (Dana Anthony, personal communication, August 1993). All four features contained burned or unburned sandstone and mussel shell along with the bone (fable 10). However, the I faunal remains from these features suggest that each represents a different individual deer, and probably, a separate event. At least three individuals are indicated by the dentition, which identifies deer of three different ages including 2-2½ years, 3 years, and 5-5½ years (fable 9). The presence of the 3 year old I suggests that at least one of these events took place in the late spring/early summer (around May/June), as this age represents an even interval from the birthing season at this time. The individuals aged on the half-year indicate a late autumn/early winter occupation of the site. The identification of different animals I in the dentition is reiterated to a lesser degree in the postcranial remains, as two of the features (Features 37 and 16) contained left proximal radii (fable 12). The presence of deer teeth in three of four features containing faunal bone suggests a functional similarity among the features. Butchering or processing I practices which processed the cranium along with postcranial portions are implied in all three areas. Features 16 and 37 are of like composition in that they contain axial elements (vertebrae and ribs) along with crania and long bones. These assemblages probably reflect the processing of an entire animal, I perhaps the singular result of a hunt. The processing of other taxa in these areas is also indicated by the presence of a rabbit in Feature 37 and a raccoon in Feature 16. Feature 15 is slightly different in containing the remains of two large game animals, deer and bison. The presence of bison in only one I of these four features may reflect sporadic and fortuitous opportunities for the exploitation of this resource. In contrast, two of three slightly earlier Zone IV features (9, 24, and 38) contained bison. Perhaps the decreased incidence of bison from the earlier feature cluster to the later feature cluster of I Zone IV is reflecting a genuine decrease in the significance and availability of bison during this portion of the Late Archaic. I Remains from Feature 21 were collected from the site's surface at a depth of 141.79 meters amsl. It thus falls toward the end of chronological unit Cultural Zone IV at 41DL270. Deer and bird bone were found in Feature 21 (N =20) (fable 11). Identified deer elements were from the front and back left legs, I including a calcaneus, metacarpal, and ulna (fable 12). The bird bone was a limb fragment. Bison is notably absent. The presence of bison in Zone IV corresponds well with previous reconstructions of environmental I and cultural prehistory in this region. During this period, climatic warming trends are believed to have encouraged the growth of shortgrass vegetation that attracted bison into this region from 2500 B.C. to I 209 I

## Page 227

I I A.O. 500 (Dillehay 1974). It is significant that although bison were present in the Denton Creek area during this time, a specialized focus on deer appears to have eclipsed the exploitation of bison. It is possible that bison were simply not numerous enough to replace deer as a dietary staple. At other sites I in the North-Central Texas area, an abundance of deer in the archeological record has been interpreted as an indication of an autumn occupation as deer tend to aggregate in herds during this season (Martin 1988:332). However, at 41DL270, both autumn and spring seasons are indicated by the eruption and I wear patterns of dentition. At Denton Creek, the preference for deer may have characterized the later Late Archaic adaptation on a year-round basis. I Cultural Zone III Faunal remains from Cultural Zone ill included 67 fragments. Zone ill correlates with the North I Central Texas Late Archaic according to radiocarbon dates from 41DL270 (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). Identified taxa include deer (N=31), turtle (N=19), a carnivore (N=l), and a small rodent. Deer was the most significant taxon according to weight (77.9%) and biomass (75. %), I followed by turtle, which forms around 10 percent of the sample by these measurements (Table 7). The lack of species diversity in the sample is notable, and suggests that a continuation of specialization in deer hunting from the previous phase may have occurred. I Deer elements identified included jaw and tooth fragments, an astragalus, sesamoid, phalange, and long bone diaphysis fragments (Table 8). A vertebral element identified as mammal may also be that of a deer. Two individuals are indicated by the presence of two left jaws. I Features 10, 35, and 39 were situated at a depth range of 141.95-142.05 meters amsl, and thus fall within Zone ill. Material from Feature 39 was collected from the site surface, while Features 10 and I 35 were recovered in Unit 13. Feature 39 was a concentration of 17 bones identified as deer and turtle (Table 11). Deer elements are from the left side of the body and include an astragalus, teeth, and vertebra (Table 12). The deer from this feature was aged at 12-15 months (Table 9). This age indicates I a summer occupation at the time Feature 39 was deposited. Feature 10 consisted of mussel shell associated with 22 bone fragments. Deer, turtle, and a carnivore were identified in this sample (Table 11). Deer elements were limited to jaw and tooth fragments of a 2½ or 3 year old (Tables 9, 12). The I carnivore fragment was also a portion of a jaw. Feature 35 was a concentration of burned sandstone, clay, and a single deer phalange. I Cultural Zone II Sixty-six bone fragments were recovered from Cultural Zone II. Zone 2 is thought to represent I the terminal Late Archaic at 41DL270 (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). Identified remains are almost exclusively limited to deer (N = 16) and turtle (N =29). Although turtle fragments are more numerous, deer represents a little over twice the amount of bone weight (57.4%) and biomass (57.3%) I of the sample (Table 7). A single snake vertebra was also present in the sample. Features 29 and 36 were excavated from Unit 11 at depths of 142.27 and 142.23 meters amsl, respectively. They thus fall within Zone II. Feature 29 consisted of mussel shell and 38 bone fragments. I Bone was identified as that of deer, large mammal (probably deer), and turtle (Table 11). The deer skeletal elements found within Feature 29, though few, are highly informative (Table 12). The skull (an incisor), vertebral column (probably deer), and long bone diaphyses represent diverse areas of the I skeleton. Two individuals are represented, as a fetal/newborn distal humerus is found in the sample along 210 I I

## Page 228

I I with the remains of at least one adult deer. The adult may have been the mother of the fawn. The presence of fetal/newborn remains identifies the season of use of Feature 29 as the birthing season, I around late May or early June. Feature 36 was a concentration of burned sandstone, six bone fragments, and mussel shell. The faunal bone is identified as the ribs of a deer. I The emphasis on deer and turtle in the Zone II faunal sample is similar to that described above for Zone m, although Zone II shows an increase in the percentage of turtle. Such a pattern suggests a continuation in the specialization of deer exploitation. It is possible that the small sample size of Zones I II and ill is responsible for the decrease in taxa diversity in these levels. However, this suspicion is contradicted by the fact that even smaller sample sizes within Zone I described below do indicate the presence of a diverse range of taxa. I Cultural Zone I - Terrace I Cultural Zone I, which dates to the Late Prehistoric at 41DL270 (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume), has a total of 58 faunal bone fragments within its levels. In this sample, deer (N =4), cottontail rabbit (N= 1), cotton rat (N= 1), turkey (N= 1, plus nine identified as "bird"), and turtle I (N = 15) are identified (fable 7). Although deer makes up about 30 percent of the sample weight (27. 8 %) and biomass (29%), bones identified as turkey, bird, and turtle amount to the majority of weight (48.9%) and biomass (45.3%) when combined. This increase in the importance of smaller taxa and diversity in game exploitation stands in contrast to the patterns described for Late Archaic Cultural Zones 2, 3, and I 4. Perhaps this earlier specialization in deer hunting was no longer possible due to changes in human or deer demography. Alternatively, another food source could have taken precedence. Martin (1988) describes the significance of maize horticulture in North-Central Texas during the Late Prehistoric period. I Increased reliance on a staple crop may have resulted in a decrease in hunting as observed in Zone I at 41DL270. I Feature 3 was recovered from Unit 3 at a depth of 142.51 meters amsl. This depth places it within Zone I. This is the only excavated feature with faunal remains associated with this late period on the terrace. The feature consisted of mussel shell and two bone fragments. The bone is identified as rabbit I limbs (Tables 11, 12). Cultural Zone I - Floodplain I Thirty-five bone fragments were found in Unit 9, which has been tentatively placed with the youngest cultural materials at the site - Zone I (fable 7). They were at elevations from 139.15 meters I amsl and below, in the gully between the ditch and Denton Creek. Since the exact stratigraphic association between this material and that from the Zone I in the terrace are not clear, this material is maintained separate in the discussion. The bones recovered from these contexts include deer (N =4), I cottontail (N-1), bird (N=23), turkey (N=2), and five unidentified fragments. The presence of high numbers of rabbit, bird, and turkey and the relatively low numbers of deer support the association with Cultural Zone I, which exhibits this type of species diversity and a relatively lower emphasis on deer. I Features 33 and 28 were recovered from Unit 9 at depths within 10 centimeters of each other (139.05 meters amsl to 139.15 meters amsl, Table 10). They both contained charcoal and bone, and clay was found within Feature 33. Only four bone fragments were found within these features, three of which I were mud turtle (Kinosternidae) located in Feature 33. The other bone fragment, identified as turtle, was I 211 I

I I located in Feature 28. The bone was not burned. Its presence in low numbers in the features implies that turtle was consumed in this vicinity, but intensive or specialized processing is not reflected. I Samples from 1/16" Screen Fraction Faunal bone was examined from 1/16" mesh flotation samples from Unit 1 of 41DL270. As I discussed previously, ¼" samples tend to over-represent larger taxa in the archeological record, and the examination of bone from smaller screen sizes can supplement 1/4 11 analysis to determine the nature and extent of small animal exploitation. Fine-screened samples from Unit 1 contained primarily fragments I of turtle bone, although fish, rodents, and birds are also present (fable 13). Fish include minnow and catfish. Variability is observed among the three levels of Unit 1 examined. Levels 2 and 3 (Cultural Zone Il) had many pieces of burned and unburned turtle carapace and plastron, while in marked contrast, I turtle is almost entirely lacking in the Level 5 sample (Zone ill). The Level 5 sample had bird phalanges, snake vertebrae, rodent molars, and a minnow vertebra and spine. These zones represent the end of the Late Archaic period at 41DL270 (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume). The increase in turtle I exploitation from Zone m to Zone Il reflected in these 1/16" samples parallels patterns described above for the ¼" samples. This correlation supports the accuracy of the ¼ 11 results. I Yates (1987:Table 43) recovered the remains of small taxa from 1/1611 screen samples at 41CO141. Among the identified species in this study were cotton rat, prairie vole, minnow, shad, and tiger salamander that were not found in samples from larger fractions. I The preliminary examination of 1/16" screen samples of faunal bone from 41DL270 suggests that a minor exploitation of small taxa such as minnows and snakes may not be reflected in the ¼ 11 samples. In contrast, the role of turtle appears to be well represented in the larger screen size samples. Further I 1/16" analysis may be warranted at a future date to provide comparative information for the Unit 1 samples examined here. However, the overall number of bone fragments in the flotation samples examined to date is low. This observation for Unit 1, if it holds true for other contexts at the site, I suggests that assessments of diversity in taxa exploitation based on ¼" samples may be reasonably accurate. The reliability of the ¼" samples is further supported by the fact that with the exception of minnows and small catfish, all of the taxa present in the fine screen samples are also recovered in the ¼ 11 I samples. Particularly, smaller taxa including turtles, birds, and rabbits are well represented in the larger screen size samples. The materials observed in both 1/16" and ¼" screen samples examined from 41DL270, thus suggest that a regular use of aquatic creek resources formed a minor element of the I Denton Creek diet. Burned Bone I The degree to which bone is burned in an archeological context may reflect patterns of animal product preparation and consumption. However, it is difficult to demonstrate a simple correlation I between burned bone and form of preparation such as roasting as fragments can be burned through a number of circumstances. The processing of animal products in activities such as butchering, cooking, or bone grease rendering in the proximity of hearths can often result in pockets of highly fragmented I burned bone (Binford 1978; Masson and Holderby, in press). In this sense, the quantification of burned bone from an archeological site is a reflection of the intensity of human processing activity and the spatial relationships of carcass preparation, consumption, and/or production. While stages of animal processing can take place in spatially discrete areas, it appears that often they do not in prehistoric and ethnographic I hunter-gatherer contexts. This variable, perhaps above all others, may be responsible for the degree of 212 I I

I In the faunal sample from 41DL270, only 47 bones (or 0.8% of the total) were burned. This percentage is low compared to others reported from sites in North-Central Texas, as 8 percent of the bone sample at Ray Roberts was burned (Yates 1987). The low frequency at 41DL270 suggests a lack of I intensive processing (in any period). Even in samples from features at Denton Creek in which burned sandstone is observed, burned bones are minimal. ## Page 231

I I Bone Breakage Forms of bone breakage have been interpreted as evidence of cultural modification, such as spiral I (green bone) fractures, longitudinal splitting, bone flakes, and impact scars (Binford 1978; Frison 1978; Speth 1983; Todd 1987). This evidence is based on analogies made through experimental reconstruction and ethnographic observation. Large game animals were the primary focus of marrow and grease I production activities, although smaller game such as rabbit has sometimes been included with large game in ethnographic accounts of the northern Plains (Vehik 1977). Bone breakage forms are thought to be the result of marrow extraction. I The faunal assemblage at 41DL270 is highly fragmented. Long bone diaphyseal fragments are almost all smaller than 2-3 centimeters in length, and bone flakes, longitudinal splits, and spiral fractures I are observed in all levels. This pattern would suggest that marrow extraction was part of the system of animal processing at Denton Creek. Such breakage of faunal elements appears to be characteristic of most Texas hunter-gatherer sites throughout the Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods (Steele 1986a, I 1986b; DeMarcay and Steele 1986; Steele and Hunter 1986; Yates 1987; Martin 1988; Brown 1989:219; Masson and Holderby, in press), which implies that marrow extraction was commonly practiced throughout these periods of Texas prehistory. Marrow extraction is also commonly recorded in the I ethnographic record, and the consistent evidence for it in archeological assemblages is not altogether surprising. I Highly fragmented faunal assemblages may also reflect the production of bone grease. Grease rendering has been ethnographically observed to involve the pulverization of faunal elements for boiling. Long bone epiphyses are often preferred for such activities (Binford 1978; Todd 1987; Brink and Dawe 1990) which yield the greatest amounts of qualitatively superior white grease (Brink and Dawe 1990). I The absence of long bone epiphyses in a faunal assemblage has been interpreted as evidence for their destruction in grease production (Binford 1978; Speth 1983; Todd 1987; Brink and Dawe 1990; Davis and Fisher 1990). I Most of the samples from 41DL270 zones are too small to assess the relative frequency of specific skeletal elements such as long bone epiphyses. Although they are small, however, many contexts reflect I a broad range of cranial, axial, and appendicular elements of deer. This distribution implies that entire animal carcasses were returned to the campsite for consumption. It is odd that while elements from almost all portions of the anatomy are often present, they are present in low frequency. If the entire I carcass were butchered and consumed at these locales, then a much greater number of bone fragments would be expected. Such mixture of elements may be interpreted as the remains of grease production. If most of the skeleton were stone-boiled for grease rendering, elements from all areas of the anatomy I would become mixed together in the cooking container and would be dumped together on the living surface. This pattern, which has been extensively documented at the Mustang Branch bone bed (41HY209) in central Texas (Masson and Holderby, in press), suggests that the entire carcass was used I in grease production rather than the long bones exclusively. Ethnographic precedent in the high Plains has also been documented for the inclusion of axial elements such as vertebrae and ribs along with long bones in grease production (White 1952). I The faunal sample from Zone IV is large enough to allow an assessment of the relative representation of skeletal elements. All portions of the anatomy are present, including long bone I epiphyses. Although femora are lacking, all other long bones are present, including the proximal tibia which is among the most optimal of grease rendering bones. I 214 I


![Image 1 - Page 231](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p231_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 231*


---

## Page 232

I I It is thought, then, that grease production is reflected in many contexts yielding faunal bone from Denton Creek. The low quantity of bones from the Denton Creek contexts remains difficult to explain, I as grease production results in considerable amounts of bone debris (Binford 1978). Selection for optimal grease-rendering elements does not appear to have taken place. The small size of these faunal samples from all periods implies that the scale of production of animal products at 41DL270 was moderate. I Evidence for intensive or cooperative production of surplus bone grease or pemmican as has been observed in the Late Prehistoric northern Plains (Brink 1990) and central Texas (Masson and Holderby, in press) is not observed at 41DL270. I Modified Bone I Two pieces of faunal bone were modified in the 41DL270 assemblage. One was carved into a fishhook and the other was filed into a conical rounded point (Figures 83a and 83b). Both were made from mammal bone, quite possibly from deer ulnae as has been reported elsewhere in North-Central I Texas at sites like the McDonald site on Aquilla Lake (Brown et al. 1987:Figure 38.20, Tables 38.22, 38.23), and 41CO141 at Ray Roberts Reservoir (Yates 1987:Figure 4.1). Fishhooks were also among the McDonald site bone tool assemblage. The size of the fishhook at 41DL270 is much larger than the size of the fish recovered in the faunal assemblage, which were minnow sized. If indeed this artifact I functioned as a fishhook, its size reflects an absence in the archeological record of medium-sized fish. An alternative possibility is that the "fishhook" was used to catch turtles, a taxon that is abundant in the 41DL270 assemblage. I SUMMARY: DIACHRONIC PA1TERNS OF FAUNAL USE AT 41DL270 I A comparison of faunal assemblages according to zone at 41DL270 provides a view of changes in strategies of animal predation over time at this North-Central Texas site. Contexts (Zones VI -IX) dating to the Middle Archaic each yielded a single taxon: bird (Zone VI), bison (Zone VIII), and deer (Zone I IX). However, the lack of a number of comparative contexts from this period at the site makes conclusions unwarranted. It is significant that deer, a dietary staple throughout all periods in this region, is present in these early levels. The presence of bison is also notable at this time, particularly as bison I remains are scarce in later periods examined. Tables 14, 15, and 16 provide a composite comparison of the relative proportions of taxa in zones I dating to the Middle Archaic, Late Archaic, and Late Prehistoric periods. As the majority of the taxa were recovered from the Late Archaic components, a greater number of diagnostic elements identifiable to species resulted in a more detailed list of taxa. However, faunal remains identifiable to most I taxonomic classes were recovered from the smaller Middle Archaic and Late Prehistoric samples as well. These tables and their consolidated illustration in Figure 84 indicate the degree to which the Late Prehistoric sample exhibits less of an emphasis on large game in favor of a more diversified strategy I which increased the significance of smaller taxa in the diet. This interpretation is offered with caution, considering the small size of the Late Prehistoric faunal sample at 41DL270. A more fine-grained sequence in the variability of faunal use at this site is observed when stratigraphic zones are compared · individually. The discussion below summarizes the patterns of accelerating and waning specialization in I hunting strategies at the site in the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods. I I 215 I

In the earliest Late Archaic (Zone V), deer is the most common taxon, but turtle, rabbit, and bird are present in significant amounts and contributed significantly to the diet. Turtle I remains in this zone make up 31.6 percent of the biomass of the Zone V sample. Zone IV marks the return of bison to the Denton Creek area around 500 B.C. Deer, small mammals, rabbit, turtle, and bird I 217 I


![Image 1 - Page 234](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p234_img1.png)

## Page 235

I I are also found in the assemblage. Although bison and small tax.a are present in the sample, deer is proportionately far more significant than any other tax.on. Deer forms 66.1 percent of the sample biomass for Zone IV. The picture that emerges is one of specialization in deer as a protein resource. I Zone IV is the largest sample of faunal remains from 41DL270, which allows for additional observations to be made regarding the season of occupation at the site. Three deer are present in the assemblage, and tooth wear and eruption stages indicate that both summer and winter occupations are represented. This I observation implies that the reliance on deer as the primary food resource does not represent a seasonally delimited phenomena. During the later part of the Late Archaic, the specialization in deer continues to be reflected in the faunal assemblage of Zone m. Deer compose 75 percent of the biomass during this I period (Table 7). At the end of the Late Archaic, a specialization in deer is still reflected in the faunal record, although an increase in the exploitation of turtles is noted. I Deer and turtle are also reported to dominate Late Archaic faunal assemblages from the Mountain Creek area (41DL184, Martin 1988:335), where the exploitation of small-medium mammals is also reported (opossum, cotton rat). While deer is always the most common tax.a represented, some sites exhibit a greater range of tax.a in the assemblage, such as turkey, raccoon, opossum, fish, mussels, I beaver, pronghorn, badger, and gopher (41DL199, Martin 1988:337). Late Archaic assemblages from Ivie Reservoir also echo the pattern of deer-dominated samples which include low numbers of turtle, fish, rabbit, bird, small mammals, bison, and antelope (Treece et al. 1992:590-591). I Table 16. Composite Table or Fauna from Late Prehistoric Periods at 41DL270. I TAXA N % wr. (G) % BIOMASS % Deer 8 8.60 9.5 38.93 213.64 40.13 I Cottontail 2 2.15 0.2 0.82 6.62 1.24 Cotton Rat 1 1.08 0.1 0.41 3.31 0.62 Rabbit or Bird 1 1.08 0.1 0.41 3.31 0.62 I Turkey 3 3.23 2.9 11.89 56.16 10.55 Bird 32 34.41 4.8 19.67 90.42 16.99 Turtle 15 16.13 2.9 11.89 64.54 12.12 I Unidentified 31 33.33 3.9 15.98 94.34 17.72 TOTAL 93 24.4 532.34 I The Mountain Creek sites are proposed to have been occupied from the summer to fall, based on I the presence of fawn teeth, aquatic species, turkey, and ethnobotanical remains (Martin 1988:337). This pattern is compatible with the results of the Denton Creek assemblage, although the season of occupation may extend to the late fall or early winter, as discussed above. I From 2500 B.C. to A.O. 500, bison are thought to have been present in the archeological record, according to Dillehay's (1974) model. Bison are thought to have returned around A.O. 1250. This chronology is presently being tested by archeological investigations. The archeological evidence for I Dillehay's model has been questioned by Lynott (1979), who observes that bison are relatively rare in North-Central Texas archeological contexts until the latest Late Prehistoric period (defined as post A.O. 1200). The late arrival of bison into North-Central Texas has been correlated to the change in climate I to drier conditions around 1000 B.P. (Prikryl 1990:vi). At Ivie Reservoir, bison were absent from 2700- 218 I I


![Image 1 - Page 235](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p235_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 235*


---

## Page 236

I -1 950 B.C. and scarce from A.O. 250-500, during periods when their presence would be expected according to the Dillehay reconstruction (Treece et al. 1992:Table 9.12). Further findings at Ivie indicated that bison were present (though scarce) during the period from A.O. 500-1250 when they were I predicted to have been absent (Treece et al. 1992:Table 9.12). At 41DL270, the presence of bison in Zone IV is estimated to be around 2650 to 3100 B.P. (the I overall zone is estimated between 2500 and 3100 B.P., see" Archeological Synthesis", this volume). This Denton Creek pattern is in accordance with the model proposed by Dillehay (1974) and the findings at Ivie Reservoir (Treece et al. 1992) described above. The lack of bison in the Late Prehistoric Zone I I of 41DL270 does not reflect Lynott's (1979) observation that bison were abundant in North-Central Texas during this period. However, the Denton Creek faunal sample from this period is small as described below (N =58), and may not reliably reflect the presence or absence of bison at the site in tpis period. I Late Prehistoric I During the Late Prehistoric at Denton Creek, the faunal assemblage is diverse, and a focus on deer is not indicated. Although the sample size is small, deer, rodent, turkey, and turtle are represented. Deer form only 29 percent of the sample biomass during this period. Based on this sample, specialization I in deer hunting appears to have declined at 41DL270 in favor of a more diversified strategy which included smaller taxa. I An increase in the diversity of exploited taxa is also reported from Late Prehistoric sites in the Mountain Creek area, compared to those of the Late Archaic (Martin 1988:338). This author suggests that an increase and intensification of human use of the area is represented during this period. The increase is observed primarily in the presence of aquatic resources at the Cobb Pool (41DL148) and I Bagget Branch (41DL149) sites (Martin 1988:338-339). Indications of seasonality suggest that the site was inhabited during all times of the year, leading the author to suggest a "sedentary or semisedentary occupation," which is believed to be facilitated by the adoption of maize agriculture (Martin 1988:349). I The influence of contact and exchange with agricultural groups was significant during the Late Prehistoric period throughout central Texas (Prikryl 1990; Creel 1991; Riklis and Collins, in press). I As mentioned previously, although bison were purportedly abundant in Texas during this period (Dillehay 1974; Lynott 1979), they are absent from Denton Creek in the few contexts recovered of this date. However, bison has been reported from other North-Central sites at this time. At the Bagget I Branch site, bison hunting is associated with a horizon dated to A.O. 1500 (Martin 1988:338). This date is further confirmed at Ray Roberts Reservoir, where bison was confined to strata which postdate 965 B.P. (Yates 1987:100). I In summary, deer was the most significant animal component of the Denton Creek diet during all periods of occupation. This pattern echoes that reported for the McDonald site (41HI105) of Aquilla I Lake (Brown et al. 1987:38:139, Brown 1989:219), in which deer formed the majority offaunal samples that also included turtles and small mammals. Despite the prevalence of these general patterns, variability is observed in hunting strategies which indicate a Late Archaic increase in specialization on deer hunting I compared to more diversified large and small taxa exploitation in earlier and later periods. The regional role of the Denton Creek site was probably that of a riverine hunting camp and periodical occupation area (see "Archeological Synthesis", this volume), similar to that proposed for the McDonald site (Brown et I al. 1987:38: 140). I 219 I


![Image 1 - Page 236](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p236_img1.png)

## Page 237

I The faunal assemblage of 41DL270 thus reflects a long history of complex interaction among I human inhabitants of North-Central Texas with the environment, a relationship that was influenced by changing physiographic and sociopolitical variables. Climatic episodes of greater and lesser moisture may have affected the availability of tax.a such as bison or aquatic species. The evolution of sociopolitical I organization in North-Central Texas and its neighboring regions appears also to have affected changes in subsistence reflected in the faunal record. Periods of demographic florescence, perhaps accompanied by population movement, may have necessitated the strengthening of territorial boundaries and an I intensification of local resource extraction as observed in the Late Prehistoric period. Earlier specialization on deer may have been facilitated by a regional population well below carrying capacity (Brown 1989) or by reliance on a staple plant food resource that is difficult to detect in the archeological I record. Interaction with sedentary agriculturalists to the east in the Late Prehistoric period may also have encouraged emulative changes in subsistence strategies in North-Central Texas and the adoption of at least a semi-sedentary way of life (Martin 1988). A complex reconstruction of the history of human adaption I in this region is presently emerging as research begins to consider variability in intersite patterning. Consideration of the faunal record occupies a critical position in building these regional models. I I I I I I I I I I I 220 I I


![Image 1 - Page 237](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p237_img1.png)

## Page 238

I I ANALYSIS OF MOLLUSCAN REMAINS FROM 41DL270 Raymond W. Neck I Remains of molluscan shells are frequently preserved in non-acidic soils for many millennia. These shell remains, when properly identified as to species and environmental preferences, are a valuable habitat proxy for investigators interested in past environments. This information is particularly readily available I for molluscan remains in Quaternary samples, because the vast majority of the species represented in th~e samples are extant, even if environmental changes have resulted in local extirpations of some of these species. I MEIBODS I Molluscan remains from an archeological site (41DL270) along Denton Creek (a tributary of the Elm Fork of the Trinity River) in Dallas County, Texas, were made available to this author. Sample types could be classed into two types. A series of unscreened sediment samples were obtained for I specimen extraction, identification, and analysis. Additionally, large series of molluscan shells recovered from various proveniences of 41DL270 were supplied to the author. Sixteen unscreened sediment samples were screened through a nested series of soil sieves with a I final minimum sieve size of 0.6 millimeters (Standard Series #30). Shell material in the remaining material was hand picked. Identification to species and counting of individuals (both adult and immature) was made from personal experience of the author and comparison to a reference collection. Familiarity I with the preferred microhabitats of the various gastropod and bivalve species recovered from this site then allow paleoreconstruction of the general biological environment of the area surrounding this site. I Additional bivalve shell remains were presented to the author for identification and interpretation. These remains were retrieved during field screening activities at the excavation site, usually on wire mesh of six millimeters (one-quarter inch) dimension. These shell remains were identified to species with I left/right valve counts being recorded. Additional notes on shell condition, particularly on amount of original valve still remaining, were recorded on the larger samples in order to investigate further the possible utilization of these shells by the inhabitants of this site. I RESULTS I Molluscan paleoassemblages recovered from the 16 sediment samples are recorded in Table 17. A total of 22 species of gastropods and two species of unionid bivalves were identified from these samples. Additional fragments that may represent these same two species or additional species were I recovered. All of these species are native to the North-Central Texas area today, except for the aquatic operculate, Oncinnatia cincinnatiensis, which was common throughout the eastern two-thirds of Texas during the Pleistocene, but has since been extirpated from Texas. Only two species of freshwater I gastropods, Oncinnatia cincinnatiensis and Planorbella tenuis, were recovered from these samples. Neither of these species was abundant in the paleoassemblages. The unionid bivalves are represented by fragments in six of the 16 samples; the species of most of these fragments cannot be identified. However, an identifiable fragment and two larger valve remnants can be identified as Amblema plicata I and Lampsilis teres. Overall, the unionid remains comprise only a minor portion of the total paleoassemblage recovered from the sediment samples. I 221 I I

## Page 241

I The freshwater bivalve samples from 41DL270 contain shell material representing 11 species I (fables 18 through 23). All species recovered are freshwater pearly mussels of the family Unionidae, except for a single valve of a fingernail clam, Sphaerium striatinum. Most of the shells have suffered some loss of shell material, and several are only fragments. However, essentially all large pieces with I some portion of the umbo and associated teeth could be identified to species. All of the recovered bivalves are native to the Trinity River system, although one species - Plectomerus dombeyanus - is more typically found in the sluggish, lower portion of the Trinity River than a third-order stream such I as Denton Creek (see further comment below in Discussion). DISCUSSION I Sediment Samples I The gastropods represented in the 16 sediment samples can be analyzed to reconstruct the general paleoenvironment at different times and site-specific locations at 41DL270. The four most universally represented species and the number of samples from which they were recovered are as follows: I Strobilops texasianus (all 16), Gastrocopta contracta (14), Helicodiscus singleyanus (13), and Oligyra orbiculata (12). The three most common of these species are small-shelled species that are generally located in restricted areas where moisture is conserved under suitable cover objects, e.g., downed wood I at 41DL270. The fourth most common species. 0. orbiculata, is found in open to closed habitats with suitable cover objects on the ground surface. Overall, these most abundant species and the remaining species recovered from these sediments indicate the occurrence of an environment dominated by woody I plants, although some of the samples (see analysis below) indicate the occurrence of widely scattered woody plants so that an open woodland or even more open habitat is formed. The molluscan paleoassemblages recovered from the 16 sediment samples can be interpreted as I representing a continuum of environmental settings from a closed woodland to an open habitat with little or no woody plant coverage. However, this environmental continuum can conveniently, and probably realistically, be divided into a series of five habitat classifications that will allow further analysis of the I environmental history of this site. The five habitat classifications with an overall description and the lots representing each type are I as follows: Gallery Woodland. This habitat classification is dominated by large woody plants - trees and I shrubs - that form a closed canopy, except for areas of treefall or other periodic disturbance. This habitat type is present today but restricted to the margin of Denton Creek, and was probably restricted to the immediate creek bank area during the time of prehistoric human occupation of this site. Dominant I trees would be the deciduous trees that are present in the general area today, e.g., cedar elm, pecan, and green ash. This habitat type provides mesic microhabitats for terrestrial gastropods under pieces of downed wood. Leaf litter accumulations can be locally thick and provide suitable microhabitats for I terrestrial gastropods. However, removal of this leaf litter by flood scour can seriously impact the suitability of these areas for terrestrial gastropods (see last habitat type below). The samples analyzed are from Trench M2, Zone 10 (Cultural Zone I), Unit 6, Level 3, (Cultural Zone II), Feature 29, Unit I 11, Level 1 (Cultural Zone II), and Unit 14, Level 4 (Cultural Zone IV). Open Woodland. This habitat classification contains the same plant species present in the Gallery Woodland, but the density of plants is lower. The canopy is not solid, allowing the development of open I 224 I I

## Page 248

I I areas with grasses, forbs, and herbaceous plants. These open habitat areas are most often unsuitable for most of the terrestrial gastropods that require protection from exposure to the heat load and desiccation problems produced by exposure to the increased solar insolation and air movement. These samples are I from Feature 3, Unit 3, Level 2 (Cultural Zone I), Unit 6, Level 2 (top of Cultural Zone II), Unit 6, Level 4 (Cultural Zone ID), Unit 13, Level 2a (Cultural Zone ID), and Unit 14, Level 3 (Cultural Zone IV). I Open Habitat. This habitat classification is more difficult to characterize in terms of specific plant communities or even types of plants present. The open nature of the habitat is indicated by the decreased I number of species present, in particular the absence of specific indicators of woody plant communities. However, the resolution of habitat reconstruction provided by the constituent species found in these particular paleoassemblages is not sufficiently detailed to discriminate between an intact grassland I community and an open woodland that has experienced extreme environmental disturbance. This habitat type at this particular site appears to represent impacted woodlands due to the occurrence of species that are usually found under downed wood. These samples are from Trench M9, Zone 6 (Cultural Zone II?), I Feature 9, Unit 12, Level 3 (Cultural Zone IV), and Unit 15, Level 2 (Cultural Zone III). Open Habitat with Flood Impact. As in the above habitat classification, the basic vegetational structure of these habitats is not obvious from primary analysis of the paleoassemblage. The basic I community type could be an open woodland or grassland. Following the same logic as was utilized in the above classification, this habitat type at this site probably indicates an open woodland that has received flood debris deposits during runoff events along Denton Creek. The same floodwaters that may I leave flood debris during the ebb portion of the runoff event may also remove resident shell accumulations (including live individuals) during the scour portion of the same runoff event. Long-term impact of these flood events includes removal of protective accumulations of leaf litter and, possibly, I deposition of piles of floating wood and leaf material. These samples were collected from the area adjacent to Feature 10, Unit 13, Level 4 (Cultural Zone ID) and Unit 12, Level 6 (top of Cultural Zone V). I Flood Deposit. Flood deposit paleoassemblages are dominated by shell material dropped by receding floodwaters. Also present will be remnant individual shells of the resident population that were I not swept away by the scouring effect of an earlier portion of the same flood event. The reduced species diversity represented in typical flood debris sites represent the long-term impact on the habitat as discussed in the above habitat classification. The typical reduction in species diversity that is found in I flood debris samples is particularly observed in sample 32, which is the only sample of the 16 analyzed that contains no individuals of the three most abundant species in these paleoassemblages (Strobilops texasianus, Gastrocopta contracta, and Helicodiscus singleyanus). These two samples were from Trench I MS, Zone 5 (Cultural Zone VII) and Unit 14, Level 5 (bottom of Cultural Zone IV). The overall environmental conditions indicated by the recovered paleoassemblages indicate the I occurrence of closed and open woodlands, some of which experienced some type of environmental disturbance. The most likely disturbance event, and the only one specifically indicated by the samples, is flood waters spreading from Denton Creek. Human impact cannot be eliminated as a possibility and I is a possible localized factor. Density of the canopy of the woodlands varies from solid to quite open in response to distance from Denton Creek, local soil conditions, and localized disturbances. Variation in woodland community development within the various samples representing Gallery I Woodland Habitat can be detected and analyzed for additional environmental information. The most 231 I I

## Page 249

I mesic of the habitats represented by the sediment samples is sample 16. Not only does this sample I contain the most species (13) of any sample, it is also the only sample to contain Pilsbryna tridens and Euconulus trochulus, which are two species that require localized mesic microhabitats with protective leaf litter in order to survive. Sample 16 is also the only sample to contain more than two individuals of I Carychiwn mexicanwn (it contains 31 individual shells, including one immature), a species that requires periodically saturated soil conditions. I Freshwater Mussel Samples The species represented in the freshwater mussel paleoassemblage are typical of moderate to large I streams of low gradient with sand and mud substrate in the Trinity River basin. The two most common species are Amblema plicata and Quadrula mortoni, both of which are common today in regional streams and reservoirs. Other moderately common species include Tritogonia verrucosa, Lampsilis hydiana, and I Lampsilis teres. The occurrence of shells of Plectomerus dombeyanus at this site is surprising, because no records exist as far up the Trinity River system as the location of 41DL270. Restriction of shells of this species I to a single lot (#99 from Feature 3, Late Prehistoric, estimated at circa 1060 B.P.) and the large size of all three shells indicate the possibility, even probability, of human transport of these shells from a site lower down the Trinity River system or farther east in the other river systems of eastern Texas. I Most of the freshwater mussel shells from this site exhibit a loss of the posterior margin of shell to varying degrees. Shell loss may be minimal and could represent normal taphonomic loss of shell I material during the period of burial. Other shells may exhibit a loss of one-third to one-half, and rarely more, of the posterior portion of the shell. However, fragments of charred shell were noted in various lots from this site. I In addition, some nearly complete shells exhibit pale "scorch marks". These pale grayish areas may be located immediately below the umbo, indicating heating by placing the living mussel upside down I on a heated surface. Additional shells have larger areas of pale grayish discoloration on the shield area (broad area below the umbo, i.e., the major or central portion of the side of the valve of the freshwater mussel). The slight degree of charring that is exhibited by most of these shells indicates that the heating I may have merely been a method by which to open the shell. Some shells of both Amblema plicata and Quadrula mortoni showed no external indications of charring except on the broken edge of the posterior margin where the inner charred areas could be observed. Even slight heating would result in the death I of the mussel, forcing the adductor muscles to relax and allowing the shell to gape. Forcing the valves totally open by manual force would then be readily accomplished. The isolated pieces or teeth of mussel valves that are intensely charred may represent post-consumption heating from subsequent fires. I Valves of all species, except for the possibly imported shells of Plectomerus dombeyanus, exhibit variably missing portions of the posterior margin. A different degree of margin loss is exhibited by the two most common species represented in these samples (Table 24). The valves of Amblema plicata I typically exhibit more margin -Joss than valves of Quadrula mortoni, even though the shells of Q. mortoni are usually smaller than those of A. plicata. Of the shells of A. plicata examined from five large lots, exactly 50 percent of the sample consisted of 75 percent or less of the original shell material; only 18 I percent of the Q. mortoni were broken to the same extent. Some of the valves in these lots were essentially entire (12.5% of A. plicata and 42.7% of Q. mortonz). The factors causing this greater I 232 I I
