I I proportional loss of shell material by A. plicata include longer time period to "heat-shock" than Q. mortoni or greater fragility of the shell of A. plicata. Additional field experiments could answer this question. I An additional use of freshwater mussels by humans is indicated by the shell material recovered from 41DL270. A few shells of Quadrula mortoni from this site have been perforated. Several have I a moderate-sized hole that is irregularly circular. The shells give the appearance of having been ground against a harder object until a hole was produced. Shells with this type of modification have been recovered from a number of sites in coastal and central areas of Texas. Various species have been I modified in this manner, but the approximate size of the valves is rather consistent (intermediate to small in size). Usually, these perforated shells are entire; however, one valve of Q. mortoni from lot #212.0 is missing the posterior third of the valve (Figure 83d). The margin of this particular fragment appears I to have a remnant of an additional hole. Apparently, this valve had two holes, but the valve broke across the axis of the central hole. At least one valve recovered from 41DL270 has a drilled hole in the umbo area (Lampsilis teres from Lot #241) (Figure 83c). The cultural purpose(s) of this modification remain I unknown at this time. An additional shell fragment (Lot #212.2, probably L. teres) has two rounded notches on the posterior margin (no umbo is present on this fragment) (Figure 83e). The existence of fairly large numbers of freshwater mussel valves from a variety of time periods I allows an opportunity to investigate possible faunal changes through time. Freshwater mussels from features with known radiocarbon age and cultural period affiliation are presented in Table 25. Unfortunately, the number of lots and freshwater mussels varies greatly among the various cultural I periods, but this analysis is presented as a first attempt to understand temporal variations in use of freshwater mussels by aboriginal populations in this area. The samples presented in Table 25 are similar to the overall samples in that the two most abundant species are Amblema plicata and Quadrula mortoni. I Species diversity of the mussel remains in these features is lowest during the Cultural Zone m period, but this low figure may be at least partially due to the single feature with only two lots that was available from this cultural period. The highest species diversity is reflected in Feature 3 with nine lots from the I Late Prehistoric period. Analysis of similar sites in this area will be required before conclusions regarding the relative importance of faunal diversity and cultural utilization diversity can be formulated. I SUMMARY The molluscan paleoassemblages recovered from 41DL270 indicate subtle patterns of heterogeneity I in general environmental conditions on both a temporal and micro-spatial basis at a site and during a time period that is generally indicated to be one of environmental homogeneity. Utilization of freshwater mussels as a food source is indicated by the large number of broken and lightly charred shells. Some I import of limited numbers of large shells of a species less common in (and probably absent from) this area may indicate local transport of large examples of these shells for cultural purposes. A few freshwater mussel shells exhibit modifications that were of human origin. I I I 233 I I

Laboratory Sorting and Identification I Each sample was scanned and sorted under a binocular dissecting microscope at 8 magnifications. All charred seeds and woody plant remains were sorted and counted. The carbonized wood samples that I were submitted for identification were identified by using the snap technique, examining them at 8-45 magnifications with a binocular dissecting microscope, and comparing them to samples in the herbarium at the Texas A&M University Palynology Laboratory. All seed identifications were made using seed I manuals and reference collections at Texas A&M University. Gasser and Adams (1981) have demonstrated that most uncarbonized seeds are consumed I or degraded by insects, small mammals, and fungal attack within three years of deposition. ## Page 256

I I All of the samples were recovered from recognizable features. Features 1, 23, 19, 28, 33, 34, 35, and 36 exhibited some evidence of burning. Carbonized "seed" taxa from the six hearth flotation samples included goosefoot (Chenopodium sp.)1, and grasses of the Paniceae tribe. Utilizing Schiffer's I terminology, Miksicek (1987) has described the criteria for evaluating carbonized plant remains from Hohokam sites in southern Arizona. He has argued that plant refuse can be placed in four contextual categories: 1) defacto, 2) primary, 3) secondary, and 4) tertiary. I De facto refuse represents large quantities of stored plant products. Primary refuse is trash discarded at the point of use. Identification of plant remains in this context would allow the I archeobotanist to assign a specific function to a feature such as a cache pit or cooking pit. But what if the plant remains fall into the category of secondary or tertiary refuse? Secondary refuse is defmed as plant remains discarded at some point other than where they were processed, used, or stored. This would I include refuse which has been cleaned from roasting pits or house floors and placed in, trash pits, middens, or abandoned buildings. Occupational and post-abandonment processes such as wind, water, and bioturbation can leave a fairly evenly dispersed scatter of plant material across a site. This is termed I tertiary, or background scatter refuse (Miksicek 1987). In either case, an archeobotanist easily could erroneously assign a function to a feature by mistaking a pit filled with secondary or tertiary refuse for a cooking pit. I The context of a flotation sample can be characterized by the diversity and density of plant remains, •·- by the ratio of small to large seeds, and by the condition and type of plant parts it contains. For example, a high density of relatively intact plant parts recovered from a burned structure could indicate I de facto refuse. A moderate density of relatively intact parts recovered from a roasting pit could indicate primary refuse. A low density of waste parts, a low ratio of small to large seeds would be expected from middens or trash-filled pits and abandoned structures. Tertiary refuse or background scatter could be I manifested as low density, small seeds, and tiny fragments of charcoal. This would be recovered from exposed occupation surfaces, including depressions, old storage pits, and old hearths, or general site fill. I The plant remains in the hearth features at site 41DL270 resemble tertiary refuse in the following manner: 1) the seeds are small, 2) seed concentrations are extremely low at 0.4 seedsniter, 3) the wood charcoal fragments recovered in the light fraction are all small, measuring less than 4 millimeters on any I side. The low concentrations of Chenopodium sp. in samples suggest that the seeds may not have been I brought to the site as a result of the prehistoric occupation, but instead were part of the normal seed rain present both at the site and in contemporaneous sediments surrounding the site (Minnis 1981). Another possibility is that some of the seeds represent scattered tertiary evidence of cultural activity. This is I possible because Chenopodium sp. greens and fruits were very important in the prehistoric economies of Eastern North America (see, for example, Smith 1992: 103). I Although a variety of Chenopodium sp. were cultivated in the southwest, the seeds from 41DL270 probably were not from domesticated plants. It is difficult to tell carbonized wild Chenopodium fruits from carbonized domesticated Chenopodium sp. fruits, because their size ranges overlap. The most easily I recognizable distinction between the two in an uncarbonized state is that the domesticates have a whitish I 1 Chenopodium sp. "seed" (and Amaranthus sp.) is actually a fruit. The pericarp adheres directly to the seed. In wild types, the pericarp is hard and lignified, giving the fruit a shiny black appearance. 239 I I

## Page 257

I I appearance due to their thin seed coat (Fritz 1984). The thickness of the fruit coat cannot be observed on carbonized material without thin-sectioning and examining it with a scanning-electron microscope (SEM). Despite these problems in separating domesticates from wild types, the very small size of the Oienopodium sp. fruits ( < 1 mm) at 41DL270 suggest that they were wild types. Wild Oienopodium I sp. may have been collected and processed as quelites (greens), or for their fruits (Wilson 1976). The few fruits in these samples may have been deposited as a result of this activity. I The carbonized wood remains from the flotation samples (Table 27) and from the macrobotanical samples were all smaller than 4 millimeters wide and proved difficult to identify. I have placed some of the charred wood fragments into taxa, giving them "c.f." (compares favorably) designations. These I taxa are Rex sp. (holly), Acer rubrum (red maple), Ulmus sp. (elm), and live oak (Quercus virginiana). /lex sp. is a genus represented in north Texas by several species, including yaupon (/lex vomitoria) and possum haw (Rex decidua). The Ulmus sp. wood type also represents several elms, incluaing American I elm (Ulmus americana), cedar elm (Ulmus crassifolia), and winged elm (Ulmus alata). These trees are representative of a variety of environments. Live oak commonly occurs in relatively open parklands. The elms and hollys usually occur as part of an understory in a wooded environment. No trees that are I commonly part of the overstory in riparian woodlands were identified in this very limited sample. SUMMARY I The 13 flotation samples submitted for analysis yielded one seed taxon and four wood types. The low concentration and the small size of the seeds suggested that the seeds were removed from a tertiary context. Because of this, great caution should be used in interpreting the functions of both the seeds and I the features from which they were removed. The economically important plant, Oienopodium sp. was present in the seed assemblage. There are at least two interpretations regarding the presence of Oienopodium sp. First, the Oienopodium sp. may have been collected and processed as quelites I (greens), or for their fruits (Wilson 1976). The few seeds in these samples could have been deposited as a result of this activity. I On the other hand, the seeds may not have been brought to the site as a result of the prehistoric occupation, but instead were part of the normal seed rain present both at the site and in contemporaneous sediments surrounding the site (Minnis 1981). The problem with the seed assemblage from 41DL270 I is that it is so limited that a definite interpretation of the material is not possible. The wood types from the flotation samples were identified as Rex sp. (holly), Acer rubrum (red I maple), Ulmus sp. (elm), and live oak (Quercus virginiana). Live oak is often associated with an open parkland type of vegetation cover. The other trees, however, are associated with a variety of habitats. When considered as a group, the hollys, maples, and elms might represent a lowland deciduous hardwood I woodland. The sample size, however, is very small, and no definite determination of the local site environment is possible from this particular assemblage of charred wood types. I Although the low concentration of all plant remains at this site prevented any thorough interpretations of the paleoenvironment or the prehistoric economy, the presence of Oienopodium sp. seeds (fruits) do present the possibility that these plants were utilized at the site for economic purposes. The results indicate the possibility that another site in the region with more extensive evidence of burning I features could produce some useful data regarding the paleoethnobotany of the region. I I ARCHEOMAGNETIC STUDIES OF SITE 41DL270 Wulf A. Gose I SUSCEPTIBILITY STUDIES x, The magnetic susceptibility, is a parameter which is proportional to the magnetic mineral I content of a rock or soil sample. If the sample contains only one magnetic mineral, then the susceptibility is a direct measure of the concentration of this mineral. The susceptibility of a soil can be modified by pedogenic processes. In the organic-rich top layer of a soil, the susceptibility increases by as much as I a factor of ten (e.g., Signer and Fine 1989). If the soil is subsequently buried, this increase is preserved and can be used to identify paleosols. I The susceptibility is also enhanced by the action of fire. In the reducing environment 'of a wood burning fire place, new magnetic minerals are formed such as magnetite or maghemite. This effect can be observed in: rocks, soils, as well as in the ash from the wood (Tite and Mullins 1971; Bellomo 1993; I McClean and Kean 1993). Thus archeological sites which contain fire places and wood ash will have susceptibility values which are increased above the values of "sterile" sites. The mixing of burned and unburned soil will I result in a large variability of the susceptibility. At site 41DL270, samples for susceptibility studies were collected in 9 profiles. The samples were I placed in 8 cc plastic cubes, weighed, and measured twice for quality control. The results for all profiles are shown in Figure 85. The individual profiles cover too short a stratigraphic interval to allow a meaningful interpretation. But because the profiles were collected in a common elevation reference I frame, the results can be combined into one data set (Figure 86). The ensuing figure is rather startling. In the range from 141.00 to 142.55 meters amsl, the I susceptibility values are much larger than in the remainder of the profile and the data exhibit a pronounced. scatter. This strongly suggests that the sediments in this interval were modified by human activity. The underlying interval has much smaller xvalues, but the data scatter more than is to be I expected for an undisturbed section. The scatter could be due either to strong bioturbation or, more likely, is due to occasional human occupation, i.e., the site was utilized much less frequently than during the time represented by the overlying zone. Above the 142.55 meter elevation, the data come from one I profile only (Trench M9). These sparse data suggest cessation of human activity at this site. MAGNETIC REMANENCE ANALYSES I Most rocks contain a small amount of magnetic minerals such as magnetite or hematite. During the process of rock formation, these minerals will align with the ambient magnetic field and impart a I remanent magnetization to the rock parallel to the field, usually the geomagnetic field. Under a wide range of conditions, this information is preserved. I The directions of magnetization of a random assemblage of rocks will have a random distribution. If the same rocks are heated in a fire place, they will acquire a new thermo-remanent magnetization (TRM) parallel to the ambient field. By collecting oriented samples from the rocks of a possible fire I place, it can be readily ascertained whether or not these rocks have remained undisturbed since their last 241 I I

## Page 261

I heating, i.e., whether or not they do represent a fire place. I The samples were collected by Hicks & Company personnel according to my instructions, using plaster of Paris to orient a horizontal plane atop the rock sample upon which a north-pointing arrow was I inscribed (Figure 87). In the laboratory, two or three 2-centimeter cubes were cut from each rock except for rock A4, which was too small for multiple specimens. The total number of specimens was 22. All samples were demagnetized in alternating fields of 50, 100, 200, and 400 Oerstedts (5 to 40 milli Tesla). I During the measuring process, the samples were kept in a magnetically shielded room. The magnetic remanence was measured with a superconducting magnetometer and the data were analyzed by the principal component method (Kirschvink 1980). The results from the individual specimens were then I combined to yield one value per rock. In order to identify stable components of magnetization, the results from each specimen are I inspected in a vector component diagram (Figure 88). A linear trend of three or more steps indicates a component of magnetization. For specimen A6.1 (Figure 88a), all data points fall along a straight line towards the origin. This specimen contains only one component of magnetization. Specimen A3.2 (Figure 88b) exhibits a small overprint readily removed by low field demagnetization. This soft I component probably represents post-sampling contamination and is of no archeological interest. The other specimens have vector component diagrams similar to these two examples. I The direction of magnetization is best shown in an equal area stereographic projection (Figure 89). By convention, directions with a positive inclination are plotted in the lower hemisphere with solid symbols, and directions with a negative inclination are plotted in the upper hemisphere using open I symbols. In this graph, the directions of magnetization of a group of samples sharing a common magnetization will plot as a tight cluster. I Samples A6, A7, A8, and A9 were collected from Feature 34 in Unit 14. The samples have experienced some moderate motion since their last heating but form a cluster typical for other in situ stone fire places in Texas (Figure 89a). These directions are not far removed from the present dipole I field direction, another indication that these rocks do represent an in situ fire place. By contrast, the two rocks from Feature 35 (Unit 13, A4, AS) have highly divergent directions and thus are not in place (Figure 89b). The same interpretation applies to Feature 36 (Unit 11, Al, A2, A3). Note that rock Al I has a negative inclination (Figure 89c). The rocks from Features 35 and 36 are either from severely disturbed fire places or they represent discard material. I SUMMARY Two types of magnetic measurements were performed on samples from site 41DL270. The I analysis of the directions of the remanent magnetization of burned rocks reveals that Feature 34 represents a fire place which has been minimally disturbed since its last use. By contrast, the burned rocks from Features 35 and 36 are not part of in situ fire places. The magnetic susceptibility of soil samples, I collected in stratigraphic order, defines the interval from 141.00 to 142.55 meters amsl as a zone of maximum human activity at this site. The data also suggest occasional human occupation for the underlying interval. Above about 143 meters amsl, human activity seems to have ceased. I I 244 I I

## Page 266

I I FRESHWATER SHELL ISOTOPE STUDIES David 0. Brown I INTRODUCTION As an integral part of the geoarcheological and paleoenvironmental studies conducted for the data I recovery excavations at 41DL270, a series of stable isotope measurements were recorded for freshwater shells collected from middens at the site. It was originally hoped to use isotope composition studies to examine soil carbonates at the site paralleling what Humphrey and Perring (1994) have done in this area. I Since soil carbonates were not present in significant quantities in the cultural levels, however, the isotope analysis was altered to focus on the midden shell, a carbonate with many of the same analytical properties as soil carbonates, but which may avoid some of the complications inherent in that analysis. Although I the analysis of freshwater shell stable isotopes has not been widely utilized, particularly in recent years, there is ample evidence to suggest that it could provide an extremely important source of paleoenviron mental data under ideal circumstances (and perhaps even under less than ideal circumstances which are, I after all, those under which most archeologists work). Following a brief discussion which summarizes the method and some of the problems, the results are presented from the Denton Creek analyses. STABLEISOTOPESTUDIBS I While stable isotope analyses have been a key aspect of geochemistry for more than four decades (Faure 1986; Hoefs 1987), they have recently gained considerable favor among archeologists and other I scientists seeking to reconstruct the relationship between past climates and past human behaviors (e.g., Price 1989). In Texas, isotope studies of human and animal bone have provided new information on past vegetation and dietary practices. Bousman (1990), Bousman and Quigg (1990), and Huebner (1990) have I conducted analyses of stable isotope ratios on materials recovered from archeological sites in Texas. A discussion of stable isotope analyses and its use in archeology has been compiled by Collins and Bousman (1990). In general, these Texas studies have focussed on isotopes in bone rather than shell and the I primary target of these investigations has been paleodiets and only indirectly paleoecology, paleovegetation, and paleoclimate. The study undertaken here, while focussed on a key component of paleodiet, mussel, is actually directed at the climates under which these shells formed rather than their I role in aboriginal subsistence patterns. The following brief discussion, compiled from a number of sources, outlines the theoretical basis I of stable isotope analysis. For more detail, the reader is referred to Faure (1986) or Hoefs (1987) or any other of the excellent texts on the subject. I The defining criteria for the identification of chemical elements is the number of protons found in the nucleus of the atom. While variation in protons would actually change the basic elemental identification, the number of neutrons found in the nucleus of any single element do in fact vary I somewhat, creating a series of isotopes of different atomic weights (atomic weights, as recorded on the periodic table of elements, sum the number of neutrons and protons in the nucleus). While different isotopes of the same element are generally similar in behavior, there are slight differences. When I unstable, radioactive elements decay, for example, other isotopes of the same element or even different elements may be created. Stable isotopes, as their name implies, do not change in form. There are some 300 naturally I occurring stable isotopes, distributed primarily among 62 chemical elements (Anderson and Arthur 249 I I


![Image 1 - Page 266](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p266_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 266*


---

## Page 267

I 1983:3). While one of the stable isotopes is usually dominant, there are slight differences in behavior I between the isotopes and slight differences in isotopic ratios may signal processual differences in the environments in which these isotopes are found. Stable isotope studies, particularly those focussing on environmental questions, have primarily tended to key on low atomic weight elements from hydrogen to I sulfur. To some degree this is because these low-atomic-weight elements tend to be among the most common found in nature. More importantly, however, the addition or subtraction of a neutron from the nucleus of these lighter elements results in a greater proportional weight change and generally greater I behavioral differences. Oxygen I There are three different isotopes of oxygen, 160, 170, and 180. 160 is the dominant form, comprising 99.763 percent of all the earth's oxygen. 180, the isotopic variant commonly studied, only I exists as .1905 percent (Arthur and Anderson 1983:4). These three oxygen isotopes, in combination with the two different isotopes of hydrogen (hydrogen and deuterium), form nine different isotopic concentrations of water, each of which has a slightly different mass and an inversely corresponding water I vapor pressure (Faure 1986:431). Although the differences in mass are only slight, there are measurable behavioral tendencies. All other factors being held constant, combinations with the lighter isotope 160 tend to exist in higher proportion in water vapor while the heavier 180 is preferentially concentrated in I the liquid state of water. Since the evaporation of water is partly dependent upon temperature, it is clear that, other factors being constant, higher temperatures will cause greater evaporation and thus an enrichment of the percentage of 180 in water. I The determination of oxygen isotope ratios is usually accomplished by measurement of the oxygen fraction of carbon dioxide which has been equilibrated with the water sample (Epstein and Mayda 1953; Epstein et al. 1953). Oxygen isotope data from water is referenced with respect to the SMOW (standard I mean ocean water) standard as originally defined by Craig (1961a, 1961b). In contrast, oxygen isotope data from carbonates is usually reported with reference to the PDB standard, based on C~ from the Cretaceous belemnites (Belemnitella americana) of the Peedee Formation from South Carolina. I Conversion of these two is accomplished by a simple formula: ~ 180 (calcite vs. SMOW) = 1.03086 ~ 180 (calcite vs. PDB) + 30.86 I Carbon I There are two stable isotopes of carbon (in addition to the radioactive 14C), 12C and 13C. The former is by far the most common, accounting for 98.89 percent of stable carbon (Anderson and Arthur 1983:4). Like oxygen from carbonates, carbon isotope data is generally always reported as a ratio to the I PDB standard, based on C~ from the Cretaceous belemnites of the Peedee Formation from South Carolina. I The most important use of the isotopic variability of carbon has been that of the chronometric radiocarbon dating procedure. The stable carbon isotope ratio has been utilized, successfully, as evidence of paleovegetation since the stable isotopes of carbon are utilized differentially in the major photosynthetic I pathways. I 250 I I

## Page 268

I I OXYGEN ISOTOPES AND PALEOCLIMATE STUDIES H. C. Urey (1947) first suggested that there should be a relationship between the ratio of 180 I trapped in marine carbonate fossils and the paleotemperature of ancient oceans. His pioneering work at the University of Chicago in the 1950s defined the field of stable isotope studies. Since then this technique has been utilized in numerous studies of ocean paleotemperatures (Savin et a/,. 1975; Savin I 1977; Savin and Yeh 1981; Buchardt and Fritz 1981). Numerous problems have been encountered in the estimation of paleotemperatures from isotopic I composition, however, and are discussed in detail in various standard works on the subject (Faure 1986:442-446; Hoefs 1987). These problems have been discussed in detail in many of the works examining paleoclimates from isotopes. Among the most important and perhaps most controversial is I the potentially changing isotopic composition of the oceans. While it is generally agreed that short term variations of 0180 are the result of glaciations in which the lighter 160 is trapped in glacial masses, thus temporarily altering the oceanic equilibrium (Hoefs 1987: 142), there are other hints at long term changes I in composition. Marine cherts and limestones, for example, tend to show a decrease in 0180 with age. This has been alternately explained as an actual oceanic change or as postdepositional exchange of the sediments with their environment. As Hoefs (1987: 142-143) and Faure (1986:442-447) explain, the I competing arguments for these explanations are flawed and the issue is not resolved. This long term change is relatively minor for the Holocene, but the effects of glacial storage of 160 may be a more difficult problem, particularly for the study of Late Pleistocene climates. Unfortunately the magnitude of the effect of isotopic entrapment in glaciers or the subsequent effect of such entrapment upon meteoric I waters is poorly understood. Other problems with studies of oxygen isotopes in biogenic carbonates are more directly related I to the organisms themselves and their manipulation of carbonates. First of all, while certain organisms appear to maintain a near-perfect isotopic equilibrium between ambient water and secreted carbonates, other organisms may perform a biological fractionation of sorts during shell secretion (Anderson and I Arthur 1983:65-75). This effect has been studied in laboratory conditions, and while the general conditions and species for which it occurs are known, it is still not completely documented. I Along these same lines, there is an apparent difference in isotopic fractionation dependent upon shell mineralogy. The two primary carbonate shell crystalline forms, calcite and aragonite, as well as biogenic silica, are found to have different 0180 values (Anderson and Arthur 1983:67). Of these, calcite I appears to be the closest to the actual ambient water values. Unfortunately, most freshwater and terrestrial molluscan species are composed primarily or wholly of aragonite which tends to undergo a slightly different fractionation and which tends to decay through time into calcite. This decay is I accompanied by a corresponding change in isotopic fractionation. Finally, there is a potential problem with post-mortem alteration of shell isotope composition. I While this has been studied somewhat for marine animals, there is little data available for potential postdepositional alteration in the active soil environment in which the specimens examined here were found. These has, however, been considerable discussion of pedogenic alteration of soil carbonates; I Humphrey and Perring (1994) have recently discussed this in relation to the isotopic composition of soil carbonates which they studied from the Aubrey site in Denton County. Their conclusion, based upon the literature, was that the effect was minimal, that is, insufficient to alter the original 0180 of the meteoric I waters. It is felt that this probably holds true for shell carbonates; postdepositional exchange with the ambient soil is probably no worse that soil carbonates, and actually probably even less in cases where I 251 I


![Image 1 - Page 268](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p268_img1.png)

## Page 269

I shells are incorporated into large shell middens. That is, individual shells whose entire surface is exposed I to the surrounding soil may be subject to greater exchange potential. While these problems plague researchers studying the potential correspondences between marine I carbonate isotopes and the ocean paleotemperatures, there are additional problems which confront the student of shell isotopes from meteoric waters. Firstly, meteoric water, evaporated from the oceans, is generally much lighter than the oceans themselves. While the nature of this relationship is reasonably I well understood, meteoric precipitation can vary quite distinctly in isotopic composition. The actual isotopic composition of precipitation varies with latitude, altitude, annual rainfall, rainfall seasonality, and temperature and is roughly predictable (Dansgaard 1964; Yurtsever 1975). Global estimates have been I constructed for precipitation values (Yurtsever 1975). The Dallas area lies along the approximate -4.0%0 (per mil) SMOW contour. I While marine shell isotope studies, both modem and fossil, have been quite common over the last several decades, non-marine shell studies have been much less frequent. Among the first to attempt such work were Clayton and Degens (1959) who were primarily interested in using oxygen isotope I fractionation as a means of separating between marine and freshwater depositional environments. Keith et al. (1964) expanded upon this work, looking at the variation among freshwater molluscan species. Freshwater sampling sites included lakes and rivers from Saskatchewan to Missouri. Overall, they I compiled a sample of several hundred isotope fractionation readings from 64 different marine and freshwater sites. In addition to individual samples of species from different environments, they sampled multiple shells of the same species from the same sample site, they ran multiple samples from different locations on single shells of several different species, and they ran comparative analyses between shell I and soft part of mollusks. Although they did not sample as far south as Texas, they included in their analyses a number of freshwater genera and species which are found in the Denton Creek area, including Proptera, Lampsilis, Quadrula, and Amblema. I With regard to the freshwater samples, Keith et al. (1964:1772) conclude that there is a definite latitude effect in oxygen isotope fractionation caused by a progressive depletion of the heavier 180 as one I moves northward, consistent with the findings of Epstein and Mayda (1953) and Yurtsever (1975). Furthermore, they conclude that while there are slight differences between different species in the same environment, the differences between lacustrine and riverine examples of the same species are much I greater than the inter-specific differences (Keith et al. 1964:1774). In general, their conclusion is that isotopic fractionation is a result of environmental differences rather than vital effects or specific differences in fractionation. In addition, while they note that carbon isotope fractionation rations can be I relatively high within a single shell, the average intra-shell oxygen isotope range is only 0.65o/oo. Based on their analysis of intra-shell and soft part variation, they chose to sample a general cross-section across the back of the shell. I Their analysis points to minimal oxygen isotope variation within the same locally cohabitant community. This variation, for both marine and freshwater species, is less than one per mil. They note I that the average intra-community range is 0.59o/oo, essentially the same as the intra-shell variation in pelecypods (Keith et al. 1964:1779). At the French Creek sampling site north of Meadville, Pennsylvania, for example, 31 individuals of 7 different species yielded 0180 values ranging only from I -9.36 to -10.34, with a mean of -9.92. Maximum intra-species range at French Creek is 0.58o/oo for 13 valves of Actinonaias carinata, with a 0180 mean of-10.07%0, and a standard deviation of only 0.135. For all 31 valves of 7 species, the total variation was only 0.98o/oo, with a mean of -10.01 o/oo, and a standard deviation of 0.237. Clearly, the range of variation in a single environment is quite small. I 252 I I


![Image 1 - Page 269](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p269_img1.png)

## Page 270

I I As an example of the different environmental ranges found by Keith et al. (1964), four shells of Quadrula quadrula from Grand River at Dunnville, Ontario, have a 0180 mean of -10.56%0 with a standard deviation of 0.084 and a total variation of only 0.21 %0. Far to the south, a dozen samples of I Cumberlandia monodonta from the Clinch River at Kyle's Ford, Tennessee, yielded a 0180 mean of only -7.64%0 with a standard deviation of 0.194 and a total variation of 0.64%0. Although Keith et al. do not make any sweeping statements about the correlation of latitude and oxygen fractionation, it is clear that I all of these values closely approximate the rainfall values given by Yurtsever (1975); for the northeastern U.S. and southern Canada area, the rainfall 0180 values range between -10.0%0 and -12.0%0 while the Tennessee area falls near the -6.0%0 to -8.0%0 rainfall contour. I The first attempt to use freshwater carbonates, with a combination of lake marls and mollusks, to examine paleoclimates was conducted by Minze Stuiver (1968) for Pretty Lake, Indiana. The core, which I was incomplete, suggested only minor variations over the last 9,000 years (Stuiver 1970:5251). More complete data for Pretty Lake and three other lakes, Queechy Lake and Henderson Pond in New York, and Pickerel Lake in South Dakota, subsequently showed much greater overall variation. Stuiver's I resulting comparisons show a distinct postglacial hypsithermal or altithermal period beginniag between 9,000 and 10,000 years ago, a general cooling period between about 5,000 and 2,500 years ago, and a slight warming trend over the last 1,500 years (Stuiver 1970:5254). Analysis of pollen cores from I Pickerel and Pretty Lakes showed excellent correlations with the oxygen isotope record. Stuiver (1970:5256) concluded that freshwater shells appear to grow in isotopic equilibrium with I their environment, a fact noted for marine shells by Epstein et al. (1953) and Mook and Vogel (1968). Like Keith et al. (1964), Stuiver noted that interspecific variation was quite small, less than 0.7%0 in the seven species which he had sampled from Lake Huron. Finally, he notes that, while the data may not be available for exact paleotemperature correlations, the general trends should be notable and the actual I correspondences might be quite close. In the early 1970s Fritz and Poplawski (1974) conducted a study of oxygen and carbon isotope I fractionation in freshwater mollusks from lakes in southern Ontario. Their study included both gastropods and pelecypods collected from lakes as well as specimens grown under laboratory conditions. Seasonal variation in oxygen isotope fractionation from several lakes showed that the annual per mil I change can be as high as 6 %0. In addition, the slope of variability was somewhat different for several of the lakes although all had 0180 highs in October and all but one had lows in April (the months of the o study were those ice-free months in Ontario, April through October). Their findings suggested that 13C I was primarily controlled by dissolved inorganic carbon in the water source rather than vital effect or food controls. For 6180 variation, it appeared that the isotopic fractionation was indeed climatically controlled and related to temperature and evaporation, but also to flushing and lake inflow/outflow. Two other I observations are relevant. One, lakes with the greatest flow had lower 0180 values than more stagnant lakes and two, 0180 values of mollusks most closely reflect the values of the lake during the growth season of the animal (Fritz and Poplawski 1974:93). The general conclusion pointed to the utility of 0180 I data as a paleoenvironmental and paleohydrological indicator. Following the study of mussels from freshwater lakes in southern Ontario, Fritz and his colleagues I (Fritz et al. 1975) examined isotope signatures from ostracods and mollusks from a core at Lake Erie with sediments dates to 15,000 B.P. The study recorded at least one sudden drop of nearly 6o/oo in isotope values around 9,000 to 10,000 B.P. They were able to correlate some of the earlier isotopic I changes with documented changes in the pollen records and paleovegetation of the lake area while some o of the later changes were attributed to changes in lake hydrology. Combining 6180 and 13C in a single I 253 I


![Image 1 - Page 270](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p270_img1.png)

## Page 271

I plot showed strong clustering of the values from different time periods, separating the clusters better than I oxygen alone. Unfortunately, there were several major gaps in this record, including much of the last 5,000 years. I While studies of freshwater molluscan species began to dwindle after the studies by Fritz and his colleagues (Fritz and Poplawski 1974; Fritz et al. 1975), shell isotope fractionation studies continued using terrestrial mollusks in their stead. One of the seminal studies in this field was that by Crayton I Yapp (1979) which is marked not only for its attempt to extend this technique from aquatic species to terrestrial ones, but also by the use of fossil land snail samples from radiocarbon-dated strata in Sudden Shelter in Utah, excavated by Jennings et al. (1980). Overall, Yapp studied several hundred modern I samples from environments extending from Yucatan to Wisconsin and from South Carolina to Washington state (also including samples from Norway). His range of sample sites was distinguished from that of Keith et al. (1964) not only by its geographic extent but by the inclusion of samples from arid areas in I the western U.S. In addition to more than 200 samples representing 4 different species and 7 different sampling localities from along Ivie Creek (near Sudden Shelter) in Sevier County, Utah, Yapp included just under a hundred samples of two species from four sample localities in Wisconsin, and 40 samples I of one species from a single locality at Hondo Canyon near Taos, New Mexico. Yapp (1979:629) found somewhat greater inter-specific variability in oxygen isotope fractionation at any one site, with two locales showing just under 2%o between species. The overall variability was, as might be expected, much I greater at 5.5%0. While the values recorded by Keith et al. (1964) for aquatic mollusks showed strong correspon I dence with latitude and plotted differences in the isotopic fractionation of meteoric waters, the land snails studied by Yapp showed very little clear correspondence with either the 6180 of rainfall, the estimated temperature, or the average annual relative humidity (Yapp 1979:632). He nonetheless assumed that there was an equilibrium between shell carbonate and the snail's body fluid. Although the larger picture I did not conform to environmental expectations, Yapp did find that there was a correspondence between the microhabitat of the snails and the 613C values; samples from the south-facing slope on the north bank of the creek did not overlap with the values of those from leaf-litter covered flat forest floor on the south I bank of the creek (Yapp 1979:629, 631). His overall conclusions suggest that there is a relationship between 0180 and evaporative processes and he was ultimately able to elicit a strong relationship (R2=0.987) between 6180 and the inverse of the annual relative humidity (Yapp 1979:634). I The analysis of fossil shells from dated strata at Sudden Shelter is quite interesting. While the general range of the specimens overlaps with those of modern specimens from nearby Ivie Creek, there I is a marked trend through time. Values from both Va/Ionia cyclophorella Sterki, and Oreohelix strigosa depressa (Cockrell) trend toward an 180 depleted state near the modern values from an initially higher value. Yapp (1979:630-631,634) notes slight differences in isotopic fractionation between these two I species through time, but more importantly, he argues that the general trend represents long term climate change in the immediate area, with more positive 6180 values around 7100-7800 B.P. representing warmer and/or drier conditions than today while the youngest fossil samples from the shelter, dated to I 3300-3500 B.P., represent conditions very close to those found at present in the area (Yapp 1979:634). Working at about the same time as Yapp, Magaritz and Heller (1980) studied the oxygen isotope I record from Levantina caesareana, an Israeli land snail. The results showed that the desert snails, which live in an environment with less than 300 millimeters annual rainfall, were enriched by 2 %0 relative to snails from a more moderate climate. The study also compared modern snails to fossil snails from the I same area, suggesting that the desert had expanded, migrating northward, in the Early Holocene. 254 I I

## Page 272

I I In a 1981 study, Magaritz and his colleagues studied the isotopic variations in an Israeli land snail, Xeropicta vestalis. They were able to show a strong correlation between the 0180 content of the outer (most recent) whorl of the shells with the mean monthly temperatures during the collection period. Their I conclusions suggest that land snail shell 0180 is strongly related to waters at the land-air interface which are in tum related to the meteorological parameters of temperature and evaporation rate, but not humidity (Magaritz et al. 1981:103). I More recently, L6Colle (1985) collected data on terrestrial gastropods from western Europe and north Africa to test these specimens as an environmental indicator. L6Colle sampled land snails from I more than two dozen different species from 125 localities extending from Oslo, Norway, to Dj. Bouthaleb, Algeria, and from Llangybi, Wales, east to Bern, Switzerland. He found good correlations of the 0180 mean of the shells with altitude (noting a strong discontinuity beginning above approximately I 1200-1300 meters above median sea level), annual mean temperature (except for populations i'n areas with annual mean temperatures lower than 6 • C), and the annual 0180 mean of precipitation. Furthermore, the results showed some variation in both slope and point of intercept for the linear relationships depending I upon whether the snails were from Mediterranean or oceanic and Alpine climates (L6Colle 1985). While aquatic mollusks have generally been ignored since the 1970s, one major study conducted I during the mid-1980s has shown the complexities of paleoclimatic inferences from aquatic species. In that study, Paul Abell (1985) focussed on the oxygen isotope ratios of three species of gastropods across the continent of Africa. As he points out, the particular gastropods sampled were' chosen because they are widely distributed in Africa and are known to grow in equilibrium with their environments. More I importantly, he points out a number of problems with the estimation of paleoclimates from aquatic mollusks which are worth repeating here. These points, as laid out by Abell (1985:184-185), include: I 1) Freshwater mollusk shells are primarily if not exclusively aragonite, an unstable crystalline form which tends to recrystallize and thus reequilibriate with the new environment. I 2) While modem mollusks can be collected alive, ancient molluscan samples may have been redeposited and the original sediments reworked. I 3) Most freshwater gastropods only have a brief lifetime. The species chose by Abell only live for a year or less and thus do not integrate climatic conditions over any length of I time. 4) Since some species of mollusks can aestivate successfully, the isotopic fractionation of I the shell may represent a very localized, highly evaporative environment such as an ephemeral pond or even an irrigation canal which is not, in tum, representative of the larger environment. I 5) Analysis of oxygen isotope fractionation is generally incapable of distinguishing between temperature effects from evaporative effects. I To determine the potential for paleoclimatic reconstruction, Abell sampled modem gastropods from approximately 80 locations in Africa. The internal consistency of the figures was quite high, with replicate samples from single individuals showing per mil various of ±0.1 while different individuals I from a single locality showed generally no more than ±0.3%0 variation. Overall, however, the 0180 255 I I


![Image 1 - Page 272](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p272_img1.png)

## Page 273

I values range from a low of -8.38%0 (vs. PDB) in Algeria at 36.5 degrees north latitude to a high of I +4.07%0 in Nigeria at 10 degrees north latitude. In general, the results roughly show a latitudinal correlation of 0180 with precipitation values as suggested by both Dansgaard (1964) and Yurtsever (1975), but there are several marked exceptions, including areas in Mozambique, South Africa, Lake Malawi, I the Sahel, and East Africa (Abell 1985: 188). Abell concludes that a close fit to Dansgaard's and Yurtsever's values is probably only possible away from major land mass areas and the data from African land snails shows a complex mosaic of environmental parameters which do not always reflect a simple I wet/dry, hot/cold, or latitude effect. In addition to these, and to the general effect of altitude (LeColle 1985), Abell also noted the correlation with permanent vegetation cover with high negative oxygen isotope ratios. And finally, Abell suggests that, in general, high negative 0180 values will correlate with I areas of substantial rainfall while high positive values will correlate with areas where evaporation is the predominant method of control (Abell 1985:192). In all cases, however, he noted the potential for substantial isotopic variation subject to highly variable local environmental conditions. , I A number of more recent studies of isotopic fractionation in land snail have continued to suggest that land snails would be a good paleoclimatic or paleoenvironmental indicator but none have really I attempted such a study. All have, in fact, focussed on the variation in modern shells and their relationship to their environment. And all of these studies have pointed out the problematic nature of interpretations due to the compound parameters which influence body water and isotopic fractionation. I Goodfriend and Magaritz (1987), for example, studied isotopic fractionation in several land snail species along a strong rainfall gradient in the Levant region of Israel, concluding that the basic pattern of oxygen isotope variation in shell carbonates was a function of the 0180 content of environmental water but could not separate clearly between rainwater, water vapor, or dew. In the same study, they concluded that the I o 13C values of the shell carbonates could be a useful indicator of the presence or absence of C4 plants (Goodfriend and Magaritz 1987:386-387). In the final analysis, they conclude that the use of 0180 from land snail shell carbonate as a paleoenvironmental indicator is problematic. I A more recent study by Goodfriend et al. (1989) is more positive about the possibilities for paleoclimatic reconstruction. In this study the team analyzed day-to-day and within-day variation in the I body water of the land snail 1heba pisana from the southern coastal plain of Israel. They found that variation in the 0180 content of body water was primarily a function of atmospheric water vapor and consequent dew from the nearby Mediterranean rather than precipitation per se. While they note that this I is a potential problem in estimating paleorainfall values from fossil land snail 0180 in coastal areas, they indicate that as continental climates prevail and as atmospheric water vapor equilibrates with rainfall, then the values will more closely approximate 0180 values of rainfall. Their final word suggests that the I potential problems in the analysis of land snail shell oxygen isotope composition are less than any other sources of data on the isotopic composition of paleorainfall (Goodfriend et al. 1989:3220). I In the final analysis, both terrestrial and aquatic mollusks have been studied with respect to their ability to yield data on past rainfall and temperatures. Unfortunately, as noted already, there has been much work with modern species and their potential relationship to the environmental parameters but little I analysis of fossil specimens. Although mussels yielded reasonably good results for Keith et al. (1964), there have been few studies of freshwater mussels as a source of paleoclimatic information. The studies by Stuiver (1968, 1970) and Fritz and his colleagues (Fritz and Poplawski 1974; Fritz et al. 1975), which I both utilized fossil freshwater mollusks from non-cultural contexts, are the major studies of continental paleoclimates from freshwater mollusks. Despite the fact that land snails present clear problems in interpretation and represent an extremely short period of time from the perspective of studies of past I climates, they have dominated the literature. The only studies which actually made use of specimens 256 I I

## Page 274

I I collected from archeological sites were those by Yapp (1979), who analyzed fossil land snails from Sudden Shelter in Utah, and by Magaritz and Heller (1981), who studied fossil land snails from several sites in Israel. I THE ANALYTICAL METHOD I A total of twenty different shells were submitted to Geochron Laboratories, Inc., in Cambridge, Mass., for analysis of the isotopic ratios for both 180 and 13C. Samples were selected from each of the major shell features at the site as well as from smaller features which were chosen to provide a more even I coverage of the entire stratigraphic section of the major cultural portion of the site. Paired samples were submitted from most of the sampled middens. In one case (Feature 25), only a single sample was available in what was considered a key zone, while for another feature (Feature 36), four different I samples were selected. Table 28 below shows the features, elevations, cultural zones, and prc5jected ages (since none of the shells or middens are directly dated, these projections are based on dates interpolated between humate and/or bulk soil radiocarbon dates as discussed elsewhere in this report) from which I samples were drawn. Overall, the samples reflect nine different features in five different cultural zones from the site. The total estimated age range of the sample extends from about 760 to 3570 B.P. Note that the projected dates are primarily based upon bulk soil and humate samples and are likely to be somewhat different than actual calendar years; the error here could easily be several hundred years. In I addition, as noted above, all of the feature dates are based on interpolation of elevations between dated layers; clearly this will result in additional error. Nonetheless, no drastic anomalies or discontinuities were noted in the cultural zone at the sites and the general trends are expected to be reasonably linear. I To avoid the problem of short term paleoclimatic fluctuations which might be present using only a small portion of the shell (and a potentially serious problem when using short-lived gastropods, for I example), one half of the entire shell, from posterior to anterior edge, was separated and mixed thoroughly for the actual sample selection. If carefully mixed, this should roughly approximate an average of conditions throughout the life of the clam. The actual life span of freshwater mollusks from I Texas is not well known but, in general, many may live some twenty to thirty years (Raymond Neck, personal communication, 1993). Thus the target sample period is thought to be roughly twenty to thirty years, although to what extent this may reflect differential growth throughout the stages of life is not I clear; it is likely that the life span is not reflected evenly. Obviously, sampling a significant portion of this life span should even out seasonal as well as extreme annual variation in temperature and evaporation. This roughly approximates the method utilized by Keith et al (1964) who took a section I across the dorsal surface of the shell. Because of the relatively recent age of the fossil shells (compared to the hundreds of thousands or I millions of years of age of many shells sampled in marine paleothermometry), it was thought that isotopic replacement would not be a major problem. Nonetheless, the shells are thought to be primarily, if not exclusively, aragonite which undergoes a slightly different fractionation than calcite and which tends to I be replaced by calcite through time. Only a careful examination of the mineralogical composition of the shells in the study would determine if this were a factor contributing to potential error. I Differences between species in water utilization and mineralogy could indeed contribute some error to the study. Amblema plicata is the most common species at the site and nine of the samples belong to this group (Table 28). Ten of the remainder belong to the genus Quadrula, including six Q. mortoni, three Q. houstonensis , and one Q. aurea. One specimen of Lampsilis hydiana was also submitted. Most I of the species identifications were made by Raymond Neck; a very few were made by the editors of this 257 I I

I I volume with assistance from Neck's identified sample collection. In the four samples where Amblema plicata and Quadrula sp. were submitted together, the former I had lower oxygen isotopic values in three of four cases; the average difference between the genera is 0.25%c. This is less than the average intra-specific differences found in three features where multiple specimens of the same species were submitted (A. plicata variation for three specimens was 0.7%c and I 1.0%c for four specimens; Q. mononi maximum range was 0.6%c for three specimens). The specimen of Lampsilis hydiana had one of the highest 0180 values in the study, a value 1.0%c greater than the Quadrula mononi submitted from the same level. No attempt has been made to correct for these apparent I specific effects; too little is known to create an accurate correction factor. The overall effect is relatively minor, however, and does not appear to affect the results reported below. I As noted above, samples were submitted to Geochron Laboratories for analysis. The following brief description of the method was provided by Marshall Otter of Geochron. I The samples first needed to be cleaned of surrounding matrix materials and I accomplished this simply by brushing it off with a small wire brush. At the same time, I could not help removing part of the outer crust of the shell which is discolored and probably isotopically I unrepresentative of the original, unaltered shell anyway. I then crushed one half of the shell and sieved it in order to produce the -200 mesh (-75 microns) powder required for acid digestion. I split the shell as shown below on the assumption that each half would represent I the isotopic character of the whole shell. We react about 50 mg of the carbonate powder so· under vacuum in 100 percent orthophosphoric acid at C and we analyze the resulting C~ on a mass spectrometer. (Otter 1993) I STUDY RESULTS The final results of the study suggest some isotopic variation among the samples submitted. Table I 28 shows the 0180 and o 13C values compared with the projected age of the samples. Figure 90 shows this o o variation graphically for 0180 while Figure 91 shows the variation in 13C. The overall mean for 13C is -6.8%0 and standard deviation is 0.8. The overall mean for 0180 is -3.4%c and standard deviation is I 0.5. Although no comparable figures are available for 013C, the 0180 value compares favorably to I oxygen isotope rainfall figures recorded for the general area. Various studies, including Keith et al. (1964), Stuiver (1970), and LeColle (1985) have noted that the carbonate values for PDB are generally close to the SMOW values for water. For example, the 0180 mean is quite close to the -4.0%c SMOW I rainfall contour as reported by Yurtsever (1975) for the general area of North-Central Texas. It is also nearly identical to the weighted mean of -3.6%c SMOW for three years between 1973 and 1975 for Waco, Texas (IAEA 1979). Annual weighted mean 5180 values for Waco ranged from a high of-2.93%c I in 1974 to a low of -5.69%c in 1972. Note that the annual extremes for Waco just during this four year period are greater than the range of 5180 values recorded for the Denton Creek samples. I While the internal variation of the oxygen samples is relatively high, it is still somewhat less than the overall variation within the total sample, suggesting an internal consistency to the measurements. As noted in the previous section, there is some slight hint of specific fractionation effects but these are I generally minimal. It is also possible that some variation may result from using specimens of different sizes and thus probably different ages. Carbon isotope variation, on the other hand, while showing some I 259 I

## Page 279

I I interesting mean value trends, exhibits much greater ranges. With all the possibility for errors, there is at least some suggestion in the data, however, that the isotopic composition values could indeed be a general environmental indicator. It is tempting to simply I say that the values represent direct environmental temperatures (as reflected in stream water temperatures) averaged over the life of the shell but all of the attendant cause and effect relationships between air temperature, water temperature, oxygenation of the water, and shell production activity are poorly known I at best. Since it is not clear whether the factors of precipitation, temperature, or evaporation would be the I dominant variable in oxygen isotope fractionation in a small stream, it is only possible to generalize in a very limited manner about the exact meaning of the results. Nonetheless, assuming that higher isotopic values represent conditions which are cooler, wetter, less evaporative, or a combination of the three, there I are some interesting trends including a possible warm/dry peak around 2850 B.P., and a possible return to warmer drier conditions beginning after 1500 B.P. (note that since the dates are based partly on humate samples, these estimated dates may be older than the actual date). Between 2500 and 1500 B.P., climates I may have been somewhat cooler and wetter. Perhaps most surprising of all is the apparent cool/wet conditions seen around 3500 B.P. I Figure 92 shows a comparison between the mean values for the Denton Creek isotope values and values for the same period in two northern U.S. lakes as recorded by Stuiver (1970). While neither curve is identical in slope, there are several striking correspondences in partial slopes as well as peaks and I valleys. Note that the actual values for Texas are much higher than the more northerly comparisons, something that should be expected based on the latitude and continental effects in isotope depletion in rainfall as noted by Dansgaard (1964) and Yurtsever (1975). I While there is as yet no standard, unquestioned climate record yet established for the late Holocene in Texas, these data do parallel some of those which have been suggested for the general Texas area. For the period prior to 2500 B.P., Toomey et al. (1993:309-310) among many others have suggested a I climate generally warmer and drier than present. While this is perhaps reflected in the values between around 2600 to 3200 B.P. for Features 9 and 16, the earliest sample, from Feature 44 in Unit 8, seems to reflect a cooler and wetter environment and is somewhat out of character with the suggested I environment. After this anomalous early sample, however, the data look quite good. Toomey et al. (1993), as well as Bryant and Holloway (1985), Holloway et al. (1987), and Hall (1988), suggest a wetter/cooler period after 2500 B.P. which is, in fact, supported by the mussel isotope data. Finally, the I rise in 180 values after 1500 B.P. suggests a return to warmer and drier conditions which is also supported by Toomey et al. (1993) as well as Huebner (1990) and Hall (1988, 1990). I Comparative data from four years of 6180 samples from Waco suggest some potential sources for the ultimate variation. The highest isotope values, interpreted here as representing warmer and/or drier conditions may represent years in which summer rainfall, with markedly higher 6180 values, is I predominant. Conversely, the lowest 6180 values in the Waco data are found in September and October. While this could potentially suggest that the seasonality of rainfall alone is important in climate (this has been suggested by numerous scholars in the field -Joel Gunn, personal communication, 1987; Michael I Blum;personal communication, 1994), a caution should be added that we know relatively little about the seasonality of shell production among freshwater species in Texas. ## Page 281

I I In conclusion, although the sample of materials collected from the isotope study is quite small and the technique perhaps not well provei;i, particularly for freshwater riverine species, the correspondences between oxygen isotope data and potential paleoclimate is generally good. In contrast, the carbon isotope I data from the shells, a data set which has been problematic for nearly every researcher in the field, is not easily interpreted at this time. It is too soon to consider any attempt to quantify the variation in oxygen isotopes, particularly since it is not clear to what degree temperature, rainfall, evaporation, or even I seasonality of rainfall may be mixed. Nonetheless, the directionality of variation seems clear enough and preliminary results suggest that the data may be useful in providing a base line for understanding the responses of prehistoric peoples to their environment. A broader comparative perspective on this is attempted in the concluding chapter of this report. I I I I I I I I I I I I I 264 I I

## Page 282

I I MUSSEL SHELL PROCUREMENT IN NORTH-CENTRAL TEXAS Dana Anthony I NORTH-CENTRAL TEXAS MUSSEL SHELL SITES One of the major feature types and one of the most visible subsistence resources at 41DL270 are I th~ mussel shell concentrations observed eroding out of both sides of the sloped walls of the drainage ditch. These were found at practically all elevations or zones on these slopes although they are clustered in· the upper Cultural Zones I through III and represent a resource item from the early Late Archaic through the terminal Late Prehistoric I. So far, a Late Prehistoric II component has not been clearly I defined at 41DL270. Discrete shell concentrations have been identified from elevations ranging from 140.83 to 142.60 meters amsl, or 1.68 meters in depth. This elevation range correlates well with the ranges determined by Gose (this volume) to be the highest in cultural occupation based on the magnetic I susceptibility of the soil. The deepest shell concentration was discovered in Trench M6 (and found to extend into Unit 8) at a depth extending below the bottom of the ditch. As this was one of the deepest excavations at the site, there is the possibility of shell concentrations buried even deeper, representing I earlier time periods. The fact that shell concentrations were not located in other backhoe trenches does not negate this possibility, although such features are expected to be rare. It should be pointed out that other trenches at higher elevations failed to expose one of these features even though numerous features -1 were exposed on the slope surface within view of the excavators. The fact that the only trench which did expose one of these features happens to be fairly deep supports the possible existence of more features at least at this basal elevation if not even deeper within the floodplain. This lowest shell concentration, I Feature 44, lies within a zone estimated to date to the early part of the Late Archaic or even to the later Middle Archaic. I Most researchers in North-Central Texas have encountered mussel shell features during archeological investigations along the floodplain. Although relatively few excavations have been conducted at such sites, these features are often readily apparent during surveys of exposed floodplain I deposits either on natural cutbanks or from construction or quarrying activities. In the following sections, some of the major mussel shell sites from the North-Central Texas area are briefly described. This is followed by a discussion of these find~. I Early Excavations During the 1940s, two features described as refuse pits at 41DL12 on the Elm Fork of the Trinity I were investigated. They were excavated by Harris, the Kirklands, and Hatzenbueler and were found to contain mussel shells along with burned rock, charcoal, ash, and artifacts of Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric age in one of them (Kirkland et al. 1949). A third pit later discovered at the site was I excavated by Hughes and Harris (1951). They reported burned rock, mussel shell, and a shell hoe. Whether or not these pit features were initially used to process the mussels, either for simply opening the shell or further roasting or baking is not clear. I Two important points were raised by these excavations. The excavators of the first two pits made mention of charring on several of the exterior shells near the umbo. It was also mentioned that the same I pattern was observed on mussel shell from the Butler Hole site in Collin County. Heat treating is a known method by which to open a live mussel. Whether or not further cooking preparation was done is speculative. Nevertheless, the observation was made which supported the initial processing strategy I of "heat-treating" the shell to get at the meat inside. 265 I I


![Image 1 - Page 282](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p282_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 282*


---

## Page 283

I Another point raised by these excavations is the nature of the pits themselves and if indeed the I function, or at least final function, was that of a refuse pit. The fact that other artifact material was also found in these pits supports a disposal pit hypothesis. The people eating the mussels may have later discarded them into a pit with ash or charcoal or burned rock remnants in order to bury a malodorous I trash item such as fish and shellfish. This practice has been proposed for other groups as well as observed ethnographically. However, not all mussel shell is associated with pits, many are simply discarded on the old land surface. I East Fork of the Trinity River I Site X41KF8 is described as a small but dense scatter of cracked quartzite, quartzite flakes, and mussel shells eroding out of the west bank of the East Fork of the Trinity River. Depth of the site was about 1.20 meters below ground surface. Total exposure was about 1 square meter. The site is presently below river water level (Richner 1976: 13). I Another mussel shell concentration was found at site X41KF9. Similar to X41KF8 but lacking lithics, this site is on a low flat shelf at the base of the river bank. The shell was observed to extend I along the bank about 20 to 30 meters (Richner 1976: 13). Site X41DL2 was regarded as the most significant site found during the survey. Orange burned I clay, mussel shells, and cracked quartzite pebbles were exposed in the cut bank of the East Fork. The exposure extended about 7 meters along the bank 40 to 60 centimeters below the present floodplain surface (Richner 1976:13-14). I A number of other sites were recorded with shell lenses. Site X41KF10 was a scatter of cracked quartzite, quartzite flakes, and shell extending about 50 meters along the base of the bank 1.5 to 2 meters I below the floodplain surface. Site 41KF11 was a small scatter of shells and two quartzite flakes extending about 1 by 2 meters in the bank, 1 meter below the floodplain surface. Site X41DL4 was a small scatter of shell and bone extending for 5 meters, 1 to 1.5 meters below the floodplain surface I (Richner 1976:15). Site X41DL5 was also a small scatter of shell similar to above (Richner 1976:16). Site X41DL6, the largest recorded in the survey, was a dense lithic scatter located along the bank of the East Fork. Site X41DL7 is small shell scatter similar to X41DL4 and X41DL5. No lithics were I recorded at either site. The Trinity Project I The Trinity Project, including the area for the proposed (but never built) Tennessee Colony Lake, was conducted in the mid-1970s. It documented 73 sites with mussel shell lenses (McGregor 1978: 158). These were described as consisting mainly of shells and charcoal, although a few features contained other I materials as well. Each shell feature was described as a single lens denoting a single deposit; some sites have multiple lenses apparent in the profiles. The sites were located on the river bank where they are generally easily observable, although access by foot can be tricky on a steep slope. Richner (1978:246) I states that mussel shells were also recorded from terrace sites to the south of the known cut bank distribution mentioned above, but that in the terrace localities stream bank exposure was not good enough to determine if shell lens sites occurred in those environs as well. I Of the 73 sites located, three (41AN49, 41FT55, and 41AN53) were chosen for testing. Aside from the potential of these sites to answer the research objectives outlined by Richner (1978:219-220), I 266 I I


![Image 1 - Page 283](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p283_img1.png)

## Page 284

I I accessibility was a major consideration. These sites have proven to be well stratified as they are covered by subsequent alluvium thus sealing the features without disturbing them. For the most part, the lenses with only shell and charcoal were interpreted as reflective of single occupations or activities (Shaw I 1978a:224). Two of the lenses tested at 41FT55 revealed the presence of shallow hearths containing charcoal and burned rock, and also bone, lithic material, and in one, pecan shells, all of which denote a variety of subsistence activities (Shaw 1978a:231-232). McGregor (1978: 162) notes the absence of fish I bone in these shell lens features so close to the river as odd, but offers the collection method as a possible reason for their absence. Another important issue brought up was the question of seasonality but growth rate studies have only been conducted on clams from the lower Trinity River, although McGregor does I infer that results from other faunal and vegetation studies from shell lens sites would by useful in reconstructing seasonal exploitation patterns (McGregor 1978: 163). I A study of the density of these features revealed the 73 shell lens sites to equal 1 per 0.8 river mile, which Richner (1978:246) stated was similar to the densities found on the East Fork of the Trinity (note that a site may contain numerous shell lenses). In all, 8,641 mussel shell valves were collected I from the Trinity Project (Richner and Bagot 1978:266). Charring or discoloration of the shells were tabulated with. 2 to 10 percent of the total exhibiting such treatment; the shell lenses with hearths displayed no more charring than those without hearths (Richner and Bagot 1978:271). This supports the I possibility of heat treatment at some stage of mussel shell processing, which may have primarily served to open the shells as well as steam cook them. Charcoal from site 41AN49 was radiocarbon dated to A.D. 206 ± 64 (uncorrected) or the Late Archaic. The dates from 41FT55 ranged from 2238 ± 72 B.C. I to 266 ± 86 B.C. covering the Middle Archaic through the Late Archaic (Richner 1978). Lake Ray Roberts I The initial Lake Ray Roberts survey resulted in the definition of several site types related to mussel procurement (Cliff et al. 1982:7-11). Site Type 4a shows a primary reliance on mussels and has an assemblage of primary and tertiary flakes, although the mussel counts far surpass the artifact counts. I Sites range in size from 0.13 to 0.61 hectares, and are located on low terraces of the Elm Fork. They are also known as seasonal macroband camps (Cliff et al. 1982:7-10). Type 4b was represented by one site (41DN175). Aside from mussel shells, bone is also present as well as a high proportion of primary I and secondary lithic reduction activities (Cliff et al. 1982:7-10). The third type, (Prehistoric Site Type 4c) is defined as relying primarily on mussel gathering activities along with the presence of fire-cracked rocks. Another criterion is the lack of tertiary lithic reduction (Cliff et al. 1982:7-11). Sites assigned I to this site type are 41DN103 (actually included with the macroband category due to the size), 41DN197, 41DN199, and 41CO134. Sizes range from 0.06 to 0.49 hectares. Significantly, all of these sites are located on the floodplain (Cliff et al. 1982:7-11). I Another site type (Prehistoric Site Type 4d) also shows a focus on mussel shell collecting activities, but, in contrast to the above, has an association with tertiary lithic reduction. Fire-cracked rocks are also I present (Cliff et al. 1982:7-11). At Lake Ray Roberts the sites in this category are: 41DN79, 41DN81, 41DN82, and 41CO139. These sites range in size from 0.09 to 0.50 hectares and are located either on the floodplain of the Elm Fork or on the lower terrace of a small Elm Form tributary (Cliff 1985:7-11). I Site 41DN101 was originally defined as a Prehistoric Site Type 4a, having a primary reliance on mussels. The site is 0.13 hectares in size and like the others in this group is located on a low terrace of I the Elm Fork (Cliff et al. 1982:7-10). Further investigations also found the mussel shell to be concentrated and intact below the surface mixed with fire-cracked rock which the researchers defined as I 267 I
