## Page 285

I a midden. Diagnostic artifacts included arrow points and Nocona Plain ceramics (Skinner and Baird I 1985:4-35). Faunal material was also collected, but no apparent association between the bone and shell was recognized, thus suggesting reoccupation of the site at different times for different activities (Skinner and Baird 1985:4-35). It is unclear if the scatter of burned rock was associated with the mussel shell, I but the researchers do point out that the concentration of shell decreased away from the rock concentration (Skinner and Baird 1985:4-35). I Site 41DN103 is interpreted as a macroband musselling camp, (originally described as a "musselling base camp") located on the floodplain of Pond Creek (Skinner and Baird 1985:4-85). Interpretations from further testing at this site revealed mussel to occur in most of the units, further I indicating procurement of this resource was a major activity at this small site in addition to hunting (Skinner and Baird 1985:4-94). I Because neither the species nor the counts or densities for these mussels were given, it is difficult to access the interpretations. However, at least at the two sites investigated above, the shells were found to occur alongside burned rock, Iithic debris, and hone, which would indicate a secondary refuse I function. Aquilla Lake I Perhaps one of the more detailed studies of a mussel shell site was that from the McKenzie Site (41Hlll5) at Aquilla Lake (Brown 1987e:39-1-39-17). The site, located on the bank of Hackberry Creek, consisted of a mussel shell midden which was found to occur within and on top of the lowest of I two buried soils. The deposit appeared to represent a 10 to 15 centimeter-thick lens. Very few other artifacts were recovered from the excavations of this feature. A total of 995 shells were identified to nine different species. The two overwhelmingly dominant species were Amblema plicata and Lampsilis I hydiana; however, there apparently was no particular pattern to the distribution of species within the lens. In fact, Brown quotes Raymond Neck, the malacologist, as stating that a distribution of species such as that at the McKenzie Site could be found naturally (Brown 1987e:39-10). These species typically are I found in slow, shallow waters such as Hackberry Creek was before inundation by lake waters. The observed breakage patterns from both the 1975 SMU excavations (Lynott and Peter 1977:52), I and the 1982 TAS excavations (Brown 1987e:39-12) documented breaks at right angles to the umbo. It is unclear whether or not the shell midden is a secondary discard locality away from the initial processing and consumption locality in order to provide distance from a smelly situation. Furthermore, it was I deduced that the shells were tossed into mud since so many were lying on edge. Careful examination of the surrounding matrix showed no sign of vertical shrink swelling (Brown 1987e). The paucity of snails from this locality would indicate the mussel shells accrued rapidly hindering the development of I snail populations (Neck 1987: 12). The lack of snails may also support the suggestion that they were covered quickly. I An analysis of the total protein content of the shells was calculated according to the formula devised by Parmalee and Klippel (1974) and found that, at most, the total number of mussel shells collected (1826 including the SMU counts) would have only fed a group of 10 to 14 people for a day or I a family of five for two to three days. If one considers the shells to be an adjunct of perhaps half the diet instead of a sole source, then it was estimated a larger group of 20 to 28 could be represented by the remains. In short, there were too many shells for a single family to consume in one day, and too few I shells for a larger group to have stayed longer (Brown 1987e:39-16-39-17). Finally it is suggested that 268 I I

## Page 286

I I mussel were eaten during times of stress due to diminishing resource bases which are postulated for the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric periods. The dating of the McKenzie Site is estimated to fall within the terminal Late Archaic or the early Late Prehistoric period (Brown 1987f:40-4). I Evidence from the McDonald Site (41HI105), another floodplain site on Hackberry Creek within the Aquilla drainage, indicates mussel shell remains with a similar distribution of species as those I identified at the McKenzie Site (Brown 1989:225). According to Neck (1987:6), the mussel species are indicative of a moderate size stream with calcareous waters, a soft mud and/or sand bottom, and water flow sufficient to result in clean water. Converse to the rapid buildup and probable coverup of the mussel I shell lens at the McKenzie site discussed above, the McDonald Site shows an abundance of land snail indicating a longer exposure time. Following the North-Central Texas chronology developed by Prikryl (1990), the dates from the primary mussel shell lenses at the McDonald Site would fall within the Late I Archaic and the Late Prehistoric I periods. - At the McDonald Site, a total of 1240 mussels collected from all seasons of work were interpreted I to be a secondary resource, yet one of importance despite the low protein yield when compared with other secondary resources (Brown 1987e, 1989). This view was further supported by Neck's analysis, which suggests a possibility of resource depletion due to the increasingly small size of the shells through time, I with the uppermost levels containing only very small ones (Neck 1987:6). Only a few shells displayed evidence of burning (Neck 1987:13). To quote: "An assumption can I be made that the people who left behind this midden debris either ate most of the clams raw, cooked them without charring the shell, or cooked them following removal of the meat from the shell". I Finally, Neck surmised that the greater diversity of the archeological mussel fauna than that found today could indicate climatic variations. The mussel from both Aquilla sites indicate larger water volume than found in Hackberry Creek at the time of the study (Neck 1987: 13). I Richland Creek Site 41FT193, excavated as part of the Richland Creek project, was exposed in the floodplain of I Richland Creek. When first tested, it consisted of three hearths and a discontinuous mussel shell lens, all in the same dark clay stratum. Dates of nutshells from two of the hearths were radiocarbon dated to ± ± A.D. 170 70 and A.D. 320 150 (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:29). Feature 401, encountered at the I base of Backhoe trench 3, was a hearth defined by charcoal and ash that also contained burned and unburned bone, mussel shell, wood charcoal, and carbonized nutshell which was radiocarbon dated to A.D. 190 ± 70 (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:39). Flotation analysis of this feature revealed three species I of mussel, much of which was burned (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:40). A mussel shell concentration was also located west of this trench not far from the creek bank on Occupation Level 1, and is thought to probably date to the nineteenth or twentieth centuries (Pheasant 1987:35). Most of the mussel from I the upper levels was concentrated to the east of Feature 202, a burned clay feature with charcoal dated ± to A.D. 1170 150 (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:39,49). The mussel shell lens originally observed in the cutbank was found to continue in the lower levels of the excavations, which coincided with the lower I dark clay stratum in which this feature was originally located (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:49-50). As indicated above by the radiocarbon dates collected from hearths from this stratum, this lens dates to the Late Archaic and possibly up to the Late Prehistoric I. I I 269 I

## Page 287

I I Although mussel shells were recovered from most of the units excavated at site 41Ff193 (Fullington 1987a:46 [in McGregor and Bohlin 1987), few could be said to be from a concentration or lens. The archeological samples from this site were found to be essentially the same as those present I today. Fullington points out that this indicates the flow of Richland Creek has changed little since A.D. 200 (1987a:46). He describes the creek as "a typical Texas stream with alternating areas of relatively deep, slow moving water with riffle areas with shallow, faster running water" (Fullington 1987a:46). This is also indicated by the preferred habitats of the 11 identified species from this site (1987a:47, Table I 4-8). Based on the size of the creek and the continuity of conditions described above, Fullington suggests that the creek never supported large population sizes. Of those shells complete enough to identify, most were found to be subadults. From this, one can infer that the people exploiting Richland Creek from this I site were placing a pressure on the mollusc population (Fullington 1987a:46). Particular localities at 41NV173 were shown to contain greater amounts of mussel shell than other I localities. This site is located on a terrace ridge at the edge of the Chambers Creek floodplain {lrv~ne and McGregor 1987:97). Although no one unit or feature produced large numbers of shells, several units or features should be looked at as a whole when the shells are from the same level or zone and may I indeed represent a single deposit. Analysis by Fullington (1987b: 120) identified 55 individual specimens to six different species, which are still common in the creeks and streams in the area today. Fullington states that these species are typical of streams with alternating areas of relatively deep slow moving water I and shallow faster water, and that the shells could have come from either Little Cedar Creek or Chambers Creek. I Excavations at Bird Point Island (41FT201) recovered relatively few mussel shells from this important residential site. As this site received more attention than any other within the project area (Bruseth and Martin 1987a:7), this is somewhat surprising. Fullington identified a total of nine different I species he stated were common to both pool and riffle areas and that these species were still to be found along Richland Creek (Fullington 1987c:140). These were recovered primarily from two features described as roasting pits (Features 45 and 102), from Zone 3 in Feature 1, the Wylie Pit, and from the I south midden (Fullington 1987c:140-141). Investigations at Adams Ranch (41NV177) revealed the greatest number and density of mussel shells were confmed to hearths or hearth-like features within Zones 2c, 2d,and 3 within the main Feature I 1 (which is divided into three major zones). Zones 2c and 2d date to A.O. 800 to 1000 (Martin 1987a:228-231). Zone 2d had the highest density of bone and mussel. In all, three deer and 193 mussels were represented. The zone is described as an intrusive pit dug into Zone 2c. The deposit is I characterized as refuse from a short-term occupation, perhaps even a single event such as a feast. Fire cracked rock and charcoal were also numerous in these two zones, which along with the bone and shell indicate cooking and/or roasting as primary activities (Martin 1987a:231). Zone 3 actually had the I highest number of mussel shell with 254 (Martin 1987a:252). This zone was high in bone, shell, and burned rock, but low in artifacts, suggesting roasting activities as well as refuse disposal (Martin 1987b:231). I A sample of mussels was taken from Zones 1, 2c, 2d, and 3 in the center of Feature 1, covering all the occupation periods at the site (Fullington 1987d:249-251). These consisted of seven species out I of a total of 557 individuals. These species listed in order of frequency are Quadrula mortoni, Lampsilis hydiana, Amblema plicata, Quadrula apiculata, Tritogonia verrucosa, Truncilla truncata, and Potamilus purpuratus. ## Page 288

I I As mentioned above, the highest frequency was from Zone 3, dating to A.O. 200 to 700. Frequencies were shown to decrease after A.O. 1000. The analysis illustrated the species remained I constant over time and are the same as can be found today. Fullington (1987d:249) claims these mussel species are poor indicators of change and therefore cannot be used to gauge environmental changes. A. plicata can be found in pools while the other species are found in riffle areas. From this, Fullington I (1987d:249) suggests that the shallow water species were exploited more than the deep water species and probably from summer to fall during periods when the creek was low. Still, the small number indicates they were not exploited on a regular basis (Fullington 1987d:251). Importantly, a lot of the mussels from Adams Ranch are adults, implying that the resource was not a major source of food (Fullington I 1987d:251), or at least in this case, not overexploited. Lake Joe Pool I At least two features were excavated at 41DL189 along the Mountain Creek Drainage that could be defined as shell lenses or at least containing remnants of them (Peter and McGregor 1988b:50-58). I The authors state this was the only site recorded in an alluvial setting during the survey for Joe Pool Lake (Peter and McGregor 1988b:57). Feature 2 was a rock-lined hearth with a scatter of burned rock and mussel shell. Feature 7 was described as mussel shell mixed with gravels which contained a deer bone, I two flakes, and charcoal. Analysis of the shells from this site identified four different species, which are listed in the order of prominence: Lampsilis hydiana (Lea), Amblema plicata (Say), Lampsilis radiati siliquoidea (Barnes), and Lampsilis teres (Rafinesque). The authors regard the mussels as an I supplementary food source even though they were present in greater numbers than other subsistence remains (Peter and McGregor 1988b:51). I The results of these investigations suggest that 41DL189 represents short-term occupations in an environment of rapid aggradation along Mountain Creek (Peter and McGregor 1988b:56-57). They include the threat of flooding as well as the paucity of artifacts and ephemeral nature of the features as I evidence for short-term occupation. Deer was the dominant faunal resource at the site. However, aquatic resources were apparently of some significance due to the recovery of the mussel shell as well as turtle and fish. The researchers postulate a summer occupation based on the presence of a fetal deer bone, I carbonized remains of a Psoralea tuber, and the exploitation of turtle and mussel, which are believed to be more available in the summer (Peter and McGregor 1988b:57,337). Charcoal radiocarbon dated between 510 ± 60 B.C. and 110 ± 100 B.C. and dart points indicate a Late Archaic date for this site I (Peter and McGregor 1988b:57). Another site investigated at Joe Pool was 41DL199, which was located on a terrace remnant above the Mountain Creek drainage (Peter and McGregor 1988b:59). Of particular interest here is Feature 4, I a burned rock and mussel shell cluster lacking any indication of a pit outline (Peter and McGregor 1988b:85). Only three mussel species, Amblema plicata, Lampsilis hydiana, and Lampsilis radiata siliquoidea, were identified. Also considered to be associated with Feature 4 were an arrow point and I dart point, a uniface, abrader, and 59 flakes (predominately of quartzite and heat-treated). A small amount of bone, including deer, turkey, and turtle, was present. The presence of aquatic species and turkey suggest a summer through fall occupation of the site (Martin 1988:337). I The authors note that this site appears to have been deposited on a stable terrace surface protected from flooding as it is some distance above the active floodplain (Peter and McGregor 1988b:86). They I describe it as "multiple occupations of a single terrace surface during the Late Holocene" (Peter and McGregor 1988b:96). Both the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric are represented here. An interesting I 271 I


![Image 1 - Page 288](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p288_img1.png)

## Page 289

I I point made by the authors is that the presence of large molluscan species indicates either that forays were made to the larger stream systems of the West Fork or Trinity, or they were products brought to the site upon initial occupation (Peter and McGregor 1988b:81,97-98). These may have been brought to the site I as shells rather than food items. Investigations at 41DL148, better known as the Cobb-Pool site, documented an apparent single I phase Late Prehistoric horticultural hamlet, including domestic structures, charred maize, and numerous domestic features thought to have been occupied for only a few years (Peter and McGregor 1988b: 194). Among the features are postholes, possible drying racks or storage platforms, hearths, storage pits, and baking ovens (Peter and McGregor 1988b:167). The site is located on a T2 terrace overlooking the I confluence of Mountain and Walnut Creeks (Peter and McGregor 1988b: 131-132). The original surface of the site is on the A horizon sandy loams and extends just into the underlying B hoi:izon clay loam (Peter and McGregor 1988b:140-141). I This site was thought to represent the best evidence for mussel exploitation from the Trinity River. The authors cite its proximity to the Trinity, the fact that more species are represented here than at any I other sites along the Mountain Creek drainage, and the fact that aside from the two most dominant species (Amblema plicata and Lam.psi/is hydiana), other represented species are more typically found in larger stream systems (Peter and McGregor 1988b: 165). Most of the mussel shell recovered from the Cobb I Pool site (32. 7 %) came from the area designated Feature 2, which consists of multiple pits. In fact, this feature and the area adjacent to it produced the most burned rock (21.5%), as well as faunal remains (40.7%). These results support the authors' hypothesis that the function of this feature was food I processing. In addition, the authors argue that the low frequencies of lithic material (7 .5 %) and ceramics (10.6%) indicate that the pit was a locus of processing rather than refuse discard (Peter and McGregor 1988b:172). I Another site investigated as part of the Joe Pool Lake Project was 41DL149 or the Baggett Branch site. This site is located on the floodplain of Baggett Branch, a tributary of Mountain Creek. Prior to I excavation, it was described as a midden containing lithics, mussel shell, ceramics, and bone eroding from the cut bank (Peter and McGregor 1988b: 199). This site too, had a pit containing an assortment of materials including burned rock, mussel shell, bone, and charcoal. This was Feature 1, dated A.D. I 1000 to 1400 and interpreted to be primarily a baking oven or storage pit, although it may have served as a refuse pit as a secondary function (Peter and McGregor 1988b:233). Mussel from this site was said to be comparable to recent molluscan fauna from the drainage due to its late date (Peter and McGregor I 1988b:230). Mussels are not considered to have been a major resource item for the people occupying any of the sites within the Mountain Creek drainage due to their low population densities as reported by I Fullington (Martin 1988:333). Furthermore, they may have been used on a seasonal basis during the spring and summer when they were more accessible in contrast to primary protein sources such as deer, which were more readily hunted in the fall and winter (Martin 1988:332). I I I 272 I I


![Image 1 - Page 289](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p289_img1.png)

## Page 290

I I DISCUSSION I Location Not surprisingly, most shell lenses are located on floodplains or lower terraces as at 41DL270. The reconnaissance along the upper and middle Trinity River for the proposed Tennessee Colony Lake I in the mid-1970s recorded 72 out of 73 shell lens sites in the cutbanks of the floodplains (Richner 1978:246). The remaining lens site was located on a T2 terrace. Several important sites, however, such as the Butler Hole Site, are apparently terrace locations. Within the Denton Creek drainage, those sites I with recorded mussel shell concentrations are either on the creek bank, the floodplain, or the terrace edge adjacent to it, except for two sites on the lower and upper terraces north of the creek (41DN290, now destroyed, and 41DN480, respectively). At Adams Ranch, 41NV177, the site itself lies on several I surfaces including a high Pleistocene terrace while Feature 1 appears to have been dug into a lower surface which could possibly be a lower terrace or the floodplain of the creek. The Cobb-Pool site, 41D1148, offers a good comparison between a terrace habitation site and a floodplain occupation site in I its mussel shell processing and discard patterns. Shell lens sites are extremely common, especially along some drainages. From the proposed river I area of the Tennessee Colony Lake (60 miles worth) 73 shell lens equalling 1 site per 0.8 mile were recorded (Richner 1978:246). It should be noted that many of these sites consisted of more than one lens; many contained a series of such lenses. Richner also reported similar densities for the East Fork of the I Trinity (Richner 1978:246). Comparable shell site densities were also observed at Aquilla Lake (Brown, personal communication 1994). The density of shell lens sites along Denton Creek seems to fall within the range of such occurrences at other drainages in the region. Little information is available on the I overall size or number of lenses at such sites, however, though it seems that 41DL270 is larger and contains more shell features than many such reported sites. I Chronology Sites with shell lenses are most commonly dated to the Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric I periods as is the case at 41DL270. At site 41RT193 on Richland Creek, mussel shell components date to the I Late Archaic (around A.D. 200) (McGregor and Bohlin 1987:46,50) through the Historic period (Pheasant 1987:35). Those from Adams Ranch, 41NV177, span the time between 1250 and 1750 B.P., or the latter part of the Late Archaic (Martin 1987a:228-231). At 41DL12, Pits A and C are dated to I the Late Prehistoric, based on arrow points and a shell hoe (associated with pottery sites). At Aquilla Lake, the McKenzie site shell midden is thought to date between about 1000 and 1700 B.P., or the Late Archaic (Brown 1987e:39-12; personal communication 1994), while shell lenses at the McDonald site I extended back to the late Middle Archaic (Brown et al. 1987). Sites with shell lenses at Joe Pool Lake are dated to the Late Archaic (41DL189 and 41DL199) as well as the Late Prehistoric (41DL199, 41DL148, and 41DL149). I Processing Methods I Parmalee and Klippel (1974) included many accounts by researchers of their interpretations or observations regarding cooking methods. Most accounts spoke of steam cooking over heated rocks. Fire-cracked rocks found near shells or shell features are mentioned for most of the sites under study in I this section but a direct relationship is difficult to define much less an exact cooking method. I 273 I


![Image 1 - Page 290](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p290_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 290*

## Page 291

I I At Cobb-Pool (Peter and McGregor 1988a: 172), the association of pits, burned rock, and mussel shell (often with bone) suggested pit ovens for the roasting or baking of mussel shells. The Feature 1 pit at Baggett Branch, 41DL149, was interpreted as a baking oven with a probable secondary function I as a refuse dump which contained burned rock, charcoal, mussel shell, and bone (Peter and McGregor 1988b:233). The shell associated with Feature 1 at Adams Ranch was also found with fire-cracked rock, charcoal, and bone, indicating roasting activities as well as refuse disposal (Martin 1987b:231). At site I 41DL12, Pits A, B, and C contained charred mussel shell. The report on the excavation of the house at Butler Hole, 41COL2, cites a possible hearth found near the center of the structure (Housewright et al. 1947: 11). It was composed of three slabs of I conglomerate sandstone with five mussel shells, charcoal, and ashes outside it. The authors doubted this feature was actually a hearth because the sandstone and shells were only slightly burned and there was only a small amount of the charcoal and ash mentioned above. Perhaps this feature comprises the I remains of a hearth which was cleaned out or in some other way disturbed after use. While charring is one clue to the manner of mussel preparation, such evidence shows considerable I variation. At the Butler Hole site mentioned above, the mussel shells surrounding the slightly burned sandstone feature were charred. This may be an indication that mussels were heat treated by different methods such as roasting in a pit or steaming/roasting on a hearth. However, the charring may be a I result of discard into a still hot hearth. At Tennessee Colony, between 2 to 10 percent of total shell sample (8,641 shells) collected demonstrated charring or burning (Richner 1978:271). Mussel shells recovered from the pit features at 41DL12 were also charred. It is suggested these pits were initially used I as roasting pits and lastly as refuse dumps. Interestingly, only a few shells recovered at any of the shell lens sites at Aquilla Lake displayed I evidence of burning (Neck 1987: 13). In light of the fact that many of the Aquilla shells were broken along the posterior at right angles, this suggests that a means other than heat-treating was utilized to open the shells. The breakage pattern does not indicate, however, whether or how the meat may have been I prepared. In addition, neither pits nor hearths were found in close association with the Aquilla shell features. I In regard to shell recovered from 41DL270, Neck (this volume) noted some shells with a pale grayish discoloration indicating they were charred. Direct association with the numerous hearths at the site is difficult to determine. The two pit features documented at the site, Features 7 and 35, showed no apparent relationship with mussel shells, although Feature 7 was not excavated, instead only examined I in the north profile of the gully cut between the drainage ditch and Denton Creek. Nevertheless, it would appear that surface hearths were more likely utilized in the preparation of mussel at the site due to their abundance and the apparent lack of recognizable pits. I Resource Indicator I That the people at the McDonald Site on Hackberry Creek in Aquilla Lake were harvesting very small shells testifies to their potential dietary value (Brown et al. 1987; Neck 1987). This reliance on less than optimal resources adds support to the postulated decrease in cultural territory size during the I Late Archaic and Late Prehistoric (Brown 1989). Increased human populations and/or decreased ranges could have stressed mussel populations in the region. On the other hand, Neck's (1987) suggestion of climatic variation based on the presence of mussels which would require greater water volumes than that I found in present day Hackberry Creek may also shed light on subsistence changes at this time. The 274 I I


![Image 1 - Page 291](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p291_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 291*

## Page 292

I I harvesting of smaller shells through time may indicate a drop in the volume of water in the creek as a result of decreased precipitation or greater evaporation which would have resulted in both smaller I individual shells and smaller mussel populations. At Richland Creek site 41FT193, most of the identifiable shell was found to be subadult. I Fullington says that the small size of Richland Creek, which has apparently remained unchanged for about 1800 years, could not support large mussel populations. The prehistoric inhabitants were therefore maintaining a constant pressure on these populations (Fullington 1987a:46). How this might relate to diminishing territory sizes as postulated for Aquilla Lake (Brown 1989) during the Late Archaic and Late I Prehistoric periods is unclear, but it does indicate that mussels were considered an important dietary adjunct despite their small size. I The mussel shells at 41DL270 are not particularly small but their utilization may still indicate resource stress from climate changes or human population shifts. The shell concentrations are most prevalent in the upper cultural zones correlated with the latter part of the Late Archaic and the Late I Prehistoric I periods. These are also times of demonstrated human population increases and smaller territory sizes as illustrated at the sites discussed above. In addition, these cultural periods at 41DL270 may correlate with time periods when large mammals were not as available contrasted with earlier periods I when they are abundant and during which mussel shells were not exploited to the extent seen in the latter periods. This is discussed in greater detail in the closing chapters of this report. I I I I I I I I I I 275 I

## Page 294

I I THE DENTON CREEK PALEOENVIRONMENT David 0. Brown I PREVIOUS RESEARCH ON REGIONAL PALEOENVIRONMENTS One of the most important aspects of the investigations at 41DL270 was the recovery of I considerable information regarding the paleoenvironment and paleoclimate in the site area. Despite the limited artifact recovery and the limitations on spatial data presented by the nature of the investigations, the access to a stratigraphic sample of features and ancillary environmental data has provided a modestly I detailed picture of the environmental context for the site inhabitants. This section briefly describes some of the more recent paleoclimatic/paleoenvironmental reconstructions of the region and examines these in light of the lines of evidence recovered from the recent investigations at 41DL270. I Although archeologists have long been interested in the paleoenvironment of sites under study, there were few attempts at regional paleoenvironmental synthesis until relatively recent times. One of I the first to consider climate and cultural change was Dillehay (1974) who noted that bison were present in Texas only during certain periods which may in turn have been related to climate. His study documented the presence or absence of bison remains in dated components at archeological and I paleontological sites from Texas, New Mexico, and Oklahoma. He proposed a series of bison presence and absence periods between 10,000 B.C. and A.O. 1550. Within the Late Holocene period under study at 41DL270, these include Presence Period II (2500 B.C. to A.O. 500), Absence Period II (A.O. 500 I to 1200), and Presence Period m (A.O. 1200 to 1550). He suggests that the low frequency/absence of bison for several thousand years prior to Presence Period II (his Absence Period I) may be due to warmer, drier climates of the proposed Altithermal period. In seeming contrast, he also notes the I correlation with his last presence period (Presence Period ill) with some of the driest climates recorded on the Great Plains, occurring just after A.O. 1200. One of the earliest and most comprehensive studies of the Late Pleistocene and Holocene I paleoenvironment of Texas was conducted by Vaughan Bryant (1977), who suggested that the Holocene environment showed a general trend of slight warming with modern vegetation communities establishing themselves by about 3000 B.P. in many parts of Texas. Bryant and Shafer (1977) extended this data into I the cultural sphere, trying to assess the impact of these changing climates on known cultural sequences. Although there was little question regarding the trend from the Late Pleistocene into the Holocene, the paper began a running controversy over the nature and extent of change during the Holocene. First and I most noticeably, their study appeared to minimize the previously supposed warming effect of the proposed post-Pleistocene Altithermal period. More controversial, however, may have been their lack of recognition of any significant variation in the late Holocene. I While Bryant and Shafer (1977) did not find clear evidence of late Holocene changes, other lines of evidence pointed to such change. Contemporary work by geomorphologists suggested the possibility I for some environmental changes. While geomorphologists had long suspected the possibility of environmental change associated with the seemingly regionally synchronous series of alluvial terraces found throughout central Texas and adjacent areas, Baker and Penteado-Orellana (1977) took this I suspicion a step farther, looking at the sedimentology and channel geometry of terraces on the Colorado River near Austin. They concluded that there was good evidence for a more mesic period between about 3000 and 2000 B.P. I I 277 I


![Image 1 - Page 294](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p294_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 294*


---

## Page 295

I I Although this debate was not carried immediately into the North-Central Texas area, archeologists in the region were at least open to the idea that such changes may have occurred. While not expressly developing a model of paleoenvironmental change, Shaw (1978b:41) noted the potential effect of such I change on the proposed climax habitat of the Blackland Prairie. It can be argued, then, that the climax state transition between upland regions into the Blackland Prairie was pronounced [in prehistoric times], since the Oak-Hickory woodland I composed an appreciable percentage of the total Oak-Hickory Forest environment. This distinction, however, would have been affected by climatic fluctuations, principally those related to precipitation. Long dry periods would cause the retreat of the forests on the I western side of the Trinity River and the advance of grasses into areas formerly occupied by the Oak-Hickory Forest; extended periods of precipitation are likely to have facilitated forest extension onto prairie regions (Shelford 1963:306). The distinction between these I zones is pronounced contemporarily since states of vegetation disclimax have resulted throughout both zones from historic land-use practices. (Shaw 1978b:43) I Dillehay's (1974) findings have remained fairly controversial, with some archeologists taking exception to the use of the term "absence" for periods where some bison are still found at sites and for his inclusion of very different regions where contemporaneous climates may have been somewhat I different and bison responses may not have been parallel. Lynott (1979) summarized some of these criticisms, with particular reference to the North Texas area. Noting that Dillehay's study area contained up to 48 botanic provinces with potentially very different bison habitat and varying degrees of I susceptibility to climatic change, he states In lumping the biotic zones into one large region, Dillehay (1974) created a false I image of homogeneity. Changes in bison population distribution, or any animal, can best be dealt with in terms of regions which are smaller and ecologically more homogeneous. By combining a large number of biotic zones, it is quite possible that critical variability in the diachronic density and distribution of bison populations is lost in the diversity of I ecological zones. I Lynott goes on to point out that the evidence accumulated up to that point suggest that prior to 800 B.P. (the beginning of Dillehay's Presence Period III), bison may have been present only in very low numbers in the North-Central Texas area. Of 15 North-Central Texas sites which were documented as having bison remains, 10 have bison remains only within the final Late Prehistoric period and only two (Ham I Creek and Bear Creek) have reasonably secure Archaic-dated bison materials. Various mid-1980s studies began to accumulate more and more evidence for some variation in the I paleoenvironments of the Late Holocene. Utilizing data from pollen analysis as well as invertebrate and vertebrate fauna! distributions, Hall (1982) proposed that the period between 2000 and 1000 B.P. was a moist one, with a change to a drier climate about 1000 B.P. He notes that the moist period I approximately but not exactly correlates with Wendland and Bryson's (1974) Scandic (1680-1260 B.P.) and neo-Atlantic (1260-800 B.P.). According to the pollen evidence, the proposed moist period showed an increase in hickory (Carya texana) relative to oak (Quercus sp.) in the Cross Timbers area of I Oklahoma, with a corresponding decrease in hickory and increase in oak after 1000 B.P. In some areas, however, such as in the Little Caney Creek Valley, increased oak woodland along streams where the vegetation had previously been grassland-dominated suggested decreasing water tables along the creek I and a more favorable soil habitat for woody species. 278 I I


![Image 1 - Page 295](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p295_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 295*


---

## Page 296

I Reanalysis of existing pollen data and the collection of new data sets in the mid-1980s began to I provide new interpretations of the Late Holocene environment which had previously been viewed as stable. Analysis of the data from Weakly Bog in Leon County, for example, suggested increased oak woodland habitat between 2400 and 1500 B.P. (Holloway et al. 1987). Although slightly later in time, I it quite effectively supports Baker and Penteado-Orellana's original notion of a more mesic interval at the beginning of the Late Holocene. After 1500 B.P., they suggest a return to somewhat warmer and drier conditions with the transformation of the oak woodland into an oak savannah type environment. Bryant I and Holloway (1985) summarized the available pollen data from the Late Pleistocene and Holocene of Texas. They report the analysis of Weakly Bog, pointing out that the data from Patschke Bog in Lee County suggest a shift from mesic to xeric conditions around 1500 to 2000 years ago (Bryant and I Holloway 1985:63). For North-Central Texas, they present the data from Ferndale Bog, in Oklahoma (Albert 1981; Hall 1983), but are skeptical of the climatic significance. I In the early to mid-1980s, archeologists began to join the debate on paleoenvironmental and paleoclimate change. Among the archeologists to side with Baker and Penteado-Orellana (1977) was Gunn who published a series of studies which supported a range of paleoclimatic/paleoenvironmental I change during the Late Holocene. One of his most important papers, co-authored with Elton Prewitt (Gunn and Prewitt n.d.), discussed his model for Holocene climate change in great detail, providing correlations with the corresponding cultural periods of Central Texas. The potential impact of this I important paper was greatly lessened by the fact that it has remained unpublished. In the late 1980s, archeologists began to pay more attention to the accumulating data for late Holocene climate change and propose models for such change in their particular regions as part of large I cultural resource management projects. The earliest of these studies were from areas on the northern and southern periphery of the North-Central Texas region, including the Aquilla, Richland Chambers, and Ray Roberts reservoirs. I Brown (1987a, 1987b, 1987c, 1989; Brown and Watson 1987), in discussing the potential for environmental and culture change at Aquilla Lake at the extreme southern end of the North-Central Texas I region, followed Baker and Penteado-Orellana (1977) and Gunn and Prewitt (n.d.) in suggesting an initial Late Holocene mesic period, and in general, more climatic/environmental variation than that originally pr-oposed by Bryant and Shafer (1977). Evidence from Aquilla Lake suggested an expansion of traditional I Central Texas groups into the southern edge of the North-Central Texas area during what had been proposed as hot and dry intervals, particularly the Early Archaic San Geronimo and Circleville phases as well as the early Late Prehistoric Austin phase (Brown 1987c:46-47). Lesser evidence of warm-period I incursions was suggested for Prewitt's (1981) Round Rock, Marshal Ford, and Uvalde phases. Geomorphic evidence for climate change at Aquilla Lake was also reflected in the geomorphic I trends of the area which saw lower terrace formation begin prior to 2850 B.P. and cease after about 1000 B.P. (Brown et al. 1987:140-145, Brown 1987f:4-5). Evidence from gastropods found at the site suggested that during much of this period, the site was under a cover of grasses with a riparian woodland I developing during the Late Prehistoric (Brown et al. 1987; Neck 1987:6). Although alluvial deposition seems to have slowed or ceased completely by around 1000 B.P. there was a subsequent major period of historic deposition due almost certainly to accelerated soil erosion from agriculture. I At the southeastern extreme of the North-Central Texas region lie the Richland Chambers Reservoir area where Bruseth and Moir (1987) and McGregor and Bruseth (1987) also supported the notion of Late I Holocene paleoenvironmental variation. Providing one of the best contemporaneous summaries of 279 I I


![Image 1 - Page 296](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p296_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 296*


---

## Page 297

I paleoenvironmental data from the region, Bruseth et al. (1987:47) divided the Late Holocene into three major periods: Period I, from 3000 B.C. to A.D. 500; Period II, from A.D. 500 to 1200; and Period I III, from A.D. 1200 to 1820. A fourth period, from A.D. 1820 to the present, is noted but not discussed in great detail. Accumulation of a cap of historic sediments on the most recent terrace, however, suggests a close correlation with the situation during this late historic period at Aquilla Lake (Brown et al. 1987). I Bruseth et al. (1987:47) suggest that their Period I, dated between about 5000 B.P. to 1500 B.P., is one of greater effective moisture, marked not so much perhaps by a different vegetation composition I but by shifts in the proportional representation of species still represented in the Post Oak Savannah. Period II, dated from about 1500 to 750 B.P., was thought,to represent an increasingly drier climate which saw the recession of the western margin of the savannah woodlands and an increase in clay I deposition on the floodplain. Period III, dated between 750 and 130 B.P., accentuates a trend begun in Period II of climatic deterioration. They project a marked decrease in rainfall and the ~tablishment of a Tall Grass Prairie, with continued decrease in the size and extent of gallery forests along the major I creeks. Contemporaneous with the studies at Aquitla and Richland Creek was the publication of results I from testing at 41CO141 in the Ray Roberts Reservoir located in Cooke County and well to the north of the Denton Creek area (Prikryl and Yates 1987). Prikryl and Perring (1987: 107) note that the floodplain stability and soil formation at the site appear to correlate with proposed moister climates of I the first millennium A.D. The paleosol identified at this site was correlated with the West Fork paleosol previously defined by Perring (1986:93). In general, Prikryl and Perring (1987:108) suggest the possibility of a lusher and more productive environment during the first millennium moist period, which in tum correlates with the latest Late Archaic and the earliest Late Prehistoric. I Closer to the Denton Creek study area, Peter and Jurney (1988) compiled one of the most comprehensive comparisons of projected paleoclimates yet assembled. Their comparison utilized I paleoclimatic reconstructions from a variety of sources in the region, including Richland Chambers and Ray Roberts reservoirs (Peter and Jurney 1988:25). Their Figure 2-9 shows little exact correspondence in such reconstructions during the last two millennia, but there appears to be a tendency toward drier I climates in the last millennium with several possible cooler and/or moister intervals noted prior to 1000 B.P. They point out a Late Holocene dry-moist-dry cycle for the Southern Plains, with the intermediate moist phase expressed as a period of greater effective moisture with associated high water tables, I floodplain aggradation, and the development of a paleosol between A.D. 1 and 1000 (Peter and Jurney 1988:24-25). They also note that this moister interval for the Southern Plains appears slightly out of synch with the mesic period proposed between 4500 and 1500 B.P. for the Richland Chambers area, I noting the conclusion by Bruseth et al. (1987:5-17) that the differences may reflect a time-transgressive relationship. I Although the conclusions to this study generally support the notion of a drier period after 1000 B.P., there is much less agreement on the period immediately prior to this (Peter and McGregor 1988a:352-353). This earlier period, between about 2500 and 1000 B.P., is marked by moderately rapid I floodplain aggradation as well as erosion of Pleistocene soils. They interpret this period as one of generally dry conditions, although less so than the period following A.D. 900-1000. I Hall's (1988) synthesis of the paleoenvironment and paleoclimate of the Osage Plains of Oklahoma is based on data collected from a number of different areas and sources. In addition to citing the previously reported data from Ferndale Bog, which indicates a potentially wetter and/or cooler period I 280 I I


![Image 1 - Page 297](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p297_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 297*


---

## Page 298

I associated with increased oak-hickory forest and decreasing grasslands between 3700 and 1900 B.P., he I reports in some detail on the post 3000 B.P. period. He suggests a drier climate between 3000 and 2000 B.P. and a comparatively moister climate beginning about 2000 B.P. and lasting about a thousand years (Hall 1988:206). This cooler period is evidence in both pollen and molluscan fauna records and I correlates with the Copan and Caddo paleosols defined in Oklahoma (Hall 1977; Perring 1986). A drier climate is suggested for the period after 1000 B.P. I More recently, Story (1990b) has summarized some of the paleoenvironmental information collected to date. Her summary presents several gen · paleoclimatic classificatory schemes, including those with the Altithermal as a prominent feature (Antevs 1948; Wright 1976) and those with a complex I series of phases based on glaciations and known changes in global climate (Bryson et al. 1970; Wendland and Bryson 1974; Wendland 1978). For the Late Holocene, these later phases include the Sub-Boreal (5060 to 2760 B.P.), a slightly cooler interval than the preceding Atlantic/Hypsithermal, the S11b-Atlantic I (2760-1680 B.P.), described as a "deterioration" in climate, the Scandic (1680-1260 B.P.), the beginning of a warming trend, the Neo-Atlantic (1260-850 B.P.), culmination of the previous warming trend, the Pacific (850-400 B.P.), a continued dry period, and the Neo-Boreal (400-100 B.P.), a return to cooler I climate (Story 1990b:25). More relevant to the immediate Denton Creek area is Prikryl's (1990) recent synthesis of the I archeology of the Trinity River in the Dallas area. Although he does not add any new references to those discussed above, Prikryl does present a good summary of Ferring's model for the development of the alluvial terraces of the area. Although most of Ferring's model illustrates the development of much earlier alluvial features, Prikryl (1990:15) does note his definition of the West Fork paleosol, dated I between 1060 and 1810 B.P. Prikryl's redefinition of the culture history of this region, presented elsewhere in this report, does not correlate precisely with the inferred climatic changes. The end of the Late Archaic, for example, is given as 1250 B.P., two hundred years before the end of the soil formation I associated with the West Fork paleosol. In discussing channel incision on the southern Great Plains, Hall (1990) adds new evidence to his I earlier (Hall 1988) proposal of a moist interval between about 2000 and 1000 B.P., followed by a drier period marked by channel incision and rapid overbank sedimentation. More recently, Huebner (1990), in a reexamination of the dating of bison remains in central Texas archeological sites, has argued that I drier climates around 1000 B.P. resulted in increases in bison populations on the southern plains and a more open, grassier Cross Timbers and Post Oak Savannah. I A number of recent studies by geoarcheologists have addressed the question of paleoenvironment and paleoclimate directly. One such study is that by Toomey et al. (1993) which focuses on the Late Quaternary environments of the Edwards Plateau. The study summaries data on the fossil vertebrates, I pollen, and plant macrofloral materials from the Edwards Plateau region (including particularly Toomey's study of the fauna from Hall's Cave), describing a series of general environments for the Late Pleistocene and Holocene. They define two different Late Holocene environmental periods, one from about 5000 I to 2500 B.P., and the other between 2500 and 1000 B.P. These also define a modem period from 1000 B.P. to the present. In the earliest of the Late Holocene periods, lasting from 5000 to 2500 B.P., they note the culmination of the general decrease in effective moisture which began at the end of the Late I Pleistocene and characterize this period as the driest of any time in the last 20,000 years (Toomey et al. 1993:309). At Hall's Cave this period was marked by the disappearance of the eastern pipistrelle bat (Pipistrellus subjlavus) and the woodland vole (Microtus pinetorium) as well as the absolute minimum I in representation of the moisture-loving least shrew Cryptotis. They suggest a generally xeric vegetation 281 I I


![Image 1 - Page 298](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p298_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 298*


---

## Page 299

I regime for the Edwards Plateau area with a strong east-west gradient, with possible desert scrub dominating the southwestern edge of the plateau and a possible mixed grassland along the southeastern I margin. The period from 2500 to 1000 B.P. marked a return to more mesic climates for the Edwards I Plateau (foomey et al. 1993:310). At Hall's Cave, the period showed a return by the woodland vole and eastern pipistrelle bat as well as increases in the least shrew with respect to the desert shrew Notiosorex. After 1000 B.P., the study suggests a turn toward more xeric conditions, suggested by a complete I dominance of the desert shrew sometime after 1430 .P (foomey et al. 1993:311). A similar study by the same authors (Blum et al. 1994) emphasizes fluvial responses to climate I change on the Edwards Plateau. Evidence from fluvial systems of the Edwards Plateau effectively supports the climatic assertions made in Toomey et al. (1993:309). During the initial !-,ate Holocene period, from 5000 to 2500 B .P., the bedload sediment of the major Edwards Plateau rivers exceeded their I transport capacity but decreased flood magnitudes resulting from a shift toward drier conditions resulted in the abandonment of early to middle Holocene floodplains, allowing soil formation on these newly stabilized terraces. River systems continued to migrate laterally and store sediment, however, and the I initial deposition of late Holocene fills began. Channel facies are dominant in the fills dating to this period. During the subsequent, more mesic period between 2500 and 1000 B.P., bedload sediment continued to exceed capacity and late Holocene filling continued with the deposition of thick vertical accretion facies. Soils which had formed on previously abandoned terrace surfaces were buried in many I places by a veneer of rapidly accumulating floodplain fills, forming late Holocene paleosols at the top of these buried older surfaces (Blum et al. 1994:17). They suggest that this period reflects frequent moderate to high-magnitude floods associated with the change to moister climate conditions and the thin, I weathered soil mantles of the uplands. After 1000 B.P., the floodplain surfaces were once again abandoned following a shift to drier climate conditions (Blum et al. 1994: 17). I Using carbon isotope data from geomorphic investigations at Fort Hood in Bell and Coryell Counties, Nordt et al. (1994) have argued for a cooler/wetter interval after 4000 B.P. which may have been marked by a brief drying episode around 2000 B.P. They estimate approximately 60 to 65 percent I warm season (C4) grasses during the 4000 to 2000 B.P. period, down from 85 to 95 percent previously, and a slightly higher 65 to 70 percent after 2000 B.P. I In the most recent published study to date, Humphrey and Perring (1994) use stable isotopes in soil carbonates to attempt a reconstruction of the paleoenvironment and paleoclimate of North-Central Texas. While their oxygen isotope curve is relatively flat, the carbon isotope data is quite interesting, suggesting I a wetter climate between about 4500 and 2000 B.P., with a dry interval lasting from 2000 B.P. until sometime after 1500 B.P., and a subsequent return to a moister climate. The intermediate dry period is marked by slow alluviation and soil development, while the earlier and late moist phases show evidence I of moderate (before 2000 B.P. and after 1400 B.P.) to rapid (only before 2000 B.P.) alluvial activity. Discussion I The twenty years from Dillehay (1974) to Humphrey and Perring (1994) has seen a radical increase in the amount of data collected on the paleoenvironments and paleoclimates of Texas. To Dillehay's credit, researchers continue to find (despite considerable new data and a plethora of revised climatic I reconstructions) that his model, with some remodeling, is still reasonably sound in many respects. The density of bison present in Texas did in fact change through time (although clearly with certain I 282 I I


![Image 1 - Page 299](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p299_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 299*


---

## Page 300

I I differences in different regions) and that change can probably be linked to paleoclimatic changes. Lynott's (1979) criticism of Dillehay's model for North-Central Texas, while taken keenly into account, does not appear to negate the model even for the southern Plains region of North-Central Texas. I Huebner's (1990) recent findings have once again emphasized the patterns of bison abundance/paucity in central Texas, emphasizing that bison appear in at least some parts of Texas during warm/dry periods when grasslands are extensive relative to woodlands. The cultural implications of dramatic changes in bison density with paleoclimates and paleoenvironments are extremely important. Bison abundance may I have generated considerable culture change, including the shift into and out of several of the cultural phases which have been defined for central Texas (Prewitt 1981, 1983). Shifting climates and environments, more so than technology or "internal" cultural change, may be responsible for much of I the culture history change in central Texas. If climate and culture history are related, what of climate? Do we know it so well that we can I precisely define the links between environment and culture in Texas prehistory, or are there gaps remaining in our knowledge? The study of Texas paleoclimates and paleoenvironments has in fact come a long way since Dillehay. The once strongly supported model of gradual Holocene change originally I presented by Bryant (1977) and Shafer and Bryant (1977) has been reinvented as an increasingly more complex chrono-spatial mosaic of environments and climates in the Holocene. Overall, there is a much greater degree of precision in assessing past environments, and even more importantly, in dating them. I The series of recent studies cited here (Toomey et al. 1993; Blum et al. 1994; Nordt et al. 1994; Humphrey and Perring 1994) form the core of modem paleoenvironmental work which sets the stage for the studies at Denton Creek. Although the dates and the extent of change are disputed somewhat, most I of these authors recognize several general trends in the late Holocene. Almost universally accepted is the notion of a warmer, drier period after about 1000 B.P. I Although the Little Ice Age may have spelled a brief return to cooler and wetter climates for a few centuries in the recent past, much of the last millennium and the modem climate probably tend to be warmer and/or drier than the two previous millennia had been. Correspondingly, this earlier period, I dated between 2500 and 1000 B.P., is thought by some scholars to have been one of generally cooler and wetter climates. Woodland vegetation may have been found where today there are only grasslands, and more important for archeological purposes, this period correlates with the development of a series of alluvial terraces along many rivers and major streams of Texas. Accordingly, the period before 2500 I B.P. saw much warmer and drier climates and changing fluvial hydrology, including the abandonment of early to mid-Holocene floodplains along some drainages (Blum et al. 1994). Whether this period is actually the warmest/driest period in Texas prehistory, or a return to warmer/drier climates following an I earlier Altithermal has not been finally determined, but Blum et al. (1994) and Toomey et al. (1993) argue for the period between 5000 B.P. and 2500 B.P. to be one of the warmest/driest periods in prehistory, a return from cooler temperatures prior to 5000 B.P. Note that this dry period beginning I around 5000 B.P. is approximately contemporaneous with Dillehay's (1974) Presence Period Il, which he dates from 2500 B.C. (4450 B.P.) to A.D. 500. In the following section, we discuss the implications of these and other paleoenvironmental/paleoclimatic inferences for the North-Central Texas region based I on the data collected from the recent investigations at Denton Creek. ## Page 301

I THE DENTON CREEK PALEOENVIRONMENT I Introduction A variety of paleoenvironmental data sets have been gathered as part of the data recovery I investigations at 41DL270. The collection of such data was one of the key aspects of the research design developed for this phase of studies. These data provide a broad perspective on the Late Holocene environments at the site and complement a number of data sets recently gathered in the region from I different sources which address the paleoenvironment and paleoclimate of the region (foomey et al. 1993; Blum et al. 1994; Nordt et al. 1994; Humphrey and Perring 1994). While the Denton Creek data are limited and not always perfectly consistent, there is enough support for certain previously proposed I phenomena that the data do support the general picture painted by these recent studies (which are not themselves completely in agreement on a number of aspects of Late Holocene environmeqts). Although the archeological data from the site are also limited, the intersection of paleoenvironmental and I archeological data sets provides some potentially interesting information which sheds some light on the proposed culture history of the region. I The following sections discuss some of the general implications of the various paleoenvironmental data sets collected from 41DL270 during the project. In the first part, the data from each area of investigation are briefly summarized, with problems inherent in each pointed out. Conclusions are I offered concerning the general trends in regional and microregional climates and environments and the implications which these have for the culture history of North-Central Texas and the understanding of hunter-gatherer systems in general. I Geomorphology I In this volume, Caran has presented a comprehensive interpretation of the depositional history of 41DL270. Only the highlights are reiterated here, particularly those which may relate to the paleoenvironment at the site. The main portion of the site, lying along the east bank of the artificial drainage ditch, is an alluvial terrace of Denton Creek whose uppermost fill, at least, dates to the Late I Holocene. The history of the earliest portion of this terrace, which was only reached in a few backhoe trenches, is somewhat less clear than the upper segment. The lower part may represent the same basic allostratigraphic unit as the upper segment although it is possible that the remnants of an earlier alluvial I fill unit may be buried in the terrace, not unlike that described by Blum et al. (1994) for Late Holocene fluvial processes or that briefly described by Perring (1991) in the brief mention of a Middle Holocene paleosol present in the Holocene terraces of the Trinity River. I In any case, deposition of the upper several meters of the Late Holocene fill began by at least around 4000 B.P. and continued more or less continuously until the abandonment of the terrace sometime I after about 1200 B.P. The date of abandonment correlates approximately with the model of fluvial activity proposed by Blum et al. (1994). Contemporaneous with the abandonment of th_e Late Holocene floodplain, by at least 1300 B.P. a low sandy floodplain deposit had begun to form along the modem I channel of the creek. Deposition in this fill unit, which is inset into the Late Holocene fill in trenches dug into the bottom of the drainage ditch, has apparently continued until the modem day. I The channel history at the site is not completely clear but at least over the last millennium or so the channel may have been approximately where its modem course is but trending more northeast perhaps. At least part of the northern portion of the large meander loop of Denton Creek which extends I 284 I I

I northward from the site may have formed during this last millennium and contain the more recent post I 1500 B.P. age fill. Because of the intensity of cultural occupation at the site during the development of the Late Holocene terrace, however, it is assumed that a channel of the creek was also near the site. The more recent meandering channel may have erased evidence of this, however. I Carbon-14 I A total of nine C-14 dates have been submitted from the site so far. These are shown in Table 29, which presents raw dates and corrections with their proveniences. The dates are arranged in the table in their approximate stratigraphic order (note that there is some possible overlap in dates between the I terrace and the floodplain which will be discussed later, as well as apparent stratigraphic reversals in the terrace itself). Most of the dates represent bulk soil (5) and/or humate (2) samples, while only two dates are from charcoal from cultural features. The general paucity of charcoal at the site, the lack of good I cultural associations for much of the charcoal which was recovered, and the generally minuscule size of the available charcoal forced greater reliance on pedogenic/depositional carbon than should be tolerated. For the most part, projectile points recovered from the site suggest that the dates at least approximately correspond to the expected values. ## Page 303

I Although dating the sediments at the site is one of the greatest problems faced in the interpretation and analysis, it has not been an insurmountable one. The earliest and latest sediments at the site are I reasonably well dated, and with minor exceptions, the dates fall into reasonable chronological order. More bulk soil or humate dates from the site would add to the analysis but the general lack of precision of such methods argues for the search for other methods of dating. AMS dates (unfortunately not I provided for in the initial budget) of the tiny charcoal samples from secure feature contexts might provide more useful data on understanding the sequence at the site, but this would be costly. As will be noted later, dating of the isotope samples is particularly problematic, but in this case little datable material is I present to provide an accurate date. Until a more reliable method of dating freshwater shell is developed, the isotope chronology will probably remain tenuous, anchored only by interpolation between soil dates. I Vertebrate Faunal Analysis , Analysis of vertebrate fauna from the site suggests some very marked changes in resource I exploitation through time. Despite the small size of the sample, and the fact that some of the sample was actually collected from the surface rather than excavated, the patterns seem very strongly expressed. Masson's analysis in this volume is quite thoroughly researched and documented and no attempt will be I made to repeat it here. Only some of the more important trends related to paleoenvironments are noted. First and foremost, perhaps, are the presence of bison remains at the site. As noted above, I Dillehay's (1974) arguments linking fauna, climate, and cultural trends were among the first of their kind in the Texas area and have withstood the test of time with only minor modifications. The bison remains from 41DL270 are recorded at estimated uncorrected dates of 3600 B.P. and between circa 2400-2600 I B.P. Both fall within Dillehay's (1974:182-184) Presence Period II, dated from 4450 to 1450 B.P. It is not clear if the bison recorded at the site during these two periods represent a low-density item hunted whenever possible throughout this period (as Lynott [1979] might imply) or if these are two discrete time periods during which bison may have been present in the Dallas area. In any case, there is little evidence I at the site for a specialized bison procurement niche during these two time periods. Huebner (1990) has argued with respect to Central Texas in general that bison may have wandered south only when extended dry periods resulted in extensive grasslands, such as may have been the case during the final Late I Prehistoric period which he discusses in that paper. Interestingly, and perhaps not coincidentally, the two occurrences occur during suspected warm/dry intervals at the site as inferred from isotopic evidence (see "Freshwater Shell Isotope Studies", this volume). I Deer and other faunal species recovered from the site fall well within the expected ranges for aboriginal sites in Texas. Deer are the most common element and second only to bison in biomass I estimates. Deer biomass is overall greater than all other species (excluding bison) from the site; however, deer are not an immutably high percentage of the total biomass of the site through time. Despite the relatively small samples, there are some very clear and interesting trends which show up through time I at the site (Figure 93). Note that the earliest samples, from Cultural Zones VI-IX (prior to 2950 B.P.), have been combined; even combined the sample is dominated by the few bison bones recovered. Otherwise, the implications of this distribution are reasonably clear. The highest percentage of deer I (Cultural Zone ill) occurs during the first half of the cool/wet period which begins after about 2250 B.P., while the lowest percentage of deer occurs in the warm/dry spike that follows 1400 B.P. This deer exploitation minima occurs in conjunction with the highest percentage exploitation of bird at the site. I Note also that the exploitation of aquatic resources, including turtle and mussel, is quite high in Cultural Zones I and II. I 286 I I

## Page 305

I Although resource availability is clearly linked to habitat, cultural biases in the exploitation of certain resources have never been fully investigated. Nonetheless, within certain limits certain I expectations are reasonable. If bison are available, for example, it seems likely that they will be first choice in the diet. For a variety of reasons, deer are important to prehistoric hunter-gatherers and would be first choice for exploitation given most circumstances. Notwithstanding the desire for variety in the I diet, deer seem to be by far the preferred resource of the hunter-gatherers of the Late Archaic and early Late Prehistoric of Central and North-Central Texas (Lynott 1977; Brown 1987e, 1989). At the other extreme, while mussel are almost certainly a secondary resource in the diet (Brown 1987b, 1987e, 1989; I "Mussel Shell Procurement in North-Central Texas", this volume), it is not clear how turtle might rank. Turtles are clearly an important secondary resource in the region (Brown 1989). Assuming, however, that they might rank less than deer, the high percentage of turtles in Cultural Zones I, II, and V may I reflect stresses on the environment during these periods. This is further supported by high percentages of small mammal and bird bone in Zone I and the small but noticeable presence of mussel jn Zones I and II. Almost no information is available on the climate or paleoenvironment of Zone V, but Zone I seems I to be a warm/dry period according to the isotope analyses. Cultural Zone II is more of a mystery, however; stable isotope analysis suggests that this zone should be somewhat cooler and/or wetter even though the faunal exploitation data do not necessarily agree. Of some interest here is the fact that the I carbon isotope data are not in synch for this period and may indicate a somewhat different climatic or environmental pattern. I Invertebrate Fauna Molluscan remains from 41DL270 have been analyzed by Raymond Neck and are discussed in some detail in "Analysis of Molluscan Remains from 41DL270", this volume. His chapter documents I considerable evidence of the cultural utilization of these specimens, including numerous burned specimens, specimens which may have been imported into the site, and a few which have been modified for use. These elements of the invertebrate faunal remains from the site are discussed in more detail in I the section on archeological conclusions. Neck's study also provides some data regarding the paleoenvironment of the site. during its I occupation. While he notes several minor differences between the various components at the site, his general conclusions suggest that the degree of environmental variability may not be great. He points out that I The molluscan paleoassemblages from 41DL270 indicate patterns of heterogeneity in general environmental conditions on both a temporal and micro-spatial basis at a site and during a I time period that is generally indicated to be one of environmental homogeneity. ("Analysis of Molluscan Remains from 41DL270", this volume). I Despite this conclusion, he does venture tenuous guesses regarding minor changes which may be indicated by the molluscan fauna throughout his section. A few of the most important are summarized below. I Although it is not clear to what extent the general breakdown of species in the freshwater mussel population might indicate the general paleoenvironment, slight potential differences between the microhabitats preferred by different molluscan species may indicate general trends. Such trends may also I indicate cultural preferences of the site's inhabitants or even just random fluctuations of population dynamics through time. Neck points out the high species diversity (9 species) of the mussels from Feature 3 in Cultural Zone I relative to the very low diversity (4 species) from Feature 10 in Cultural I 288 I I

## Page 306

I I Zone III. While he suggests that it may be due to the number of features investigated, it is equally likely to be a result of sample size since diversity is a function of sample size. Nonetheless, it is interesting to note that the species diversity is lowest in the samples from Cultural Zones II and III, the zones which approximately parallel the wet/cool period described in the section in this report on shell isotope studies. If this relationship to climate is a real one then changing species diversity might be expected to be represented at different sites. Although mussels have been collected from numerous sites in North I Central Texas, there are few sites which resemble 41DL270 in depth and age of cultural deposits. Although somewhat distant, the McDonald Site (41HI105) in the Aquilla Reservoir (Brown et al. 1987) has a deeply stratified series of cultural deposits which include mussel shell. Additionally, Hackberry I Creek, on which the site is located is about the same order drainage and also flows along the boundary between the Cross Timbers and the Blackland Prairie. A total of 608 freshwater mussels were collected from the deep excavation unit in Area B at the McDonald Site, in comparison to 568 individuals from I 41DL270. At both sites, Amblema plicata is the dominant species through time comprising 42 percent of the total at 41DL270 and 62 percent overall at the McDonald Site. Both sites have relatively similar overall indices of diversity as well with an overall value of 4.3 for the McDonald Site and 3.2 for I 41DL270, using the following measure S-1 d=- logN I derived from Odum (1971: 144). Table 30 below shows a comparison between these two sites in species diversity through time. The dates for the McDonald Site, like those at 41DL270, are based on I interpolations between uncorrected radiocarbon dates based on a reexamination of the strata and available dates by the author (note: this does not represent a reinterpretation of the·M cDonald Site stratigraphy - only the creation of periods hypothetically contemporaneous with those at Denton Creek). Like the Denton Creek site, precise dating was a problem at the McDonald Site. Although altering the dates I slightly one way or the other does affect the diversity indices, the general trends remain the same. That general trend suggests fewer total shells and lower overall species diversity before the project beginning date for Cultural Zone II at 1750 B.P. The clusters of high species diversity at the McDonald Site are I quite clearly defined and well-separated (though perhaps poorly dated). Although the numbers are far from identical, it is interesting to note that the lowest number of I individuals and the lowest species diversity falls at about the same time range at both sites, between about 1750 to 2100 B.P. With respect to the climatic interpretations offered in the chapter on shell isotope studies, this falls in the first half of the cooler/wetter period that begins after around 2500 B.P. It also I coincides with the maximum exploitation of white-tailed deer at the site. Another interesting correlative factor is the numbers of Quadrula relative to Amblema. The former I are much more common relative to the latter in the suggested cooler/wetter period of Cultural Zones II and III. Clearly the sample sizes involved are too small for any convincing demonstration of such a relationship but it is nonetheless interesting and could reflect a real ecological indicator. If so, however, I it is not clear what ecological parameter since wetter times in Zones II and III would seem more likely to increase diversity rather than decrease it. Perhaps a cultural selection factor is operating as well; when more species are available, Quadrula are preferred, but in drier times, Amblema and other species are I chosen to fill the plate. Data from the McDonald Site suggest the opposite relationship. Amblema is overwhelmingly dominant (up to 93 % in period 3) during periods 2 and 3 while Lampsilis hydiana and L. teres become increasingly important (though still a minority species) during earlier and later periods. He identifies five different I microhabitats which form a continuum of potential habitats at the site: gallery woodland, open woodland, open habitat, open habitat with flood impact, flood deposits. Most of these habitats are (or have the potential to be with respect to the flood deposits) present on site today or within less than a hundred I meters. At any such large site, one might expect periodic changes in microhabitats on the basis of various hydrological effects such as flooding, increased water table, or meander cutting. Nonetheless, it is also possible that some long term trends are represented as well. I A brief scan of the correlation of microhabitats with the paleoclimate/paleoenvironmental periods determined on the basis of the shell isotope studies shows little suggestion of any strong correlation. Multiple habitats appear to be present during brief intervals of the wettest and driest periods. Gallery I forest is found, for example, during what is interpreted as one of the driest periods found at the site (around 2550 B.P. est.), while open habitat is found in one of the samples which spans Zone II, supposedly one of the wetter periods at the site. Remembering, however, that all habitats are found I today, such apparent discrepancies are irrelevant. What is interesting is that four of the five samples which represent Zone II represent either gallery woodland or open woodland habitats. In contrast, only two of the five samples from Zone IV represent woodland. Interestingly, two of the five from this time I period also represent flood deposits, one found in open habitat. An interesting analysis would be the actual micro-location of the sample on the site to see where I the creek might be. In today's case, the remains are for the most part parallel to the modern stream and within what one might think of as habitat which is equidistant from the creek. If the creek ran southwest northeast across the site at some point in the past, as it is thought to have done, there is a greater I likelihood of gallery forest in the units to the north than those to the south. In fact, four of the six units and backhoe trenches which are included with Neck's gallery and open woodland categories (Units 3, 11, and 14, and Trench M2) are located at the northernmost end of the area, closest to the projected channel. I If this is true, then the most significant samples for determining forest cover would be those from the units farthest from the channel, Units 14 and 6. In fact, the projected depth of these samples places them squarely within the cool/wet period between 2250 and 1250 B.P. The converse also seems to be true. I 290 I I
