I All of the samples which show open habitat or open habitat with flood impact (Units 12, 13, and 14, and I Trench M9) are from the central or southern portion of the sampled area, away from the proposed channel. Additionally, the densest cluster of materials, including the densest mussel shell features, is found at the northern end of the site, near this projected paleochannel. This is also discussed in the I section on archeological conclusions. Paleomagnetic Studies I Archeomagnetism has been utilized for dating of remains from archeological sites for many years (Aitken 1960; Bucha 1971; Michaels 1973). The basic method has changed little during that time, I although the gathering of numerous samples from various parts of the world has vastly improved the chronology of archeological samples. In recent years, the prospects of archeomagnetism for expanding beyond simple dating into providing a whole new set a data on archeological features (Schmidt 1980; I Riklis and Collins, in press) has opened up new horizons in this field of research. A limited set of experiments were conducted at 41DL270 with sandstone recovered from suspected hearths at the site, much like those conducted at the Camp Pearl Wheat site (Collins et al. 1990) and the Wilson Leonard I site (Collins 1993). As Gose reports in his section on archeomagnetic studies, several of the sandstone fragments from one feature (34) were determined to be approximately in place while fragments from two other features (35 and 36) were determined to have been disturbed. In general, the conclusions support I that this technique is highly applicable to the study of prehistoric sandstone features found in the region. This is discussed further in the archeological conclusions section of this report. I In addition to his work with the orientation of the rock samples, Gose studied the magnetic susceptibility of soil samples collected from the site. As he indicates, magnetic susceptibility measures the magnetic mineral content of a rock or soil sample. High susceptibility values have been linked to soil formation and to intense human land use, particularly when it involves the use of fire. A series of I samples were submitted from a number of controlled excavation units and backhoe trenches at the site. The combined pattern of these studies suggested three levels of magnetic susceptibility which Gose interprets as 1) significant human activity, 2) occasional occupation, and 3) little or no occupation. The I highest level of activity was recorded between about 141.00 and 142.00 meters elevation. The correspondence between this zone and the highest density of prehistoric remains from the site is staggering. Of all the cultural features recorded in the Late Holocene alluvial terrace at the site, only two I features and a few isolated artifacts were noted above or below this zone. Those few recorded below this zone all fall within his area of occasional occupation. I It is clear that this magnetic intensity is primarily due to fire-building in these levels at the site. More than 30 hearth features were observed along the eroded bank of the drainage ditch in the area where these samples came from. Some of the increased intensity, however, may also relate to the possible I interpretation of this area as a paleosol or at least an alluvial surface upon which deposition may have slowed or stopped for a short time. Unfortunately there is no way at present to certainly determine if the increase in human activity in this level at the site is due to actual increased population density, I increased utilization of the floodplains at this time, or, as suggested by this study, a combination of these with a slight slowing of deposition and at least the brief initiation of pedogenic processes. I Shell Isotope Studies One of the most interesting analyses undertaken in the process of studying the assemblage from I 41DL270 has been the examination of stable isotope data from freshwater mussel. Although this type 291 I I


![Image 1 - Page 308](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p308_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 308*


---

## Page 309

I of analysis has not been widely utilized, the preliminary results suggest at least some possible correlations with paleoclimate and ultimately with paleoenvironments as well. The section authored by Brown (this I volume) on stable isotope studies of shell provided a model of paleoclimate/paleoenvironment from both oxygen and carbon isotope analysis. While it is difficult at this point in the analysis to specify whether the values generated are due to rainfall, temperature, rainfall seasonality, or even evaporation values, I there is clearly some effect which is most dramatically expressed in the oxygen isotope values but which is generally mirrored by the carbon isotope values as well. Thus, while it is impossible to clearly state that the climate at this time was definitely warm, dry, or a combination of the two, etc., it is possible to I state that Cultural Zones I and IV, at least, appear to represent warmer and/or drier periods, while Zones III and VII may be cooler and/or wetter. Cultural Zone II also appears to be cooler/wetter but the carbon isotope data, as well as some of the faunal evidence, are equivocal in this regard. Nonetheless, there is I at least some support for the notion of a cooler/wetter period after 2500 B.P as noted by Toomey et al. (1994) among others, as well as a warming trend in the later period. I At this point the major problem with the isotope model, aside from the rather small overall sample size, is the lack of solid dates to anchor the interpretations. Even if it were proven that the isotope values were excellent climatic predictors, and even if it were possible to project rainfall and/or temperature I amounts (as has been attempted in some similar studies), the dates, based primarily on humate samples, could be hundreds of years off in either direction. Hopefully, any future studies at the site might take this into consideration. I PALEOENVIRONMENTAL SUMMARY This chapter has presented a summary and synthesis of the paleoenvironmental data collected from I the site and placed in the most current regional paleoenvironmental context. In the brief summary that follows, an overall picture emerges of the Late Holocene climates of North-Central Texas. This general synthesis of the paleoenvironmental data is in tum compared to those questions raised in the research I issues chapter. Two primary questions were raised in the research issues chapter, both regarding possible climatic changes and their correlation with the remains from 41DL270 and with the established cultural periods in the area. Both of these issues are dealt with again under different guises in the subsequent I chapter but a brief summary is also provided here. Paleoclimatic Change and Artifact/Feature Recovery I Several distinct types of features were recorded at 41DL270. On the eroded bank, the vast majority of features have a substantial component of sandstone. Despite this, the attempt to find fully I intact rock features was not wholly successful. All of the features were partly disturbed by erosion and/or ditch excavation and most were completely or near completely destroyed. While the project was able to pinpoint the original surface of the features and find one or more rocks still in place, it was very I difficult to create a useful and comprehensive typology of stone features at the site. Two feature types that were evident, however, are the rock-lined or rock-paved surface hearth and the partly rock-lined or rock-sealed pit. Only two definite examples of the latter were found (Features 35 and 7) although I certainly many more must exist. The latter of these two is found in Cultural Zone I and the former group (35) in Cultural Zone III. There is no immediate correspondence between these two zones either in projected paleoclimates or in subsistence patterns. I With respect to the non-pit rock-lined and paved features, only a few possible intact specimens were found at the site. The best of these (Figures 2 and 3) was actually excavated before the previous I 292 I I


![Image 1 - Page 309](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p309_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 309*


---

## Page 310

I project was initiated by Jan and Paul Lorrain (1993). Several other rock features recorded during the I testing phase are partly intact, however. Overall, the majority of the rock features investigated seem to have been surface hearths rather than pits. If this is the case, then the density of this particular type of feature is essentially the overall density of features. The highest density, of course, is recorded in I Cultural Zones II, III, IV, and V, with Zone IV having the apparent greatest density of all. Most of Zone IV and the nearly as dense Zone III fall within the projected cool/wet period following 2500 B.P. indicating that there may be a correlation between the use of these features and the climatic changes I suggested for the region. One other non-stone feature type, the mussel shell concentration, was common at the site. As a I subsistence resource, mussel utilization has been discussed in the previous chapter and is also discussed below. As a feature, however, it appears to have a somewhat limited distribution. True shell lenses, as opposed to scattered mussel shell, were primarily limited to the uppermost levels of the site, falling I primarily in Cultural Zones I and II. This seems to correlate with the suggestion that mussel shells were most commonly exploited during times of food stress (Brown 1987e), although the evidence from 41DL270, from other sites in the region (Anthony, "Mussel Shell Procurement in North-Central Texas," I this volume), and from sites at Aquilla Creek, also indicates that mussel were probably always a minor component of the diet at riverine sites. Given the level of typically low resolution of archeological data, it is unlikely that we will ever know if such minor mussel procurement events were due to very brief (and I thus essentially unrecognizable) conditions of stress, or were actually preferred under certain circumstances. Paleoclimatic Change and Cultural Periods I Going back at least as far as Dillehay (1974) there have been attempts to correlate cultural changes with environmental changes. If the data in this and the following chapter are to be believed, it would I seem that hunter-gatherers respond rather directly to even minor changes in the environment. And in fact, the notion that during bad years hunter-gatherers are satisfied to scrounge for whatever they can get, while in good years, they might tend to gorge themselves on overabundant high-ranked resources, is hard I to challenge. What is questionable is the extent of climatic change and the corresponding effect on biotic communities. Much of the evidence previously gathered and presented for climate change in Texas has been somewhat equivocal and the evidence for corresponding biota change has been even less certain. I In the present study, we have shown on a small scale at one site how changes in resource exploitation patterns may correlate with climate change. The evidence for change in artifact assemblages, overall technology, or cultural periodization is minimal at best, however. I This lack of evidence for cultural change is further complicated by the generally vague notion of cultural periods in North-Central Texas. Prikryl's (1990) critical re-evaluation of the cultural periods of I North-Central Texas has rescued what had previously been a basically untenable chronology and provided a new version which may make more sense within the broader regional perspective. Prikryl's study, however good, is somewhat hampered by the paucity of well-stratified sites and extensive excavations .in I the region, particularly prior to the Late Prehistoric, and more data is necessary to understand the finer details of the cultural chronology of the region. More carefully excavated sites are necessary, as are more chronometric dates and more subsistence related data. I I 293 I I

I I ARCHEOWGICAL SYNTHESIS David 0. Brown In this final section, several of the issues initially raised in the research design other than I paleoenvironmental studies (see "The Denton Creek Paleoenvironment", this volume, for a separate discussion of the paleoenvironment) are addressed. Two primary questions are addressed: chronology and settlement/subsistence patterns. In many ways both issues are only treated in a very cursory nature, I not because they are thought less important than the paleoenvironmental data, but because the nature and quantity of data recovered from the site during the most recent investigations is more suited to answering questions regarding the paleoenvironment. Along the same lines, the paucity of data relevant to the issues I of chronology and settlement/subsistence is not due to a lack of such data at the site. The site clearly still contains large areas of intact cultural deposits which have the potential to inform about many diverse aspects of the Late Holocene (and earlier?) cultures of North-Central Texas. I CHRONOWGY I Prikryl's (1990) chronology was presented in the "Research lssues"chapter in this volume. The relatively small amount of work conducted at 41DL270 is hardly sufficient to refine a chronology based on numerous well-documented and well-dated sites. Dating has been a particular problem at the site and I any future research conducted at the site should focus heavily on collecting a large series of dates from stratigraphic layers. Nonetheless, some very brief statements can be made regarding the research questions which were presented with respect to the topic of chronology. I Temporal Span or Occupation Although no dramatic surprises were encountered, new radiocarbon dates from the site have slightly I expanded the span of occupation from that recorded in the survey. The approximate span of occupation as estimated from the calibrated radiocarbon dates (see Table 31) is 750 B.P. to about 4800 B.P. Projectile points and other diagnostic artifacts recovered from the site during the excavations and/or I studied from Bill Young's collection have generally verified these dates. Table 29, in the previous chapter, presents the C13 corrected radiocarbon dates from the site while Table 31 below presents dates calibrated with the most recent version of CALIB (Stuiver and Reimer 1993; Stuiver and Pearson 1993; I Pearson and Stuiver 1993). The dates are shown in hypothetical stratigraphic order (as is discussed in "The Denton Creek Paleoenvironment", the stratigraphic ordering between the terrace and floodplain deposits is not exactly clear; since the dates collected overlap somewhat, there is little hope in resolving I this issue with the data at hand). As is clear from the table, there are a few minor changes which can be attributed to the calibration. I The youngest calibrated dates from the site tend to be somewhat younger while the older dates tend to be slightly older than the uncalibrated ones. There are several dates which reflect reversed stratigraphy, at least from the uncalibrated value. The sample from Zone 1 of Trench M7 (GX-19562), taken from I an elevation between 141.65 and 141.55 meters amsl, appears to be older than the sample from Trench n C4 (described as the original Cultural Zone in the survey report - Anthony et al. 1992), taken from approximately 141.40 meters amsl. These samples are obviously quite close in elevation and actually I only a short distance apart in horizontal distance. Despite this reversal, the samples overlap at a two sigma interval, and in any case may indicate a relatively long-stable surface associated with features in what we have designated as Cultural Zones IV and V. Another similar reversed age situation is found I in Cultural Zones VIll and IX where the sample from Trench 5, Zone 7 (GX-19561), talcen at an 295 I I

How do the proposed radiocarbon dates fit with the artifact sequence and Prikryl's (1990) proposed I chronology for the area? Only a handful of potentially diagnostic projectile points were recovered from the recent excavations. While insufficient to absolutely verify the radiocarbon ages from the site, much less to refine Prikryl's chronology, they do seem to be synchronous with the proposed chronology. The I highest diagnostic projectile points recovered from the site were two specimens found on the surface with what was designated as Feature 51, a Scallorn, and a Godley. As noted in the lithic artifact section (Anthony, this volume), the Scallorn was observed in the middle of a small rill and is thought to have I washed down from above. The Godley specimen, however, was found adjacent to the rill and may have eroded out in situ. I order - see Table 31), the projected date of this feature is around 2500 B.P. While this is obviously I too early for the Scallorn type, it is also a bit early for the Godley point as well. Prikryl (1990:57) lists only two dates for Godley points, neither of which is in the Dallas area. These are 1865 ± 70 B.P. from the Britton Site (41ML37) in McLennan County (Story and Shafer 1965:95), and after 1650 ± 70 B.P. I from the Ernest Witte Site (41AU36) in Austin County (Hall 1981:49). Turner and Hester (1985:102) do not list an absolute date range for this style, noting only that it occurs from the Late Archaic to the Late Prehistoric. Despite the apparently greater age (which is, after all, just interpolated) of the Godley, I it does fall well within Prikryl's Late Archaic Period (3500 to 1500 B.P.). Not far below the Godley point in relative elevation and not too distant horizontally as well was I a projectile point recovered from Unit 12, Level 3, Feature 9, at an elevation of 141.59 meters amsl, which has been identified as a Motley. Motley is relatively common in northeastern Louisiana where it was defined, but does occasionally occur in East Texas as well (Turner and Hester 1985: 130). Turner I and Hester assign it to the period between 500 and 1500 B.C. (2450 to 3450 B.P.). The estimated date for this point at 41DL270, based on the interpolation of dates from humate samples, is approximately 3100 B.P. I The only other definitely identified projectile point from the recent investigations is a Yarbrough type which was found in Unit 8, Level 2, at an elevation of 141.19 meters amsl (within Cultural Zone I V). This type is very poorly dated. Turner and Hester (1985: 160) note it as an Early Archaic style, possibly extending later in time, while Prikryl (1990:57-58) assigns it to his Late Archaic period and ± ± suggests that the dates, which include a dated range between 4550 270 and 3555 215 B.P. at the ± ± Scott Site (34LF11) in Oklahoma (Galm and Flynn 1978:79), and between 2060 120 and 1940 100 I B.P. from the Curtis Lake Site (34LF5A) in Oklahoma (Galm 1978), are problematic. Nonetheless, the interpolated date from 41DL270, which is approximately 3300 B.P., does fall between these two dates and within the Late Archaic period (albeit at the very beginning of the period). I Two other points from the investigations are tentatively identified (a Gary and a possible Palmillas) but neither identification is certain and, in fact, neither point style is tightly dated. Turner and Hester I (1985: 101) date the Gary between 2500 B.C. and A.O. 700-800, while they only assign the Palmillas style a designation as Middle to Late Archaic (Turner and Hester 1985: 134). Prikryl (1990:55-57) notes that the Gary has a long time span and may extend into the Late Prehistoric, while he only notes that the I Palmillas type is similar to the Godley. Both are found at elevations which give them an interpolated date which is Late Archaic (interpolated date for the Gary is 2950 B.P., for the Palmillas, 3000 B.P.), but both specimens were recovered from the eroded surface of the site and may have moved downward. If I they are in place, the dates are quite satisfactory. In general, the occupation of the site falls within Prikryl's Late Prehistoric I and Late Archaic I periods as well as the end of the Middle Archaic. The lack of earlier cultural materials may reflect either the limited investigations in deeper sediments or the possibility that the alluvial fill of the terrace overlies a much earlier unit and no Early Holocene sediments are present (see "Geomorphology and Quaternary I Stratigraphy of Denton Creek", this volume). It may also reflect cultural trends, such as increased population (Skinner 1981; Brown 1989) in the later periods. The density of features at various levels seems to correspond more closely with population estimates rather than geomorphic trends. See the I discussion below regarding cultural zones at the site. ## Page 315

I Cultural Zones I During the survey/testing phase, three tentative cultural zones were defined based on the seeming density of cultural materials at these elevations. Old Cultural Zone I was located at about 142.50 meters amsl, Old Cultural Zone II was located about a meter lower at 141.40 meters amsl elevation, and Old I Cultural Zone m, the least well-defined of the three, was estimated to lie between 140.75 and 141.00 meters amsl. Obvious clusters of features at the approximate elevations of the first two seemed to support this argument. While vertical changes in density are still apparent, the data recovery investigations I suggested a larger number of zones occupying much of the vertical extent of the bank. Overall, nine cultural zones were defined in the recent investigations. These zones were defined on the basis of apparently discrete vertical clusters of features exposed in the bank and were created for heuristic I purposes only to try to understand potential temporal variation in cultural activities at the site. In fact, the zonation is much less clear than at first perceived and it may be that new erosional exposures will create a completely different series of zones. Nonetheless, the zones utilized allowed the various I researchers to have convenient compartments within which to analyze variability. As several of the specialist chapters have shown, this variability is definitely present. I Care should be taken not to attempt any identification of the vertical zonation of features as cultural periods. It may in fact be that future excavations in the region will subdivide the Late Archaic into a number of subperiods, but that was not the intent of the zonation, nor is it likely that any such zonation I or subperiodization would hold up based on the evidence from a single multi-component site. With this in mind it is possible to actually analyze the zones as presented without further apology. These zones are shown below in Table 32. I Research Issue Summary Four questions are asked in the chronology section of the research issues chapter. The brief I discussion below offers tentative answers to these questions and poses several new ones. Span or occupations. Occupations at the site lasted between about 4200 to 750 B.P. While there I may be earlier occupation remains at the site, particularly in areas not sampled to the south (the current project sampled intensively only the northernmost one-fifth or less of the site), the trenches placed in the sampled area did not hint at any extensive occupations. There also remains the possibility of later I occupations at the site, again possibly to the south, but the current investigations did not reveal any substantial evidence of this. One possible clue to later occupations is the presence of a few ceramic sherds in Bill Young's collection from the site. Finally, as noted above, there are still few chronometric I dates from the site, and most of these are on bulk soil or soil humates, potentially unreliable materials. While the suggested dates are probably generally valid, there may be several hundred years variation in the actual dates of occupation. I Nature or Occupations. All evidence suggests that the cultural remains at the site are from a large number of ephemeral campsites. The vast majority of artifact remains are found in close association with I features and the density of cultural materials between features appears to be very low. Although interesting faunal remains were found in the excavations, the density of these remains does not suggest the kind of middens that accumulate during long-term occupations or permanent villages. It should be I noted, however, that the type of erosional exposure present and the nature of the archeological investigations do not lend themselves to the identification of structural remains or the discovery of patterning which might indicate a more permanent campsite. Furthermore, while the floodplain and first I 298 I I

I Correlation with Geomorphic Trends. The temporal span of the site can roughly be correlated with geomorphic trends (see above for correlation with paleoclimatic trends) in that these occupations may be generally contemporaneous with the formation of the terrace. Although the possibility has been I suggested that site density may have been a limited function of depositional rate, this does not generally seem to be the case. The greatest number of occupations of the site may well have occurred while the terrace was actively forming rather than while it offered a stable surface. On the other hand, the slowing I of deposition is certainly associated with the latest occupations of the site, during Prikryl's (1990) Late Prehistoric I. This may also represent Ferring's (1986) West Fork Paleosol. Of some interest here is the notion offered above that during the majority of the occupations the creek may have run across the north end of the site. ## Page 317

I mental reconstructions, is not unequivocal, but the proposed location just north of the apparently abrupt end of cultural materials in the ditch bank explains several aspects of the site (such as the abrupt end of I the materials) which do not make any particular sense with the creek in its present channel. Correlation with &tablished Cultural Sequences. Finally, although the dates of the zones and I particularly the individual features are generally interpolated from a very limited set of chronometric dates, there is a reasonably good correlation between known diagnostic materials from certain cultural periods and the approximate dates. In general, it is felt likely that the use of humate and bulk soil dates I may offer ages which are slightly older than the actual date (this is apparently the case with the Godley point which seems to be dated a bit too old, but it may have moved downward on the bank as well). Nonetheless, while the entire sequence might be slightly younger (in the extreme outside case, possibly I as much as 500 years), the ages seem relatively consistent with both the paleoenvironmental and cultural data. In general, the data do not support any major revision of the periods as proposed by Prikryl, although it may ultimately be possible to subdivide his Late Archaic period if the differences in I subsistence patterns observed at 41DL270 are widespread and if they can be correlated with changes in prehistoric social systems. I SE'ITLEMENT/SUBSISTENCE Only very limited data can be brought to bear on the nature of the campsites present at 41DL270. I The data from artifacts and features do not suggest any dramatic differences in time in the manner of utilization of the site. The site appears to have always been, as it is today, a riparian environment whose possibilities were variously exploited through time. There is no evidence, for example, of more permanent occupations or special purpose utilization of the site at any particular time. Overall, however, I the range of activities seen for the site appears limited, certainly more so than one might expect from some kind of permanent base camp. As noted above, this impression may be in part a function of the investigative method, which focused on vertically oriented data sets rather than horizontal ones. Other I than the most general correlations, it is generally impossible to establish even the relative contemporaneity of features found within the same cultural zone. This is a problem of any such multi-component site; even careful wide-area horizontal excavations would not necessarily demonstrate such contemporaneity. I Subsistence data, on the other hand, provide a much clearer picture of the activities of the inhabitants of the site and suggest the possibility of changing patterns of resource utilization through time. I These changes in resource procurement are thought to be directly related to paleoenvironmental changes at the site. While the changes may be microlocal, due to conditions in the immediate area of the site, some of the data (particularly the isotope studies) suggests that the changes may be part of a regional I process. The research questions pertinent to questions of settlement and subsistence, presented above in the "Research Issues" chapter, are addressed briefly below. I Range or Site Activities. When the site was first visited, it was noted that there were few lithics present. Since that time, more materials have been uncovered or observed in private collections. One feature (25) may even be a small chipping station. It would appear that, particularly given the relatively I low density of artifacts observed or collected, that there is a reasonable variety of lithic artifacts present at the site, in contradistinction to the earlier observations. In her chapter on lithic analysis in this volume, Anthony has discussed the implications of this sample. The small sample, however, does not I allow much more than speculation concerning such activities. I 300 I I


![Image 1 - Page 317](assets/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery/00 Anthony and Brown 1994 - 41DL270 Data Recovery_p317_img1.png)


*Extracted 1 images/charts from Page 317*

## Page 318

I I Feature Associations. There has been considerable discussion of mussel procurement at the site, and at least some discussion of the function of the hearths. The presence of other faunal materials does not suggest sole utilization of the site as a mussel procurement area. While mussel shell remains are a very visible part of the surface component of the site, the calculation of meat weights and relative food I values suggests that mussel may have never been more than a few percent of the diet overall. Examination of the distribution of mussel and rock features does not suggest any particular correlation, although the recovery of some burned rocks within shell features and the common charring found on I shells at the site suggests that rock features may have been used to cook or at least steam open the mussels. Nonetheless, rock features are well distributed throughout the area of occupation while mussel shell features are more limited in their distribution. The lack of shell observed with many suspected I hearth features suggests that they were primarily utilized for cooking other foods such as deer and other mammals. I Ground Stone and Plant Processing. No ground stone was found at the site, despite the fact that Woodbine sandstone, a potentially excellent material for grinding implements (and used for such at many prehistoric sites), was quite common in the burned rock features at the site and is probably available in I local stream gravel outcrops or even in sandstone bedrock outcrops to the west. Since the level ef investigations at the site was not very intense, it may be that such implements are present in low numbers but as yet undiscovered. Nonetheless, there is very little clear evidence of plant processing. Plant I remains from the site indicate some species which might be economic plants but there is no indication that they were definitely used as such. I Lithic Raw Material Utilization. The range of lithic raw materials and the proposed nature of lithic procurement is discussed in the "Artifact Analysis" chapter. As noted there, it is not possible to determine the exact source of the lithic materials utilized at the site, but most are available within the locally abundant terrace gravels which derive ultimately from the Ogallala formation of West Texas. I Whether these gravels were found on the now destroyed Pleistocene terrace remnant adjacent to the site or on recently quarried terrace remnants that lie across the creek to the northwest, or whether they came from some greater distance simply cannot be stated with confidence. The general paucity of lithic I materials at the site and the absence (save one primary flake) of primary reduction debris suggests that the area of procurement was not close by. Finally, only a few materials at the site suggest long-distance procurement. One fairly clear specimen is the Motley point collected from Feature 9. This point is I manufactured from a chert-like material that is not found in the local terrace gravels and probably, like the point itself, comes from some distance to the east of the project area. Whether it was traded in and utilized by local peoples or whether the particular feature that it was associated with might represent a I non-local band travelling through the area simply cannot be ascertained. Subsistence Changes. The changes in subsistence have been documented in the chapter on faunal I remains from the site and in "The Denton Creek Paleoenvironment", which attempts a synthesis of paleoenvironmental data. In overall perspective, it is clear that the site's inhabitants relied largely on hunting large mammals, particularly deer, as a primary subsistence strategy. In all cases except during I the earliest periods at the site when the sample is quite small, deer comprise the largest single item in the diet, particularly in biomass estimates. The percentage of deer biomass harvested during the different cultural periods ranges from less than 50 to more than 85 percent. As noted in "The Denton Creek I Paleoenvironment", there is a reasonably strong correlation between changes in subsistence patterns and suggested paleoclimatic changes. Figure 94 shows the general trends in subsistence recorded in the cultural zones at the site compared to the paleoclimatic reconstruction as provided by freshwater shell I isotope data reported in this volume. 301 I I

## Page 320

I As presented in the composite graph, the apparent changes in subsistence patterns are reasonably I predictable given some knowledge of prehistoric behavior and resource value. As predicted by optimal foraging theory (Winterhalder and Smith 1981), subsistence is narrowly focused upon the procurement of deer, theoretically ranked second only to bison in subsistence value (Keene 1981), in what appears to I be one of the cooler/wetter periods at the site while one of the corresponding warm/dry periods witnesses a relatively dramatic broadening of the diet through the inclusion of lower ranked resources such as small mammals, turtles, birds, and shellfish. During one other warm period prior to about 2500 B.P., a slight I broadening of the resource base is also marked by the presence of bison, the theoretically highest ranking resource. The inclusion of bison in the diet is almost certainly a factor of the presence or absence of bison in the area, and in tum, following Huebner (1990) suggests a warming trend which increases the I regional density of the bison's favored habitat, grasslands. In sum, the apparent close correlation between subsistence changes and climatic trends supports the premise that hunter-gatherer subsistence patterns are a direct function of climate. I A final point might be made with respect to resource procurement changes at the site. For a more complete understanding of faunal changes at the site, a more comprehensive picture of resource value, I linked to potential availability changes, will be necessary. Given equal density of resources and relatively equal energy expended in procurement, the ranking of resources is generally apparent. When present, bison may be the most important single resource. When not present, deer is clearly the predominant food I source. When bison disappear from the spectrum of resources exploited, it seems likely that they do not change in actual value, but rather the energy expended in procurement when there are few or no bison in the area is prohibitive. When deer percentages drop, the assumption is that deer availability drops and thus the cost of capture increases dramatically. At that point, the lesser total energy expended for smaller I mammals and stationary mussel populations as well as other aquatic resources makes them a much more attractive resource. Despite extensive data which suggests such changes, there has been little comprehensive effort to establish any model for the area of central or North-Central Texas (beyond that I which was attempted by Shaw and Huckaby 1978; Brown 1987b; and Brown 1989). Such a compilation is long overdue. I CONCLUDING REMARKS The recent investigations at 41DL270 have yielded an interesting body of data which answers a I number of questions posed at the site and which provide additional data regarding the region as a whole. Overall, the data collected from the site provide an excellent view of occupations there during the Late Holocene. While limited, the investigations suggest that the site has enormous potential to yield further I data on local and regional adaptations despite the damage from a variety of potentially destructive sources over the years (Anthony et al. 1992). Furthermore, the data collected have been brought to bear on regional issues with some degree of success. While no single site will ever be sufficient to characterize I the lifeways of a region, the data from 41DL270 can now be added to a growing regional data base through which we can gain a more detailed perspective on Late Holocene occupation of the region. I I I 303 I I
